MAN, GOD, and CIVILIZATION






CHAPTER ONE
The Early Ages of the World
MAN is a child of this earth, and if we would understand his origin and
evolution, we must learn something about the history of the planet
upon which he dwells. Any theory as to how the world began must, of
necessity, contain a large element of speculation, since none of us was
present to witness that important event. As we all know, the earth is
one of a number of planets spiraling around the sun as it floats through
space.
Now, the sun is a star, and it is similar to many other stars which
twinkle like jewels set in the dome of the night sky. The other stars are
also blazing balls of fire, but they are so far away that they appear only
to be little points of light. With the unaided eye, only a few thousand
stars are visible, but the telescope and camera reveal them in such vast
numbers that attempting to count them would be a sheer waste of time.
The stars of the cosmos are as numerous as all the grains of sand on all
the seashores of the world. As these stars wander through space, many
of them travel in groups, but the majority of them just journey along as
solitary vagabonds.
The cosmos is so vast that a star is like a ship on an empty sea. Imag
ine stars as ships on a great ocean, so far apart that one ship will rarely
come within a million miles of another. With millions of stars wan
dering blindly through space for millions of years, it seems probable
that occasionally two stars might come close to each other, or even ac
tually meet in a head-on collision.
Cosmologists have promulgated theories of the origin of the world.
The late Sir James Jeans thought that billions of years ago, as our sun
roamed aimlessly through space, it was approached by a larger wan
dering star; and that the gravitational pull of the passing star was so
powerful as to raise gigantic tides on the sun. This gravitational stress



4 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
was so great that fragments of the sun were ejected into space; and
after the roving star passed on, the detached fragments continued to
circulate around the sun. In time they cooled off and became planets,
and among them was this old world of ours.
This theory, though plausible, has in recent years been superseded
by another, promulgated by Professor Raymond Lyttleton. Astrono
mers have found huge stars scattered throughout space that sometimes
become unstable and explode like huge hydrogen bombs. These stars
are called supernovae, and Lyttleton opines that one of these celestial
giants was a binary partner of the sun about five billion years ago. This
star was destroyed by a supernova explosion, and some of the debris of
the great blowup was captured by the sun. Eventually this matter
differentiated into the several planets of the solar system, of which our
own world is one. (For details of this hypothesis, the reader is referred
to 148,* pp. 170-74.)
For thousands of years after separating from the sun, the earth was a
flaming sphere of gas, but the extreme cold of interstellar space
( — 273° centigrade) gradually reduced it to a state of liquidity. Then
came the birth of the moon. While the earth was still in the fluid condi
tion and rotating more rapidly than at present, a large blob developed,
somewhat like the knob on a lemon, and this protuberance became de
tached and rounded itself into our neighbor satellite, the moon.
After more thousands of years of cooling, the earth began to wear a
solid crust. The heavier elements sank into the interior, while the
lighter ones floated to the surface. In those days, the earth was a white
hot sphere surrounded by a thick mantle of red-hot gases: hydrogen,
oxygen, nitrogen, and carbon dioxide. As the cooling process pro
ceeded, oxygen and hydrogen combined, forming water, which hov
ered over the hot crust of the earth as a blanket of steam. With the pas
sage of much time, the steam condensed into water, and settled down
over the world as a boiling ocean. There was not, at that time, much
land in the world—just a few ridges poking up through the primeval
sea—and the water was fresh, for all the salt in the sea was later washed
out of the land.
But let's not travel too fast; from whence did the land come? The
original solid crust of the earth was probably as smooth as the surface of
a billiard ball. Had it remained that way, the primordial ocean would
have covered the entire surface of the globe. In other words, there
* References throughout this volume are keyed by number to entries in the Bibliography.



THE EARLY ACES OE THE WORLD 5
would have been no dry land. Bur as the heavier elements of the inte
rior of the earth lost heat through volcanic activity, they contracted;
and this caused the outer crust to fall in toward the interior, and to
crumple up like the skin of a baked apple; and the ridges and depres
sions thus formed were the first mountains and valleys. The battle beT
tween land and sea began as soon as the world cooled enough to hold
an ocean. The struggle has been a long one, but the land steadily
gained on the water. Life arose in the sea, but the greatest episodes in
the drama of life took place on the land.
The problem of the origin of life is one of great interest. The world
was in existence hundreds of millions of years before it became a suita
ble abode for living things, for life can occur only within a very re
stricted zone of temperatures. There can be no life in the stars, since
they are too hot. In outer space, the intense cold likewise makes life im
possible. Thus life must have originated when the earth developed a
temperature that was neither too hot nor too cold, for life cannot sur
vive where it is either roasted or frozen. Of the one hundred-odd ele
ments of which the stuff of the universe is composed, only a very few
show any affinity for life. Living matter consists mainly of atoms which
possess the property of forming large molecules. Atoms of hydrogen
(H), for instance, may combine to form molecules of hydrogen (H2
and H 3 ); or atoms of oxygen may form molecules of oxygen (0 2 ) or ozone (0 3 ); and atoms of hydrogen and oxygen may combine to form
molecules of water (H 20) and hydrogen peroxide (H 2 2 ). But none of
these compounds has more than four atoms per molecule, and the nitro
gen compounds behave in a similar way. But, if to the atoms of hydro
gen, oxygen, and nitrogen we add carbon, a most extraordinary thing
happens. The atoms of hydrogen, oxygen, nitrogen, and carbon com
bine to form molecules consisting of hundreds, even thousands, of
atoms. Carbon is of prime importance in the chemistry of life.
So life first arose in the warm primeval ocean, beginning with the
evolution of chemicals capable of assimilating carbon. Of the early his
tory of life we have no record, since the earliest living things had nei
ther bones nor shells, and hence left no traces in the rocks. At the end
of the Precambrian era, animal life had not yet reached the level of the
insect or the fish. The next geological age, the Paleozoic era, is divided
into six periods.
The earliest subdivision of the Paleozoic era is known as the Cam
brian period. The five other periods, in order of succession, were the



MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Ordovician, Silurian, Devonian, Carboniferous, and Permian. The
dominant animal type of the Cambrian period was the trilobite—a flat
animal with a broad head shaped like a shovel, and with a three-lobed
shell on its back. It possessed numerous legs, with which it crawled and
swam, and had large compound eyes. A forerunner of the shrimp and
the crab, the trilobite reached its climax in the Ordovician period and
then gradually declined. The teeming life of the Cambrian seas (be
sides the trilobites, there were shellfish, sponges, jellyfish, and corals)
continued to develop in the Ordovician period. It was a time of great
volcanic activity, and the Ordovician seas brought forth the earliest
vertebrates. These primitive vertebrates were strange- looking crea
tures, half fish and half arthropod, and are known as ostracoderms.
They looked like primitive armor-plated fish, though they lacked artic
ulated jaws.
In the Silurian period, great revolutions occurred. There was an ad
vance of the sea on the land. The first land plants and land inverte
brates (scorpions) appeared, Echinoderms or sea lilies were the charac
teristic marine fauna. But the great event of the Silurian period was the
emergence of the true fish, and the first animal of this type was proba
bly a species of shark. The ancestor of the fish is believed to have
evolved from the cartilage notochord of some Cambrian or Silurian an
nelid (a primitive worm). Later, this cartilaginous rod divided into sec
tions (vertebrae) and hardened into bone. This backbone not only
stiffened the body of the fish, but ako provided protection for the spinal
cord; and the upper part of the backbone expanded into a boxlike struc
ture to enclose the brain. As these early fish wriggled through the
water, folds of skin sprouted out at the sides and on the back and
evolved into fins. The fish was also improved by the appearance of gill
slits in the throat, and the formation of jaws contributed to the develop
ment of the mouth. In the Devonian period, the fish were the highest
type of life in the world. There was a rise of land, and many lakes dried
up. The land was covered with plants, and insects were numerous. The waters became overcrowded, and some species of fish evolved into land
animals. A group of Devonian fishes, who lived about four hundred
million years ago, known as the Crossopterygii (lobe-finned fish), pos
sessed lungs as well as gills, so when overcrowding occurred in the
water, some of these fish, using their fins as rudimentary feet, took to
the land and changed over from gill breathing to lung breathing. In
switching from sea to land, some of the fins of these primitive fish were
evolved into feet, the rays of the fins developing into the toes of the



THR EARLY AGKS OF THE WORLD 7
feer. The evolution from worm to fish was a great ehapter in the story
of life. It may seem hard to believe that ancient wormlike animals
evolved into mollusks, echinoderms, crustaceans, and insects, and that
from the echinoderms (starfish, sea urchins, sea lilies, etc.) were devel
oped the chordates, the ancestors of all the vertebrates, including fish and man.
In the worm, eyes are pits in the skin, lined with pigment cells; in
the fish they have become crystalline lenses, able to concentrate light
and convey it to the optic nerve. In the worm, the ears begin as sensi
tive pits on the skin, in each of which lies a little stone, which give the
worm a sense of direction. In the fish, there are closed vesicles in the
skull, which have developed a sensitivity to sound waves. In the evolu
tion from worm to fish, we see the nose changing from two pits in the
skin of the head into nostrils opening into the mouth, and with an ol
factory nerve lining the passages.
The brain grows from a clump of nerve cells to an intricate structure
with a primitive cerebrum. The heart, only a swollen part of a blood
vessel in a worm, becomes in the fish a two-chambered organ for the
pumping of blood. And just as certainly as the paleontologist can trace
the fish back to the worm, the biologist can trace man's ancestors back
to the fish.
The Carboniferous period was a time of shallow seas, vast swamps,
and low-lying lands; and there was a moist and warm climate all over
the earth. The Carboniferous was the age of coal forests. The world
was blanketed by a humid atmosphere containing a high concentration
of carbon dioxide. The forests consisted of gigantic club mosses, ferns,
and horsetails. Some club mosses reached heights of over a hundred
feet, and tree ferns reared themselves twenty to sixty feet into the air.
In these dismal forests, the amphibians enjoyed a golden age. The am
phibians were evolved from the crossopterygians (lobe-finned fishes).
The double pair of fins of the lobe-fins were developed into the legs
and feet of the amphibians. The five rays of the piscine fin became the
five toes of the amphibian foot. Giant insects, spiders, and myriapods
swarmed over the land, so the sluggish amphibians had an ample diet.
The insects (over a thousand species) were of a primitive type: Orthop
tera (crickets, locusts, cockroaches, etc.) and Neuwptera (dragonflies,
mayflies, etc.). The more advanced insects, such as the Coleoptera (bee
tles) and Hymenoptera (ants, bees, etc.), had not yet evolved.
After millions of years of perpetual summer, the world began to grow colder. There was a rise in the land, which was instrumental in



8 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
bringing on an ice age. Thar ended the Carboniferous and ushered in
the Permian period. This was the Third Ice Age. (The two earlier ice
ages occurred before there was any land life, so we need not consider
them.) The rise in the land was not the sole cause of the increasing
cold. The vast coal forests had absorbed an enormous amount of carbon
dioxide from the humid Carboniferous atmosphere, and the combina
tion of a purer, thinner atmosphere and the elevation of great land
masses brought on the great Permian revolution. The colder climate
destroyed the great forests of the Carboniferous swamps, which we
now find buried underground in the form of coal.
Some of the amphibians, meanwhile, were changing with the cli
mate. They evolved from amphibians into reptiles. The appearance of
the reptile heralds the dawn of a new era; for from the reptiles were to
be developed the birds and mammals, the future rulers of the air and
the land, respectively.
With the close of the Permian period, the Paleozoic era reached its
end. This era began about 550 million years ago, and lasted for over
350 million years.
The next great division of geological time is known as the Mesozoic
era, the geological middle ages. This era began about 200 million years
ago, and lasted for more than 150 million years. It is subdivided into
three periods: Triassic, Jurassic, and Cretaceous. The Paleozoic era is
known as the Age of Fishes, because the fish was the dominant type of
animal life in that time. Similarly the Mesozoic era is called the Age of
Reptiles. How did the reptile branch off from the Paleozoic amphibian
and become the new monarch of the animal world? An explanation,
both accurate and clear, has been advanced by an outstanding author
ity:
It seems to be a well-established fact that profound changes in the natural environment have produced fundamental changes in the plant and animal
realms. Thus the late Paleozoic and early Mesozoic was a time of one of the
most profound and far-reaching physical disturbances in the known history of the earth. Great mountains were being made in many parts of the world,
particularly in eastern North America and in Europe; the lands were in
creased in size and height; one of the two greatest known Ice Ages was a
feature of the Permian; and the ocean waters were affected in various ways.
These physical changes in turn caused climatic changes, altered habitats of
plants and animals, and modified sources of food for the animals. Accom
panying these changes the giant lycopods, seed ferns and cordaites became
extinct, while higher plants such as cycads and conifers began to clothe the



THE EARLY AGES OF THE WORLD 9
earth. Large groups of animals, such as tetracoralla, Mastoids, orthoceras,
trilobites and eurypterids disappeared from the waters; amphibians culmi
nated; and hexacoralla, insects, reptiles and mammals made their appear
ance. (171, p. 222)
The reptiles became the ruling race of the Mesozoic era because
they were the best fitted for survival under the prevailing natural con
ditions. Both birds and mammals branched off from the reptilian stem
in this era, but conditions were not then favorable for their rise and de
velopment; so they remained in the background while the reptiles con
quered the land, the sea, and the air. The dominant animals of land and
air during the Mesozoic were known as the Ruling Reptiles. In their
early days, many of them were small, rapid-running bipeds, standing
half-erect on their hind legs.
From such b ginnings we got the dinosaurs. Some dinosaurs were
huge beasts whose skeletons may be seen in museums, and others were
no larger than our contemporary lizards. The first carnivorous dino
saurs appeared early in the Triassic period, and they were followed by
herbivorous dinosaurs, who did not show up until near the end of the
same period. Fossil remains of dinosaurs were first uncovered in Lower
Triassic rocks in Germany, but they were widely distributed in both
the Old and New Worlds, spreading from Europe to central Asia, over
southern and eastern Africa, to India and Australia; to Patagonia in
South America; and in North America, from the Atlantic coast to the
Rocky Mountains and British Columbia. At first they lived in the drier
areas, such as the Connecticut Valley in New England. Here the geol
ogists have found their huge footprints in the rocks. In the Jurassic pe
riod, the dinosaurs seem to have dwelt mainly in low-lying coastal
lands, where the climate was hot and moist, and in swamps, lagoons,
and sluggish streams. The largest of the Jurassic dinosaurs was Bronto
saurus Excelsus, a monster who was about seventy feet long, with a
weight of about thirty-seven tons, supported by four stout pillarlike
legs, attached to a short compact body, fitted with a long neck and tail
and a relatively small head, with spoon-shaped teeth in the front of its
jaws. Brontosaurus was too heavy to walk on dry land, so he spent his
days wading in shallow waters and devouring large quantities of water
plants. A contemporary of Brontosaurus was Allosaurus, a thirty-four
foot carnivorous brute, armed with daggerlike teeth and huge talons.
The meat-eater and the vegetarian were most certainly not close
friends. As a neighbor, these beasts had Stegosaurus, who was bigger



10 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
than the largest elephant, walked on all fours, was heavily armor
plated, with two rows of bony plates sticking up on its back, and a
spiked tail. Accompanying all this bulk was a small head topped by a
horny beak; bur the poor animal had practically no mental equipment;
for its diminutive skull housed a brain that weighed only about two and
a half ounces. Another Jurassic dinosaur was an unarmored bipedal
vegetarian known as Camptosaurus, only ten feet in length, who lived
in North America. By the end of the Jurassic era, the Brontosaurus and
his friends (and enemies) had passed off the stage of evolving life. In
the Cretaceous period we meet with a new collection of monsters; for
in those days the ruling reptiles were the Trachodon, Iguanadon, Tri
ceratops Elatus, and Tyrannosaurus Rex. The Trachodon had webbed fore and hind limbs and a laterally
compressed tail which permitted it to swim, as well as wade, through
the waters. Its skin was covered with small scales, arranged in a definite
pattern, and it had a broad toothless beak shaped like that of a duck,
covered with a leathery or horny skin. The rear half of each jaw bore numerous rows of vertical teeth. (There were twenty-seven to thirty
rows, each row containing ten to fourteen teeth.) This strange-looking
animal was entirely harmless, spending his days in wading along shal
low streams and munching horsetail rushes. Iguanadon bore a close re
semblance to Trachodon, but was larger in size, and not so specialized,
except for large spikelike thumbs. Triceratops Elatus was one of the
Ceratopsia, or horned dinosaurs, and had a body of about twenty-five
feet in length and a skull over eight feet long. An enormous head was
connected with a bulky body, which had bowed legs and a short tail.
Triceratops was an ugly monster, for on top of his head was a big bony
crest, and in front of the crest was a long face and a deep muzzle,
armed with a beak. On the nose and above the eyes were two long
sharp horns, pointed straight forward. But the greatest of the dinosaurs
of the Cretaceous period was Tyrannosaurus Rex, for he was the mon
arch of all he surveyed.
There were other great reptiles, some of which took to the high seas,
and others to the air. The typical marine reptile of Mesozoic times was
the ichthyosaurus. This ancient lizard was shaped like the modern por
poise, with a long mouth full of prehensile teeth, a potent propelling
sharklike tail, with a vertical fin, a dorsal fin, and reduced hind limbs.
The fore limbs, once adapted to locomotion on land, had evolved into
paddles. It roamed the seas and subsisted on a diet of fish and cephalo
pods.



THE EARLY ACES OK THE WORLD
The great flying reptile was the pterodactyl, which developed a bat
like method of flight by means of a membrane stretched from its arms.
The fourth finger became greatly elongated and gave support to the
membranous wing. Some of these flying reptiles were as small as rob
ins, others as large as eagles. By the end of the Cretaceous period, after
millions of years of dominance, the ruling reptiles passed from the
scene.
Today the only surviving branches of the dynasty of dinosaurs are
the crocodiles and the alligators. While the ruling reptiles held the cen
ter of the stage during the Mesozoic era, there were other animals in
the wings: the birds and mammals, the dominant types of future ages.
Birds were developed from egg-laying reptiles; and this fact answers
the question, "Which came first, the hen or the egg?" Since a hen is a
bird, and the first birds evolved from egg-laying reptiles, then the egg
must have come first. Two skeletons of the oldest fossil bird (archaeop
teryx) have been uncovered in the Jurassic strata of Germany. The ar
chaeopteryx was decidedly reptilian in certain characteristics; for it had
a long dinosaurian tail with feathers growing on the sides; and it had
teeth in its jaws. Its wings possessed feathers, but resembled reptilian
arms, and ended in clawed fingers. Modern birds differ from reptiles in
having warm blood, feathers, wings instead of arms, and a horny beak
without teeth; but teeth are present in the modern bird embryo. Feath
ers were evolved from the scales of reptiles, and the avian foot still re
sembled that of the dinosaur. The mammals evolved from a group of reptiles known as the cyno
donts (the word cynodont meaning dog-toothed). In most reptiles, the
teeth vary in size in different parts of the jaw, but in the cynodonts the
teeth were differentiated into incisors, canines, and cheek teeth. The
mammals of the Mesozoic era were small animals, ratlike in both size
and appearance. While the dinosaurs roamed over the land and waded
in the waters, the primitive mammals, the ancestors of man, took to the
trees. At the end of the Cretaceous period, the ruling reptiles became
extinct, and the Age of Reptiles was over. The era following, known as the Cenozoic, was the Age of Mam
mals. But the question naturally presents itself: What killed off the
mighty dinosaurs? The answer seems to point to a change in climate.
Reptiles are coldblooded animals, and during the millions of years of
the Mesozoic, when climate was generally mild, they laid their eggs on
the surface of the earth, and let the heat of the sun hatch them out.
When the climate became cooler and culminated in a great chill, the

THE EARLY AGES OF THE WORLD 13
nificance of this fact is pointed out by Admiral C M. Beadnell:
The blood of the higher mammal is relatively hot, and its temperature is
more or less constant—that is, it is not unduly influenced by the surround
ing temperature. The blood of a reptile is relatively cold, with however, a
wide range of temperature variation that is directly related to the changing temperature of the environment. If the monotrematous mammals, Echidna
and Platypus, are really connecting links between the lower reptiles and the
higher mammals, we should expect their blood to be neither hot nor cold,
but lukewarm, and we should further expect it to have a fairly wide range
of temperature variation. Now the temperature of these animals is actually
intermediate between that of reptiles and the higher mammals, while the
range of temperature-variation is very liberal, as much as 1 3 degrees Cen
tigrade in the case of Echidna. (10, p. 117)
The most familiar living example of the Metathem is the Opossum,
for the eggs of the opossum are hatched within the body of the mother. The young opossums are born small and immature, and spend their
nursing period in a pouch on the belly of the mother, where they cling
fast to the nipples. Other living forms of the marsupials (pouched
mammals) are the kangaroo and rock wallaby of Australia.
The Eutheria are those mammals which nourish their young in the
uterine stage via a structure known as the placenta, and the higher
mammals, including the human family, are members of this subclass. The earlier members of the higher-mammalian stock were small ani
mals with long slender rails, five-toed limbs, forty-four teeth, and a
small head and brain. Our modern Imecttiora can be traced back to certain shrewlike creatures of the Eocene, and the insectivores are in the main line of mammalian evolution. According to Shipley and
McBride:
The most interesting circumstance about the Insectivores is the fact that when, by means of fossils, we trace back the higher groups of mammals,
they all seem to merge imperceptibly into Insectivores—i.e. the primitive
insect-eaters. There is really good ground for supposing that the living In
sectivores, though modified in special details, nevertheless represent, so far
as their general organization is concerned, the earliest type of Eutheria which appeared on the globe. From these original Insectivores advance
seems to have taken place along five lines: I. Some Insectivores took to attacking larger prey, including their own
less fortunate relations, and gradually developed into the Carnivore, or
flesh-eating mammals. These are tigers, cats, dogs, wolves, etc. II. Some became vegetable feeders, and gave rise to the great group of



J4 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
hoofed animals, relying either on their swiftness or size or strength for de
fense. These are horses, cows, elephants, etc.
III. Some took to burrowing, and developed into gnawers or rodents,
relying chiefly on their holes for safety. These are rabbits, rats, mice, squir
rels, etc.
IV. Some took to the air, the fore-limb becoming changed into a wing.
These are the bats.
V. The remainder took to escaping into trees when hard pressed, and
eventually gave rise to the great group of the primates, which includes
monkeys and man. (Cited in 10, p. 124)
The order Primates is composed of the lemurs, monkeys, apes, and
man, and these animals have the following common characteristics:
prehensile hands and feet; a clavicle (collarbone); flat nails on the
fingers and toes, instead of claws; one pair of breasts, as a rule; and a
brain, which may attain a relatively large size and great intricacy of de
velopment. There are three suborders of Primates: Lemuroidea, Tar
soidea, and Anthropoidea. In the Lemuroidea the eyes are directed out
ward, and are set in a bony ring. In the other suborders, the eyes have
the surrounding bony ring and, in addition, a bony back wall shaped
like a bowl; and the eyes are directed forward in such a way as to cause
the fields of vision to overlap, thereby creating a stereoscopic image.
The Anthropoidea have other characteristics in common: namely, the
occipital lobes of the cerebrum hang over the cerebellum; the brain is
relatively large and complex; and there is only one pair of breasts, lo
cated on the chest. In the suborder Anthropoidea, there are five fami
lies: two families of New World monkeys—the Hapalidae and the Ce
bidae; and three families of Old World apes—the Cercopithecidae,
Simiidae, and Hominadae. The New World monkeys are platyrrhine (broad-nosed), and the Old World apes catarrhine (down-nosed). Of
special interest to us are the Simiidae, or anthropoid apes, of which
there are four genera: the gibbon, orang-utan, chimpanzee, and gorilla.
These first anthropoids have been traced back to the Oligocene period,
having evolved from small tarsiods of the Eocene. The anthropoid an
cestors of the gibbon, orang, chimpanzee, gorilla, and man branched
from the common ape stock about twenty-five million years ago, in the
Miocene period. In other words, man is descended from a Miocene ape
who lived twenty-five million years ago. We find the first traces of
human fossils in the Pliocene period, after which came the Pleistocene,
better known as the Great Ice Age.
Our remote ancestors were tree-dwelling mammals; but, as they in



THE EARLY AGES OF THE WORLD 15
creased in size and weight, they became roo bulky and heavy to live in
trees, and finally had to give up arboreal life. The ancestors of the great
apes and man, in the words of Professor McCurdy,
. . . had gained from the arboreal habit erect posture, with all that it
implies, and safety from enemies. There remained little else to gain and much to lose; so by degrees arboreal life was abandoned. This step might have been taken by man's ancestors as early as the Miocene Epoch. It was a momentous step, the first step toward man's conquest of his environment
and hence of the earth. . . . Man's arboreal ancestor had been a brachiating
animal, since the erect posture, when sitting, requires this means of locomo
tion for its development. His arms were not excessively long, and his legs
were longer and stronger than those of his anthropoid contemporaries.
With erect biped gait, there came certain bodily changes, especially in the
pelvic girdle, the muscles of the legs, and above all, in the foot. The acquir
ing of the ground-dwelling habit necessitated the transformation of the foot
from a prehensile to a supporting organ. Relatively little change took place in the arms and hands. The complete freedom of the hand brought about the further opposability of the thumb, which became long enough to be op
posed to any of the other digits, and increasingly useful on that account.
The freedom of the hand led to tool-using and to tool-producing habits, and
opened the door to cultural evolution. (159, p. 17)
Up to this point, we have been dealing for the most part with the
story of organic evolution. A discussion of the childhood of man brings
us to the realm of social evolution; but, before proceeding with the
study of the early history of mankind, it is advisable to devote a chapter
to the factors and evidences of the evolutionary process.



CHAPT E R TWO
The Factors and Evidences of Evolution
NO properly educated person denies the reality of the evolutionary
process, although there is considerable disagreement among the author
ities as to the precise causation of evolution. On the fact of evolution
there is no dissent. The disagreement is on the relative values of the
causal factors of the process'.
There are several theories of evolution which attempt to explain how
the various factors have worked together to produce the actual process
of change. The principal theories are: (1) natural selection, (2) use and
disuse, (3) mutation, (4) orthogenesis, (5) isolation, and (6) hybridiza
tion.
We shall discuss natural selection, or Darwinism, first. It is a matter
of common observation that like produces like—that the offspring of
most living things resemble their parents. We recognize that cats beget
cats and not dogs; but if we examine a litter of kittens, we find that no
two of them are exactly alike. In other words, they vary. looking at
the world around us, we notice that more plants and animals come into
existence than can possibly survive; and that from this prodigality of
nature there results a struggle for existence. In this struggle, the living
forms best suited to the various environments in which they dwell will
survive; all others will perish; that is to say, only the fittest will survive.
A good example of natural selection at work may be seen in the ex
periments of Dr. Frank E. Lutz on a species of crickets near Oyster
Bay, Long Island. Dr. Lutz collected specimens of the cricket Gryllus
from four different locations: (1) on the mainland, where there was a
good grade of soil; (2) at the base of a sandbar, where the soil was poor
and sandy; (3) in the middle of the sandbar, which jutted out into the
water about 2,100 feet; and (4) at the tip of the sandbar, where there
was no soil, but only sand. The experimenter measured the ovipositors
16



THE FACTORS AND EVIDENCES OF EVOLUTION 17
(egg-laying rubes) of the female crickets found in rhese four sires. The
oviposiror prorrudes from rhe end of the body of rhe cricker and is easy
to measure. On a mainland cricker, the average length of the ovipositor
was 18 mm. (about eight-tenths of an inch); the average lengrh of rhe
organ ar the base of the sandbar was 19 mm.; in the middle of the bar,
19/2 mm.; at the tip, 20 mm. The crickers deposit their eggs in the
ground in autumn and, if they are not buried deeply enough, they are
destroyed by frost in the winter. In rhis case, soil and temperarure seem
to be the selective agents. Crickets with 18-mm. ovipositors flourish in
good soil on the mainland; but, in order for the cricket to live in pure
sand at the tip of the bar, the ovipositor must have an average length of
20 mm. These differences cannot be due to chance; so here we see a se
lective force at work, and a very discriminaring one, for it utilizes dif
ferences of only one-fifrieth of an inch. (The original report of the ex
perimenrs on crickers outlined above was entitled "The Variations and
Correlations of Certain Taxonomic Qualities of Gryllus" by Frank E.
Lutz, Carnegie Publication #101.)
Many evolutionists argue that natural selecrion by itself cannot ac
count for the entire process of evolution, but few would deny that it is
an important element in that process.
It is by no means clear [says Haldane] that natural selection will explain
all the facts. But the other suggested causes are unverified hypotheses, while
selection can be observed by those who take sufficient trouble. . . . Finally,
no facts definitely irreconcilable with Darwinism have been discovered in
the 60 years and more that have elapsed since the formulation of Darwin's views. Such a fact would be, for example, a convergence in the course of
geological time of members of two or more groups to form a single species. Actually, we observe the convergence of forms as we go down and not up a geological series. And there have been quite enough anti-Darwinian
paleontologists to have seized on such a case had it existed. (105, pp. 18-19)
Next to Darwinism, rhe mosr famous explanation of evolution is La
marck's theory of rhe inheritance of acquired characteristics, also
known as rhe theory of use and disuse. Most Darwinians have little re
spect for Lamarck, but Charles Darwin did not categorically reject the
Lamarckian principle. "It is of rhe greatest interest to recognize the
fact," as Professor Dendy points out, "that Darwin himself saw noth
ing incompatible between the so-called Lamarckian factor of use and
disuse and the direct action of the environment, and the principle of
natural selecrion, but on the other hand, that the one set of factors
might supplement the other." (62, p. 393)



18 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
In 1809, the year Darwin was born, J. B. Lamarck, a distinguished
French scientist who was both a biologist and paleontologist, published
a book with the title Zoological Philosophy, in which he formulated the
first modern scientific theory of evolution. In the first edition of his fa
mous work, Lamarck expressed his theory in the form of two laws; and
in the 1815 edition, the two laws were expanded into four. These laws are given below in Lamarck's own words:
1st Law: Life, through its own forces, continually tends to increase
the volume of every body which possesses it, and to increase the size of
its parts, up to a certain limit determined by life itself.
2nd Law: The production of a new organ in an animal body results from a new need supervening and continuing to make itself felt and
from a new activity which this need arouses and maintains.
3rd Law: The development of organs and their functional capacities
is constantly in ratio of the use which they receive.
4th Law: Everything which has been acquired, impressed upon, or
changed in the organization of individuals during the course of their
life, is preserved by generation, and transmitted to the offspring of
those which have undergone the changes.
There has arisen much controversy concerning this theory, most of it stemming from Lamarck's fourth law. The principle of use and dis
use is regarded as valid by some evolutionists, but they do not accept
the idea as originally formulated by Lamarck. The theory was revised
by Herbert Spencer, and this improved version, known as neo-La
marckism, has been accepted by some eminent evolutionists, among them being Ludwig Buchner, Ernst Haeckel, Lester F. Ward, G. Elliot Smith, E. W. MacBride, C. Tate Regan, Arthur Dendy, Alfred Giard, and William McDougall. Professor J. B. S. Haldane was a Dar
winist and, although he thought that most experiments on the inherit
ance of the effects of use and disuse had led to negative results, he still
believed that Lamarckism should not be dogmatically rejected, 'it must be remembered," said he, "that however many experiments fail, it is al
ways possible that the effects of use and disuse may be impressed on a
species at a rate not susceptible of experimental verification, yet rapid
enough to be of importance in geological time." (105, pp. 5-6)
Some of the evidence cited against the inheritance of acquired char
acteristics is not very impressive. In speaking of Lamarck, Professor Dorsey noted:
Biologists have driven a hundred daggers into his theory of evolution
through the inheritance of acquired characters; the theory is alive as ever!

To find our if acquired characters could be inherited, rhousands of ani
mals were murilared; Weismann himself cur off mice's rails for rwenry-rwo
generarions! They gave ir up, realizing, as Conklin purs ir, rhar wooden legs
are nor inherired, bur wooden heads may be. . . . The marvel is rhar Weis

they perform? The inherirance of acquired characters seems ro have been
rhe decisive factor. (72, pp. 102^4)
True scientific perspective concerning the Lamarckian factor has
also been displayed by Theodore H. Savory, an outstanding authority
on the Arachmda (spiders) and an able student of evolution. He tells us:
Biologisrs are agreed in accepring rhe rheory of evolurion as rhe only hy
porhesis able ro give a reasonable inrerprerarion of all rhe facrs of adaprarion
and disrribution of plants and animals. The problem rhar remains is the
problem of merhod—how has rhe evolurion come abour; whar has made the
organism change? A number of answers ro rhese quesrions have been pro
posed in rhe pasr, and are associated wirh rhe names of Darwin, Lamarck, Weismann, and deVries. The discussions which srill conrinue should be



20 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
concerned nor so much to decide whether these answers are right or
wrong, as to determine the extent to which each is to be considered respon
sible, or to appraise the contribution made by each method to the sum total
of evolutionary progress. Each hypothesis renders some of the facts intelli
gible, but none explains them all; only to this limited extent can there be
any rivalry between them.
There is a clear degree of dichotomy between the different theories, in so
far that some theories maintain that evolution is a transformation of one
structure into another, cliange in function and habit thereupon following,
while the others maintain that evolution is a change in habit and function
followed by and producing changes in structure. The latter view, associated with the name of J. B. Lamarck, and now usually known as neo- Lamarck
ism, is the one to which the study of the Arachnida seems to lead. (221, pp. 1 39-40)
Professor Hugo dcVries is generally recognized as the father of the
mutation theory of evolution, although the same principle was formu
lated independently by Professor Lester Ward. The mutationists claim
that evolution proceeds by sudden large changes rather than by gradual
small ones. Thus, according to deVries and his school, the small varia
tions postulated by the Darwinians would be of no value in producing
new species; for one species to evolve into another it is necessary to
have much larger variations. This theory had quite a vogue for many
years, but it is not now regarded as a serious rival to natural selection.
Mutants, or sports, as a rule have slight survival value in a state of na
ture, and they would therefore be weeded out by natural selection in
most cases.
The theory of orthogenesis, ably advocated by Professor Henry
Osborn and Dr. Robert Broom, attributes purpose to the evolutionary
process. According to this school of thought, variations do not crop up
aimlessly among life forms. The orthogeneticists believe that a mysteri
ous life force permeates all living things, and causes them to evolve in
certain predetermined directions. This elan vital, or life force, is teleo
logical in its effects, and operates independently of the environment.
Although orthogenesis seems to have a greater appeal to philoso
phers than to scientists, several outstanding scientists have adopted the
theory.
Dr. Robert Broom, the paleontologist who has done some fine work
in digging up fossils in Africa, for example, is a convinced teleologist.
"In the evolution of the horse from the little four-toed Eohippus that
lived 40,000,000 years ago to the large one-toed horse of today," he



THE FACTORS AND EVIDENCES OF EVOLUTION 21
opines, "we seem to have a nearly steady succession of forms, gradually becoming larger and larger, and with more and more complicated
grinding teeth. In the opinion of many paleontologists, this steady evo
lution cannot be explained by natural selection, nor by Lamarckism,
and certainly not by sexual selection. And it looks as if some agency
has been driving the evolution along to a definite end." (25, pp. 94-95)
Dr. Broom refers to an unnamed eminent English anatomist who
differed with Professor Le Gros Clark and himself on the affinities of
the South African ape men. "From the point of view of the phyloge
netic significance that is attached to the material both by Dr. Broom
and Prof. Le Gros Clark," the anatomist asserted, "one is again in dif
ficulty because it would seem, in particular from Broom's monograph,
that the evolutionary ideas that are implied differ from views estab
lished in relation to modern studies of genetics and selection." (Cited
by Dr. Broom in 25, p. 95) To this criticism, Broom replied: "I do not know what are the opin
ions of Le Gros Clark, but 1 have long ago stated that I do not think
that Darwin's theory of natural selection gives us a satisfactory expla
nation of how evolution has come about, and I may here state as defi
nitely that 1 am equally convinced that Hunt Morgan and the other
geneticists have also completely failed to find an explanation." (25, p.
96) The theory of isolation is based on the idea that, if a group of animals
or plants of the same species are allowed to intermingle freely, there
will be little or no variation away from the type; but if the larger
groups are broken up into several smaller groups, and these are pre
vented from interbreeding, each group will vary in different directions.
The importance of isolation as a factor of evolution has been explained
by Wells, Huxley, and Wells, in their popular work on biology, as fol
lows:
When there is no geographical break, one sub-species fades gradually
into another across an intermediate zone. This is beautifully illustrated by the wrens of South America. There is an almost continuous wren popula
tion over the continent; the wrens of different regions differ markedly in
size, in color, in pattern, in proportions, but the various types grade into each other. Here and there, however, an isolated patch of wrens is separated from their congeners by some barrier, such as a high mountain range, a
stretch of sea, or a desert, and then we find no transitional forms linking them with other races. . . . This effect of isolation in helping to bring new
types into being, is one of the everyday facts of systematic biology. (267, pp. 617-18)



22 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
In 1916, the Dutch biologist J. P. Eotsy wrote a book with the title
What Is a Species? in which he argued that evolutionary progress had
been brought about largely by the crossing of species, and the resultant
development of fertile hybrids. This theory of hybridization has been
adopted by several other biologists in a modified form. We are all per
haps familiar with the amazing results of experiments in plant hybridi
zation by the plant wizard, Luther Burbank; through the genius of
whom many new plant species were created. Hybridization is not as
common among animals as in the plant kingdom, but it is not so rare as
is generally supposed. For instance, in the cattle country of western
North America, breeders have developed crosses between domestic
cattle and long-haired yaks from Tibet, between Alaskan caribou and
Siberian reindeer, and between fat-tailed Persian sheep and English do
mestic sheep.
The role of the factor of sex in evolution is of great importance, and
something must be said about it. Though the higher forms of plant and
animal life are sexually propagated, the primary role of sex in evolution
was not reproduction.
Since the male sex has been in a position of dominance in modern
cultures, it has been erroneously assumed that this male dominance has
always existed from a remote beginning in the animal kingdom many
ages ago; but all the evidence we have is adverse to the theory of male
superiority; for in nearly all animal species, the female is the superior
and dominant type. Even in the human race, before the rise of civiliza
tion, female rule was practically universal. Should we trace the stream
of life back toward its source, we find the female growing in size and
importance, whereas the male decreases in size and power until it van
ishes altogether. Below the vertebrate level, the male sex is relatively
unimportant. Among the bees, the female is the queen, whereas the
male is just a drone; and the mosquito that bites us in the summer is the
female of the species, since the male mosquito dies in early spring as
soon as the mating season is over. Among the Nephila chrysogasier, a
species of spider, the female is thirteen hundred times the size of the
male; and after the marriage ceremony is over, Mrs. Spider devours her
diminutive husband. Among the barnacles, the male is only a parasite
residing in the valves of the female shell; and Mrs. Barnacle carries
around from two to seven of these miniature mates for the purpose of
reproduction. In primitive human society, the women formed the rul
ing class; and even the male dominance of a later age was probably
brought about through sexual selection on the part of the females.



THE FACTORS AND EVIDENCES OF EVOLUTION 23
The evidences of evolution may be treated under the following
headings: (1) the evidence from fossils, (2) the evidence from embryol
ogy, (3) the evidence from comparative anatomy, and (4) the evidence
from blood. A fine example of what the fossil record teaches us may be
learned by examining the family tree of the horse. The earliest fossil
horse so far discovered is Eohippus, found in lower Eocene strata of the
western United States. Eohippus had four toes each on the front limbs
and three each on the hind feet. The vestige of another toe (the first
digit or thumb) is visible as a splint on the forefoot, but is completely
missing on the hind foot. The remnant of the fifth digit, however, is
present in the form of a splint on the hind feet of Eohippus. This an
cient relative of the modern horse was only twelve inches high, or
about the size of a modern terrier. It is believed that the hypothetical
five-toed horse, the ancestor of Eohippus and the entire horse family,
first saw the light of day in the Cretaceous period; and that he indubita
bly nad five toes on both fore and hind limbs, and was no larger than
the average rat. Professor Richard Swann Lull, a paleontologist of Yale
University, has traced this evolutionary history through ten distinct
stages in a fine little book entitled Fossils. This work is long out of
print, but for the enlightenment of the reader, a summary of Lull's
findings is given below.
We start with Eohippus, the four-toed horse of the Lower Eocene,
which grew in size and eventually evolved into Orohippus, a mountain
horse of the Middle Eocene, and finally into Kpihippus, of the Upper
Eocene strata. Epihippus stood about fourteen inches high and showed
marked development of the middle digit on both front and back feet. Mesohippus branched off from Epihippus in the Lower Oligocene to
become the first three-toed horse, and reached a height of eighteen
inches. Miohippus, of the Upper Oligocene, grew to a height of
twenty-four inches. In the main line of the evolution of the horse were
three Miocene types. The first was Parahippus, a browsing horse with
short crowned teeth; the second, Merychippus, was forty inches tall, with short crowned milk teeth and long crowned permanent grinders,
the latter being an adaptation to a rough herbage; and the third was
Protohippus, who possessed long crowned milk teeth, and likewise
with the permanent set of teeth. In Pliohippus of the Pliocene, we meet
the first one-toed horse; and in Upper Pliocene strata are fossils of Ple
sippus, an advanced type of the one-toed horse, almost equal in stature
to Equus (the modern horse). The genus Equus emerged in the Pleistocene period, and flourished



24 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
in both the Old and New Worlds. The American horses became ex
rincr during the Great Ice Age, but the Asiatic and African horses con
tinued to develop. The horse is a remarkable animal: He walked on his
toes for ages, the middle toe expanded and the other toes vanished, and
then the nail of the middle toe became the hoof.
The embryological evidence for evolution has been formulated con
cisely in Haeckel's Biogenetic Law, which states: "Ontogeny is a brief
and condensed recapitulation of phytogeny." The gist of said law is
that the individual organism in its embryological development passes
through stages in which it duplicates, in an abbreviated form, the his
tory of the species. The human embryo, for instance, commences as a
single cell, and then passes through fish, amphibian, and reptilian
stages. In the fish stage, the embryo has gills; and then the two-cham
bered heart of the fish phase develops into the three-chambered heart of
the amphibians and reptiles; and finally into the four-chambered heart
of the birds and mammals. We all have tails shortly before birth—a fact
which reminds us of our tree-dwelling ancestors of the distant past.
Occasionally, due to atavism, certain individuals are born with tails,
which later have to amputated. Surgeon-General Ornstein of the
Greek army has reported several cases of tailed men in Greece; and
Dr. Granville Harrison amputated a five-inch tail from a six-month-old
child in the United States. Dr. Harrison reported that the aforesaid tail
contained muscles and rudimentary vertebrae, and was capable of brisk
motion when the child was excited.
The evidence from comparative anatomy may be illustrated by a
comparison of the physical structure of man and the frog. Many people
object to being reminded of their relationship to the great apes, but
their more remote kinship to the frog is not generally discussed. Both
men and frogs have internal skeletons composed of bone. They each
have a spinal cord enclosed in a tube formed by the vertebrae of the
backbone; a brain, and a skull enclosing the same; a mouth, teeth, eyes,
ears, and a nose arranged according to the same general structural pat
tern; and two pairs of limbs, with a skeleton corresponding almost bone
for bone.
It has been said that man's superior brain brought him to a position
of dominance in the animal kingdom; but the difference between the
brain of man and his nearest kin, the anthropoid apes, is one of degree,
and not of kind. In the words of Sir G. Elliot Smith: "No structure
found in the brain of an ape is lacking in the human brain; and, on the



THE FACTORS AND EVIDENCES OF EVOLUTION 25
other hand, the human brain reveals no formation of any sort that is not
present in the brain of the gorilla or chimpanzee."
Man corresponds closely with his ape cousins—not only in brain
structure but also in respect to bones, muscles, nerves, blood vessels,
and most other organs. Sir Arthur Keith in his researches in compara
tive anatomy discovered that man has in common with the chimpanzee
98 characters, 87 with the gorilla, 56 with the orang-utan, 84 with the
gibbons, 60 with the New World monkeys, and 53 with the Old World monkeys.
At the base of the human brain is a little organ known as the pineal
body which exercises control over the functioning of the glands of in
ternal secretion. In the words of a distinguished neuro-surgeon: "The
main function of the pineal organ is to retard the action of the hor
mones, or endocrines of the other organs, e.g. pituitrin, thyroxin, insu
lin, oestrin, and the testicular hormone, of the pituitary, thyroid, pan
creas, ovary and testis, respectively. The pineal organ is thus a
super-endocrine organ that checks overactivity on the part of any
member of the endocrine system." (10, p. 107n)
The pineal body is described in Starlings Physiology as "a vestigial
remnant of a primitive dorsal eye." McCabe in discussing the pineal
organ states, "It is clearly the relic of a third eye that once functioned
on the top of the head in a primitive animal. One can often see this
third eye, though now covered with skin, standing out like a large mar
ble on the top of the head of a crocodile. In a very primitive New Zea
land lizard, the tuatara, it plainly shows the structure of an eye, with
retina and optic nerve in a state of decay. From the reptile level we
trace it through the higher animals, sinking deeper and deeper in the
head until it becomes the pineal body." (154, p. 244)
Admiral Beadnell made a study of the tuatara, and in explaining the
evolutionary significance of the pineal body, gave his objections to call
ing it a third eye in the following words: "Remains of this third eye (as
it is wrongly called) are present in lampreys, frogs and lizards, and can
sometimes be seen in the frog on the top of the head between the nor
mal lateral eyes as the so-called Stieda's organ. In the extinct amphibia
and reptiles of the Paleozoic and Mesozoic periods, this median eye, or
rather pair of eyes, was much better developed. Recent embryological
research has demonstrated that the remaining pineal eye in Sphenodon
[tuatara] is the left eye of the ancestral pair, while that in the lamprey is
the right one. Birds and mammals also possess vestiges of this extra me
dian pair of eyes " (10, pp. 106-7)



26 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Finally we consider the evidence from blood, and the wonderful
story that it tells. If blood cells from one animal are introduced into the bloodstream of another animal, they are destroyed by certain chemicals
known as cytolysins (cell dissolvers). If the two animals concerned are
of the same, or closely related, species, the rate of cell destruction is
slow; but if the mutual kinship is remote, the destruction is rapid. If
human blood cells are injected into the veins of a horse, they are
quickly destroyed. The same human blood cells injected into the blood of a monkey are destroyed at a much slower rare. And when the human blood cells are mingled with those of an anthropoid ape, the reaction is almost the same as mixing the blood cells of two human
beings. There are four principal types of human blood, and the great
apes have the same four types of blood characteristic of the human spe
cies. Drs. C, P. Miller, Jr., and K. Landsteiner of The Rockefeller In
stitute carried out a series of blood tests on men and animals, using two
different experimental methods. The results were published in the
magazine Science, May 8, 1925, and we quote from the two scientists as
follows:
This finding is in agreement with the view of zoologists that the anthropoids are not placed on a line leading from the lower monkeys to man, but
that at a certain stage of evolution there separated one line in the catarrhinae and another leading to the anthropoids and man. The differences between the races of man were imperceptible by our technique in experiments made up to the present by the comparison of white and American Negro bloods. . . . Both methods gave reactions with anthropoid blood in every way identical with those of human blood, while the blood of the other animals behaved differently. These reactions give additional proof of the
close relationship between man and the anthropoids. . . . Very probably
the group specific factors characteristic for human blood, appeared in the phylogeny of the primates prior to the genesis of man.
Scientific experiments have shown that the proportions of calcium, potassium, and sodium ions in blood serum and sea water are almost
identical; but the saline concentration of the sea water is higher, since
the oceans have become saltier since its circulation many ages ago in
the bodies of our sea-dwelling ancestors. Admiral Charles M. Beadnell, an eminent surgeon, has written instructively on the evolution of
blood. The charm and lucidity of his style are exemplified in his sum
mary of the researches of Dr. R. Quinton, a noted physiologist, on the origin of blood and other body fluids:
Independently with Professor Macallum, he conceived the idea that the blood of living animals furnishes a valuable clue to the temperature and



THE FACTORS AND EVIDENCES OF EVOLUTION 27
composition of the early pre-Cambrian seas. Our naked ancestors dwelt im
mersed in a saline fluid; each of their cells was, consequently, saturated with
salt water. When, with the advent of a protective and enclosing membrane
or skin, the cells got shut off from direct contact with the sea water, they
retained some of it as their vital fluid; which, circulating, became the blood
and lymph streams. . . . When these aquatic skin-enclosed animals left the
seas and became terrestrial, they carried with them, so to speak, this
modified saline fluid sealed up in their bodies. Hence it is that our body
fluids generally — the blood in the vessels, the lymph in the lymph-spaces,
the tears cleansing the surface of the eye, the lubricating oil of our joints,
the fluid bathing each cell, the amniotic liquids in which the foetus is im
mersed —are, in essence, a dilute sea water. The pre-Cambrian seas are
known to have been less saline than modern seas, and so our body fluids
contain a smaller percentage of total salts than do the seas of today, but are
more or less identical in salinity with those of pre-Cambrian times. With
out the regular and constant presence of this miniature salt sea, not one of
our cells could live a moment. An amoeba or a human blood corpuscle,
placed in pure water, dies immediately. (10, pp. 282-83)



CHAPTER THREE
Ice Ages and Men
IT seems that the earliest forms of life appeared on our planet about
one and a half billion years ago. Apes and men have been traced back
to a common ancestry, the first apes showing up about forty million
years ago. Then around twenty-five million years ago, man became
differentiated from the common ape stem. The first true men made
their presence known in the world only about two million years ago
very brief period of time, speaking geologically.
The popular opinion that Charles Darwin was the inventor of the
doctrine of evolution is false. There were numerous evolutionists be
fore Darwin; but with the publication of The Origin of Species in 1859,
Darwin made the theory of evolution intellectually respectable. After
that date, evolutionism became a part of the lore of modern science,
and hence had to be reckoned with by all thinking men and women.
The bearing of the theory of evolution on human origins was scarcely
mentioned by Darwin in the concluding passage of The Origin of Spe
cies. "Much light," said he, "will be thrown on the origin of man and
his history." (55, p. 373)
In 1871 Darwin's Descent of Man was published, and in this book
impressive evidence was adduced, tending to show that man and the
anthropoid apes could be traced back to a common ancestor. Most of
Darwin's contemporaries judged the continent of Asia to be most likely
the birthplace of humanity, but Darwin held that Africa had a better
claim to the title of the Cradle of Mankind. "We are naturally led to
enquire," he asserted, "where was the birthplace of man. ... In each
great region of the world the living mammals are closely related to the
extinct species of the same region. It is therefore probable that Africa
was formerly inhabited by extinct apes closely allied to the gorilla and
28



ICE AGES AND MEN 29
chimpanzee, and as these two species are now man's nearest allies, it is
somewhat more probable that our progenitors lived on the African con
tinent than elsewhere. " (55, p. 520)
Even in the early years of the present century, scientists investigat
ing prehistoric eras were inclined to look chiefly to Asia as the original
home of man; but in the year 1921 this position was strikingly chal
lenged by the publication of The Origin and Evolution of the Human
Race by Dr. Albert Churchward. This distinguished medical man held
membership in both the Royal Council of Physicians and the Royal
Council of Surgeons; and besides this he was an anthropologist and ar
chaeologist. The earliest members of the human race appeared in the
interior of the African continent about two million years ago, Church
ward claimed; then from the region of the Great Lakes they spread
over the entire continent. Groups of these early men wandered down
the Nile Valley, settled in Egypt, and then later dispersed themselves
to all parts of the world. The first migration was of the primitive little
men known as pygmies. After them came waves of Nilotic Negroes
who also spread out widely over the face of the globe. As these early
Africans wandered over the world, they differentiated into the various
human subspecies that now inhabit our planet. The men who remained
in the tropical and equatorial regions retained their dark complexions,
whereas those that settled in the temperate zones lost a portion of their
dusky pigmentation and developed a fairer skin. One of Dr. Church
ward's friends, the English scholar, Gerald Massey, had earlier
broached this same theory in a two-volume work, A Book of the Begin
nings (164); but this book was largely ignored by the world of science and scholarship. Even Churchward's arguments were not at first favor
ably considered by his contemporaries, but the tide eventually turned and Dr. Churchward's stand was proven to be fundamentally sound.
Joseph McCabe was a sound popularizer of science and history, and in
1931 he issued The New Science and the Story of Evolution ( 1 54). This
informative book was certainly not directly influenced by the re
searches of Dr. Churchward, yet McCabe's opinions on the early mi
grations of mankind blend harmoniously with those of Churchward.
To cite McCabe directly:
First there was the distribution of the dusky pygmies or Negritoes, who
survive, or did until the nineteenth century, little modified in the Tasmani
ans, pure Veddahs of Ceylon, Andamanese, several jungle tribes of Malaya, and the Bushmen. Next was the distribution which gave the earliest Ne



30 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
groes (or dusky men who would become Negroes) to Africa, the aborigi
nals of Australia, and the Melanesians. From the more temperate part of
that region the ancestors of the Turk and Mongol peoples seem to have
streamed north and northeast, the ancestors of the Malays eastward along
the coast, the ancestors of the Aryans moved northwestward, and their
cousins, the Polynesians, crossed the already crowded eastern region to the
Pacific. (154, pp. 270-71)
Late in 1924, in the town of Taung, Bechuanaland (now called the
Republic of Botswana), a limestone quarry was being operated by the
Northern Lime Company. During blasting operations, the skull of an
ape flew out of the debris and landed at the feet of a visiting geologist,
Professor R. B. Young. The manager of the quarrying operations, a
man named A. E. Spiers, knew that Dr. Raymond Dart, an anatomist
of Johannesburg, would be interested in examining this fossil skull, so
he requested that Young deliver the same to Dart; which was done.
Professor Dart, in studying this fossil, noticed that, though the skull
was that of an ape, the teeth approached the human type closer than
those of any other ape, extant or extinct. Almost immediately he wrote
a paper entitled "The First South African Manlike Ape," which was
published February 7, 1925. The great value of this discovery, Dart de
clared, was that "it exhibits an extinct race of apes intermediate be
tween living anthropoids and man"; and he named the newly found
fossil Australopithecus africanus (southern ape of Africa).
Professor Dart's claims were examined by a committee of four an
thropologists tn London: Sir Arthur Keith, Sir G. Elliot Smith, Sir Ar
thur Smith-Woodward, and Dr. W. L. H. Duckworth. Their verdict
was not favorable to Dart. Keith regarded the Australopithecus as an
ape with some manlike characters. Elliot Smith opined that the fossil
skull was that of an anthropoid ape, but admitted that the Taung ape
had possessed certain human brain features. Smith- Woodward re
garded the fossil as a type of chimpanzee, and held that it had little
bearing on the question of the origin of man. Dr. Duckworth, of Cam
bridge University, was cautious, but seemed to be more favorable to
Dart's claims than the other members of the committee.
But Raymond Dart was not without friends in this scientific camp.
There was a medical doctor from Paisley, Scotland, practicing his pro
fession in Maquassi, a little town on the edge of the Karoo Desert. His
name was Robert Broom, and he had been professor of geology and
zoology at the Victoria College in Stellenbosch. Besides practicing



CE ACES AND MEN 31
medicine, Dr. Broom carried on researches on the evolution of ancienr
mammals and, when he read Dart's paper in 1925, he journeyed to
Johannesburg to take a look at the Australopithecine skull. An examina
tion of the fossil convinced Broom that Dart was right, and he regarded
it as practically the missing link, and one of the most important fossil
discoveries ever made. "As a paleontologist," Broom stated, "1 did not
greatly worry about the size and shape of the brain or the convolutions,
but 1 was convinced that the Taung child was not allied to either the
chimpanzee or the gorilla, and that it was closely allied to man.' 1
(25, p. 25) The Taung ape-man was judged to be about two million years old,
which made him four times as old as the man of Heidelberg and five
times the age of the Java ape-man. But Broom and Dart failed to con
vince the other experts that their claims were valid, so they went out
and sought more evidence. Dr. Broom gave up his medical practice and
took the post of curator of vertebrate paleontology and physical anthro
pology at the Transvaal Museum in Pretoria; and when time permit
ted, he and Dart visited the Karoo Desert to look for fossils. Early in
August, 1936, two of Dart's students (G. W. H. Schepers and H. le
Riche) called Broom and told him that they had found some small ba
boon skulls in caves at Sterkfontein. Dr. Broom visited the caves, lo
cated in a limestone quarry managed by G. W. Barlow. The manager
was requested to look out for fossils, which Broom agreed to purchase.
A few days afterward Barlow found part of a fossil skull, and Broom,
after hours of digging, located most of the rest of it. The doctor found
that he had discovered another ape-man, which he appropriately named
Plesnwthropus transvaalensis (a being from the Transvaal closely allied
to man).
On a visit to the quarry in the forenoon of Wednesday, June 8,
1938, Dr. Broom was greeted by Barlow, who said, "I've something
nice for you this morning," as he handed the doctor the palate of a
large fossil ape-man, containing one molar.
Broom replied, "Yes, it is quite nice. I'll give you a couple of pounds
for it."
"He was delighted," the doctor observed, "so I wrote a check and
put the specimen in my pocket. He did not seem quite willing to say
where or how he had obtained it; and 1 did not press the matter."
On the following Saturday, Broom visited the quarry and, Barlow
being absent, he learned from the workmen on the job that the fossil had not come from their quarry. "On Tuesday forenoon, 1 was again



32 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
at Sterkfontein," to cite Broom further, "when I insisted on Barlow
telling me how he had got the specimen. I pointed out that two teeth
had been freshly broken off, and that they might be lying where the
specimen had been obtained. He apologized for having misled me, and
told me it was a schoolboy, Gert Terblanche, who acted as a guide in
the caves on Sundays, who had picked it up and given it to him." (25,
p. 49) Learning that the schoolboy, aged fifteen, lived at Kromdraai
Farm, about two miles distant, Broom immediately headed for the
farm. Finding that Gert was at school, he then took off for the school
house. tl
It thus happened," as related by Herbert Wendt, "that a fa
mous museum curator suddenly burst into a Boer village school and an
nounced that he must speak to one of the pupils at once. The teacher,
tremendously impressed and yet clever enough to take advantage of the
occasion, said he would gladly release the boy if South Africa's great
fossil hunter would be good enough to say a few words about his re
searches on prehistoric man." (268, p. 478)
After Broom's lecture was over, and school let out, the doctor was
able to ask the student a few questions. Broom whipped the fossil bone
out of his coat pocket and pointed it at young Terblanche, and then ex
citedly asked, "Where did you get this from? Come on, tell me! Take
me immediately to the place where you found it."
Then Gert, in the words of Broom, "drew from his trouser pocket
four of the most beautiful teeth ever found in the world's history. Two
of the four fitted on the palate Barlow had given to me. The other two
had been weathered off." Broom, of course, gladly purchased the teeth from the young man. Less than an hour later, Broom was shown the site at Kromdraii, where the fossil palate had been found. Gert Ter
blanche pointed to a wall of rock and said to Dr. Broom, "That's the
place. The best thing to do would be to pour gunpowder into the hole and blow the whole show up. We always do that when we need stones
for building."
This last remark shocked the doctor, who retorted, "For heaven's
sake! You'd better help me instead to chisel the bones out. Sure you
haven't got anything more in your pockets?"
Then the boy confessed, "Yes, I've got one more tooth. Can I keep
just that one?"
When Broom saw the tooth, he groaned and said, "Well, it would be a premolar!" Then he turned to his youthful companion and said,
"Look here, I'll give you five slabs of chocolate for it." The doctor got
his fossil tooth; for what schoolboy could turn down an offer of five



ICE AGES AND MEN 33
slabs of chocolate? Dr. Broom found his meering with Gcrr Terblanche
highly rewarding, since the pupil had hidden another fossil find which
he turned over to the doctor. This one was a jaw containing some
teeth, and in a fine state of preservation; and after a search for the rest
of the fossil, Broom finally assembled most of the left side of the skull
and the right lower jaw, with many teeth in good condition. This dis
covery turned out to be a new genus of the Australopithecines, with a
bigger brain and jaw than any hitherto found, and with teeth more
hominid. The new genus was labeled Paranthwpus robustus (robust
near-man) and, since the fossil was unearthed at Kromdraai Farm, it is sometimes called the Kromdraai Man.
The field work of Dr. Broom was halted during the second world
war, and was not resumed until 1947. In April, 1948, another fossil was
unearthed—the skull of an adult female Plesianthropus, which was nick
named "Mrs. Pies"; and then another species of ape-man was dug up in
October at Swartkrans. This was an outsized lower jawbone of an Aus
tralopithecine with large teeth, so Broom called it Paranthwpus crassidens
(coarse-toothed near-man). A fifth species, Australopithecus prome
theus, was discovered by Dart in 1949, at Makapansgat. Broom passed
away at the age of eighty-four in 1951, but his work was continued by
his assistant, Dr. John T. Robinson. Over a period of years, Robinson
found fossils of another species, Telanthwpus capencis, a more advanced
type than any of the Australopithecines and hence more hominid. The
five Australopithecines so far mentioned are all members of one genus,
consisting of two species. The four earlier types belong to the species
Africanus, and the later to the species robustus. A French paleontolo
gist, Yves Coppen, uncovered a fossil in June, 1961, in the Republic of
Chad, which turned out to be a sixth Australopithecine, and is believed
to be older than its South African relatives. This ape-man has been
named Tchadanthropus JJxoris. These South African ape-men were
closer to man than the anthropoid apes, but they are not considered di
rect ancestors of Homo sapiens. The search for the earliest members of
the human race leads us to the Olduvai Gorge, in the Republic of Tan
zania, Hast Africa.
In 1911, a German entomologist named Kattwinkel was traveling
through German East Africa in search of rare insect specimens. By chance he wandered into the Olduvai Gorge and observed some fossil bones embedded in the wall of a steep cliff. The bones were excavated
and taken to Berlin, where interested paleontologists recognized them
as remains of Hipparion, an ancient three-toed horse. This intelligence



34 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
arrested rhe arrenrion of Hans Reck, a geologist of the University of Berlin, and convinced him that Olduvai ought to be a good place to
prospect for fossils. So, organizing an expedition, Dr. Reck in 1913
journeyed to the gorge, and his subsequent discoveries were downright
spectacular. Fossils of extinct species of animals were found numbering
in the hundreds, and several of them were unknown to science. One of
Reek's finds was a skeleton of Homo sapiens which he believed to be
one million years old, but later investigations showed this to be an
error, and the correct age was established as twenty thousand years. At
the outbreak of the first world war in 1914, Reek's party had to sus
pend operations, and explorations were not resumed for seventeen
years.
In the organization of a new expedition to search for Stone Age arti
facts at the Olduvai site in 193 1, the moving spirit was a paleontologist
born in Africa—a young man named Louis Leakey. His parents were
English missionaries, working among the Kikiyu of Kenya, and there
Louis was born in a thatched hut. He grew up among the Kikiyu,
played with their children, and spoke their language. At age sixteen he
left for England to study anthropology and archaeology. Five years
later, in 1924, he returned to Kenya, and engaged in the exploration of
Stone Age sites until 1929. In that year, he visited Berlin and asked
Professor Reck to join him for further work at Olduvai. Since Leakey
occupied the post of curator of the Coryndon Museum in Nairobi,
Kenya, he had been compelled to limit his work at the gorge to a
seven-week period each year, and prospecting in a chasm three hun
dred feet in depth and twenty-five miles in length has kept him busy
over the years. It has been his good fortune that Mrs. Mary Leakey, his
wife, is also a paleontologist, and that she has ably assisted him in his
work, besides making some important discoveries of her own.
In the Olduvai Gorge there are five strata of rocks; and in the lowest
layer many stone tools of a pre-Chellean (Abbevillian) design have
been found. The stratum immediately above contains fossils and arti
facts of a Chellean type; and here Leakey and his associates found
twelve distinct stages of a hand-axe culture. In the search for fossil
men, Olduvai for many years was not encouraging. Leakey had better
luck at Kanam and Kanjera, where in 1932 he found fossil skulls of
Homo sapiens of uncertain age. In 1948, Mrs. Leakey unearthed a fossil
ape, on Rusinga Island, in Lake Victoria. This extinct primate lived
about twenty-five million years ago in the Miocene epoch. He was
given the name Proconsul Africanus and seems to have been a close rela



ICE AGES AND MEN 35
rive to a remote ancestor of Homo sapiens. "He cannot be considered a
direct ancestor of man," asserts Mr. Simak, "but he probably was not
far from man's line of descent. Somewhere during this time must have
lived another animal, a cousin of Proconsul, which was man's remote
ancestor." (232, pp. 278-79)
The first big discovery at Olduvai did not occur until July 17, 1959; and this was another triumph of Mrs. Leakey. While Professor Leakey
was inactivated by a spell of illness, his wife located a skull embedded
in rocks eighty-three feet from the bottom of the gorge. The fossil was
fragmented, and after nineteen days of labor was excavated and recon
structed. The skull was hominid in type, somewhat flat on top, and
crowned with a bony crest, very much like that of a gorilla, and with a
cranial capacity of about 650 cubic centimeters. This ancient man was
named Zinjanthropus boisei by Leakey. The Zinj part of the name is
from the Arabic name for east Africa, and the boisei refers to the last
name of Charles Boise, one of the financial sponsors of the Olduvai ex
plorations. Professor Ashley Montagu regards this Olduvai man as the
most primitive, though not the oldest, known form of man.
The next great discovery at Olduvai, in 1960, was again made by
Mrs. Leakey who, after noticing a tooth embedded in the side of the
gorge, dug into the earth and uncovered several parts of a skeleton.
Over the following three years six additional specimens were exhumed.
The new find was labeled Homo habilis (man with ability). This an
cient man possessed an erect carriage, his height was about four and a
half feet, he had a cranial capacity of about 700 cc, and his dentition was of a primitive human type. His hands were equipped with opposable thumbs, which endowed him with a toolmaking capability; and his
feet were definitely modern in structure. He flourished between one
and two million years ago. Professor Leakey regards him as a direct an
cestor of Homo sapiens. In an orange grove owned by Fred Wicker at Fort Ternan, Kenya, Leakey found the fossil of an ape which he named Kenyapithecus wickeri (Wicker's Kenya ape). This primate lived about twelve million
years ago, and he certainly had few friends among his neighbors. An
Associated Press release, dated Washington, DC, May 15, 1968, was
published in the New York Times, May 16, 1968, with the caption: SCIENTIST KINDS HAMMER I 2 MILLION YEARS OLD, from which We quote
the following:
A National Geographic Society scientist has unearthed evidence that a
bone smashing African ancestor of man used a crude stone hammer 12 mil



36 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
lion years ago. This would be 10 million years earlier than previous esti
