mates of the beginning of toolmaking on earth. The society said today that
the evidence was found by Dr. Louis S. B. Leakey, British anthropologist,
who has made a number of notable findings concerning prehistoric man. The announcement said that Dr. Leakey, working at Fort Ternan in
Kenya, had found evidence that the prehuman Kenyapithecus zuickeri appar
ently hammered open animal skulls and bones with a crude stone hammer.
The above intelligence must certainly have arrested the attention of
the anthropological fraternity, for it was regarded as axiomatic until
quite recently that a toolmaking ape must be considered to be a man.
According to the English anthropologist, F. A. Brooke: "The differen
tiation between what constitutes man and ape must be considered as
having taken place at that point when an ape was able with his own
hands to shape wood and stone in a very crude way, so that he could
make them serve for a special purpose. Thus it may be said that the first
man was the first ape that made a tool." (24, p. 1 1)
The above viewpoint, as expressed by Brooke, is not considered en
tirely correct by the most recent authorities. The best modern state
ment of the matter has been expressed by Clifford I). Simak as follows:
"Man is not the only tool-using animal, nor the only tool-making ani
mal. Chimpanzees in captivity, have fashioned tools—fastening two
sticks together to knock down a bunch of fruit placed out of their
reach. But man was the first and only animal to make and carry a tool
against a future need. Here we see that the distinction is not so much in
the ability to make or use a tool as it is in the intelligence which can see
the future need of one." (232, p. 282)
The African genesis of the human race is now generally accepted in
the realm of modern science as a fact beyond all dispute. "It is quite
probable," declares Professor Montagu, "that man's immediate ances
tors as a group had already lost a considerable part of the hairy coat
which is characteristic of all anthropoids, indeed, of all primates. What
the skin color was we cannot be sure, but since the ancestors of man
were almost certainly tropical animals of African origin, they were al
most equally certainly black-skinned." (173, p. 42)
Or. Eric Higgs of Cambridge University has made a study of the
migrations of ancient men, and he claims that the first men of Europe
came to that continent from central and eastern Africa about 250,000
years ago. Professor Chester Chard, of the University of Wisconsin,
has studied the routes of early men who left Africa to colonize the rest



ICE AGES AND MEN 37
of the world; and he has concluded that there were four prehistoric mi
gration routes from Africa to Europe. Professor Leakey was asked if he
thought that any of the early African migrants reached the New
World, and he answered thusly: "It is inconceivable that man, the most
curious and mobile of all animals, would not have come to America,
when the elephants, the tapirs and deer came from Asia. . . . Man
spread out from Africa to Asia to Europe. It is inconceivable that he
would stay out of America. ... At least people should go look for re
mains of man before they say he wasn't in America." (204)
Up to this point we have been discussing mainly the ape-men and
prehistoric men of Africa, but to get a balanced picture of the story of
evolution we must say something about some of the ape-men and pro
tomen of Asia and Europe. Remains of ancient types of ape-men were
discovered by Professor G. H. R. von Koenigswald, in Lower Pleisto
cene strata of the Sangiran district in central Java in 1941. The fossil
fragments unearthed consisted of parts of two lower jaws. This ancient
Javanese ape-man has been called Meganthropus paleojavanicus. In 1952
a more complete jaw of this early manlike type was found at Sangiran
by Dr. Pieter Marks. These jaws have been described as unusually
massive, of about the same proportions as the jaws of adult male goril
las. Weidenrich and Von Koenigswald held that Meganthropus was
seven feet six inches in height; and they concluded that early man
passed through a stage of gigantism, which gradually subsided into men
of normal stature. The opinion has been expressed by Von Koenigs
wald that Meganthropus was an ancestor of Pithecanthropus erectus, the
famous Java ape-man. In 1886 the young Dr. Eugene Dubois was lecturer in anatomy at
the University of Amsterdam, but this position brought .him no joy, for
he was interested in the study of the origin of man. The young physi
cian had already concluded that the earliest men dwelt either in Africa,
the habitat of the gorilla and chimpanzee, or Asia, which is the home of
the orang-utan. Since Java and Sumatra had not been covered by gla
ciers during the Great Ice Age, Dubois thought these areas would
prove rewarding in a search for the remains of early ancestors of mod
ern man. He secured an appointment as a surgeon in the Royal Dutch
Army, sailing for Padang, Sumatra, in November, 1887, with high
hopes of finding fossils of the earliest types of men. Exploration of caves
in Sumatra was disappointing, and Dubois persuaded the government
to transfer him to Java so he could search for fossil vertebrate fauna.



38 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
During the years 1890 through 1897, the young scientist found the
missing link which he had so long sought, for at Trinil, Java, he found
the top of a skull, a fragment of a lower jaw, three teeth, and a thigh
bone. The skull-cap was apelike, whereas the thigh bone was decidedly
manlike. This combination suggested an ape-man who had walked
erectly; hence this missing link was called Pithecanthropus erectus (the
ape-man who walked erect). A more recent classification gives this old
ape-man the name Homo erectus erectus. (For details on the ancestry of
man, and the classification of early types of man and his forerunners,
the interested reader is referred to Man: His First Two Million Years
by Professor Ashley Montagu [173, 174].) The brain capacity of the
Java ape-man (Homo erectus erectus) ranged from 775 to 940 cc, a vol
ume which approaches that of Homo sapiens (modern man).
In 1937, in mid-Pleistocene strata, at Sangiran, Java, Von Koenigs
wald excavated several other specimens of Homo erectus erectus; and
later on, in 1939, the same scientist discovered a more robust type of
the same ape-man, which was appropriately labeled Homo erectus ro
bustus. This fossil find consisted of the base of the skull and the upper
jaw with teeth in their sockets. The evolutionary significance of Homo
erectus robustus is admirably explained by Professor Montagu:
The teeth are essentially human in form, except that the canines project
beyond the level of the other teeth, and there is a bony space between the
canine and the lateral incisor for the reception of the tip of the lower ca
nine, just as in the apes. In Homo erectus erectus, this space has disap
peared. The evidence, therefore, points to this evolutionary order of
changes in the jaw region, namely that the first canine tooth underwent re
duction, as we can see in Homo erectus robustus, and this was followed
after a time by disappearance of the premaxillary diastema, as the space be
tween the canine tooth and the lateral incisor is called. In this way the pro
jection of the upper jaw was reduced to the more or less straight form
characteristic of modern man. (174, p. 56)
Professor von Koenigswald prospecting for fossils in the vicinity of
Ngandong, Java, near the Solo River, in 193 1, came across eleven fossil
skulls. A study of the fossils showed resemblances to the famous Java
ape-man and the Neanderthal man of later times. The average brain
capacity was 1 100 cc. At Wadjak, also in Java, two human skulls were
found in 1889 and 1890 by Van Rietschoten, and then sold to Dr. Eu
gene Dubois. Dubois claimed that the skulls were relics of the Pleisto
cene age, and the cranial volume of one was 1550 cc. and of the other



ICE AGES AND MEN 39
was 1650 cc, and the skulls are undoubtedly of sapiens type. Some stu
dents of prehistory have suggested that there seems to be a straight line
of evolution, leading from Java man through Solo man to Wadjak man,
and thence to the Australian aborigines.
Professor Davidson Black, a Canadian anatomist, on the faculty of
Peiping Union Medical College, found a fossil tooth at Choukoutien,
near Peiping, in 1927. By 1939 remains of more than forty individuals
had been exhumed. New excavations, beginning in 1943, have pro
duced additional relics. These remains found in Middle Pleistocene
strata are now known as the Pekin Man. The scientific name is Sinan
thropus pekinensis (Chinese Man from Peking). This ape-man is also
known as Homo erectus pekinensis and is believed to be a more advanced
type of Homo erectus erectus, better known as the ape-man of Java.
"The resemblances between Java and Peking man are striking," Pro
fessor Montagu notes, "and all in all it seems justifiable to conclude that
the latter represents a slightly more advanced geographic variety of the
former, and is to be included among the Pithecanthropines." (174, p.
60)
In China, fossil bones, when found, are customarily sold to pharma
cists, and these so-called dragon bones are concocted in medicines that
are supposed to cure all kinds of diseases. The Dutch geologist, Von
Koenigswald, paid a visit to a Chinese apothecary and discovered three
giant manlike molars. Two were lower third-year molars, both right
and left, derived from different individuals. There was also an upper
molar, probably from another individual. The volume of the crown of
the lower third molar was found to be six times the size of the same
tooth in man, and almost twice the size of the equivalent tooth of the
gorilla. Von Koenigswald assumed that he had discovered an ancient
giant of a man, and called the new species Gigantopithecus blacki. (The
blacki part of this name refers to Dr. Davidson Black, who was thus
honored by having an ancient fossil named after him.)
Later discoveries discredited Von Koenigswald's theory that he had
discovered an ancient man of gigantic stature. In a Middle Pleistocene
site in Kwangsi province in China, in 1957, a Gigantopithecus mandible
was excavated, with teeth intact. Examinations by experts have shown
that Gigantopithecus was an advanced type of ape, but not an ape-man,
and was not in the ancestry of man. These ancient apes were certainly
robust, but they were not giants, and there is not reason to think, as
some experts suggested, that they were related to the Australopithe



40 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
cines. But Von Koenigswald still believes that Gigantopithecus blacki has
a closer kinship to man than any other known ape, extant or extinct.
In the year 1907, in a quarry at Mauer, near Heidelberg, Germany,
a workman uncovered a large lower jaw of a primitive species of man
with all the teeth in situ. The fossil was found in lower Middle Pleisto
cene strata, and is thought to be about 500,000 years old. This Mauer
or Heidelberg man may have been a precursor of the Neanderthals and
a relative of Solo man. Much is known about the Neanderthal man, since we now have the
relics of more than one hundred individuals. The Neanderthaloids
flourished from 1 50,000 to 40,000 years ago, and they evidently had
more brains than modern men: The average brain capacity of the Ne
anderthals was 1,550 cc, while the average for modern man is only
1,350 cc. Neanderthal man possessed well-developed brow ridges, a
heavy jaw, minus a chin, a sloping forehead, and a projecting occiput
(the back of the head). Some reconstructions show Mr. Neanderthal
with a bull neck, stoop-shoulders, knock-knees, and features of a gro
tesque nature. It has been said that he was of low intelligence because
of his low forehead. But we have it from a high authority that:
All these statements are quite indefensible. Neanderthal man walked as
erect as any modern man, he did not have a bull neck, and he was not knock-kneed, and it has long ago been proven by many independent scien
tific investigators that neither the form of the brow nor the shape of the head have anything whatever to do with intelligence. . . . There is, in fact,
every reasofi to believe that Neanderthal man was every bit as intelligent as contemporary man. . . . For a long time it was believed that Neanderthal man was exterminated by men of our own type. There was absolutely no
evidence for this belief except the kind of theorizing that characterized
nineteenth-century thinkers of The Survival of the Fittest school, who be
lieved that warfare was old as man, and was the means by which one race conquered and exterminated another. Since Neanderthal remains are known for almost every part of the earth where human fossils have been found, it is inconceivable that Neanderthal man should everywhere have been exterminated. It is doubtful whether he was exterminated anywhere. The truth seems to be that he mixed with whatever populations he encountered and in the course of time was absorbed by such populations. Certainly
this was the case in the Middle East and in Europe, where many persons
may still be seen who bear the traces of their remote Neanderthal ancestry.
These traces may be observed in the rather heavy brow ridges, deepsunken eye sockets, receding foreheads, and weakly-developed chin re
gions. (174, pp. 67-69)



ICE AGES AND MEN 41
A prehistoric type of fossil man was discovered at Les Eyzies,
France, in the Cro-Magnon rock shelter; and hence was named the Cro-Magnon man. This find was made in 1898. Then from 1872 to
1902 remains of thirteen individuals of the same species were located in
the caves of Cote d'Azur, near Mentone, Italy. These Cro-Magnons
have been called the Apollos of the prehistoric world. They averaged
five feet eleven in height, had a cranial capacity of 1,660 cc, a straight
face, a well-developed nose, a high forehead, and strong jaws. The
Cro-Magnon was definitely a modern type of man, but of his early his
tory nothing certain is known.
We have discussed the remains of ape-men and prehistoric men who
lived in Africa, Asia, and Europe in times remote from the present.
Though African types are now regarded as the most ancient, there is
no consensus of opinion as to how the ancient forms blended together
to produce modern man. For instance, at Garusi, on the shores of Lake
Eyassi in East Africa, Kohl-Larsen in 1939 found fragments of a max
illa (upper vertebrate jaw) in an upper Paleolithic deposit. This fossil
was given the name Meganthropus Africamts, and it was announced in
1943 by its discoverers to be an Australopithecine; but two other ex
perts, A. Remane and H. Weinert, in 1950-5 1, examined the fossil and
concluded that it was a pithecanthropine and probably related to the
Meganthropus of Java. John T. Robinson, in 1953, stated his dissent
from that hypothesis by declaring Meganthropus Africanus to be closely
related to Plesianthropus transvaalensis, an African Australopithecine
originally discovered by Robert Broom at Sterkfontein in 1936.
In June, 1954, at Ternifine, Algeria, Professor C. Arambourg, of
Paris, found two lower human jaws in Middle Pleistocene strata. He
estimated their age to be about 500,000 years. This fossil type has been
named Atlanthropus Mauritanicus, and is thought to be closely related
to the pithecanthropines. A fragment of a hominid lower jaw was also
found in 1954 in a Middle Pleistocene site at Sidi Abderrahman, near
Casablanca, Morocco; and seemingly of the same type as Mauritanicus
of Algeria. These discoveries establish the presence of pithecanthropine
types in Africa.
A laborer, digging for mineral ores at Broken Hill, Northern Rho
desia (now Zambia), in 1921, discovered a Neanderthaloid type of
skull. Further discoveries were made at Broken Hill by A. S.
Armstrong (in 1921), and A. Hrdlicka (in 1925). This Rhodesian man
was given the official name of Homo rhodesiensis. This ancient man was



42 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
dolichocephalic (long-headed), with a cranial capacity of about 1,400
cc. Another skull of this type of man was found at Saldanha Bay, South
Africa, 1,500 miles from Broken Hill, and shows that Rhodesian man
ranged widely over the African terrain. Besides Neanderthaloid traits,
this early hominid displayed resemblances to Solo man and modern
Homo sapiens, 'in Rhodesian man, then," to cite Professor Montagu,
"we have perhaps a link between Solo man of Java, Neanderthal man
of many parts of the world, and the modern, or neanthropic, type of
man, as he is sometimes called." (174, p. 65)
The paleontologists and physical anthropologists have broadly clas
sified mankind into two groups: (1) paleanthropic, or ancient men; and
(2) neanthropic, or modern men. We have discussed briefly the an
cient human types, and now something must be said about neanthropic or modern man.
The principal representatives of modern fossil men are Swanscombe
man of England, Fontechevade man of France, and the men of Kanam
and Kanjera, in Kenya, East Africa. (There are many other examples
of neanthropic fossil men, which cannot be discussed here, due to limi
tations of space; but the interested reader might profitably read the
chapter on Homo sapiens in Man and His Ancestry by Alan Houghton
Brodrick [22].) In a quarry at Swanscombe, England, in 1935 and
1936, two bones of a single skull, the left parietal and complete occipi
tal bone, were dug out of a Middle Pleistocene deposit. (The parietal
bones are the pair of bones forming the top and sides of the skull. The
occipital bone is the bone at the back of the skull.) In 1955 the right pa
rietal bone of the same skull was found; and Swanscombe man was esti
mated to have had a cranial capacity of 1,325 cc. These bones are
thicker than those of more recent men, the parietals being flatter and
the occipital broader. Swanscombe man may be considered a link be
tween paleanthropic and neanthropic man, and is believed to have lived
about 200,000 years ago.
In 1947 in a cave not far from Montbrun, in western France, Miss
Germaine Henri-Martin discovered parts of two skulls. These fossils
were the remains of a pre-Neanderthal type of man. Having been found in Fontechevade cave, it was called the Fontechevade man. The
face was distinctly of a modern hominid type, and this early man could
boast of a cranial capacity of about 1,470 cc, and he lived over 100,000
years B.P. (before the present). It is believed that the Fontechevade
people later intermixed with the Neanderthals.



ICE AGES AND MEN 43
Reference has already been made ro the discoveries of Leakey of the Kanam and Kanjera men. The first discovery was made ar West Kanam, on the south shore of Kavironda Gulf, in Kenya. It was a parr
of a hominid mandible (lower jawbone), for which the discoverer
claimed a Lower Pleistocene date. 1 he general appearance of Kanam
man was distinctly that of the species sapiens. The next find was at
Kanjers, only a few miles from West Kanam. From the fragments of
four skulls found by Leakey, Brodrick gives us an idea of what the
Kanjera man looked like:
The bones are thick (for a Homo sapiens), the supra-orbital ridge is
slightly developed, the skull was very markedly dolichocephalic. In fact,
from the remains as they exist, it is fairly clear that Kanjera man was Homo
sapiens. A fragment of femur indicates that Kanjera man walked as we do
and was probably a pretty muscular fellow. The fauna and the artifacts at
the Kanjera hominid level are such as to suggest a later dating than that of
the Kanam deposit. . . . We may hope to discover other evidence sup
porting the theory that Homo sapiens existed in Africa long, long ago when
on that continent (as well as elsewhere) other types of hominid (e.g., nean
derthaloid and pithecanthropoid) were still surviving. (22, p. 207)
We have tried to keep the story of the evolution of man as simple as
possible. This is not easy, for even the experts seriously disagree among
themselves as to the how, why, and wherefore of the origin and devel
opment of the human race. "At one time," a leading authority points
out, "things used to be quite simple when anthropologists discussed the
origin and evolution of man. Modern man was the last and highest type
of man to be evolved. The most primitive type of man was held to be
Homo erectus (Pithecanthropus) erectus or possibly Homo erectus (Pithe
canthropus) rohustus. All the types of men in between the pithecanthro
pines and modern man were held to be simply intermediate stages in
the evolution of man from the most primitive to the most advanced
type. However, certain recent finds of fossil men render this earlier
and apparently perfectly logical interpretation of the evolution of man
seriously open to question." (174, p. 76) It is one of the merits of Pro
fessor Montagu that he has clearly grasped the network pattern of evo
lution, especially i.n relation to the early development of neanthropic
man. "If Swanscombe, Fontechevade, Kanam and Kanjera man are representatives of the modern like type of man," Montagu observes,
"the conclusion is unavoidable that a type of man resembling modern man is much more ancient than was previously supposed. It is quite



44 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
possible that Neanderthal man, for example, represents the product of
admixture between such types as Solo with neanthropic man, rather
than being a descendant of Homo erectus or Solo man in the direct
line. . . . Evolution is best likened not to a straight line, but rather to a
reticulum or network, in which all sorts of crisscrossing lines (groups)
pass in every direction, with interconnections established between the
different cords of the network." (174, pp. 84-86)
Should the reader be told that ancient Egyptian kings of the Middle
Empire made a signal contribution to modern prehistoric science, he
would probably find the statement hard to believe. Yet it is, neverthe
less, a fact. Andre Senet, a French scientific writer, has produced a val
uable and instructive work, Alan in Search of His Ancestors. The best
part of this book is Chapter IV: ''From African Apes to Modern Na
tions." At the very beginning of this chapter we are informed that:
The pharaohs of the Xllth Dynasty (Middle Empire), especially Sesos
tris II, were zealous reformers and very wise rulers, and were anxious to as
sure their lands a healthy economic prosperity. So they conceived the idea
of methodically exploiting an old prehistoric habitat watered by an arm of
the Nile. In the process, aided by a skilful system of canals and locks, they
created an artificial oasis southeast of Cairo, which became one of the most
fertile regions of Egypt and has remained so for 4,000 years. Simultane
ously they made it possible for the paleontologists of the Twentieth century
to discover the first apes to inhabit our planet. (223, p. 98)
The artificial oasis referred to by Senet is today called Lake Karoun,
but the ancient Egyptians knew it as Lake Moeris. Again to cite Senet:
The farmers who settled at the artificial oasis of Fayum soon became nu
merous. Sesostris II himself lived there in an immense palace in the town of
Shedet, consecrated to the crocodile-god Sebek, whence the name Croco
dilopolis given it by the Greek historian Herodotus. The oasis has been
continuously inhabited ever since, and has not failed to attract the attention
of paleontologists, prehistorians and archaeologists, who have made there
some very interesting discoveries. ... It is enough for the moment to note
that in this part of Africa 3 5 to 45 million years ago, there lived the first of
all the known apes, discovered by an Austrian, Max Schlosser, in 1910.
(223, p. 98)
This primitive ape, parapithecus, is represented only by a fossilized
lower jaw, but from the size of the jaw and the tooth structure much of
value has been learned; for parapithecus possessed a hominid dental pat
tern: two incisors, one canine, two pre-molars, and three molars. By



ICE AGES AND MEN 45
studying a jaw that can be held in the palm of the hand, paleontologists have learned about an anthropoid species from which the human family
branched off millions of years ago. The same jMax Schlosser discovered at Fayum the fossil jaw of a larger and more advanced type of ape, pro
pliopithecus, believed to be a descendant of parapithecus. In recent
years Leakey and Hopwood have collected fossils of nearly three hun
dred apes that lived in Kenya around twenty-five million years ago,
ranging in size from that of a small gibbon to that of a large gorilla. Of
these ancient Kenya apes, Limnopithecus shows the closest relation to
mankind, since his molars were quite similar to human molars.
As a result of these discoveries [Sener argues] the genealogical tree of the
earliest apes takes a fairly clear shape. It was in Egypt first of all in the Fayum region, that parapithecus and propliopithecus appeared. From the latter descended in more or less direct line the Kenya Limnopithecus, the European Pliopithecus (France, Switzerland, Bavaria, Slovakia, etc.) and the East African proconsul. All these apes are remarkable for the shape of
their teeth, which are not specialized, and especially for their canines which are always of medium size as in men, and never form fangs overlapping the other teeth as in the large apes of today. Furthermore judging by the shape of their thigh and ankle-bones, these apes seem to have walked on their hind feet and to have used their arms little as a means of locomotion. . . .
So it was at this stage of evolution, let us say about 25 million years ago, that two branches seem to have definitely diverged from the stem of African apes, one leading in the direction of man and the other of modern apes, known as anthropoids, represented by the gibbons, chimpanzees, gorillas
and orang-utans. (223, p. 101)
Among modern experts who have studied the origin and evolution of
the human race are Hrdlicka, Boule, Vallois, Arambourg, Teilhard de Chardin, and Le Gros Clark. The views of these prehistorians may be grouped into four hypotheses, which are outlined by Senet as follows:
The first hypothesis, which is only mentioned here as a reminder, is Hrdlicka's; he makes the four principal stages in the evolution of humanity
descend directly from one another. . . . The second hypothesis makes the Mauer (Heidelberg) man descend almost directly from the Australopithecus, and from him modern man descends through the Swanscombe-Fontechevade line. . . . In the third hypothesis the Mauer man is still at the origin of modern, but this time by way of the progressive Neanderthal men (the oldest). On their side, and still more or less deriving from Australopithecus, came first of all the pre-men, who gave rise to the typical Neanderthalians. The Swanscombe-Fontechevade line evolved independently, though no one knows its
origin or end.



46 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The fourth hypothesis admits purely and simply that at least four at
tempts have been made in the human direction: Australopithecus, pre-man,
the Neanderthal man, and finally modern man, the last having alone suc
ceeded. In this hypothesis it could equally well have been in the Swanscombe-Fontechevade line that modern man had his origin. (223, pp. 121
23)
Ice ages have played an important role in the history of evolution,
and something must be said about them. Geological time is divided into
six eras; these are listed below, with estimates of time duration for each
era in years B.P. (before present):
1. Eozoic 1,750 million years B.P.
2. Archeozoic 500 million years B.P.
3. Proterozoic 500 million years B.P.
4. Paleozoic 550 million years B.P.
5. Mesozoic 200 million years B.P.
6. Cenozoic 60 million years B.P.
There is a close connection between ice ages and geological eras.
There have been five ice ages in the history of the world, and each of
these ice ages came at the close of a geological era. The first ice age
ended the Archeozoic, the second great chill was at the end of Protero
zoic, the third at the termination of the Paleozoic, the fourth at the con
clusion of the Mesozoic, and the fifth or Great Ice Age brought the
Cenozoic era to its close. The first and second ice ages occurred before
the development of land life, so we can only speculate concerning their
effects on the marine life of those remote days. The third ice age, at the
end of the Paleozoic, brought on the downfall of the amphibian and the
dominance of the reptile. The fourth ice age, at the end of the Meso
zoic, initiated the decline of the reptile and the rise of the mammal.
And the fifth, or Great Ice Age, raised man to a position of dominance
in the animal kingdom. The late Joseph McCabe, who was a keen stu
dent of the history of ice ages, called the Pleistocene glaciation "the ice age that made man."
Everything we know [McCabe tells us] tends to show that social life
began round the fires which man learned to make when the cold set in, and
under the rock shelters where some protection was afforded from the biting winds. That was the real making of man. There is no reason whatever to
suppose that the pace of human progress would have increased as it did if
there had been no Ice Age. (151, pp. 120-21)



ick ac;ks and men 47
The ice ages of the past have a perrinent message for rhe present; for
the record of rhe rocks shows rhar rhe rime inrervals berween ice ages
grow ever smaller, and rhar rhe earth grows colder conrinuously. Afrer
each chill rhe earrh warms up again, bur ir does nor become as warm as
ir was before rhe previous ice age. Today we have vast polar ice caps
around rhe poles, borh Norrh and Sourh; bur rhey were nor rhere be
fore rhe Grear Ice Age. In rhe ages of rhe furure as rhe earrh rolls on
rhrough space, ice ages will come closer rogerher in rime, and rhe polar
ice caps will increase in size, unril finally rhe creeping norrhern and
sourhern polar caps will meer in rhe equatorial regions and rhe world
will be a dead and frozen globe, like unro rhe moon. This will happen
millions of years in rhe furure, and man will long ere that time have
gone the way of the dinosaur and the mammoth.
Consider the mammoth—his rise and his fall! When the Great Ice
Age set in, certain elephants, rather than freeze, grew shaggy coats of
hair and evolved into mammoths. The cave man was a neighbor of the
mammoth, and he did not grow a shaggy coat; but he learned to build
fires, to make coats out of the skins of animals, and to dwell in caves.
Both mammoth and man survived the last ice age; but soon after the re
treat of the ice the mammoth went the way of all flesh; whereas man
continued to increase and multiply. The poor old mammoth perished
from overspecialization; since in a temperate clime he had no need of
his shaggy coat, but he nevertheless was stuck with it. In times past he
had roamed over icy tundras and dined on dwarf willows and mosses,
and now his habitat was covered by forests. With hooves and trunks
developed for rooting in the snow, and a digestive system adapted to
the Arctic scrub, the mammoth found himself in surroundings to which
he was entirely unsuited; so he just hauled off and died. Man was far
more flexible; for when the climare gor warmer, he rook off his coar
and pur our his fire. The cave man invenred weapons, slew mammorhs, and dined on mammorh sreaks; and when rhere were no longer any mammorhs the cave man would find ir no hardship ro swirch over ro
beefsreaks. Bur how, we ask, did mankind manage ro survive rhe rigors
of rhe Grear Ice Age? For man is inferior ro orher animals in many re
specrs. He does nor have rhe furry coar of rhe polar bear ro prorecr him
from rhe winds of winrer; he cannor ourrun rhe hare or rhe osrrich; nor
has he rhe wings of rhe eagle, rhe keen vision of rhe hawk, or rhe physi
cal prowess of rhe riger.
In rhe comparatively short evolutionary history documented by fossil remains [we are told by Professor Childe] man has nor improved his inherited



48 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
equipment by bodily changes detected in his skeleton yet he has been able
to adjust himself to a greater range of environments than almost any other
creature, to multiply infinitely faster than any near relative among the
higher mammals, and to beat the polar bear, the hare, the hawk, and the
tiger at their special tricks. Through his control of fire and the skill to make
clothes and houses, man can, and does, live and thrive from the arctic circle
to the equator. In the trains and cars he builds man can outstrip the fleetest
hare and ostrich. In airplanes he can mount higher than the eagle, and with
telescopes see farther than the hawk. With firearms he can lay low animals
that a tiger dare not tackle. . . . Man's compensation for his relatively poor
bodily endowment has been the possession of a large and complex brain,
forming the center of an extensive and delicate nervous system. These per
mit a great variety of accurately controlled movements, being adjusted ex
actly to the impulses received by keen organs of sense. It is only so that
man has been enabled to make himself protections against climate and
weather, arms and weapons of attack and defense that, because they can be
adapted and adjusted, are really superior to furs, or teeth, or claws. (46, pp. 27-28)
If man survived because he had a better brain than any of his com
petitors, how did he get this superior mental equipment? This brings
up the question of the evolution of the mind; and we use the word mind
here in the strictly scientific sense. "The mind," says Professor J. P.
Guilford, "if it has any scientific meaning at all, is the functioning of
the brain. But since the brain cannot function alone, but only in con
junction with nerves, receptors and effectors, mind is better described
as the functioning of the entire nervous system. " (102, p. 24)
There are three main sections of the brain, namely: (1) fore, (2)
mid, and (3) hind brains. The fore brain is divided into two parts,
bound together by a band of fibers, and these two sections of the fore
brain are called the cerebral hemispheres. It is here that consciousness
has its seat, and the place where the thinking process takes place; and
were it not for the cerebral hemispheres men could never learn to per
form the numerous tasks requiring skill and ingenuity. The mid and
hind brains in man are much smaller than the fore brain, and they con
trol only the automatic and unconscious activities of the nervous sys
tem. Messages are brought from the outside world to the cerebral
hemispheres of the brain by the afferent or sensory nerves; then the
messages are relayed from the central hemispheres to all parts of the
body by the efferent or motor nerves, and movements in the various
muscles are thereby produced. Each part of the body is connected to a



ICE AGES AND MEN 49
special area of the brain; there are areas controlling sight, speech, hear
ing, and smell; and motor centers governing the movements of the var
ious parts of the body. Then there are large tracts of the cerebral hemi
spheres, that are known as association areas; for here mental pictures
are formed and memories stored. By far the most complex part of the
brain is the prefrontal area; for it is the master control center of the
brain, and the seat of the higher mental faculties. In the words of an
able student of evolutionary history: "As we study men of old times, it
will be seen that this (the prefrontal area) is the last part of the brain to
develop, and that since the days of the first ape-men it has increased
very much in size and complexity. This marvelous brain, with its well
organized association areas, and dominating prefrontal area, has been
the chief factor in man's evolution." (58, p. 12)
The lowest animals are devoid of brains. They have instead a nerve
running from head to tail, with smaller branch nerves radiating from it.
The head part of this nerve increased in size and evolved into three
nodules, and this was the earliest type of brain. The nerves of smell ran
into the fore-brain, the optic nerves were connected with the mid
brain, and the taste nerves hooked up with the hind brain. In the rep
tile, the pallium or smell-brain is predominant. In the apes and man the
pallium has shrunken down almost to insignificance. In the primates
the smell-brain has been overshadowed by the neopallium, a structure
dominated by vision. The earliest mammals had snouts and smelled
their way through the world; but when the more intelligent mammals
took to the trees, they had to rely more on sight and touch than on
smell; so they lost their snouts, and their brains expanded. As their
heads bulged to accommodate the growing brains, the eyes crept from
the sides of the head and began to fall frontward. The fields of vision of
the two eyes then overlapped, thus producing stereoscopic vision. Life
in the trees, besides stimulating the growth of the brain and improving
vision, also brought about the differentiation of the forepaws into
hands. It was this combination of brain, eye, and hand that brought the
forerunners of man to the top of the heap. "Man's intellectual preemi
nence," in the words of Elliot Smith, "is based primarily on the evolu
tion of macular vision in a primate with adaptable hands, which at
tained the erect attitude when the cerebral cortex under the conscious
influence of vision came to control and regulate posture." (Cited by Jo
seph McCabe, in 155, p. 32) The fifth or Great Ice Age is estimated to have begun about a mil



50 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
lion and a half years ago and to have lasted until about 25,000 years
ago. The ice advanced and retreated four times in all, so that there
were, in those distant days, four cold glacial periods and three temper
ate interglacial ones. In North America the ice cap covered an area of
four million square miles and in Europe about two million square miles.
In the Miocene period, from twenty-eight million to twelve million
years ago, the human species differentiated into two main types: (1)
Paleoanthropic (ancient men) and (2) Neanthropic (modern men).
These two types lived through the Pliocene period (twelve million to
two million years before present), and struggled for survival in the ice
fields of the Pleistocene period (beginning about two million years
ago). Before the Great Ice Age terminated, Paleoanthropic man had
become extinct, leaving the future to Homo sapiens, the culmination of
the Neanthropic line. Pleistocene men are appropriately known as the
men of the Old Stone Age, for in the words of Professor G. G. Mac
Curdy, "the paleolithic is practically coextensive with the pleistocene
epoch and the Ice Age." The Old Stone Age is divided into seven cul
ture stages; the first four being called the Lower Paleolithic, and the
last three, the Upper Paleolithic. The seven stages of the Paleolithic
age are designated by the archaeologists as follows:
1. Abbevillian (also called Mesvinian or pre-Chellean). The imple
ments of this stage, in Europe, date back to about 125,000 B.C.,
and the workmanship is crude, with distinctive types being con
spicuous by their absence. The earliest tool of European man
belongs to this epoch, being a stone implement shaped to fit the
fist, crudely flaked and pointed, known as the coup-de-poing, or
fist-hatchet. [The earliest Stone Age artifacts in Europe were
found near Abbeville, France, by Boucher de Perthes, in 1844,
and hence this culture stage was called Abbevillian. Later similar
stone relics were unearthed at Mesvin, Belgium, and some au
thorities have named this stage the Mesvinian. Since this culture
stage preceded the Chellean, it is also widely known as pre-Chel
lean. In recent years stone artifacts of an Abbevillian type have
been found in East Africa, and are believed to be about 300,000
years old.]
2. Chellean. The relics of the Chellean culture stage were first found
at Chelles, France, hence the name. They date back to about 100,000 B.C. The pre-Chellean coup-de-poing was improved at
this time by roughly flaking it on both sides and giving it an a 1



ICE AGES AND MEN 51
mond-shaped point and better fitting to the grasping hand. The
men of Chelles also manufactured stone scrapers and spokeshaves
of stone, but these were chipped on one face only.
Acheulean. The name is derived from St. Acheuel, near Amiens,
France. This culture was widespread around 80,000 years ago.
Acheulean stone relics have been found not only in Europe, but
also in Greenland, Canada, our own U.S.A., Mexico, Africa,
Western Asia, India and China. This culture was a refinement of
the Chellean stage; the coup-de-poings, or hand-axes were more
expertly made; and among the abundant Acheulian relics are ar
rowheads, spear-heads, knives, hammers, scrapers, and anvils.
Among the makers of these artifacts are believed to have been the
ancient men of Mauer (Heidelberg) and Peking.
Mousterian. This culture stage is named after the cave of I,e
Moustier, on the Vezere River, in France. The fist-hatchet or
hand-axe continued in use among the Neanderthals of the Mous
terian culture level, but it was no longer the dominant imple
ment, for the scraper and point largely superseded it. This cul
ture stage is found on all continents, especially in association with
the remains of Neanderthals of about 50,000 years ago. These an
cient men also produced artifacts of bone.
Aurignacian. The name is from Aurignac, France, where these
remains were first found. In this phase of culture we enter the
Upper Paleolithic, a culture epoch, in which a type of Homo sapiens, known as Cro-Magnon Man, seems to have been the domi
nant type. These men continued to use the older type tools, but
also invented new ones, such as the dart-thrower, the needle
(made of bone or ivory) and a harpoon made from reindeer horn. These cave men were also artists; as they engraved and drew pic
tures in relief and in the round on the walls of their caves. These
figures were often skillfully colored with crayons of ocher or
manganese. These men lived in Europe about 30,000 years ago.
Solutrean. Named after artifacts discovered at Solutre, France.
The Solutrean epoch has been divided into upper and lower
phases. The Lower Solutrean was characterized by implements
with laurel-leaf blades, chipped on both sides and reduced to
great thinness. The Upper Solutrean, on the other hand, was
characterized by the production of small pointed willow-leaf
shaped implements, with a single lateral notch at the base. In this
phase of the Solutrean culture, around 22,000 years ago, in Spain,



52 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
France, Czechoslovakia, and Poland, clever primitive men and
women made from bone and ivory the earliest needles containing
an eye.
7. Magdalenian. This culture stage is named from La Madeleine, on
the Vezere River, in France. The laurel and willow-leaf tools of
the Solutrean epoch did not survive among the Magdalenians.
The graver still persisted, and a new type of parrot-beak imple
ment was invented. At the close of the Magdalenian, the harpoon
made of reindeer horn survived in a much-improved form. The
Magdalenian cave artists produced some of the best artwork of
the Old Stone Age—especially the polychrome frescoes of the
Altimira Cave in Spain.
The Great Ice Age was now over, but man, on the road to civiliza
tion, had yet to pass through the Mesolithic or Middle Stone Age and
the Neolithic or New Stone Age; but, before we proceed, there is a
question relating to the Old Stone Age that should be noted. Since the
Paleolithic chronology is based mainly on finds of European origin, the
question arises: What is the relevance of these European relics to the
rest of the world? The conclusion that Paleolithic cultures were of Eu
ropean origin would certainly be unwarranted. At the present time it
seems reasonable to believe that the Paleolithic period commenced in
Africa, and in later days spread to Europe. Future discoveries will indu
bitably bring to light new evidence, but the gist of the African discov
eries was foreseen some years ago by Sir Arthur Keith, and has been
summarized in one sentence by a distinguished historian of civilization:
The skull and cave-painting found in Rhodesia in 1921, the flints discov
ered in Egypt by De Morgan in 1896, the paleolithic finds of Seton-Karr in
Somaliland, the Old Stone Age deposits in the basin of the Fayum, and the
Still Bay culture of South Africa, indicate that the Dark Continent went
through approximately the same prehistoric periods of development in the
art of flaking stone as those which we have outlined in Europe; perhaps, in
deed, the quasi-Aurignacian remains in Tunis and Algiers strengthen the
hypothesis of an African origin or stopping point for the Cro-Magnon race,
and therefore for European man. (76, p. 94)
The study of the prehistoric archaeology of Africa is a project still in
its infancy, but there is a good brief discussion of it in Men of the Dawn
by Dorothy Davison. (58)
The Epi-Paleolithic or Mesolithic period may be properly desig



ICE AGES AND MEN 53
nared as the Dark Ages of prehistoric times. "It was," says Dorothy
Davison, "a time of degeneration for Europe, when old ideas passed
away, and new ones had not yet travelled from the earliest civilizations
of the Eastern Mediterranean to take their place." (58, p. 151) The
Mesolithic period has been divided into six culture phases, bearing the
following names: (1) Azilian, (2) Tardenoisian, (3) Maglemosean, (4)
Asturian, (5) Shell-Heap, and (6) Campignian.
The Azilian phase was widespread in western Europe; and in the
fossil deposits are a plenitude of the bones of stags, but reindeer bones
are absent. The lithic remains show small flint blades and disk-shaped
scrapers, and pebbles used as paring knives and chisels. The Azilians
manufactured a flat staghorn harpoon with a perforated base. Among
the Azilian artifacts were found pebbles painted with geometrical de
signs in red; for what reason we do not know.
The next culture stage in western Europe was the Tardenoisian.
Painted pebbles were absent, but small stone implements, called micro
liths, were abundant; and these were frequently of geometric design,
being shaped into the forms of crescents, triangles, and trapeziums; and
scratchers and small blades were likewise plentiful. The Azilian and
Tardenoisian cultures seem to have overlapped; for in the Cave of
Valle, in northern Spain, both Azilian and Tardenoisian tools are found
in the same layer.
The Maglemosean culture seems to have been restricted to Scandi
navia, the best remains having been found in sites in Denmark. It per
haps was an offshoot of the Tardenoisian, since both cultures had much
in common—especially a composite implement, made of bone, with an
armature of microliths.
The Asturian culture, named after the province of Asturias, in
Spain, is probably a local and late phase of the Mesolithic. The typical
tool of the Asturian phase was a pick, chipped to a crude point, from an oval pebble. Bone tools were few in number, and these were crudely
made.
The Shell-Heap culture, located on or near present shorelines, was
named after the great heaps of shells left on the shorelines by the pre
historic inhabitants of those regions. Among the stone implements of
this culture were picks, and a type of tool, triangular in shape, with
transverse edge, ranging in size from arrowhead to hatchet. These peo
ple had no polished stone artifacts, and their pottery was crude, bur
they used awls and chisels made from antlers of stags, and possessed
some objects made of bone.



54 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The Campignian phase seems to have been restricted to France. The
ancient site of the village of Campigny consisted of land habitations
marked by hut pits; and the Campignians had flint tools similar to those
used by the people of the Shell-Heap culture. They were unfamiliar
with the art of polishing stone, but they did have pottery and milling
stones. From that circumstance we gather that they no longer relied
exclusively on the chase as a means of subsistence. Among the animal
population of the Campignians were horses, stags, and oxen; and from
fossil remains we learn of the presence of the oak tree and the ash
among the flora.
Up to now, man had been a gatherer of food, but at the beginning of
the Neolithic period (New Stone Age), he became a food producer.
This was a great step forward in cultural evolution. Man continued to
hunt and fish but he no longer had to rely entirely on these pursuits for
his supply of food and clothing. By the domestication of animals and
plants, and the invention of the ceramic and textile arts, human society
was put on a foundation of greater stability and security. Neolithic men
reduced flint chipping to a fine art, and established flint mining on a
commercial scale. They also shaped and finished stone tools by a proc
ess of fine polishing. Pile villages were built on lakeshores and over
streams and swamplands, and men traveled on water in dugout canoes.
There were two epochs of the New Stone Age. The first was charac
terized by two types of flint axes: an early model with a pointed head,
and a later one with head flat and quadrangular in section. The second
epoch was represented by a new type of flint axe, with the head square
in section. The second epoch has been divided into four phases, charac
terized by the monumental remains predominant in each phase. They
were successively: (1) simple dolmens; (2) many-chambered dolmens;
(3) stone cists; and (4) individual graves. A familiar feature of the Neo
lithic period was the huge stone monuments, known as megaliths.
Megalithic monuments are widely distributed in the Old World. In
Africa they have been found in Egypt, Ethiopia, the Sudan, Algeria,
Morocco, Libya, Tunisia, and Madagascar. In Asia we find them in
India, Indonesia, China, Japan, Persia, Syria, Palestine, Russia, and
Turkey. And in Europe megalithic monuments are found in Denmark,
the United Kingdom, Belgium, the Netherlands, Germany, Italy,
France, Norway, Portugal, Spain, Switzerland, Sweden, Bulgaria, and
Russia.
A Swedish archaeologist, Oscar Montelius, traces Neolithic remains
on the site of the ancient Elamite city of Susa back to an antiquity of



ICE AGES AND MEN 55
20,000 years; and Sir Arthur Evans, the English expert on prehistory,
has traced the culture of Knossus, in Crete, 14,000 years into the past.
The New Stone Age began much later than this in western Europe.
After the New Stone Age, there came the ages of Metals; but since
the discovery of metal-working is closely connected with the rise of
civilization, we shall postpone discussion of it to a later chapter.



CHAPTER FOUR
Totemism and Exogamy
THE human race in its long history has developed two fundamentally
different systems of government. The first plan, the foundation of an
cient society, was a social organization based on the kinship group
that is to say, the clan or tribe. The second scheme, the foundation of
modern society, was a political organization based upon territory and
property. In this chapter, we propose to discuss briefly the primitive so
cial organization. There is an extensive literature on this phase of social
anthropology, examples of which will be noted as we proceed; but a
concise survey of the field is here attempted, with optimum clarity and
with the necessary technical terminology defined and explained.
A typical tribe is composed of several kinship groups, known as
clans; and these clans are generally organized into two intermediate
groups known as phratries, with half of the clans in each phratry. A
good example of a typical tribal organization is the Seneca Iroquois, a
famous Amerindian tribe, which consisted of eight clans, grouped into
two phratries as follows:
Phratry A: Clan 1, Bear; Clan 2, Wolf; Clan 3, Beaver; Clan 4,
Turtle
Phratry B: Clan 5, Deer; Clan 6, Snipe; Clan 7, Heron; Clan 8,
Hawk
It will be noticed that all the clans of the Seneca-Iroquois tribe are
named after animals. The animal from which a clan derives its name is
supposedly the ancestor of the clan. A member of the Bear Clan will
tell you that his remote ancestors were bears; a member of the Wolf
Clan will argue the original progenitor of his clan was a wolf, and so
on. This clan ancestor is called the totem. Totemism is one of the most
important institutions of primitive society and it is closely connected
56



TOTEMISM AND EXOGAMY 57
with another primitive institution known as exogamy. The word exog
amy literally means marrying out; and it is that rule in primitive so
cieties which forbids a clan member to marry one of his own group. In
other words, the aforesaid clan member must marry outside the group.
Totemism has been falsely defined by certain writers as a species of
animal worship; but a totem is not a god and, hence, is not an object of
worship. There is considerable literature on totemism, but the classic
work on the subject is Totemism and Exogamy by Sir James George
Frazer (90). The best definition of totemism was given by Professor A. C. Haddon of Cambridge University in an address before the anthro
pological section of the British Association for the Advancement of
Science.
Totemism as Dr. Frazer and I understand it in its fully developed condi
tion [Haddon informs us] implies the division of a people into several totem
kins (or, as they are usually termed, totem clans), each of which has one or
sometimes more than one totem. The totem is usually a species of animal,
sometimes a species of plant, occasionally a natural object or phenomenon,
very rarely a manufactured object. Totemism also involves the rules of ex
ogamy, forbidding marriage between the kins. It is essentially connected
with the matriarchal stage of culture (mother-right), though it passes over
into the patriarchal stage (father-right). The totems are regarded as kins
folk and protectors of the kinsmen, who respect them and abstain from kill
ing and eating them. There is thus a recognition of mutual rights and obli
gations between the members of the kin and their totem. The totem is the
crest or symbol of the kin.
The ideological basis of totemism is a primitive theory of evolution;
for the totemites believe themselves to be descended from the totem
species. "To be descended from a crocodile or blood-brother to the
crow," Professor Read reminds us, "or the same as a kangaroo, must
be, we think, the grotesque notion of a lunatic. We never meet people
who believe such a thing; it has no stronghold in our own breasts. . . .
But every student of early institutions finds it necessary to give himself
some intelligible account of its nature and sources." (202, p. 223)
The totemic complex has a widespread geographic range, being
found in Asia, Australia, Africa, Polynesia, and the Americas. "If we
exclude hypotheses and confine ourselves to facts," Sir J. G. Frazer as
serts, "we may say broadly that totemism is practiced by many savage
and barbarous peoples, . . . who occupy the continents and islands of
the tropics and the Southern Hemisphere, together with a large part of



58 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
North America, and whose complexion shades off from coal black
through dark brown to red. With the doubtful exception of a few
Mongoloid tribes in Assam, no yellow and no white race is totemic."
(90, Vol. IV, p. 14)
The question of the origin of totemism has been discussed exhaus
tively by Frazer in the voluminous work cited above and in some of his
other scholarly studies. The complexity of devising a plausible theory
capable of explaining totemism proved so great that Frazer finally suc
ceeded in framing three different theories of totemic origins, and these
three attempts to explain the phenomena of totemism bear the fol
lowing names: (1) the External Soul Theory, (2) the Industrial
Theory, and (3) the Conceptional Theory. We shall discuss this triplet
of explanations in turn, and try to assess their theoretical value.
To the primitive savage the soul was not some mysterious immaterial
entity; quite the contrary, for in the words of Frazer, "the savage
thinks of it (the soul) as a concrete material thing of a definite bulk, ca
pable of being seen and handled, kept in a box or jar, and liable to be
bruised, fractured or smashed in pieces." This line of thought leads us
logically to the doctrine of the external soul; for, as Frazer contends:
It is not needful that the life, so conceived should be in the man; it may
be absent from his body and still continue to animate him by virtue of a sort
of sympathy or action at a distance. So long as this object which he calls his
life or soul remains unharmed, the man is well; if it is injured, he suffers; if
it is destroyed, he dies. . . . But there may be circumstances in which, if
the life or soul remains in the man, it stands a greater chance of sustaining
injury than if it were stowed away in some safe and secret place. Ac
cordingly, in such circumstances, primitive man takes his soul out of his
body and deposits it for security in some snug spot, intending to replace it
in his body when the danger is past. Or, if he should discover some place of
absolute security, he may be content to leave his soul there permanently.
The advantage of this is that so long as the soul remains unharmed in the
place where he has deposited it, the man himself is immortal; nothing can
kill his body since his life is not in it." (87, p. 668)
In many savage tribes possessing the totemic organization, there ex
isted a ritual of death and resurrection; and in these rites, the young
men of the tribe, at the period of puberty, were subjected to a cere
mony of mock-death. While pretending death, the souls of the young
men were transferred to the tribal totems and, in turn, the souls of the
totems were supposed to enter into the bodies of the young men. The



TOTEMISM AND EXOGAMY 59
ceremonies ended with a rite which purported to restore life to the ini
tiates. The meaning of this ritual death and resurrection is explained by
Frazer: "The lad dies as a man and comes to life again as an animal; the
animal's soul is now in him, and his human soul is in the animal. With
good right, therefore, does he call himself a bear or a wolf or what not,
according to his totem; and with good right does he treat the bears or
wolves or what not as his brethren, since in these animals are lodged
the souls of himself and his kindred." (88, p. 363)
(The foregoing passage is a good short statement of Frazer's Exter
nal Soul Theory; for, according to this theory, the totem is nothing
more than the receptacle in which a man keeps his soul.)
Frazer's Industrial Theory of Totemism is based on the magical Inti
chiuma Ceremonies of the Arunta tribe of Central Australia; and the
purpose of these rites is to multiply the tribal totems, which are usually
edible plants or animals. Each clan strives to multiply its own totem for
the benefit of the other clans, which other groups are permitted to
freely dine on the particular plant or animal of which the aforesaid
totem is representative; but, except on special occasions, no clan is per
mitted to adopt its own totem as an article of diet. A fine example of
this primitive totemic ritual has been transmitted to us by Professor Haddon:
The Wichetty Grub Intichiuma Ceremony is performed in a special cave, where lies a large block of quartzite surrounded by small round
stones. The former represents the perfect insect, and the latter its eggs.
The headman and his associates tap the large stone and chant songs, the
burden of which is an invitation to the insect to lay eggs; the headman
strikes each man with one of the small stones, saying, "you have eaten
much food." Later they go to a large rock which they tap, and invite the animal to come from all directions and lay eggs. After various symbolic
ceremonies, they enter a long narrow booth made of bushes, which represents the chrysalis case from which the perfect insect emerges, and there
they sing of the animal in its various stages and of the sacred stones. There are many similar ceremonies which the men of a totem group make in order to increase or produce their particular totem; thus taking the tribe as a
whole, the object of these ceremonies is that of increasing the total food supply. . . . After the ceremony, when, as a consequence, the animal or
plant has become abundant, the men of all classes and totems go out and bring supplies into the main camp. No one as yet may eat it. The headman of the totem, in the presence of all in the camp, solemnly ears a little and hands the remainder over to the men of the other totems, telling them to eat
freely. If the headman did not eat a little, he would lose the power of per
forming Intichiuma successfully." (104, pp. 42-^}4)



60 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
According to Frazer's Industrial Theory, totemism grew out of the
attempts of primitive man to maintain a plentiful supply of the necessi
ties of subsistence.
The general explanation of totemism to which the magical ceremonies
called lntichiuma seem to point [as explained by Frazer] is that it is pri
marily an organized and cooperative system of magic designed to secure for
the members of the community on the one hand, a plentiful supply of all
the commodities of which they stand in need, and, on the other hand, im
munity from all the perils and dangers to which man is exposed in his struggle with nature. Each totem group on this theory was charged with the superintendence and control of some department of nature from which it took
its name, and with which it sought, as far as possible, to identify itself (90,
Vol. I, p. 116)
Finding both the External Soul and Industrial Theories of totemism,
in turn, inadequate as satisfactory explanations of the origin of the to
temic complex, Frazer framed his Conceptional Theory of Totemism.
Among truly primitive peoples, the true nature of childbirth is not
known, and hence among such groups, the family was matrilineal; that
is, descent was traced through the mother, since the true nature of pa
ternity was unknown. And, indeed, even today, such beliefs are cur
rent among certain tribes of Australia and the Trobriand Islanders of
Melanesia. In central Australia, childbirth is attributed to the agency of
spirits; and these spirits or ghosts are supposed to dwell in trees, rocks,
rivers, etc.; and they leave these habitations to enter women at the mo
ment of conception. When a child is born, it is thought to be a spirit in
human form. If a woman happened to be near a tree haunted by spirits
of kangaroo people at the moment of conception, her child would be
come a member of a clan whose totem was the kangaroo. Or, if a woman became aware of the fact that she was to become a mother
while near rocks inhabited by the spirits of emu people, her child
would be assigned to a clan recognizing the emu as a totem. In such
primitive cultures, human beings are regarded as essentially spirits of
various animals and plants masquerading in human form.
Here at last we seem to find a complete and adequate explanation of the
origin of totemism [Frazer asserts]. The conceptional theory, as I have
called my third, and so far as I can see, my final theory of totemism, ac
counts for all the facts in a simple and natural manner. It explains why people commonly abstain from killing and eating their totemic animals and
plants or otherwise injuring their totems. The reason is that, identifying



TOTEMISM AND EXOGAMY 61
themselves with their totems, they are naturally careful not to hurt or de
stroy them. . . . Lastly, it explains why totemic peoples often confuse their ancestors with their totems. The reason is that, regarding their ancestors as animal or plant in essence, though human in form, they find it hard to distinguish even in thought between their outward human appearance and their bestial or vegetable nature; they think of them vaguely both as men and as animals or plants; the contradiction between the two things does not
perplex them, though they cannot picture it clearly to their minds. . . .
Thus in the conceptional theory we seem to find a sufficient explanation of all the facts and fancies of totemism. (88, pp. 83-85.)
Frazer's three theories of the origin of totemism may be considered
as complementary to each other, rather than contradictory. This, at any rate, was the opinion of Frazer himself.
Perhaps after all [he finally concluded] my first theory is not wholly irreconcilable with my two later theories. Thus, for example, a man whose totem was an emu because the spirit of an emu was supposed to have entered his mother at conception (on the Conceptional Theory) would natu
rally be thought peculiarly qualified to multiply emus for the good of the
tribe (on the Industrial Theory), and it might be deemed advisable that at puberty he should deposit his soul in an emu (on the External Soul Theory) either for safety or in order to strengthen his connection with the birds and increase his power of magically multiplying them. On this view, my three successive theories of totemism would turn out to be, not inconsistent with, but complementary to, each other. (88, p. 363 n)
There are numerous other theories concerning the origin of totem
ism, but here we shall consider only one more. The Right Honorable
John M. Robertson in his scholarly study Pagan Christs, in a discussion
of the relations between totemism and sacraments, attempted to arrive
at a plausible theory of totemic origins; and, finding the current
theories unconvincing, Robertson formulated his own solution of the
problem. In his opinion, totemism arose out of the efforts of primitive
groups to name themselves.
On the difficult subject of totemism [Robertson declares] the suggestion
may here be incidentally offered that, the totem was, in origin, merely the group's way of naming itself. Such group-names were as necessary as individual names; and while a person could readily be labeled from the place of
his birth or any family incident at that period, or by a physical or moral pe
culiarity, clans of the same stock could with difficulty be distinguished in the nomadic state save by arbitrary names, which could best be drawn from the list of natural objects. Indeed, it is hard to conceive how otherwise no
madic clans could first name themselves. (210, p. 100)



°2 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Readers interested in the various ramifications of totemism are re
ferred to Man and His Superstitions by Professor Carveth Read (202).
Exogamy is an institution with its roots in a remote past, but it is
probably not as old as totemism. Its antiquity, however, is so great that
there is no record of its beginnings; for exogamy, like totemism, is of
prehistoric origin. The first scientific study of exogamy was made by
the Scottish anthropologist John F. McLennan, who promulgated a
theory which he thought adequately explained its origin. According to
McLennan, certain savage tribes adopted a custom of killing all female
children at birth, and such tribes would, of necessity, have to seek
wives in other tribes, and hence a practice of marrying outside the
group would naturally develop. This hypothesis is no longer seriously
entertained by anthropologists, since there is no evidence that female
infanticide was ever so prevalent as to have been able to bring about the
practice of exogamy. A far more plausible explanation was offered by the American anthropologist, Lewis Henry Morgan, who held that
groups which resorted to outbreeding would, through the operation of
natural selection, have a great advantage over those subjected to the de
generative effects of inbreeding. If inbreeding were invariably harmful
to a species, Morgan's view would be tenable, and we could believe
that exogamy arose because primitive peoples found by chance that
such a practice was advantageous in the struggle for existence and, as a
result, incorporated it into the tribal mores. "Unfortunately for this
view," an English scholar cogently noted, "there is little or no scientific
evidence that the interbreeding of near relations, provided both are
healthy, has"any biologically injurious consequences." (207, p. 14) (On
the question of the effects of inbreeding on both animals and men, we
recommend to the reader a little book by the late Lord Raglan entitled
Jocasta's Crime [195].) Another famous theory of exogamous origins was advanced by J. J. Atkinson and Andrew Lang, who thought the primitive family was a
patriarchy, consisting of one grown man, his wife or wives, and their
children. The man, being a jealous and greedy brute, invariably drove
away his growing sons and annexed the women to his own harem; and,
of course, the dispossessed sons would, of necessity, have to find them
selves wives in some other group. Exogamy, according to this theory,
was the result of paternal jealousy; and Sigmund Freud later adopted
the jealous brute of Atkinson and Lang to buttress his Oedipus-com
plex idea. Freud expanded the original scheme by speculating that "one



TOTEMISM AND EXOGAMY 63
day the expelled brothers joined forces, slew and are the father, and
thus put an end to the father horde." After this act of cannibalism, in
stead of seizing the women for themselves, the young men, regrerring
whar rhey had done, soughr rheir wives ourside rheir own kinship
group, and thus established exogamy as an accepted practice. We may
dismiss this hypothesis as being a ground for the origin of exogamy,
since there is no evidence that the jealous primitive patriarch and his
harem ever existed. The earliest human group was made up, not of a
father, a mother, and their children, but of a mother and her descend
ants in the female line; since in those early days no other line of descent j
was known. This fact is brilliantly demonstrated by Dr. Robert
BrifTault in his anthropological classic The Mothers. Exogamy, it seems,
was the natural outcome of the matriarchal basis of primitive society.
"The men lived with the clan into which they married," we learn from
Professor Thomson, "and were obliged to surrender their products to
the members of that clan. Thus, the practice of getting husbands from
other clans enables each to extend its diet by obtaining access to foods
which it did not produce itself. The initial function of exogamy was to
circulate the food supply." (248, p. 14)
A most important feature of tribal society is a complex organization
of matrimonial classes which is called by anthropologists the classifica
tory system; and this societal complex is, in fact, a natural outgrowth of
the institution of exogamy. The basic principle of the classificatory sys
tem is that kinship is reckoned between groups rather than between in
dividuals. Under this system, a man calls father, not only his actual fa
ther, but all the men who, according to the tribal mores, might have
been his father; and he names as his mother, not only his real mother, but an entire group of women, any one of whom might have been his
mother, according to the tribal customs. He designates as his wife, not
only his real wife, but also all of the women he might have married, in
agreement with the marriage laws of the tribe. He, likewise, gives the
names of sons and daughters, not only to his own children, but to the children of all the women of the tribe whom he might have married.
This strange system of social kinship must have grown out of a wide
spread primitive practice of group marriage. In the sagacious words of
Sir J. G. Frazer:
Such a system of group marriage would explain simply why every man gives the name of wife to a whole group of women, and every woman gives the name of husband to a whole group of men, . . . why every man and



64 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
every woman apply the names of father and mother to whole groups of men and women of whom it is physically impossible that more than two in
dividuals can be their parents; why every man and every woman apply the name of brother and sister to whole groups of men and women with whom they need not have a drop of blood in common; and why, finally, every man and every woman claim as their sons and daughters whole groups of men and women whom they neither begat nor bare. In short, group mar
riage explains group relationship, and it is hard to see what else can do so.
(88, pp. 156-57)
The classificatory system is based on the division of the tribe into
two intermarrying groups; and, besides the two-class system, some
tribes have a four-class or an eight-class system. The two-class system
is fundamental, whereas the four- and eight-class systems are derived from it. It was the opinion of Frazer that "the two-class system of ex
ogamy, or dual organization, as it is often called, suffices of itself to
create the classificatory system of relationship, which appears not to
have been materially affected by the subsequent adoption of the four
class and eight-class systems in certain tribes. This observation is im
portant because, while the classificatory system of relationship is found
to be diffused over a great part of the world, the four-class and eight
class systems have hitherto been detected in Australia alone." (88, p.
1 59) This system of matrimonial classes is of a most ingenious character; the purpose of such a scheme seems to have been to restrict mar
riage between certain groups. The two-class system prevents the mar
riage of brothers with sisters; the four-class system bars the marriage of
parents with children; and the eight-class system prohibits the marriage
of certain first cousins, the children of a brother and of a sister, re
spectively. The marriage of all other first cousins, the offspring of two
brothers, or of two sisters had already been forbidden by the two-class
system.
(The two kinds of first cousins listed above are known as cross-cous
ins and ortho-cousins. The children of a brother and of a sister, respec
tively, are called cross-cousins. The other types of cousins, the children
of two brothers, or of two sisters, had no special name in English until
Frazer called them ortho-cousins.)
The working of the classificatory system in practice is best illus
trated by studying the social organization of a tribe in which the sys
tem is operative; and we select for illustrative purposes the Kamilaroi
tribe of southeast Australia. The tribe is divided into six clans, and the
clans are evenly distributed into two divisions known as phratries or
moieties, as follows:



TOTKMISM AND KXOCAMY 65
Moiery A: Clan I, Dinoun (Emu); Clan 2, Bilba (Bandicoot); Clan 3, Nurai (Blacksnake)
Moiery B: Clan 4, Duli (Iguana); Clan 5, Murriira (Kangaroo); Clan 6, Mure (Opossum) The marrimonial groups are eighr in number, four of which are
composed exclusively of males and four of which are composed exclu
sively of females. Since each group of males is linked with a corresponding group of females, and vice versa, for mating purposes, the
eight groups are actually reduced to four marriage classes, and this type
of exogamy is known as the four-class system. The Kamilaroi classes
are grouped according to sex as follows:
MALE
Ippai
Kumbo
Murri Kubbi
FEMALE
1. Ippata 2. Buta 3. Mata 4. Kapota
All Ippais, of whatever totem, are brothers to each other, since they
are theorerically descended from a common female ancestor. The same
thing is true of all the Kumbos, Murris, and Kubbis respectively. Simi
larly, all Ippatas of whatever clan are sisters to each other; and the same
is true of all the Butas, Matas, and Kapotas, respectively. Furthermore,
the Ippais and Ippatas are brothers and sisters to each other; and the same thing goes for Kumbos and Butas, Murris and Matas, and Kubbis
and Kapotas.
The upshot of all this was clearly explained by Morgan, to wit: 'if
an Ippai and Ippata meet, who have never seen each other before, they
address each other as brother and sister. The Kamilaroi, therefore, are
organized into four great primary groups of brothers and sisters, each group being composed of a male and a female branch; but intermingled
over their area of occupation." (177, p. 51) The rules or laws regulating marriages among the Kamilaroi, as re
corded by Mr. Morgan, are as follows:
"Ippai can marry Kapota and no other. Kumbo can marry Mata and no other. Murri can marry Buta and no other. Kubbi can marry Ippata and no other." (177, p. 51)
In an instructive anthropological srudy, The Science of Social Development, F. A. Brooke, Fellow of the Royal Anthropological Society,



66 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
has adopted the happy expedient of using familiar English names in
place of the native Australian names in order to simplify the explana
tion of the classificatory system; and this practice is illustrated in the
following table:
MOIETY A MOIETY B

From the above table we see that each moiety is made up of two
divisions as listed; and that a man in Moiety A, Division I, picks his
wife from Division I of Moiety B. In other words, a Paul marries an
Alfreda. A woman in Division 1 of Moiety A will marry a man from
Division I of Moiety B. That is to say, Pauline marries Alfred and so
on, as shown by the table. Since descent is traced through the female
line, the children are always members of the same clans as their moth
ers, but the children become members of a class different from that of
either parent, which fact is shown in the following tabulation:
PARENTS CHILDREN
