TOTEMISM AND EXOGAMY 67
It will be noticed that one set of classes possesses totems in common
with Moiety A and the other set of classes has totems in common with
Moiety B, and that the two divisions of the moieties are equally distrib
uted under the totems. Since the totems are symbolic crests of the
clans, we see that the three clans of each moiety are nothing more than
subdivisions of an original clan; and this circumstance would seem to
confirm Sir J. G. Frazer's opinion that the classiflcatory system is
merely the logical outgrowth of the dual organization.
The Kamilaroi tribe exemplifies the four-class system of exogamy in
its simplest form. However, there is another tribe, the Urubunna,
which enjoins the marriage of second cousins, and this necessitates an
arrangement of the moieties into near and distant sections of the two
divisions of each moiety. Under such a scheme, a man is permitted to
marry a maternal cousin and a woman is allowed to marry a paternal
cousin. The actual working of the system is somewhat complicated, but
the English anthropologist, F. A. Brooke, has a neat explanation of it, which we cite:
A man marries his father's distant sister's daughter, or what is the same
thing, his mother's near brother's daughter, but not his father's near sister's
daughter. A woman marries her father's near sister's son, or what is the
same thing, her mother's distant brother's son, but not her mother's near
brother's son. Distant in this connection is used to denote all those members
of the division who are neither natural brother nor sister nor cousin to a
given individual. For this relationship anthropologists have used elder, which
is incorrect and confusing. 1 have substituted distant as expressing best ex
actly what this relationship is, as they really are distant cousins, or second
cousins. The effect of this was to divide each division into two sections, as it
were, one consisting of those who were regarded as distant in the sense just
defined, or second cousins, and the other of those regarded as near, being
natural brothers and sisters or just cousins, and thus the kin (clan) fell into
four sections. This form of marriage brought about marriage between the
distant and near sections of the two intermarrying divisions of two kins. A
man, then, always marries into what is to him the distant section of the in
termarrying division of his father's kin, i.e., among his maternal cousins,
and the women into the near section, among the paternal cousins. (24, pp. 41-43)
The kinship relations of the Urubunna tribe, which Brooke dis
cussed above, are arranged in a diagram, shown on page 68.
In discussing the marriage systems of Australian tribes, Frazer calls
our attention to certain facts of great importance, for instance: "The

marriage of orrho-cousins (the children of two brothers or of two sis
ters) is prevented by both the two-class system and the four-class sys
tem: the marriage of cross-cousins is prevented by the eight-class sys
tem alone. Hence, Australian tribes which have adopted the eight-class
system ban the marriage of all first cousins whatsoever." (88, p. 1 72n)
The eight-class system of exogamy is operative in the Arunta tribe
of Central Australia; and this tribe practices patrilineal descent. An
other tribe, the Dieri, also possesses the eight-class system, but with
matrilineal descent. In either case, the system operates in the same
way; for, regardless as to whether descent is through mothers or fa
thers, the children always belong to the same moiety as their parent,
but to a different section of it. If the parent is a member of the near sec
tion of Division I, the children will be in the near section of Division II.
This has been neatly tabulated by F. A. Brooke, who appends the fol
lowing remark: "I have used ordinary common names to indicate the
men and women belonging to each section, for the sake of simplicity,
instead of native names, which are apt to make the scheme appear very
complicated, though in reality, it is not." (24, p. 44)

in the Bibliography, so that the reader will know where to look for fur

CHAPTER FIVE
The Origin of Magic and Religion
RELIGION has been defined by that master hierologist, Sir James
George Frazer, as "a propitiation or conciliation of powers superior to
man which are believed to direct and control the course of nature and
of human life." (86, Part I, "The Magic Art and the Evolution of
Kings," Vol. I, p. 222) If this definition be accepted, and we know not
of a better or more accurate one, we are compelled to conclude that re
ligion is a late development in the realm of human thought. "When
theologians contend that primitive man is everywhere possessed of a
belief in religion," declared the American sociologist, Calverton, "what
they really mean is that he everywhere possessed a belief in magic."
(40, pp. 31-32) Frazer has brilliantly demonstrated in his classic thirteen-volume i
work, The Golden Bough: A Study in Magic and Religion, that human
thought in its evolution has passed through three stages, or, as he has
called them, three ages: the Age of Magic, the Age of Religion, and the
Age of Science. For countless thousands of years before religion was
known, man believed in the principles of magic and strove to put them
into practice in his daily life. One feature of magic is universality.
"Magic is world-wide," Professor William Howells truly stated, "by
which I mean that it is the property not only of all primitive people,
but also of ourselves and all our ancestors, and it is the same thing pre
cisely wherever it is found." (121, p. 48) The statement above from
the pen of Dr. Howells is a vindication of the theoretical position taken by Sir J. G. Frazer in Volume I, Part I, of The Golden Bough.
The ancient doctrine of sympathetic magic is based on two princi
ples of thought; the first principle being that like produces like, or that
an effect resembles its cause; and this tenet is known as the Law of
Similarity; and the second principle of thought, basic to magic, is that
71



72 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
things which have at one time been in contact with each other continue
to act upon each other at a distance, after the physical contact has
ceased to exist; and this principle is known as the Law of Contact or
Contagion. The system of sympathetic magic comprises both positive
and negative precepts: The positive precepts are charms; the negative
ones are tabus. Charms founded on the Law of Similarity are known as
Homeopathic Magic; while charms based on the Law of Contact are
designated as Contagious Magic. The two branches of sympathetic
magic are related as shown in the following diagram:
SYMPATHETIC MAGIC
(Based on the Law of Sympathy)
Homeopathic Magic Contagious Magic
(Based on the Law of Similarity) (Based on the Law of Contact)
Magic, in theory, may properly be considered as a pseudo-science,
and in practice as a pseudo-art. Practical magic is also of two types:
positive magic or sorcery, and negative magic or tabu.
This is best shown by illustration—as follows:
MAGIC
Theoretical Practical
(Magic as a pseudo-science) (Magic as a pseudo-art)
Positive Magic Negative Magic
(Sorcery) (Tabu)
There is an ancient magic practice known as envoutement.
This word, which is often used in French books on Magic [we learn from Sir Wallis Budge] means the act of making a figure of wax of a certain
person as exact as possible as regards the face (the Latin in + voltus) and
form and special characteristics, with the intention, after having performed
upon it certain baleful acts and ceremonies, of making the person repre



THE ORIGIN OK MAGIC AND RKLIGION 73
sented by the wax figure to suffer all the pains and indignities which the
magician inflicts on the wax figure. Whilst the magician is attacking the
wax figure, he repeats spells and incantations which effect the transmission
of aches and pains and sufferings to the human being wheresoever he may
be. Thus, if a needle was thrust through the knee joints of the wax figure,
the human being which it represented would suffer agonies in his knees; if a
needle was thrust through the heart of the wax figure, the human being
would die. But evoutement could be practiced with a good as well as a bad
object. For there is on record the case of a seaman who, during a fight at
sea, was shot in the eye with an arrow and as a result he suffered indescrib
able agony. But his friends made a figure of him with the arrow in his eye
and took it to a famous shrine of the Virgin Mary in Syria. The figure of the Virgin drew the arrow out of the eye of the figure, and at the same moment, the arrow which was in the eye of the seaman who was still on the
sea, fell out from it, and the pain ceased from that moment. (29, p. 481)
Contagious magic is based on the belief that things which once had
been in contact with each other would retain a sympathetic connection
even after such contact had been severed. This type of magical attrac
tion is particularly supposed to exist between an individual and his bod
ily relics, such as hair, nails, and navel-string; and clothing and bodily
impressions are also often utilized in the practice of contagious magic.
"This belief explains why a magician," in the words of A. C. Haddon,
"wishing to influence or act upon some particular individual, desires to
obtain some portion of his body or something actually connected with
him. A few hairs from the beard, -a lock of hair, some nail-parings, a
drop of blood from the nose which has fallen to the ground, and which
has not been rendered impalpable by effacing it with the foot, are used by Basuto sorcerers, and indeed by workers of magic everywhere."
(104, p. 3) (In England, even in our own time, a girl deserted by her lover has
been advised to resort to the practice of magic. The girl is supposed to
get a lock of the hair of the faithless suitor; the hair is then to be thrown
in a pot of boiling water and, while the hair simmers in the pot, the
original owner of the same enjoys no rest.)
The essential difference between magic and religion is that magic is
a form of primitive science, whereas religion is a primitive type of met
aphysics. Although the theoretical foundations of magic are definitely
unscientific, both science and magic have similar objective aims. The
position taken by Frazer that magic was an earlier stage of belief than
religion has been denied by many anthropologists in recent years, but,



74 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
in our opinion, these critics have not proven their case against the eru
dite Scot.
As a case in point, we refer to Jocasta's Crime: An Anthropological
Study (195) by Lord Raglan. Originally published in 1933, this book
appeared in a new edition in 1940. In the preface to the 1940 version,
the noble author makes a confession: "At the time when I wrote this
book, I accepted Sir James Frazer's view of the relation of magic to re
ligion—namely, that magic arises from a simple association of ideas and
is a necessary stage on the upward path to religion. For reasons which I
have given elsewhere, I have since' come to the conclusion that this
view is untenable." A perusal of Lord Raglan's arguments against
Frazer has convinced us that his Lordship failed to prove his case. In
fact, he seems to have had some doubt as to the soundness of his own
position, for he concludes the preface of Jocasta's Crime with the fol
lowing admission: "Finally, the solution of the problem which I have
put forward seems to me even less satisfactory than it did seven years
ago, but I cannot now, any more than I could then, suggest a better."
Among the aboriginal inhabitants of Australia, religion is conspicu
ous by its absence, but magic abounds on all sides. Our most reliable
guides to the indigenous culture of the Australians are Sir Baldwin
Spencer and F. J. Gillen, and these anthropologists have given the fol
lowing description of the role of magic among these primitive peoples
of the Antipodes:
Almost every action in the life of a Central Australian savage from the
day of his birth to the day of his death is associated in some way or another
with magic. No sooner is the Arunta child born than a black line is painted
over his eyebrow in order to ward off sickness. How or why it should do so
does not in the least trouble the parents. They have been told by the old
men that it will and that is enough for them. The idea of putting any of
their beliefs to the test of experiment never enters their heads. If the young
child avoids sickness, then, of course, it is a result of the black line—if the
desired result does not follow, then it means that some other person has
worked evil by counter magic, and the savage goes on his way perfectly
content with this simple explanation." (238, p. 342)
The central Australians are not the only peoples devoid of religion.
An Englishman traveling through Africa asked certain individuals whether or not they offered up prayers to God, and was answered
thusly: "We never pray to God because we do not know him, but we
pray to leaves, fetishes, and to the dead." (49, p. 151) The same out



THE ORIGIN OF MAGIC AND RELIGION 75
look prevails in Zululand. A Zulu was asked if he knew of a god who
controlled the universe, and caused the crops to grow and the sun to
shine. "No, we know of none," he replied. "We know that we cannot
do these things, and suppose that they come of themselves." In the
shadow of Cape Horn, on the wild shores of Tierra Fuego, there is a
group of people known as the Yahgans, and they were studied for two
years (1882-83) by Hyades and Deniker, who reported that they
"never detected the least allusion to any kind of cult or religious idea."
T. Bridges, a missionary, spent twenty years among the Yahgans and,
concerning their religious beliefs, he recorded that: "They have neither
hope nor fear beyond the grave. For them, there is neither God, nor
good, nor evil. . . . Death is the end of existence, and they have no
idea of a spiritual life, or of the composition of man from a body and a
soul." (Cited by Joseph McCabe in 1 58, p. 3 1) Before man reached the
hunting stage, he showed no trace of religion, since the struggle for ex
istence took up all of his time, and hence he could not afford to sit
around and speculate on the whys and wherefores of existence.
The earliest element of religion seems to have been the idea of the
soul; and this conception was based on certain speculations concerning
the meaning of shadows and dreams. "Anthropologists are agreed," de
clared Mr. Clodd, "that the impulse to man's conception of his person
ality, and to that of a general doctrine of spirits comes from dreams, the
inference from these having support in the phenomenon of shadows,
reflections, and echoes, and in the abnormal mental states of hysteria,
swooning, epilepsy and allied disorders." (50, p. 27) In languages rang
ing from Ainu to English, the words for breath and spirit are the same,
and among western Australians the word wang means both breath and
spirit. A similar view prevails among the Melanesians, who hold that,
"the soul goes out of the body in some dreams, and if for some reason it
does not come back, the man is found dead in the morning."
This soul or spirit is imagined to be a sort of vapor, composed of
breath and shadow and reflection. It is a well-known fact that in the
Latin language the word animus (the mind) is cognate with anima (air
or breath), and in the Irish language (a member of the same lingual
family as Latin), anal (breath) is closely related to anam (life or soul).
To primitive man the shadow was a mysterious double of the body; he
could see his shadow on the ground, and in the quiet lake or stream it
was an exact duplicate of the living man. The way the shadow followed
the body about appearing and vanishing silently, and increasing and



76 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
decreasing in size was most puzzling so that our savage forebear con
cluded that he was a dual being; a combination of body and soul. He
explained death by assuming that it occurred when the shadow or soul
permanently left the body; and dream phenomena reinforced this be
lief. This idea was vividly expressed in u I)e Perum Nature" [Concern
ing the Nature of Things] by the Roman poet, Lucretius:
"When sleep has chained down our limbs in sweet slumber and the
whole body is sunk in profound repose, yet then we seem to ourselves
to be awake and to be moving our limbs, and amid the thick darkness
of night, we think we see the sun and the daylight; and though in a
confined room, we seem to be passing to new climates, seas, rivers,
mountains, and to be crossing plains on foot, and to hear voices, though
the austere silence of night prevails all round, and to be uttering speech,
though quite silent."
The primitive belief that all forms of force and motion are products
of spiritual agency is known as the doctrine of Animism. Among the
legions of spirits, some in time were glorified and magnified and turned
into gods; and this brings us to a consideration of how spirits evolved
into gods. To the ancient animist, there was a soul or spirit in all things.
Every tree, for instance, had a spirit in it. Finally primitive thinkers
began to doubt that each tree had an individual spirit of its own and, in
stead, they conceived of a great spirit of the forests in general. The
multitude of individual souls of trees were spirits in the proper meaning
of the word. The numerous souls, combined into a generalized spirit of
trees as a class, became a god. The gods have proper names, as a rule,
whereas spirits are usually nameless. In ancient Rome, Sylvanus was
the god of forests, but ordinary tree spirits had no names. Originally
each wind had a spirit of its own. Then the various wind spirits were
consolidated into one, and personified as Aeolus, god of the wind. So,
we see that gods are the natural result of spiritual evolution. Animism
(the belief in spirits) gives birth in time to polytheism (the belief in
gods). The earliest gods seem to have been deified chiefs or ancestors.
On the evolution of religious ideas, there has been much speculation,
but the main stages of the process have been exhumed by the experts.
Slowly nature-gods rose above the deified ancestors in importance.
The ancient magicians were the first men to form a leisure class in
primitive society and, when human society reached the agricultural
stage, some of the magicians had evolved into priests and kings. In fact,
the priestly and kingly offices were frequently combined in the person
of one man. In the pyramid texts of ancient Egypt, it was recorded that



THE ORIGIN OF MAGIC AM) RELIGION 77
kings were more ancient than the gods, and the same belief was held by
the Brahmans of India. The doctrine of the divine right of kings stems
from the belief that kings in olden times were actually gods. We can
easily imagine how, in the process of social evolution, priests in certain communities might rise to positions of dominance and, hence, become
kings. A good way for such a king to make his position secure would be
by declaring himself a god. We may reasonably believe that this is just
what did happen. "It does not need much research to show," in the
words of Professor W. J. Perry, "that the claim to divine descent is
based on the fact that certain men actually called themselves gods."
How the early priest-kings formed a ruling class and reduced the
masses to slavery is not known in detail, but we have a good general
idea of how it came about. The subject is discussed at length with both
skill and insight by V. F. Calverton in The Passing of the Gods (40),
and by Robert BrifTault in Rational Evolution (20).
This priestly rule, powerful as it was, did not go unchallenged; for
even in the ancient empires the voice of the skeptic was heard. Around
4,000 years ago, there was a period of chaos in ancient Egypt which
lasted about 200 years. The country was invaded by Asiatic nomads,
and plague and famine swept the land. The rulers and priests could do
nothing for the people, and the disillusioned masses flared up into open
rebellion. This uprising has been called by Hector Hawton, "the first
revolution in history."
"Confronted by the spectacle of tottering thrones, the hungry
plague-smitten masses, whose miseries no words of power could re
lieve, lost faith in kings, priests and gods. They ceased even to search
for the empty boon of a paradise after death." (115, p. 74) An un
known Egyptian skeptic of those far-off days wrote: "Ah! If I knew
where God was I should certainly make an offering to him!" About 2000 B.C., a poem, "The Song of the Harper," was very popular
among the Egyptian people. Professor James Henry Breasted has given
us an English translation of the same, from which the following extract
is quoted:
Let not thy heart be weary,
Follow thy desire and thy pleasure,
And mould thine affairs on earth
After the mandates of the heart Till that day of lamentation cometh to thee,
When the stilled heart hears not the mourning.
For lamentation recalls no man from the dead.



78 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
We have been informed by Professor W. Max Muller that the
priests repeatedly attempted to repress "The Song of the Harper" be
cause of its "atheistic joy in life," but they had no success, for the song
was popular for centuries. Even the best-organized system of tyranny
must eventually collapse; and, as civilization spread around the world, a
middle class of merchants and traders developed. As these men traveled
about and made contact with new and strange ideas, they began to
doubt the old myths and legends of the gods. Some became mono
theists and agnostics, and others even became atheists, denying the
very existence of the gods. The course of the evolution of religion in
ancient Egypt was accurately charted by Joseph McCabe in 158, p.
1 59, from which we transcribe the following scheme:
(1) Belief in Shadow or Soul
(2a) Animism and Fetishism (2b) Ancestor worship
(3a) Deification of Sun, Moon, etc. (3b) Deified ancestors
(4) Polytheism (5) Monotheism (6) Atheism
This process of religious evolution was not confined to Egypt, for
we find that it eventually reached the New World. An Aztec poet and
philosopher, who died twenty years before Columbus first visited
America, expressed his doubts about the current religion, as follows:
The caverns of earth are filled with pestilential dust which once was the
bones, the flesh, the bodies of great ones who sat upon thrones, deciding
causes, ruling assemblies, governing armies, conquering provinces, possess
ing treasures, tearing down temples, flattering themselves with the pride,
majesty, fortune, praise and dominion. The glories have passed like the dark
smoke thrown by the fires of Popocatepetl, leaving no monument but the
rude skins on which they are written. (240, p. 240)
Sir J. G. Frazer's theory of the evolution of human thought through
successive ages of magic, religion, and science is not taken seriously by
most contemporary anthropologists. But we think it only fair that his
case should be heard. The reader is referred to the first volume of the
complete The Golden Bough (86, pp. 220-22). If human thought in its growth and development has successively
passed through magical, religious, and scientific phases, what is the
meaning of it all? Is science the final stage of human thought, or will
the human mind discover a more advanced stage of thought? Frazer's



THE ORIGIN OF MAGIC AND RELIC; ION 79
answer to the foregoing question was, as usual, comprehensive and is
properly transmitted to the reader in the flawless prose of the author:
In magic, man depends on his own strength to meet the difficulties and
dangers that beset him on every side. He believes in a certain established order of nature on which he can surely count, and which he can manipulate
for his own ends. When he discovers his mistake, when he recognizes sadly
that both the order of nature which he had assumed and the control which
he had believed himself to exercise over it were purely imaginary, he ceases
to rely on his own intelligence and his own unaided efforts, and throws
himself humbly on the mercy of certain great invisible beings behind the
veil of nature, to whom he now ascribes all those far-reaching powers
which he once arrogated to himself.
But, as time goes on, this explanation in its turn proves to be unsatisfac
tory. For it assumes that the succession of natural events is not determined
by immutable laws, but is to some extent variable and irregular, and this as
sumption is not borne out by closer observation. . . . Thus the keener
minds, still pressing forward to a deeper solution of the mysteries of the
universe, come to reject the religious theory of nature as inadequate, and to
revert in a measure to the older standpoint of magic by postulating explic
itly, what in magic had only been implicitly assumed, to wit, an inflexible
regularity in the order of natural events, which, if carefully observed, en
ables us to foresee their course with certainty and to act accordingly. In
short, religion, regarded as an explanation of nature, is displaced by science.
. . . Here at last, after groping about in the dark for countless ages, man
has hit upon a clue to the labyrinth, a golden key that opens many locks in
the treasury of nature.
Yet the history of thought should warn us against concluding that be
cause the scientific theory of the world is the best that has yet been formu
lated, it is necessarily complete and final. We must remember that at bot
tom the generalizations of science or, in common parlance, the laws of
nature are merely hypotheses devised to explain that ever-shifting phantas
magoria of thought which we dignify with the high-sounding names of the
world and the universe. In the last analysis, magic, religion and science are
nothing but theories of thought; and as science has supplanted its predeces
sors, so it may hereafter be itself superseded by some more perfect hypothe
sis, perhaps by some totally different way of looking at the phenomena—of
registering the shadows on the screen—of which we in this generation can form no idea. The advance of knowledge is an infinite progression towards
a goal that forever recedes. (88, pp. 3 39^42)
The student, or the general reader, who has a special interest in the
subject matter of this chapter should become acquainted with the writ



80 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
ings of Sir James George Frazer, especially The Golden Bough. This
unusual book was first published in two volumes in 1 890; a second edi
tion, in three volumes, was issued in 1900; then from 1911 to 1915 a
third edition in twelve volumes made its appearance; and finally, in
1937, a thirteenth volume was added to the series, with the title After
math: A Supplement to the Golden Bough. This scholarly work is now
out of print, but this need not cause concern, for abridged editions of
the book have been published.
The Golden Bough may be conveniently subdivided into eight parts, which we briefly summarize:
Part 1: In ancient times, in the Alban Hills of Italy, near the town
of Aricia, on the shores of Lake Nemi, there was a sacred grove in
which the goddess Diana was worshiped. The priest who officiated at
this shrine bore the title of King of the Wood, and was originally an in
carnate spirit of forests. This priest-king was charged with the regula
tion of vegetation and fertility by means of the practice of the magical
art. As King of the Wood, he was consort of the nymph Egeria, with
whom he annually contracted a sacred marriage. This custodian of the
sacred grove, with drawn sword, perambulated about a certain oak,
which among its branches harbored boughs of mistletoe. The King of
the Wood had obtained his post by slaying his predecessor and pluck
ing a sprig of the golden bough (mistletoe) from the sacred oak. His
tenure of office was only safe when he remained alert and in good
health. Finally, when too feeble to protect his perilous post any longer,
he fell by the sword of a younger and more vigorous contender who
severed the golden bough from the sacred tree and became the new King of the Wood.
Part 2: The ancient priest-kings were believed to be gods in human
form; and, to protect their souls from injury, their persons were sub
jected to numerous restrictions, known as tabus. A tabu was a thing
forbidden, and all sorts of things were tabu, such as certain foods, the
uttering of certain words, sharp weapons, association with strangers, or
sometimes even with near kin, etc.
Part 3: A sacred kingship was no sinecure, for the occupant of that
high office, on displaying loss of health or virility, was slain and re
placed by a healthy and vigorous successor. Among some primitive
peoples, the kings were killed at the end of fixed periods so as not to
risk peril to the people from any infirmity that might afflict the mon
arch.
Part 4: Some of the ancient gods, such as Adonis, Attis, and Osiris



THE ORIGIN OF MAGIC AND RELIGION 81
were regarded as personifications of various aspects of nature, espe
cially vegetation. These dying and resurrecting gods were connected
with the changing seasons and with the corresponding growth and decay of vegetable life.
Part 5: Among the ancients, the spirit of fertility was sometimes
represented as a genius of the corn crop. The figures of the corn
mother and the corn-maiden in ancient folklore were symbolic respec
tively of the harvested corn and the new crop; and their mythological
duplicates may be found in the Demeter and Persephone of the classi
cal Greeks. Sometimes the corn spirit was personified in animal form,
such as a pig, goat, bull, cat, dog, wolf, etc. This brings to mind Dion
ysus, a genius of fertility, pictured in the form of bull or goat. Such sa
cred animals, representing certain gods, were often slain in the rituals
of ancient cults.
Part 6: Primitive peoples believed that evil spirits could be trans
ferred from afflicted bodies to other objects, both animate and inani
mate. The evil influences were transferred to a scapegoat (human or an
imal), which was then expelled from the community. Such scapegoats
were driven out periodically and also on special occasions in times of
crises.
Part 7: In the period intervening between the deposing of an old
king and the enthronement of a successor, normal life was in a state of
chaos in ancient societies. Certain gala periods of license were permit
ted to commoners and even slaves. Relics of this custom were con
tinued in the Babylonian Sacaea, the Roman Saturnalia and the medie
val European Feast of Fools.
Part 8: Each candidate for the office of King of the Wood at Lake
Nemi had to pluck a sprig of the golden bough from the higher
branches of the sacred oak and then engage the incumbent monarch in
mortal combat. The King of the Wood was a human incarnation of the
sky-god Jupiter, who came down from the sky to dwell in the mistletoe
on the sacred oak in the forest of the sky-queen, Diana, the wife of Ju
piter. (The curious reader may by now wonder why the mistletoe was
called the golden bough. That is a fair question, and something will be
said about it in the next chapter.)



CHAPTER SIX
The Worship of Nature
THE worship of nature is based on the personification of natural phe
nomena; for the men and women of olden times personified the various
objects and forces of nature and worshiped them as gods, 'in these na
ture-worships," that great humanist, the late Edward Carpenter, ob
served:
There may be discerned three fairly independent streams of religious or
quasi-religious enthusiasms: (1) that connected with the phenomena of the heavens; the movements of the sun, planets, and stars, and the awe and wonderment they excited; (2) that connected with the seasons and the very
important matter of the growth of vegetation and food on the earth; and (3)
that connected with the mysteries of sex and reproduction. It is obvious that these three streams would mingle and interfuse with each other a good deal;
but as far as they were separable, the first would tend to create solar heroes
and sun-myths; the second, vegetation- gods and personifications of nature
and the earth-life; while the third would throw its glamour over the other
two, and contribute to the projection of deities or demons worshipped with
all sorts of sexual and phallic rites. (41, pp. 19-20)
The terrestrial gods seem to be older than the celestial deities, so the
earthly divinities will be our first objects of study. The earth itself,
worshiped under the name of the Great Mother, was the oldest of the
gods of nature. This earth-worship was basic in the religions of the
early Amerindians. "The earth was universally believed," wrote E. J.
Payne, "to be the most ancient object in nature; the sun, moon and
stars were considered to be comparatively recent. All living creatures
proceed from the earth; all are sustained by the earth; all are finally re
duced to dust, and return to the earth. Her motherhood was in aborigi
nal America no mere figure of speech, but an article of positive belief."
(183, Vol. I, p. 464)
82



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 83
The Winnebago Indians worshiped the earth as a goddess under the name of Mama (Earth), and Kunika (Grandmother). The Lenni Lenape of Pennsylvania regarded the earth as the universal mother, and
claimed that their ancestors were created in the interior of the earth, and dwelt there before coming out upon the surface. According to
some Amerindian myth makers, their early forebears lived in the bow
els of the earth in human form, while others held that their progenitors
existed underground in the shape of tortoises, rabbits, and ground hogs.
The Caribs of the Antilles considered the earth to be a benevolent
mother who gave forth all the necessities of life. Among these people,
an earthquake was interpreted as a sign given by Mother Earth for all
and sundry to dance for the health and welfare of the tribe; so fol
lowing an earthquake, they danced for four days and four nights by the light of the moon. The dancers were bedecked in gaudy costumes; they wore multicolored marks and loaded themselves down with belts, gar
ters, bracelets, and necklaces, all covered with little shells which
clashed and banged together as they jumped around and about, while a group of old women shook rattles and crooned a doleful song as an accompaniment. The Indians of Peru worshiped the earth as a divinity under the name of Mama Pacha (Mother Earth); and the priests, after making sacrifices to the sun, the thunder, and the moon, and after praying for
the health, prosperity, and victory of the reigning monarch, also sac
rificed to Mother Earth and prayed to her as follows: "Oh, Mother
Earth! Preserve the Lord Inca, thy son, who stands upon thee in peace and safety." The village of Mama (Mother), located on the banks of a tributary of the Rimac River, was the seat of a famous sanctuary of the Earth Mother, who was there worshiped as the consort of Pachacamac (the Creator). Near the village, two streams met below the site of the
sanctuary, and they were called the breasts of Mother Earth. The an
cient Mexicans also worshiped the earth with elaborate rites and cere
monies, but the story is too involved to be discussed in these pages. A
detailed account of these exotic practices may be found in "The Scape
goat," which comprises Part VI, Vol. IX, of Frazer's The Golden
Bough (86). Among the ancient Egyptians, the earth was personified as a male deity by the name of Seb, Keb, or Geb, and his consort was the skygoddess Nut. In Egyptian art, Seb is shown in human form and with
his head adorned with either a crown or a goose. A species of geese
called seb geese was sacred to the god, because of the belief that, once
upon a time, Seb had changed himself into a goose and had traveled



84 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
through the air in that shape; and in the hieroglyphic inscriptions, he is
symbolized as both a goose or an egg. Seb was a personification of both
the earth as a whole and of the surface of the earth and its green mantle
of vegetation. The earth was said to be the body of Seb as well as his
house; for the earth was called the House of Seb, just as the underworld
was called the House of Osiris, and the air, the House of Shu. Strange
to say, on one occasion, Seb laid an egg; and this egg, in due time, burst
asunder and gave birth to the sun-god, in the form of a mysterious bird
known as the Phoenix; and on account of this notorious egg-laying
feat, the earth god earned the title of the Great Cackler. Seb was quite
properly called the Father of the Gods, for the following divinities
were his children: Osiris, Set, Horus the Elder, Isis, and Nephthys.
The Greek mythologists identified Seb with their own Cronus; for,
as Osiris was said to have mutilated his father Seb, likewise Cronus is
said to have mutilated his father Uranus. The analogy does not stop
here, for Osiris, in turn, was mutilated by his brother Set and Cronus
was similarly treated by his son Zeus. This close similarity of Egyptian
and Greek mythic beliefs should not surprise us, for, as Gerald Massey
observed: "When Herodotus visited Egypt and recognized the African
originals of the Greek deities, he makes the sage remark that his coun
trymen, in their ignorance of the beginnings, counted the birth of their
gods from the time when they first acquired a knowledge of them."
(167, p. 2) In ancient Sumer, the earth god was named Enlil, and was a member
of a trinity; his partners were the sky god Anu and the water god Ea. The name Enlil in the Sumerian language meant Lord of the Wind, or
Lord of the Storm; so we see that he was originally a god of the air
rather than of the earth; and we may note that he was the local god of
Nippur, the holy city of the Sumerians. The later Semitic Babylonians
adopted Enlil and changed his name to Bel, or Baal, which title meant
Lord or Master. In the center of the ancient city of Nippur, the sanctu
ary of Enlil was built on an artificial mound; and the outstanding fea
ture of the sacred precincts was the great ziggurat (temple tower). The
structure was pyramidal in shape, and built of brick, with a winding
ramp or stairway encircling it from bottom to top. Such temple towers
were quite common in Babylonia and they, no doubt, gave rise to the
Biblical legend of the Tower of Babel. The great ziggurat of Nippur
was built by Ur-Engar, first king of Ur of the Chaldees, who reigned
about 2,400 B.C. Clay figures of Enlil, unearthed by the Assyriologists,



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 85
show him as a man with long hair and a beard. As a symbol of divinity
he was fitted out with a horned headdress. Enlil was called the Great Mountain and his temple at Nippur was named E-Kur (House of the Mountain); and he was also titled Lord of the Lands, King of Heaven
and Earth, and Father of the Gods. It was Enlil who, at the very begin
ning of things, fought and subdued the powerful dragon, Tiamat, sepa
rated the earth and sky and created the world. Though properly the
God of Earth, Enlil held that title as ruler of the surface of the earth,
for he was not a personification of the material earth as a whole.
Closely related to earth worship is the worship of vegetation. Tree
worship, especially, is of great antiquity and importance in the religious
history of mankind; for from trees ancient peoples derived food, cloth
ing, and shelter; and the primitive animist regarded trees as the abode
of spirits or gods. To appreciate this viewpoint we must attempt to look
at a tree through the eyes of our untutored savage ancestors of the far
distant past. This was done by Edward Clodd, as follows:
Although the tree is rooted to one spot, it responds to every influence
without. Swayed by the breeze, or smitten by the storm, it is never at rest. Murmurs are heard in its leaves, or its branches creak or writhe as in agony; sounds are emitted from the gaunt stem or hollow trunk; voices, the savage doubts not, of the indwelling spirit whose life seems permanently associated
with the fixed tree. While whatever was mysterious about it added to his
fears, he would honor as the gift of the good power the fruit-yielding tree. Where the fermented juices, as of the same plant, brought on frenzy; or
where the fragrant tobacco was sacrificed to the well-pleased spirits; there
and by other means, close relations between god and man and plant were
set up. (50, pp. 72-73)
The Dravidians of India have for ages paid homage to trees, and from them the tree cults of Buddhism and modern Hinduism have un
doubtedly been derived. The sacred Bo tree has been adored by the
Buddhists of Ceylon for over 2,000 years; and it is said that this sacred
plant was propagated from a slip of the famous tree under which Prince
Gautama reclined after his temptation in the wilderness; and it is fur
ther alleged that this slip was sent to Ceylon by the great Indian ruler,
King Asoka, 250 years B.C. The Yakuts of the Siberian Steppes hung
presents on the branches of larches and other trees to propitiate the
spirits thought to reside therein. Even today, the Irish hate to cut down
the white thorn tree, for it is held by them that the Biblical Crown of Thorns, placed on the brow of Christ, was made from the wood of that



86 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
species of tree. The Dyaks of Borneo have a dread of felling certain trees which are supposedly haunted by the souls of the dead; and cer
tain natives of the West Indies will on no account cut down a silk-cot
ton tree; fearing the revenge of the indwelling duppy, or ghost. A trav
eler in the south of Nigeria was shown a sacred tree by a citizen of the
district. "Yes, we say this is our life—the big tree," the visitor was told.
"When any of us dies," his informant added, "his spirit does not go to
another country,' but into the big tree; and this is why we will not have
it cut." Closely connected with tree worship is the reverence paid to the
mistletoe—the parasitic plant so popular as a decoration during the
Christmas holidays. Known among certain ancient peoples as The Golden Bough, this humble plant has been immortalized by Sir James George Frazer in his celebrated and voluminous study of magic and re
ligion. In Book VI of Virgil's epic poem The Aeneid, Aeneas is informed that, before he can visit the underworld, he must take with him an offering to the Queen of the Underworld. This offering was a golden bough, which had to be plucked from a certain tree in a shady
grove sacred to Diana, and located on the shore of Lake Nemi, in the
Alban hills near Rome. The priest who officiated at the sylvan shrine
reigned as the King of the Wood until a fugitive slave should somehow
snatch a branch of mistletoe from the sacred oak, strike the priest with
the plucked twig, and then slay him in a duel. The fugitive slave then became the new King of the Wood, and his reign would terminate
when another fugitive slave challenged his rule and disposed of him in mortal combat. This woodland shrine was sometimes called the grove of Aricia after a nearby town of that name. The strange ritual which
anciently occurred in the Arician grove was expressed in poetic form
by Macaulay in the following lines:
The still glassy lake that sleeps
Beneath Aricia's trees Those trees in whose dim shadow The ghastly priest doth reign,
The priest who slew the slayer,
And shall himself be slain.
Frazer has pictured the ghastly priest as prowling round the sacred
tree day and night with drawn sword, ever on the alert to the challenge
of a rival claimant to the kingship of the wood:
To gentle and pious pilgrims at the shrine, the sight of him might well seem to darken the fair landscape as when a cloud suddenly blots the sun on



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 87
a bright day. The dreamy blue ofltalian skies, the dappled shade of summer
woods and the sparkle of waves in the sun can have accorded but ill with
that stern and sinister figure. Rather, we picture to ourselves the scene as it
may have been witnessed by a belated wayfarer on one of those wild au
tumn nights when the dead leaves are falling thick, and the winds seem to
be singing the dirge of the dying year. It is a sombre picture set to melan
choly music—the background of forest showing black and jagged against a
lowering and stormy sky, the sighing of the wind in the branches, the rustle
of the withered leaves underfoot, the lapping of the cold water upon the
shore, and in the foreground, pacing to and fro, now in twilight and now in
gloom, a dark figure with a glitter of steel at the shoulder whenever the pale
moon, riding clear of the cloud-rack, peers down at him through the matted
Doughs. (87, pp. 1-2)
But why, should not we inquire, was the mistletoe called the golden
bough? That parasitic plant, with its pale green leaves and whitish-yel
low berries, is possessed of no golden luster; but a plausible explanation
was offered by Frazer, who surmised: "Perhaps the name may be de
rived from the rich golden yellow which a bough of mistletoe assumes
when it has been cut and kept for some months; the bright tint is not
confined to the leaves, but spreads to the stalks as well, so that the
whole branch appears to be indeed a Golden Bough. Breton peasants
hang up great branches of mistletoe in front of their cottages and in the
month of June, these bunches are conspicuous for the bright golden
tinge of their foliage." (87, p. 704)
Critics have pointed out the poet Virgil in the Aeneid does not say
the Golden Bough is the mistletoe, but only makes a comparison. In
the poem, we are told how two doves guided Aeneas through a gloomy forest, in whose depth grew the Golden Bough. The poet tells of how
the doves alighted on the branches of a certain tree, "whence shone a
flickering gleam of gold. As in the woods in winter cold, the mistletoe

a plant not native to its tree—is green with fresh leaves and twines
its yellow berries about the boles; such seemed upon the shady holm
oak the leafy gold, so rustled in the gentle breeze the golden leaf." The Golden Bough is here described as growing on a holm oak, and is com
pared with the mistletoe; but we think it reasonable to conclude with
Frazer that: "The inference is almost inevitable, that the Golden
Bough was nothing but the mistletoe seen through the haze of poetry
or of popular superstition." (87, p. 703) The priest of the Arician grove seems to have been a personification of the sacred tree, on whose limbs grew the Golden Bough: hence, as



88 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the tree was an oak, the King of the Wood was an incarnation of the
oak spirit; and as an oak spirit, his life was in the mistletoe on the oak.
There is an analogy in the myth of the Scandinavian god, Balder, who
could only be killed by having a bough of mistletoe hurled at him, and
by being struck by it. As oaks were sacred to Jupiter, the ancient
Roman sky-god, the King of the Wood may have been a personifica
tion of Jupiter himself, who had come down from heaven in a flash of
lightning to dwell among mortals in the Golden Bough, growing on
the sacred oak on the shore of the Lake of Nemi.
If that were so [again to quote Frazer], we need not wonder that the
priest guarded with drawn sword the mystic bough which contained the
god's life and his own. The goddess whom he served and married was her
self, if I am right, no other than the Queen of Heaven, the true wife of the
sky god. For she, too, loved the solitude of the woods and the lonely hills,
and sailing overhead on clear nights in the likeness of the silver moon she
looked down with pleasure on her own fair image reflected on the calm,
burnished surface of the lake, Diana's Mirror. (89, p. 695)
Sky gods were quite popular in ancient times, and Father Sky was
generally regarded as the husband of Mother Earth. Here the ancient
Egyptians chose to be different; for among them the earth was the hus
band and the sky was the wife. This arrangement may have been due
to the grammatical structure of the Egyptian language; for in that lan
guage the word for sky (pet) is feminine, whereas the word for earth
(to) is masculine. In ancient Egyptian art, the earth god Seb is shown
in human form, lying on the ground, with one arm raised skyward; and
the sky goddess Nut is pictured as a woman with her body arched over
that of her husband; her feet resting on the ground at one of his ex
tremities and her hands touching the earth at the other; and in some
cases, to emphasize her identity with the sky, her body is covered with
stars. According to Egyptian tradition, the earth and sky once touched
each other; the sky goddess Nut was depicted as lying flat on her hus
band, the earth god Seb. Then Shu, god of the air, and father of the sky
goddess, raised up the sky to its present elevation above the earth. In
scenes from the art of ancient Egypt, Shu is shown lifting up Nut with
his upraised hands; and in a text from a royal tomb at Thebes, we read
that Shu "has divided the heaven from the earth; he has uplifted the
heaven in eternity above the earth." The sky goddess is spoken of in
the inscription as "Nut, who gave birth to the gods, the lady of heaven,
the mistress of the two lands." The Egyptian mythologists sometimes



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 89
represented the sky as a huge cow instead of a woman, and the legs of
the heavenly cow were held in place by various gods, while the air god,
Shu, supported the body of the cow. In one inscription, a row of stars
decorates the stomach of the celestial cow, and the sun is shown travel
ing in a boat between her forelegs. (This heavenly cow was sometimes
identified with the goddess Hathor instead of Nut.)
The Babylonians worshiped a trinity, which consisted of the sky god
Anu, the earth god Bel, and the water god Ea. The Semitic Babyloni
ans inherited these gods from the Sumerians, the first civilized inhabit
ants of Mesopotamia. The original Sumerian names of these gods were
Anna (the sky god), Enlil (the earth god), and Enki (the water god);
but since the Semitic equivalents Anu, Bel, and Ea are preferred by
most Assyriologists, we shall follow that usage here. Anu was especially
worshiped at Uruk or Erech, named in the Bible (Genesis X, 10), as
one of the oldest cities of Babylonia. The name, Anu, means "the one
on high," and may be traced back to the Sumerian "An," which meant
"the sky." Anu, as a personification of the sky, corresponds with the
Greek Zeus and the Roman Jupiter. Also like these two sky gods, he
was ruler over all the other gods. Anu was known as both king
(sharru), and Father of the Gods (Abu Hani); and his high rank is
shown by the allotment to him of the number sixty. This number (60)
was the unit of the sexagesimal system of enumeration; it was held to
be a particularly sacred number and was henceforth appropriately as
signed to the King of the Gods. The gods Bel and Ea, as lesser divini
ties, were given the numbers fifty and forty respectively. Anu, as a sky
god, was a dweller in the celestial realms, where he occupied a throne
in the northern sky; and since the King of the Gods must have a
queenly consort, he was provided with a wife, the goddess Antum. In
an inscription written by King Agumkakrime of Babylon (about 1650
B.C.), the monarch made the following prayer: "May Anu and
Antum, who live in heaven, send a blessing on A gum, the good king,
who built the sanctuaries of Marduk and freed from obligation the workmen." Several eminent scholars have contended that sun worship was prob
ably the earliest form of astral theology. "The pre-eminence of the sun,
as the fountain-head of life and man's well being," we have been told,
"must have rendered it at a date almost contemporaneous with the
birth of the race, the chief object of man's worship." (180, p. 141) The
fact is that the sky god was the oldest of the celestial deities, whereas



90 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
stellar, lunar, and solar divinities were a later development. Sun cults
were of great import in the religious history of early man, but solar
worship was nevertheless a late stage in the evolution of natural theol
ogy. "Solar worship was not universal, nor was it essentially basic,"
was the considered judgment of Dr. Wile. "The Chinese did not wor
ship the sun; no actual solar cult existed in Oceania, although Rivers
connects the megaliths of Oceania with the sun cult. . . . The belief of
the stellar cults was taken over by the sun cult." (274, pp. 302-3)
The researches of Sir Norman Lockyer and Sir Wallis Budge have
shown the priority of star worship in the valley of the Nile. The pyra
mids of northern Egypt were oriented east and west (that is, to the
sun) while in southern Egypt the more ancient step pyramids face
southeast. "With such an amplitude, that it could not have been a ques
tion of the sunlight entering the shrine, we are driven, therefore, to star
worship." (34) Some important temples of the south of Egypt and of
Ethiopia were oriented to Alpha Centauri. One of the brilliantly shin
ing stars of the southern skies is Canopus, which figures prominently in
the stellar worship of ancient Egypt. The findings of Lockyer, relating
to ancient star worship in the Nile Valley, have been briefly summa
rized by a noted student of ancient astronomy as follows:
Lxxkyer tells us of a series of temples at Edfu, Philae, Amada and Sem
neh, so oriented at their erection, 6400 B.C. as to show Canopus heralding
the sunrise at the autumnal equinox, when it was known as the symbol of
Khons, or Khonsu, the first southern star god; and of other similar temples
later. At least two of the great structures at Karnak, of 2100 B.C. and 1700
B.C., respectively, pointed to its setting; as did another at Naga, and the
temple of Khons at Thebes, built by Rameses 111 about 1300 B.C., after
wards restored and enlarged under the Ptolemies. It thus probably was the
prominent object in the religion of southern Egypt, where it represented
the god of the waters. ( 1 , pp. 70-7 1
That the worship of the moon is of greater antiquity than that of the
sun is a fact that calls for some sort of explanation.
In fact, as has been repeatedly noted [we learn from the meticulous studies
of Dr. BrifFault], the cults of lunar deities occupy in early phases of religion
a far more important place than those of any other deities. If the fact has
long been overlooked, ... it is owing to the assumption that religious ideas
have arisen as philosophical interpretations of the universe. Were this the
case, the sun would naturally occupy the first place among natural objects
as the most conspicuous image of the powers ruling human destiny. Since



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 91
religion did not, however, originate as a philosophical system, but as the
practice of magic, those considerations are irrelevant and misleading. In
point of fact, the interpretations giving rise to solar cults and mythologies
do not make their appearance until relatively advanced stages of culture
when agriculture on a large scale had developed and primitive notions have
lost much of their significance and the functions and attributes of sun gods,
which are then theoretically and artificially evolved, are found to be bor
rowed from earlier lunar deities. It may indeed be said without exaggera
tion that the great majority of supreme gods possessing cosmic and univer
sal attributes have originally arisen as moon gods, who were primarily
regarded as controlling the powers of generation. (21, pp. 117-18)
The anthropological works of Dr. Robert Briffault are brilliantly po
lemical, especially his three-volume masterpiece, The Mothers, the clas
sic statement of the matriarchal theory of social origins. Most anthro
pologists refuse to admit the theoretical soundness of Briffault's
opinions, but even these critics have been compelled to admit that his
conclusions are backed up by impressive documentation. But we do not
doubt the accuracy of Briffault's contention that moon worship was an
earlier phase of religion than sun worship. An independent study of
this question was made by an American scholar, Ernest Busenbark, in
Symbols, Sex and the Stars in Popular Beliefs (38), which work is rec
ommended to any reader who is interested in the study of ancient sym
bolism. This book, written in a popular style, treats of the origins of
moon and sun worship, astrology, sex symbolism, the mystic meaning
of numbers, the Cabala, and many popular customs, myths, supersti
tions, and religious beliefs.
In the great civilizations of antiquity, the solar cults finally attained a
dominant position in the religious realm; but the other nature gods did
not lose out altogether. The Egyptian moon god, Thoth, for instance,
managed to retain a position of great power in the Nilotic pantheon,
even after the pharaohs began to call themselves Sons of the Sun. On the monuments, Thoth is depicted as an ibis, with mingled black and
white feathers, representing the dark and bright sides of the moon. The
name Thoth literally means the measurer, an appropriate epithet for the
moon god; so he was shown wearing the lunar crescent and disk, and
holding a stylus and a notched palm branch; and was identified with
Hermes Trismegistus, author of the sacred books and the father of
magic. Egyptian sun worship was a most complex system, for the sun was
known by several names, and its various attributes were differentiated



92 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
and made the objects of divine worship. "The Egyptians in the deifica
tion of the sun," as noted by Olcott, "considered the luminary in its
different aspects separating the light from the heat of the sun, and the
orb from the rays. Egyptian sun worship was therefore polytheistic,
and several distinct deities were worshiped as sun gods. Thus, there
were sun gods representing the physical orb, the intellectual sun, the
sun considered as the source of heat, and the source of light, the power
of the sun, the sun in the firmament, and the sun in his resting place."
(180, pp. 150-51) It seems that the greatest of the Nilotic sun gods was Ra or Re, who
was a personification of the physical sun; for, indeed, Ra was the ordi
nary everyday Egyptian name for the sun. Ra was part of a solar trin
ity, the other members being Osiris and Horus. As a solar divinity, Ra
was usually pictured as a hawk-headed man, holding the regal scepter
in one hand and the crux ansata in the other. On his head was a globe
or disk, around which a uraeus or asp was coiled, the serpent being em
blematic of the power over life and death. The image of Ra and the
disk on his head was generally painted red, since the deity was particu
larly associated with the heat of the midday sun. Osiris, on the other
hand, was symbolic of the setting sun. Osiris, as a dweller among men,
assumed the form of the bull, Apis. As a judge of the dead, he was de
picted as a mummy of sacred blue color, armed with the rod of author
ity and the cross of life and adorned with the crown of upper and lower
Egypt. His eldest son, Horus, was the rising sun, who slew the Dragon
of Darkness with a spear (ray). Another among the Egyptian solar
divinities was Amen (or Ammon), and he was a personification of the
sun after it had descended below the western horizon, and was thus hidden from sight. Amen was imaged as a man with the head and horns
of a ram, and his pictures and statues were painted blue, the sacred
color of the source of life. In the Egyptian language, the word ram
means concealment, and the solar god Amen was called the concealed one

an appropriate title for the sun after it had disappeared in the western
sky and descended into the underworld. In Thebes, the cults of Ammon and Ra were consolidated, and the sun was there worshiped
under the name Amen-Ra. Solarism reached its zenith in Egypt during
the reign of Ikhnaton (Amenhotep IV), for this sovereign forbade the
worship of all gods except the great living disk of the sun. He ordered
the names of the other gods erased from the monuments and decreed
that their images should be destroyed. This worship of the solar disk
under the name of A ton came to an end on the death of the heretic



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 93
king, and the old gods again returned to their temples. Among the minor Egyptian sun gods were Ptah, an embodiment of the life-giving
power of the sun, and Mandu, who personified the power of the mid
day sun of summer; and even the rays of the solar orb were deified, under the names of Gom, Kons, and Moni, who were known as Sons of
the Sun.
The Sumerians adored a sun god named Utu, or Babbar, whom the later Semitic Babylonians adopted and worshiped under the name of
Shamash. On ancient cylinder seals, this sun god is shown traveling
across the sky on foot; but, later on, he was considerately provided with
a chariot, drawn by two fiery horses. Shamash was pictured as an old man with a long beard, and with sunbeams radiating from his shoul
ders. His emblem was a four-pointed star within a round disk, with
flickering flames between the points of the star. In the Babylonian
firmament were two doors—one in the east, the other in the west. In the morning, Shamash came from behind the dome of heaven and
passed through the eastern door. Then, after pausing on the summit of
the Mountain of the Sunrise, at the edge of the world, he began his
daily journey across the sky. In the evening, he arrived at the Moun
tain of the Sunset, and then disappeared through the western door into
the underworld. A Babylonian solar demigod of considerable importance was Gilgamesh, whose exploits, known as "The Epic of Gilgam
esh," were inscribed on twelve tablets.
"Noteworthy," as was observed by F. J. Gould, "is the heroic legend of Gilgamesh, whose twelve adventures mark him out as a solar
demi-god, passing through the zodiacal episodes, slaying the lion,
wooed by the virgin Ishtar, sickening and wasting at the close of his
journey, and renewing his glory in the new year. From Chaldea the
myth migrated to Phoenicia, where the hero took the form of Mel
karth; thence another step brings us to Herakles of Greece." (100, p.
118) Herakles is best known to the modern world as Hercules, the Latin form of the Greek name. Most students of mythology should be fa
miliar with the story of the twelve labors of Hercules; for Hercules
is a personification of the sun, and his twelve labors are an allegory
of the passage of the sun through the twelve constellations of the zo
diac. Before proceeding further, we must say something about the signs and constellations of the zodiac. To prevent any confusion concerning
this matter, we direct the attention of the reader to an authoritative ex



94 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
planation of the same, as given by an eminent mathematician and as
tronomer:
It is necessary to make a distinction between the signs of the zodiac and
the constellations of the zodiac. Each sign is exactly thirty degrees long—one
twelfth of the complete zone—and the first sign (Aries) begins with the
vernal equinox. That is, the sun always enters the sign of Aries as it passes
through the vernal equinox. It then traverses Taurus and Gemini and en
ters Cancer at the summer solstice, after which it traverses Leo and Virgo
and enters Libra as it passes through the autumnal equinox. The sun then
traverses the six signs south of Aries again when it arrives at the vernal
equinox.
Hie twelve groups of stars (constellations) within the boundaries of the
twelve signs originally had the same names as the signs, and each constella
tion would remain forever within the corresponding sign if the vernal equinox did not move among the stars. However, Hipparchus discovered (125
B.C.) that the vernal equinox is not a fixed point in the sky, but that it
slowly slips westward along the ecliptic. What a close observer he must
have been to make this discovery seventeen centuries before the invention
of the telescope! This movement of the equinoxes, which is called preces
sion, carries the signs of the zodiac westward around the ecliptic with a con
stant speed that causes them to make a complete circuit of the sky in 26,000
years. Thus the constellations of the zodiac remain fixed but the signs slip steadily westward.
The sign of Aries is now in the constellation Pisces, each sign having
backed into the constellation west of it in the last 2000 years. The signs and
constellations, of the zodiac coincided about 300 B.C., and also about 26,000
B.C. We know they were in use before 300 B.C. They must, therefore, have been invented not later than 26,000 B.C. This gives us some informa
tion as to how long man has been studying the stars. . . . The Greek
writer, Hesiod, says that A returns rises at sunset fifty days after the winter
solstice. In our calendar, this date would be February 19th. This bright star
now rises at sunset in Greece on March 30th. From the known rate of the
shift of the constellations of the zodiac, we can determine that Hesiod lived
about 2800 years ago. (113, pp. 251-52)
The solar zodiac can be traced back to Egypt, where it was known
at a remote period of time, but the solar zodiac was preceded by a lunar
zodiac, which was divided into twenty-seven or twenty-eight lunar
mansions instead of twelve signs. "Although the zodiac is now deter
mined by the course in the heaven apparently pursued by the sun in his
annual journey through the sky," we are told by Professor Arthur
Harding, "it is very probable that it was originally determined by the



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 95
path of the moon, which follows very closely the path of the sun, and
which can be observed at the same time as the stars. The moon moves
eastward among the stars at such a rate that it accomplishes a complete
circuit of the sky in about 28 days." (113, p. 250) Since the lunar
month is between twenty-seven and twenty-eight days, the lunar man
sions were sometimes listed as twenty-seven, although twenty-eight
was the usual figure. The Hindus had a lunar zodiac of twenty-seven
mansions, whereas the Chinese and Arabs counted twenty-eight.
The symbolism of the zodiac is elegantly displayed in the myth of
Hercules. u Of all the ancient divinities," to cite Edward Carpenter,
"perhaps Hercules is the one whose role as a sun god is most generally
admitted. The helper of gods and men, a mighty traveller and invoked
everywhere as the savior, his labors for the good of the world became
ultimately defined and systematized as 12, and corresponding in num
ber to the signs of the zodiac." (41, p. 48) The myth of Hercules was known to the Sumerians, Phoenicians, and Greeks. Herodotus specu
lated on its origin and traced it back to the Egyptians. (See 118, pp.
96-97.) There is no god named Hercules in the official pantheon of
Egypt, but an ancient Greek tradition identified Hercules with the an
cient Nilotic deity, Khonsu. George St. Clair in a scholarly study of
Egyptian mythology, after discussing the better-known gods of ancient
Egypt, directs our attention to a small group of deities standing apart:
These are Amen, Mut and Khonsu, often spoken of as the Triad of
Thebes, or the Trinity of Ethiopia. . . . Budge tells us that the Theban triad had nothing whatever to do with the Book of the Dead, and from this we may suspect that they were either gods newly come up or gods of foreign derivation. For some good reason, the orthodox Egyptian of the old
school kept them out of his sacred books. They were the divinities of Thebes, and that city was hundreds of miles south of Heliopolis; they were
the Trinity of Ethiopia and not of Egypt. (219, p. 404)
In the myth of Hercules, the sun (of which Hercules is the personi
fication) begins his zodiacal journey in the constellation Leo, the Lion;
so the first labor of Hercules was the slaying of the Nemean lion. After
killing the lion, the hero flayed the beast and used its skin thereafter as
a shield. The leonine skin has been compared to the tawny clouds
which the sun trails behind him as he fights his way through vapors,
which he eventually overcomes.
When the sun enters Virgo, the constellation of the Hydra sets; and
thus the second labor of Hercules was the destruction of the Lernean



96 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
hydra. The monster had several heads, one of which was immortal; and
as the hydra raised his heads, one after another, to attack Hercules, the
demigod in turn burned off the heads, and he disposed of the immortal head by burying it beneath a stone.
As the beast was possessed of many heads [writes Olcott] so the storm wind must continually supply new clouds to vanquish the sun; but the
lighter vapor and mist, the immortal head, is only conquered for a time. The sun easily burns up the heavy clouds, the mortal heads, but only hides temporarily the immortal head which raises again and again to daunt him.
In the fight Hercules was attended by his friend Iolus, this name recalls
Iole, signifying the violet tinted clouds, the attendants of the sun in its serene moments. (180, pp. 72-73)
With the commencement of autumn, the sun enters Libra. At this
time of year, the constellation of the Centaur rose above the horizon;
and Hercules, in his third labor, was entertained by a centaur. Still later
on, he slew a group of centaurs fighting over a cask of wine. When the
sun was in Libra, the constellation of the Boar rose in the evening sky;
so, after killing the centaurs, Hercules met the Erymanthian boar and
disposed of him in mortal combat.
When the sun enters Scorpio, the constellation Cassiopeia, anciently
known as the Stag, rises into view, and the fourth labor of Hercules
was the capture of a stag with golden horns and brazen feet.
As the sun passes into Sagittarius, three constellations named after
birds rise, and these three star groups are called the Vulture, the Swan,
and the Eagle:Tn his fifth labor, Hercules kills three birds with arrows.
The constellation Capricorn was also known as the Stable of Augeas,
and the sixth labor of Hercules was the cleansing of the Augean stable.
When the sun is in Aquarius, the Lyre, or celestial Vulture, sets.
Prometheus also sets, while the Bull of Europa is on the meridian. In
the seventh labor, Hercules slew the vulture which had preyed on the
liver of Prometheus, and also captured the wild bull which had laid
waste the island of Crete.
While the sun is in Pisces, Pegasus, the celestial horse, rises; and in
his eighth labor, Hercules carried off the horses of Diomede.
As the sun enters Aries, the Ram of the Golden Fleece, the Ship Argo rises in the evening sky, and Andromeda sets. One of the stars of
Andromeda is called her girdle. Hercules, in the ninth labor, sailed in
the Ship Argo to search for the Golden Fleece; he fought the Amazons,
and captured the girdle of Hippolyte, their queen, and rescued Hesione
from a sea monster, the same as Perseus did Andromeda.



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 97
The sun passes into the Bull or Ox, as the Pleiades rise and Orion
sets; and the tenth labor of Hercules was to restore the seven kid
napped Pleiades to their father, after killing their abductor, King Busi
ris (Orion). Then he went to Spain and stole the oxen of Geryon.
When the sun moves into Gemini, Sirius, the Dog Star, rises; and in
the eleventh labor, Hercules conquered Cerberus, the guardian dog of Hades.
The sun enters Cancer as the constellations of the River and the
Centaur set in the western sky. The constellation Hercules descends to
ward the west, followed by Draco, the Dragon of the pole, guardian of the Golden Apples of the Hesperides. Hercules is represented in star
atlases as crushing the head of the dragon with his foot. In his twelfth and last labor, Hercules journeyed to the Hesperides to seek the Golden Apples. Then he put on a robe dipped in the blood of a cen
taur, slain by him at the crossing of a river. The robe mysteriously
caught fire, and Hercules perished in the flames.
In this death scene of the solar hero [asserts Olcott] and in the glories of his funeral pyre, we have the most famous sunset scene that has ever been presented for our contemplation. All the wondrous coloring that adorns the western sky at set of sun illuminates the canvas, and the reflection of the scene streams afar, lighting the waves of the Aegean and its clustering isles, and painting in enduring hues a scene that all nations proclaim the sub
limest that nature offers to man's vision. (180, p. 74)
The great sun god of the Roman world was Mithra; but it seems that
Mithra originally was not a personification of the light of the sun: He
represented the luminous sky in general. Mithraism was the resultant
of Persian dualism and Chaldean star worship. Just how, where, and when Mithra was transformed from a god of light in general into a sun god in particular we have no certain knowledge. "The change perhaps
took place in Babylonia," Frazer surmised, "where, under the powerful
influence of Chaldean theology and astrology, the Iranian deities were
assimilated to their nearest Semitic counterparts, the supreme god Ahura Mazda being identified with the sky god Bel, while the goddess Anahita was confused with Ishtar, the goddess of the planet Venus, and Mithra was equated with the sun god Shamash." (91, p. 503) The role of Mithra in the Zoroastrian cult of ancient Iran has been so well ex
plained by the Greek Scholar, Plutarch, that we are pleased to repro
duce it here:
Many suppose there to be two gods of opposite inclinations, one delight
ing in good, the other in evil; the first of these is called particularly by the



98 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
name of God, the second by that of Genius or Demon. Zoroaster has denominated them Ormuzd and Ahriman, and has said that of whatever falls
under the cognizance of our senses, light is the best representation of the
one, and darkness and ignorance of the other. He adds that Mithra is an in
termediate being, and it is for this reason the Persians call Mithra the medi
ator or intermediator. . . . The Persians also say, that Ormuzd was born or
formed out of the purest light; Ahriman, on the contrary, out of the thickest
darkness; that Ormuzd made six gods as good as himself, and Ahriman op
posed to them six wicked ones; that Ormuzd afterwards multiplied himself
three fold (Hermes Trismegistus), and removed to a distance as remote
from the sun as the sun is remote from the earth; that he there formed stars,
and among others, Sirius, which he placed in the heavens as a guard or sen
tinel. He made also twenty-four other gods which he enclosed in an egg;
but Ahriman created an equal number on his own part, who broke the egg, and from that moment good and evil were mixed (in the universe). But Ahriman is one day to be conquered and the earth to be made equal and smooth, that all men may live happy. Theopompus adds, from the books of
the Magi, that one of these gods reigns in turn every three thousand years,
during which the other is kept in subjection; that they afterwards contend
with equal portion of time, but that in the end, the evil Genius will fall
(never to rise again). Then men will become happy and their bodies cast no
shade. The god who mediates all these things reclines at present in repose,
waiting till he shall be pleased to execute them, (Isis and Osiris by Plutarch,
as cited by Count Volney in 258, pp. 138-39)
In a commentary on the above citation from Plutarch, Count Vol
ney explained its meaning:
There is an apparent allegory through the whole of this passage. The egg
is the fixed sphere, the world; the six gods of Ormuzd are the six signs of
summer, those of Ahriman, the six signs of winter. The forty-eight other
gods are the forty-eight constellations of the ancient sphere, divided equally between Ahriman and Ormuzd. The office of Sirius, as guard and sentinel,
tells us that the origin of these ideas was Egyptian; finally, the expression
that the earth is to become equal and smooth, and that the bodies of happy
beings are to cast no shade, proves that the equator was considered as their
true paradise. (258, p. 139)
Mithra was not content to be one of the minor gods of the Persians,
so he migrated to Chaldea; and from there, his worship spread to Ar
menia and Anatolia. The worship of Mithra soon established itself
among the ruling classes of Pontus, Cappadocia, and Lydia. In the first
century B.C., Mithridates Eupator, King of Pontus, an ardent devotee



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 99
of the sun god Mithra, waged war with the legions of Rome. In this
struggle, Mithridates was aided by Cilician pirates, and it is believed
that these pirates were converted to Mithraism by their royal patron.
At any rate, we know that the pirates, when they were afterward sub
dued by the Romans, proceeded to convert their captors to the worship
of the Unconquered Sun. This solar cult became popular among the sol
diers of the Roman armies. Then it spread among the civilian population, and eventually became the dominant religion of the Roman Em
pire. It may seem paradoxical that a bunch of pirates from the wilds of
Cilicia could introduce a new religion into the august Roman Empire;
but these sea rovers were no ordinary ruffians. The pomp and cere
mony in which they habitually indulged is vividly conveyed to us by
Sir J. G. Frazer in his characteristically charming style:
Outside the Anatolian tableland, the first to observe the rites of Mithra
are said to have been the Cilician pirates. During the civil wars which dis
tracted the attention and absorbed the energies of the Romans in the first
century of our era, the daring rovers seized the opportunity to issue from
the secret creeks and winding rivers of Cilicia and scour the seas, landing
from time to time, harrying islands, holding cities to ransom, and carrying
off from some of the most famous sanctuaries the wealth which had been accumulated there by the piety of the ages. Gorged with plunder and elated
by the impunity which they long enjoyed, the corsairs rose to an extraordi
nary pitch of audacity and effrontery, marching up the highroads of Italy,
plundering villas, and abducting Roman magistrates in their robes of office;
while at sea they displayed a pomp and pageantry proportioned to the
riches which they had amassed by their successful forays. Their galleys
flaunted gilded sails and purple awnings, and glided along to the measured
plash of silvered oars, while the sounds of music and revelry wafted across
the water, told to the trembling inhabitants of the neighboring coasts the
riot and debauchery of the buccaneers. (91, pp. 505-6)
At the end of the first century of the Christian Era, Mithraism had
so far progressed in the conquest of the Roman Empire that the poet
Statius seriously suggested that the old sun god Apollo might desire to
have his worshipers address him as "Mithra, who under the rocks of the
Persian cave twists the bull's struggling horns." The poet was referring
to a sculptured scene that is carved on many Mithraic monuments
throughout the length and breadth of the Roman Empire. The carving
depicts Mithra in a cave, kneeling on the back of a bull. With one hand
he is twisting the head of the bull, while with the other hand he is driv
ing a knife into the flank of the animal.



100 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
In commenting on the sentiment expressed by Statius, the scholiast
Lactantius Placidus tells us that Mithra was the sun, worshiped in caves
by the Persians; and the horned bull was symbolic of the horned moon.
The scene was imaged in a cave as suggestive of an eclipse of the sun, which was caused by the moon coming between the sun and the earth.
The same scholiast also noted that the Mithra was sometimes pictured
with the head of a lion—a circumstance which he connected with the
entrance of the sun into the constellation Leo (the Lion), as it travels
through the zodiac; or as an emblem of the superiority of the sun over
