all other gods, which may be justly likened to the primacy of the lion
over all the other beasts. The last speculation is both ingenious and er
roneous; for the lion-headed god could hardly have been Mithra. We
have learned from the researches of Cumont and Frazer that the image
of a lion-headed deity, with a serpent twined around his body, and
grasping one or two keys in his hands, was the Persian god Zervan Ak
arana (Infinite Time), a personification of time. A sect of the Magi re
garded this divinity as the progenitor of all things, and the creator of both Ormuzd and Ahriman. In the numerous monuments showing the
sacrifice of the bull in Mithraic art, besides the god Mithra, there are
two other male figures, dressed like the god and, like him, wearing
peaked Phrygian caps. These two associates flank the bull-slaying
deity, and one of them named Cautes holds a burning torch upright
while the other, Cautopates, holds a burning torch upside down. It has
been suggested by the French scholar Montfaucon that the three
figures represent a solar trinity; with Cautes as the rising sun, Mithra as
the midday sun, and Cautopates as the setting sun. The two torch
bearers may also be interpreted as symbols of the sun at the vernal and
autumnal equinoxes, respectively. On some of the monuments, Cautes
is displayed as holding in his hands the head of a bull, or a bull is seen
browsing or resting by his side. Cautopates is, on the other hand,
shown either with a scorpion in his hands, or one is crawling at his feet.
In ancient Babylonia, five thousand years ago, the sun entered the zodi
acal sign of Taurus (the Bull) at the vernal equinox, and the passage of
the sun into Scorpio (the Scorpion) took place at the autumnal equi
nox. About three thousand years later in classical Rome, due to the pre
cession of the equinoxes, the sun was in the sign Aries (the Ram) at the
beginning of spring, and in the sign Libra (the Balance) at the begin
ning of autumn. "It is tempting to conjecture," as Frazer noted, uthat
the traditional emblems of the constellations which once marked the
beginning of spring and the beginning of autumn were transmitted



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 101
from Chaldea to the west and preserved in the symbolism of the myste
ries long after they had ceased to correspond with the facts of astron
omy." (91, p. 516)
Sun worship was by no means peculiar to the ancient peoples of the
Old World, for it also flourished in the great empires of ancient Amer
ica. The sun was the reputed founder of the Incan dynasty of Peru,
and the Incas called themselves children of the sun. The Great Temple
of the Sun at Cuzco was an edifice of great splendor consisting of a vast central auditorium which was surrounded by a group of smaller build
ings. The roof of the temple was fabricated from timber plated with
gold, and so much of the precious metal was used in the interior deco
rations that this building was called the Golden Palace. The exterior
walls were encircled by a gold frieze six inches wide, and all apart
ments of the interior were similarly embellished. The entrance of the
temple faced east, and above the altar was a golden disk with a human
face. This disk was studded with precious stones and, at certain seasons
of the year, the rays of the rising sun were reflected from it with great
brilliance. Around the sacred solar disk was arranged a semicircle of the
mummies of past Incas situated on thrones of gold. The smaller ad
juncts to the main temple were sacred to other gods. Chief among
these lesser shrines was the Temple of the Moon, who was the consort
of the Sun; and over the altar of the moon goddess was a silver disk,
flanked by the mummies of departed queens. The lesser fanes were
dedicated to the stars, to lightning, and to the rainbow. Outside the
temple was an extensive garden stocked with beautiful and exotic
plants, and scattered throughout the garden were numerous images of
trees, flowers, and animals made of solid gold.
Interesting relics of ancient sun worship may be observed in the
ceremonies, symbols, and ritual of modern Freemasonry. According to Masonic tradition, the word Freemason can be traced back to ancient Egypt. It was derived from two Egyptian words, Phre (the sun), and
Mas (a child), meaning children of the sun, or sons of light; and from
this combination we derive the word Freemason. A Mason is advised to
travel to the east in search of light, for the sun rises in the east. The
Masonic initiation rites, like those of the ancient mysteries of Egypt,
are a drama based on the astronomical allegory of the death and resur
rection of the sun, and are intended to teach the candidate the doctrines
of the unity of God and the immortality of man; and these tenets are
taught in the ritual of the Third Degree. We learn from Dr. Oliver's
Dictionary of Symbolic Masonry that a lodge has three lights, situated in



102 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the east, the south, and the west respectively. They are so placed in
allusion to the sun, which rises in the east, crosses the meridian by way
of the south, and sets in the west. There is no light in the north part of
a lodge, since the sun directs no rays from that area. Every Masonic
lodge should be oriented due east and west because the sun, the glory
of the Lord, rises in the east and sets in the west, and in the lodge, the
Master stands in the east, the Junior Warden in the south, and the Sen
ior Warden in the west, which positions are representative of sunrise,
noon, and sunset, respectively.
The three steps depicted on the master's carpet refer to the three de
grees by which the candidate becomes a Master Mason. The three
steps are also emblematic of the zodiacal constellations Taurus, Gem
ini, and Cancer, by which the sun ascends to the summit of the Royal
Arch, which is the starry vault of the sky, or zodiacal arch, reaching
from the vernal to the autumnal equinox.
In Masonic lodges, two pillars are erected, surmounted by globes,
and these are called the Pillars of the Porch. They are said to have been
derived from the pillars of the porch of King Solomon's temple. In
even more ancient Egyptian temples, there were two pillars, one called
Boaz on the north side, representing the sun, and the other named Ja
chiny on the south, pertaining to the moon. It may be of interest to note
that the cornerstone of a Masonic Lodge should be oriented to the
northeast, because the sun rises in the northeast on June 21st, the long
est day in the year. (The above data on the relations between ancient
sun lore and modern Freemasonry were obtained from Stellar Theology and Masonic Astronomy by Robert H. Brown, 32nd Degree Mason
[27].) Thomas Paine, the great hero of the American Revolution, wrote an
essay, u The Origin of Freemasonry," in which he traced that fraternal
order back to the Celtic Druids. u When the Christian religion overran
the religion of the Druids, in Italy, ancient Gaul, Britain and Ireland,"
Paine asserted, "the Druids became the subjects of persecution. This
would naturally and necessarily oblige such of them as remained at
tached to their original religion to meet in secret and under the strong
est injunctions of secrecy. Their safety depended upon it. A false
brother might expose the lives of many of them to destruction; and
from the remains of the religion of the Druids, thus preserved, arose the
institution which, to avoid the name of Druid, took that of Mason, and practiced under this new name, the rites and ceremonies of the
Druids." (181, p. 335)



THF, WORSHIP OF NATURE 103
Paine's explanation of the origin of Freemasonry is somewhat specu
lative, but it was indubitably a step in the right direction. It is well
known that in pre-Roman days western Europe was inhabited by the
Celts—a people whose priests were called Druids. The name Druid
means a wise man, and the Druidic religion was based on the worship
of the sun. Their chief god was Esus or Hesus, and his symbol was the
solar fire. The Druids also paid homage to the solar gods of alien cults,
especially the Greco-Roman divinity Apollo. The historian Diodorus
Siculus noted: "Apollo is worshipped with solemn rites by the inhabit
ants of a large island, which lies off the coast of Gaul in the Northern
Ocean. This island is inhabited by the Hyperboreans, so named be
cause they lived beyond the region of the north wind. The god has
there a remarkable temple, circular in form, and a magnificent forest is
consecrated to him." The large island off the coast of Gaul (France),
we may reasonably conclude was Great Britain, and the circular tem
ple, Stonehenge, which was an important seat of Druidic sun worship.
There is an ancient system of natural religion known as phallic wor
ship, or sex worship, which merits some discussion. The worship of sex
is referred to by scholars as phallism or phallicism; these names being
derived from the Latin form of the Greek word Phallus, which means
the male organ of generation. In a philological sense, phallus is related to
the Sanskrit Phal, which means to produce or to be fruitful; and modern
survivals are found in the German Pfahl and the English Pole. In the
extensive literature on the sex cults and the curious symbolism con
nected therewith, the Sanskrit words Lingam (denoting the male
organ) and Yoni (the female organ) are frequently used.
Certain writers have judged the rites and ceremonies of the phallic
cults to be of an indecent or obscene nature; but such a conclusion is
not supported by a study of the relevant evidence.
In discussing totemism, we noted that certain writers who referred
to this ancient institution as animal worship were not stating the truth.
However, there is a branch of natural religion known as zoolatry (the
worship of animals). The best short statement on this topic was made
by the late F. J. Gould, whom we are pleased to quote:
The animal world, with its changing colors, its variety of forms, its many
languages, its jewelled beauty alternated with creeping ugliness, its amazing
skill and agility, its sweetness in some shapes, its terror in others, has cast a
natural fascination over the uncultured mind. The savage has not the pride
of the civilized man, and does not differentiate himself too broadly from the



104 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
brute. Why, indeed, should he when, as is frequently the case, he believes
the creature has a human, or human-like spirit? Some Redskins forbear
from hurting rattlesnakes fearing the vengeance of the reptile's spirit.
Kaffirs tell the slain elephant that they did not kill him intentionally. When
the Ainus have despatched a bear, they salute the carcase. Other Asiatics
will beg the beast's pardon. In various Pacific islands, the gods are supposed
to find embodiment in birds, who, when they descend upon the food
offered by the worshippers, utter cries which are taken as oracular answers.
The Tongans presented scented oil to the passing whales, thinking the di
vinity would be gratified. The Maoris reverence the spider and see in its
filmy gossamer the ladder by which spirits climb to heaven. In the Philip
pines, the alligator is worshipped; in Ceylon, the goose; in Guinea, the
crocodile. To the ancient Mexicans the owl was an evil spirit. Ducks which
fly abroad at night were regarded by the Abipones as souls of the dead. The
Apaches believe rattlesnakes to be incarnations of wicked souls. When a
Samoan was drowned or slain, his friends would sit near the body with a
sheet spread out on the earth; and the ant or butterfly, or whatever lit upon
the sheet first was looked upon as the spirit, caught and carefully wrapped
up, and buried with ceremony.
In ancient days, Egypt was a singular devotee of the animal. Its deities were honored with statues, which compounded the human trunk with the head of a hawk, cat, cow, etc., and it symbolized man's soul by the emblem of a humanheaded hawk. Sacred animals were housed in the temples and
embalmed after death. The ibis especially was consecrated perhaps because
of its welcome appearance with the overflowing Nile, it being regarded as a
divine messenger of that happy event. India is, in modern times, a great
venerator of animals, such as the cow. Siva the god is incarnate in the mon
key, Durga in the jackal, Vishnu in the eagle; and the image of Ganesa is
adorned with an elephant's head. Curious survivals of zoolatry are seen in the worship which Christianity even now pays to the Holy Dove and the Lamb of God. (100, pp. 37-38)
So far, we have discussed gods connected with the earth, sky, stars,
moon, sun, trees, animals, and the like; but some gods were composite
in nature; that is, they absorbed the traits and attributes of many other
deities. A good example of this type of god is Osiris, the most popular
divinity of ancient Egypt.
Osiris was the son of the sky goddess Nut, who was the wife of the
sun god Ra. The father of Osiris, however, was not Ra, but the earth
god Seb. When Ra discovered the infidelity of his wife, he pronounced
a curse upon her, predicting that the expected child would be born in
no month and no year. In the meantime, Nut had acquired another



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 105
lover, the god Thoth, and this divinity had previously played a game of
draughts with the moon, and had won from the lunar orb one seventy
second part of each day of the year. The fractional parts were com
pounded into five whole days and were added by Thoth to the Egyp
tian year of 360 days. This was a mythological way of accounting for
the five supplementary days which were added to the Egyptian year in
order to bring the lunar and solar calendars into agreement. And, since
these five extra days were regarded as entirely outside the year of
twelve months, the curse of the sun god Ra did not apply to them. Osi
ris was born on the first of the supplementary days. At his nativity, a
mysterious voice rang out, announcing the earthly advent of the Lord
of All. Osiris was not an only child: On the second supplementary day,
Nut gave birth to the elder Horus; she became the mother of Set on the
third day, of the goddess Isis on the fourth day, and of the goddess
Nephthys on the fifth. In due time, Set married his sister Nephthys,
and Osiris married his sister Isis. Osiris, it is said, withdrew from the
realm of the gods and became an earthly king. He found the Egyptians
savages and conferred upon them the blessings of civilization; since the
inhabitants of the Nile land had been cannibals before the earthly pil
grimage of Osiris. Queen Isis found barley and wheat growing wild on
the banks of the great river, and King Osiris introduced the cultivation
of these grains among the people, who then eschewed cannibalism and
accommodated themselves to a diet of corn. Osiris was the original
gatherer of fruit from trees; he trained the creeping vines to twine
themselves around poles and was the first to tread the grapes. The good
king then turned over the government of Egypt to his queenly wife,
Isis, while he traveled over the world distributing the blessings of agri
culture and civilization to all mankind. In lands where the soil and cli
mate did not permit the culture of the vine, Osiris taught the people to
brew beer from barley. On returning to Egypt, the benevolent mon
arch, on account of the blessings he had diffused among men, was rec
ognized as a god and thus was worshiped by a grateful populace. But,
unfortunately, Osiris had a wicked and jealous brother named Set; and
the evil Set, with seventy-two accomplices, plotted the death of Osiris.
Craftily, Set obtained the measurements of his brother's body and con
structed a coffer of attractive design and of exactly the same dimen
sions. Then at a banquet, when drinking and revelry were at their
height, the wily Set brought in his coffer and offered to make a present
of it to anyone whose body would fit into it exactly. All present, with



106 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the exception of Osiris, tried out che coffer and none of them fitted into
it; then Osiris lay in it, and it fitted him exactly. Immediately the con
spirators slammed down the lid of the chest, fastened it with nails,
sealed it with molten lead, and then hurled it into the Nile. According
to Plutarch, all this happened on the 1 7th day of the month Athyr,
when the sun entered the zodiacal sign of Scorpio (the Scorpion), and
in the twenty-eighth year of the reign of Osiris.
The widowed Isis soon thereafter went into exile in the papyrus
swamps of the Delta, and there she gave birth to a son, the younger
Horus. Meanwhile, the floating coffin of Osiris had drifted into the Mediterranean Sea and was finally washed up on the coast of Phoeni
cia. Here a mysterious tree sprang up and enclosed the chest in its
trunk. The local king saw the tree and so admired it that he had it cut
down and fashioned into a pillar for his palace at Byblos. Isis wandered
over the face of the earth seeking the body of her dead husband, and
eventually arrived at Byblos. After splitting the tree pillar and retriev
ing the coffer, Isis swathed the pillar in linen, poured ointment over it,
and presented it to the King of Byblos. Then the sacred pillar was in
stalled in a Temple of Isis, where it was thereafter worshiped by the
natives of Byblos. The body of Osiris was taken back to Egypt by Isis,
and there hidden in a secret place. But one ill-fated evening while Set
was hunting for boars by the light of the full moon, he chanced to dis
cover the hidden chest. Proceeding to open it, he took the body of Osi
ris and chopped it into fourteen pieces, which he scattered all over the
land of Egypt, Isis later made a search and found all the parts of Osiris,
except one. Each fragment was buried by Isis where she found it, and
that is why so many cities of Egypt claimed possession of the grave of
Osiris. The missing part of Osiris was the phallus, so Isis made an
image of it for use in the religious festivals of the Egyptians. Such is the
myth of Osiris as told by Plutarch in his treatise On Isis and Osiris,
with some fragmentary data from the literature of ancient Egypt.
The story of Osiris has a happier ending in some of the native Egyp
tian versions which supplement the account written by Plutarch. We
are told that, after Isis had discovered the body of Osiris and collected
the fragments thereof, she and her sister Nephthys sat down and wept. This lament was heard by the sun god Ra, who, moved by compassion,
dispatched from heaven the jackal-headed god Anubis. This divinity,
with the help of Horus, Isis, Nephthys, and the ibis-headed Thoth, re
assembled the body of Osiris from the numerous fragments; then the
gods made a mummy of the corpse. Isis, who by good fortune was



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 107
fitted out with wings, fanned the mummy with them. The breath of
life returned to Osiris and, in consequence, his resurrection from the
dead occurred. He then betook himself to the other world to reign in
perpetuity as King of the Dead. His son Horus, having grown to man
hood, became a king and ruled on earth. Later on, he became the third
person of the great Egyptian trinity of Osiris, lsis, and Horus. The res
urrection of Osiris is pictured in a series of bas-reliefs on the walls of
his temple at Denderah. First we see the dead god as a mummy lying
on his bier. Then he rises up gradually. Finally, we see him standing
erect between the guardian wings of lsis, who stands behind him. In
front of the risen god is a male figure who holds up to his sight a crux
ansata, the symbol of life eternal. Another representation of the great
mystery is depicted in the Temple of lsis at Philae. In this sculpture,
we see the body of Osiris with stalks of corn growing out of it; and
nearby stands a priest pouring water on the cornstalks from a pitcher.
Accompanying this scene is an inscription reading: "This is the form
of him whom one may not name, Osiris of the Mysteries, who springs
from the returning waters." From the above, we gather that Osiris was
a personification of the corn, which sprouts up out of the fields after
they have been fertilized by the annual inundation of the Nile. In fact,
Osiris was sometimes called the crop or the harvest; so we may reasona
bly conclude that in one of his aspects this god was a personification of
the corn, which annually died and came to life again.
Besides being a corn spirit, Osiris was, among other things, a tree
god. His tree aspect was probably the older of the two, since tree wor
ship is thought to be an earlier form of religion than the worship of ce
reals. The church father Firmicus Maternus wrote a book, The Errors
of the Profane Religions, where a pagan religious ceremony is described,
in which Osiris was featured as a tree spirit. The worshipers of the god
first chopped down a pine tree, then hollowed out the center of the
tree. From the excavated wood an image of Osiris was fashioned; and
the image was then buried like a corpse in the hollow of the pine. The
enclosed image was kept for a year and then burned. In the Hall of
Osiris at Denderah, there is a picture of a coffin within which is shown
the hawk-headed mummy of the god, and the coffin is portrayed as en
closed within the trunk of a tree. This scene tends to confirm the accu
racy of the account of the ceremony given us by Firmicus Maternus.
Osiris was also a god of fertility, for phallic images of him were dis
played in the temples and carried in processions. "As a god of vegeta
tion," Frazer pointed out, "Osiris was naturally conceived as a god of



108 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
creative energy in general, since men at a certain stage of evolution fail
to distinguish between the reproductive powers of animals and plants.
Hence, a striking feature of his worship was the coarse but expressive
symbolism by which this aspect of his nature was presented to the eye
not merely of the initiated but of the multitude. At his festival, women
used to go about the village singing songs in his praise and carrying ob
scene images of him which they set in motion by means of strings. The
custom was probably a charm to ensure the growth of crops. " (87, p.
381)
A familiar role of Osiris was that of a god of the dead; among his ti
tles were Ruler of the Dead, Lord of the Underworld, and Lord of
Eternity. On the monuments, he is shown occupying the judgment
seat, armed with the staff of authority and holding the crux ansata.
Carved on his breast is a Saint Andrew's cross; and the throne on
which he is seated is adorned with a pattern of squares in two colors,
like a checkerboard, representing the good and evil which he was called
upon to judge.
Osiris likewise shone as a moon god. That this deity was sometimes
identified with the moon we know from the fact that he was on occa
sion depicted as a mummy, wearing on his head a crescent moon in
place of the usual crown. Plutarch, in his Isis and Osiris, discusses the
theory that Osiris was the moon, and that Set, or Typhon, was the sun,
"because the Moon," says he, "with her humid and generative light, is
favorable to the propagation of animals and the growth of plants; while
the sun, with his fierce fire scorches and burns up all growing things,
renders the greater part of the earth uninhabitable by reason of his
blaze, and often overpowers the moon herself.
1'
Osiris is said to have reigned twenty-eight years as ruler of Egypt.
This may be taken as an allusion to the twenty -eight days of the lunar
month. The rending of the body of Osiris into fourteen parts by Set
and his fellow conspirators refers to the waning of the moon, which
was imagined to lose a portion of itself on each of the fourteen days of
the second half of the lunar month. In the myth, Set finds the body of
Osiris at the time of the full moon; the dismemberment of the latter
consequently began with the waning moon.
The spring season in ancient Egypt began at the new moon of the month Phamenoth. At this time in the temples, a ceremony was cele
brated, called the entry of Osiris into the moon. At the ceremony of
the burial of Osiris, his worshipers constructed a crescent-shaped chest;



THE WORSHIP OF NATURE 109
because the moon, as it approaches the sun, assumes the shape of a cres
cent, and then vanishes altogether.
The bull Apis was representative of the soul of Osiris, and was said
to have been born of a cow impregnated by means of the divine in
fluence emanating from the moon. Once a year, at the full moon, pigs
were sacrificed simultaneously to the moon and to Osiris. And last, but
not least, in ancient Egyptian art, Osiris is sometimes depicted as a hu
man-headed mummy, grasping in his hands the characteristic emblems
of power, and wearing on his head, in place of the customary crown, a
full moon within a crescent.
That Osiris was a sun god, in addition to his other attributes, few, if
any, students of comparative religion would deny. An outstanding
scholar rendered the following verdict: "Mythologically, Osiris was the
sun after its disappearance in the west, where he was slain by the envi
ous Night, and yet, destined to rise again the next morning." (100, p.
108) Sir J. G. Frazer, on the contrary, thought of Osiris as a vegetation
god, and considered theories identifying that god with the sun as ill
founded. Frazer stated his position as follows:
The ground upon which some modern writers seem chiefly to rely for
the identification of Osiris with the sun is that the story of his death fits bet
ter with the solar phenomena than with any other in nature. It may readily
be admitted that the daily appearance and disappearance of the sun might
very naturally be expressed by a myth of his death and resurrection; and
writers who regard Osiris as the sun are careful to indicate that it is the di
urnal, and not the annual course of the sun to which they understand the
myth to apply. Thus, Renouf, who identifies Osiris with the sun, admitted
the Egyptian sun could not, with any show of reason, be described as dead
in winter. But if his daily death was the theme of the legend, why was it
celebrated by an annual ceremony? This fact alone seems fatal to the inter
pretation of the myth as descriptive of sunset and sunrise. Again, though
the sun may be said to die daily, in what sense can he be said to be torn in
pieces? (87, p. 384)
The objections to identifying Osiris with the sun, which were urged
by Frazer, have been ably criticized by the Right Honorable John M.
Robertson, whom we cite:
Rightly intent on establishing a hitherto ill developed principle of mytho
logical interpretation, the cult of the vegetation spirit, Dr. Frazer has
unduly ignored the conjunction seen deductively to be inevitable and inductively to be normal between the concept of the vegetation god and that



no MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
of others, in particular, the sun god. He becomes for once vigorously po
lemical in his attack on the thesis that Osiris was a sun god, as if that were
excluded once for all by proving him a vegetation god. The answer is that
he was both; and that such a synthesis was inevitable. Mithra, who, so far as
the records go, was primordially associated with the sun, and was thereby
named to the last, is mythically born on December 25th, clearly because of
the winter solstice and the rising of the constellation of the Virgin above
the horizon. Dionysus and Adonis, Dr. Frazer shows, are vegetation gods. Yet they too are both born on December 25th as was the Babe Sun God Horus, who, however, was exhibited as rising from a lotus plant. Now, why
should the vegetation god be born at the winter solstice save as having been
identified with the sun god? (208, p. 3 3)
In studying the mythology and religion of ancient Egypt, it becomes
apparent to any reader that he or she is contemplating a system of be
liefs and rites based on the science of astronomy. The priests of ancient
Egypt possessed a profound knowledge of astronomy, and this fact has
been recognized by eminent modern students of the subject. To illus
trate this point, we cite Gerald Massey:
Osiris as light giver in the moon was torn in fourteen pieces during the
latter half of the lunation by the evil Sut (Set), the opposing power of dark
ness. He was put together again and reconstituted by his son, beloved
Horus, the young solar god. This representation could not have been made
until it was known that the lunar light was replenished monthly from the
solar source. Then Horus, as the sun god and the vanquisher of Sut, the
power of darkness, could be called the reconstituter of Osiris in the moon.
(163, p. 187)
One of Massey 's friends, the late George St. Clair, noted: "The
myths of Egypt are all related to one another, and are neither separate
fables nor idle fancies. They reveal an astro-religious system, and tell a
true story of astronomical progress, calendar correction, and theological
changes before the time of our written histories." (219, p. vii)



CHAPTER SEVEN
Myths and Mysteries: Pagan and Christian
SINCE this chapter will discuss myths and mysteries, a few words of
explanation are called for. First, what is a myth? We may say that a
myth is a form of tradition from a Latin word meaning that which is
handed down. Myth is often confused with folklore and legend, which
are also types of tradition. The following definitions of these branches
of traditional lore are those of Lewis Spence, Fellow of the Royal An
thropological Institute of Great Britain and Ireland:
(1) Myth. A myth is an account of the deeds of a god or supernatural
being, usually expressed in terms of primitive thought. It is an attempt to
explain the relations of man to the universe and it has for those who recount
it a predominantly religious value; or it may have risen to explain the exist
ence of some social organization, a custom, or the peculiarities of an envi
ronment.
(2) Folklore. Folklore means the study of survival of early custom, belief,
narrative, and art.
(3) Legend. A story, generally of r?al places, often (though not necessar
ily) of real persons, handed down by tradition. (236, pp. 1 1-12)
Myths are closely connected with the ancient ceremonial rites which
are sometimes called the ancient mysteries. These mysteries were mag
ico-religious dramatic performances, and are sometimes called Mys
tery-Dramas or Passion Plays. In the words of Lord Raglan: "Myth
and ritual are complementary; ritual is a magic drama to which myth is
the book of the words which often survives after the drama has ceased
to be performed." (195, p. 106) These ancient mysteries conveyed
their message by three forms of teaching: (1) things said, (2) things
shown, and (3) things performed or acted. The things said were the
myths. The things shown were symbols. The things performed or
111



112 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
acted were rites. So we see that these ancient mystery-plays were based
on mythology, symbolism, and ritualism.
The mythology of the Osirian cult was discussed in our previous
chapter. Now it is necessary to say a few words about the ritual and
symbolism of the worship of Osiris and Isis. As we have hitherto stated,
there is a voluminous literature on the magic and religion of ancient
Egypt, but the best short account of the Osirian rites, so far as we
know, was written by the late Robert Brown, whom we quote:
The Egyptian Mysteries of Osiris and Isis were in the form of a mystic
drama, representing the death by violence of Osiris (the sun god), the
search for his body by Isis, the moon, and its finding and being raised to life
and power again. In the celebration of these mysteries the neophyte was made to perform all the mysterious wanderings of the goddess amid the
most frightful scenes. He was guided by one of the initiated, who wore a
mask representing a dog's head, in allusion to the bright star Sothis (Sirius
or the dog star), so called because the rising of that star each year above the
horizon just before day gave warning of the approaching inundation of the
Nile. The word Sothis means the barker or monitor.
The candidate was by this guide conducted through a dark and mysteri
ous labyrinth. With much pain he struggled through involved paths, over
horrid chasms, in darkness and terror. At length he arrived at a stream of water which he was directed to pass. Suddenly, however, he was assaulted
and arrested by three men, disguised in grotesque forms, who, taking a cup
of water from the stream, forced the terrified candidate to first drink of it.
This was the water of forgetfulness, by drinking which all his former
crimes were to be forgotten, and his mind prepared to receive new instruc
tions of virtue and truth.
The attack of Typhon, or the spirit of darkness, typical of the evil powers
of nature, upon Osiris, who is slain, was also enacted as the initiation pro
gressed, and amid the most terrible scenes, during which the judgment of
the dead was also represented, and the punishments of the wicked exhibited
as realities to the candidate. The search for the body of Osiris, which was
concealed in the mysterious chest or ark, followed. The mutilated remains
were at last found, and deposited amid loud cries of sorrow and despair.
The initiation closed with the return of Osiris to life and power. The can
didate now beheld, amid effulgent beams of light, the joyful mansions of the
blessed and the resplendent plains of paradise. . . .
At this stage of the initiation, all was life, light, and joy. The candidate
was himself figuratively considered to have risen to a new and more perfect
life. The past was dead, with all its crimes and unhappiness. Henceforth,
the candidate was under the special protection of Isis, to whose service he dedicated his new life.



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 113
The sublime mysteries of religion and the profoundest teaching of
science were now revealed to him, and satisfied his thirst for knowledge,
while the possession of power as one of the hierarchy gratified his ambition.
The mysteries of all the other nations of antiquity were quite similar to
those of Egypt, and were no doubt derived from them. (27, pp. 10-11)
The Osirian mysteries were celebrated in various seasonal ceremo
nies. There was a harvest festival that began on the first day of the
ninth month (Pachons), when Ernutet (the goddess of the granary)
gave birth to the god Nepri (the corn god), and when the king
marched in solemn procession to the temple; and, after libations and in
cense were offered to the god, his image was carried in procession im
mediately behind the sovereign, who was preceded by a white bull,
supposedly an incarnation of Min (the god of sexual reproduction),
who was regarded as another form of Amen. The climax of this harvest
festival was the ritual reaping of a sheaf of corn by the pharaoh, who is
said to have performed this service for his father. The king was in this
instance acting in the place of Horus, as the son of Osiris. Min in the
ritual (The Book of the Dead) is identified with Horus, as the champion
of his father, and also with Amen, a cosmic god of Thebes, who, in
turn, was identified with Ra of Heliopolis. This ancient ceremony was
enacted annually at Koptos, where the pharaoh impersonated Horus
and walked in the procession between the sacred bull and the image of
Min.
There was another great festival of Osiris, held at Sais in the month
of Athyr. According to Plutarch, Osiris was slain on the 17th day of
the month Athyr, and the Egyptians then mourned his passing for four
days following the event. Since Plutarch used the Alexandrian calendar
for dating, the four days of mourning for Osiris took place on the 1 3th,
14th, 1 5th, and 16th of November. At this time of year, the level of the
Nile was falling, and the north winds were subsiding. The nights were
growing longer, and leaves were falling from the trees. During the
four-day ritual an image of Isis in the form of a cow wrapped in a black
pall was exhibited. On the 19th day of Athyr, the people went to the
shore of the sea, with priests carrying a shrine containing a golden cas
ket. After the priests poured fresh water into the casket, a multitude of
spectators shouted that Osiris had been found. These ceremonies rep
resented dramatically, first, the search for the dead body of Osiris; sec
ond, its joyful discovery, followed by the resurrection of Osiris from
the dead.



114 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Another cycle of Osirian mysteries was celebrated all over Egypt in
the month of Khoiak, which immediately followed Athyr. These rites
lasted eighteen days from the 12th to the 30th of Khoiak. Images were
made of Osiris, and then these were cast in pure gold, which repre
sented the god as a mummy wearing the white crown of Upper Egypt.
The festival opened on the 12th of Khoiak with a ceremony of plowing
and sowing. The plow, pulled by two black cows, was made of the
wood of a tamarisk, and the share was of black copper. Seed was scat
tered by a boy, who sowed one end of the field with barley, the other
end with spelt, and the middle with flax. During this operation the chief
celebrant recited the ritual chapter of the sowing of the fields. These rites
were enacted in the presence of the cow goddess Shenty, represented
by the image of a cow made of gilt sycamore wood.
On the twenty-second of Khoiak, at the eighth hour [we learn from
Frazer], the images of Osiris, attended by thirty-four images of deities, per
formed a mysterious voyage in thirty-four tiny boats made of papyrus,
which were illuminated by three hundred and sixty-five lights. On the
twenty-fourth of Khoiak, after sunset, the effigy of Osiris in a coffin of
mulberry wood was laid in the grave, and at the ninth hour of the night, the
effigy which had been made and deposited the year before, was removed
and placed upon boughs of sycamore. Lastly, on the thirtieth day of Khoiak,
they repaired to the holy sepulchre, a subterranean chamber over which ap
pears to have grown a clump of Persea trees. Entering the vault by the
western door they laid the coffined effigy of the dead god reverently on a
bed of sand in the chamber. So they left him to his rest, and departed from
the sepulchre by the eastern door.
In the foregoing account of the festival, drawn from the great inscription
of Denderah, the burial of Osiris figures prominently, while his resurrection
is implied rather than expressed. This defect of the document, however, is
amply compensated by a remarkable series of bas reliefs which accompany
and illustrate the inscription. (89, pp. 403^)
The final rites, on the 30th of Khoiak, we are informed by Dr. A. H.
Gardiner, represented l incontestably the day of the resurrection of
Osiris," when "the dead king was recalled in the tomb to a semblance
of his former life." (Journal of Egyptian Archaeology, Vol. II, p. 123,
1915) Professor E. O. James regards Dr. Gardiner's opinion as quite sound and makes the following commentary:
This contention is supported by the bas reliefs on the walls of the temple
where Osiris is represented as a mummy, ithyphallic and bearded, lying on



MYTHS AND MYSTKR1ES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 115
a bier at which stand various deities, Anubis, Isis and Nephthys; Hathor the cow goddess and manifestation of the Mother goddess and her brother Heger, together with the frog goddess who probably was a symbol of resurrection. In the twentieth scene, two hawks hover over the body and feet of Osiris and in the twenty-second scene, he is shown wearing the white crown of Upper Egypt with plumes, holding in his hand the sceptre and
flail, in process of raising himself up on his knees. Finally, in the last relief,
he appears as rising up out of his chest with Isis behind him stretching out her wings, while a male bearded god holds before him the crux ansata, or symbol of life. (124, pp. 108-9)
Among the Greek mysteries, the most famous were those celebrated
at Eleusis at five-year intervals. These rites, known as the Eleusinian
Mysteries, were later established among the Romans by the Emperor
Hadrian. A magnificent temple of vast proportions was erected at El
eusis, and the celebration of the mysteries was an impressive and spec
tacular affair. The most important officers who conducted the ceremo
nies were (1) the Hierophant, (2) the Torch Bearer, (3) the Priest, (4) the Archon or king, and (5) the Mystagogue. The Hierophant sat on a splendid throne embellished with gold. He
was attired in royal robes. Over his head was a multicolored picture of
the rainbow, and nearby were images of the moon and seven stars. Around his neck was a chain attached to a golden globe. The Hiero
phant represented the sun. Before him stood twenty-four attendants
clad in white robes and wearing crowns of gold, and these represented
the twenty-four ancient constellations of the upper hemisphere. Around him burned seven bright lights denoting the seven planets of
the ancients. The Torch Bearer, who led the procession representing the wanderings of Rhea in search of the lost god, Bacchus, was em
blematic of the light of the moon. The Priest officiated at the altar, and
wore on his costume a symbol of the moon, since, like the Egyptian
priests of Isis, he was dedicated to the service of the lunar deity. The
Archon preserved order and offered prayers and sacrifices. The poten
tial initiate was qualified by a probationary period, in which he prac
ticed fasting, prayer, chastity, and penitence. Then, after being clad in
sacred garments, crowned with myrtle, and blindfolded, he was turned
over to the Mystagogue, who started the initiation with the following
proclamation: "Depart hence, all ye profane!" The aspirant then started on his arduous pilgrimage, w hich was very
much like the ritual we have already discussed in connection with the
ceremonial of the Osirian cult. To relate them here would be redun
dant; hence they are omitted.



116 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The purpose of this chapter is to make a comparative study of pagan
and Christian creeds, and the associated mythology and ritualism of
those systems. A few good books have been written along these lines,
but they are not known to the general public. Professor E. O. James
has written a scholarly work entitled Christian Myth and Ritual: A His
torical Study (123). This book is based on lectures delivered at the Uni
versities of London and Oxford, and hence appeals mainly to an audi
ence of scholars. Another work of similar nature is Myth and Ritual in
Christianity by Professor Alan W. Watts (261). Professor Watts states
in the prologue:
A book on Christian Mythology has not, I believe, been written before.
There are some sound reasons for this omission, for the subject is one of ex
treme delicacy and complexity, not because of the actual material, but be
cause the whole problem is, in a very special way, touchy. There are ex
treme differences of violently held opinion about Christianity itself—both
as to what it is, and as to whether or not it is a good thing. Similarly, there
are rather wide differences as to the nature and value of mythology, which
has only recently become a subject of serious study. But when one takes the
two together, one is doing something best expressed by the colloquialism
sticking one's neck out—and sticking it out very far. (261, p. 5)
The above work by Professor Watts also will appeal mainly to
scholars and students. We believe that the lay reader also has an inter
est in these subjects and that a popular presentation is in order. So, to
start off, we propose to say a few words about the cardinal doctrines of
the Christian religion, and then to compare them with similar ideas of
the ancient pagans.
The cardinal doctrines of the Christian religion are (1) the Fall of
Man and (2) the Atonement. The liberal Christian apologists no
longer subscribe to a literal belief in the Fall of Man, for they relegated
Adam and Eve to the realm of mythology. These liberals are opposed
by the orthodox apologists, who maintain the position that belief in the
Atonement implies belief in the Fall of Man. Logic, to us, seems to be
on the orthodox side, as has been so clearly expressed by T. W. Doane:
These two dogmas cannot be separated from each other. If there was no fall, there is no need of an atonement, and no redeemer is required. Those,
then, who consent in recognizing in Christ Jesus a god and redeemer and
who, notwithstanding, cannot resolve upon admitting the story of the Fall
of Man to be historical, should exculpate themselves from the reproach of
inconsistency. (69, p. 17)



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN \\7
Anyone familiar with the historical criticism of the Bible knows that
there are two stories concerning the creation and fall of man in the
book of Genesis. The first or priestly account was written in the fifth
century B.C. and extends from the beginning of Genesis through verse
3 of Chapter II. The second or Jehovistk account begins with verse 4
of Chapter II and extends through Chapter 111, and this version of the
story was written in the eighth century B.C. (It is of interest to note
that the second narrative antedates the first by three hundred years.)
Assyrian terra cotta tablets discovered in the ruins of Nineveh were
examined by George Smith, an Orientalist of the British Museum.
These tablets date from 1 500 to 2000 B.C. and contain stories of the
Creation of Man, the Deluge, and the Tower of Babel. In reference to
an engraving on an Assyrian cylinder, Smith noted: "One striking and
important specimen of early type in the British Museum collection has
two figures sitting one on each side of a tree, holding out their hands to
the fruit, while at the back of one (the woman) is scratched a serpent.
. . . Thus it is evident that a form of the story of the Fall, similar to
that of Genesis, was known in early times in Babylonia." (233, p. 91)
In the original Babylonian Eden myth, as translated by Professor
Edward Chiera, there is the story of a great conflict among the gods,
who cannot decide whether man should be created or not. The opposi
tion to the creation of man was led by a female reptile, the dragon Ti
amat, and this brought against her the hostility of the great god Bel,
who favored the creation of the human race; so the dragon and the god
engaged in hostilities. Finally, Bel overcame Tiamat by blasting her
with thunderbolts; and, the opposition having been crushed, man was
created.
This conflict between Bel and the Dragon bears a close analogy to
the story of the revolution in heaven as recorded in the Apocalypse:
"And there was war in heaven. Michael and his angels fought against
the dragon and the dragon fought and his angels, and prevailed not;
neither was their place found anymore in heaven. And the great
Dragon was cast out, that old serpent, called the Devil, and Satan,
which deceiveth the whole world; he was cast out into the earth, and
his angels were cast out with him." (Revelations, XII, 7-9)
We have a variant of this myth in the story of Saint George and the
Dragon. This fable has been traced back to ancient Egypt. Saint
George, patron saint of England, was originally an Egyptian god,
Horus, and the dragon was the Egyptian devil Set. The struggle be



118 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
tween Horus and Set was an important feature of Egyptian mythology.
Set represented the destructive forces of nature. He was the setting of
the sun, the waning of the moon, and the decrease of the waters of the
river Nile. He was depicted as the left or black eye of the waning sun,
ruling over the year from the summer to the winter solstice, while his
diametrical opposite was the right or bright eye of Horus, the waxing
sun, symbolic of the growth of life and the increase of light from the
winter to the summer solstice.
The hypothetical first man of the Bible is rightly named Adam.
Now the word Adam means man, and we learn from the sacred anthol
ogy that Adam (man) was made out of adamah (ground or earth). Sim
ilarly, in ancient Rome, the first man was called Homo, because he was
reputedly made from humus (the earth). According to an ancient
Egyptian myth, Khnumu, the Father of the Gods, molded the earliest
men out of clay on a potter's wheel. A similar tale is told among the
modern Shilluks of the White Nile. The creator, Juok, wanders over
the world creating men out of earths of different colors. In the land of
the Shilluks, in East Africa, he found black earth and shaped black men
from it; while in Egypt he shaped dark brown men out of the mud of
the Nile; where he found yellow earth he fashioned yellow men, and
wherever he found white earth, he made white men. The Chaldean
historian, Berosus, recorded that the great god Bel decapitated himself
and that the other gods mixed his blood with clay and out of it molded
the first man. In Greek mythology, Prometheus is pictured as manu
facturing the first men from clay at Panopeus.
The myths of the Fall are based on the attempts of primitive man to
explain the origin of death. Due to the habit of certain species of snakes
of periodically shedding their skins, our savage forerunners got the idea
that serpents were immortal; so the natural vanity of man persuaded
our distant ancestors that the gods had intended the gift of eternal life
for humanity alone, and that the crafty reptile had stolen this precious
prize from the human race. The Biblical version of the Fall of Man is
incomplete. The role of the serpent is not explained, and the Tree of
Life is not given due prominence in the story. The original narrative,
which we are able to piece together from the mythology of many
lands, reads somewhat as follows: God placed the first man and woman
in a delightful garden, and in this garden were two trees: the Tree of Life and the Tree of Death (the Biblical Tree of Knowledge). The
first human couple had the choice of eating of the fruit of the Tree of
Life and of becoming immortal, or of partaking of the fruit of the Tree



MYTHS AND MYSTKR1KS: PAGAN AND CHRIS! IAN 119
of Dearh and, hence, becoming mortal. The serpent was sent by God
to tell Adam and Eve to eat of the fruit of the Tree of Life so that they
might live forever; and to warn them against tasting the fruit of the
Tree of Death for, should they dine on this forbidden fruit they would
surely die, and the curse of mortality would descend to their children
from generation to generation. The wily reptile, however, reversed the
message and told the first human pair that they would obtain immortal
ity by eating of the fruit of the Tree of Death. Unfortunately, Adam
and Eve believed the message of the diabolical snake, and ate the for
bidden fruit. As a consequence, they were expelled from Eden and, at
the same time, became mortal. The old serpent, on the other hand,
helped himself to the fruit of the Tree of Life and, hence, gained im
mortality for himself and for his kind. Myths told by savages to explain
the origin of death are of three types: (1) the story of the Perverted
Message; (2) the story of the Cast Skin; (3) the composite story of the
Perverted Message and the Cast Skin.
In the southern part of Africa, the Hottentots connect the phases of
the moon with the idea of immortality. They consider the rising of the
moon as its birth and the setting of the lunar orb as its death. After each
death, there is a new birth, the waxing moon manifesting growth, and
its waning, decay. In days of old, say the Hottentot mythologists, the
moon desired to confer the boon of immortality on the race of man,
and the hare volunteered to bring the glad tidings to earth. So the
moon god instructed the hare to deliver the following message to man
kind: "As I die and rise to life again, so shall you die and rise to life
again.
The hare made the journey, but on account of a bad memory, or just
plain cussedness, he reversed the message, saying: "As I die and do not
rise to life again, so you also shall die and not rise to life again." When
the moon learned of the hare's error, he became angry and threw a
stick at him. The stick struck the hare and split his lip and that is why,
to this day, the hare is afflicted with a cloven lip. By garbling the
moon's message, the hare robbed man of the gift of immortality, and
the Hottentots are still angry with him for that reason. As a result,
when a youth of the tribe comes of age and assumes his station among
men, he is forbidden to partake of the flesh of the hare.
Among the Nandi of East Africa, a popular myth relates the origin
of death to the mean disposition of a certain dog. In the time of the first
men, a dog trotted up one day and delivered the following message:
"All people will die like the moon but, unlike the moon, you will not



120 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
return to life again unless you give me some milk to drink out of your
gourd, and beer to drink through your straw. If you do this, I will ar
range for you to go to the river when you die, and to come to life again
on the third day." The first men laughed at the dog and gave him some
milk and beer to drink off a stool. The dog, angered at not being served
the drink in the same vessels as his human contemporaries, then an
nounced: "All people will die, and the moon alone will return to life."
So the ill-tempered dog, presumably the messenger of the moon,
doomed the human race to mortality.
In some of the myths of the origin of death, two messengers are in
volved. Such a tale is told among the Masarwas, who dwell in Bot
swana, the Kalahari Desert, and parts of Southern Rhodesia. In the
olden days, the moon wished to send a message to men. The moon
called the tortoise and said to him: "Go over to those men there, and
give them this message from me. Tell them that, as 1 dying live, so they
dying will live again." But the tortoise, besides being a slow traveler,
had a bad memory, so the moon lost patience with him and summoned
the hare. To the hare, the moon said: "You are a swift runner. Take
this message to the men over yonder: \As 1 dying live again, so you
dying will live again/ " But the hare was also afflicted with a defective
memory. She tore off with great celerity and promptly forgot the mes
sage. On reaching the vicinity of the men, the hare delivered the
moon's message thusly: "As 1 dying live again, so you dying will die
forever." Eventually, the tortoise arrived, and, despite his bad memory,
delivered the right message; but alas, he was too late.
Stories of the perverted message are quite common in the simpler
cultures of Africa, and are based on a widespread belief that the gods
intended to make man immortal, but the benevolent plan came to
naught due to error or malice on the part of the messenger chosen to
deliver the good news. Many savage tribesmen, in both the eastern and
western hemispheres, regard lizards, serpents, and beetles as immortal.
They observe that these animals periodically cast their skins and natu
rally infer that the same animals renew their youth perpetually, and
hence never die. So myths were framed to explain how certain animals
attained immortality, and why men lost, or failed to gain, life everlast
ing.
The Wafipa or Wabende of East Africa tell of how God visited the
earth and asked the following questions of all living creatures: "Who
wishes not to die?" Man and all the other animals, except the serpent,
were asleep. The serpent promptly answered, "I do"; so that is why



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 121
man and the other animals die and the snake does not. The belief is that
the serpent expires only when he is killed; for every year he changes
his skin and, hence, renews his youth endlessly. We have been told by
the Arawaks of British Guiana that the creator once came down to the
world to see how mankind was getting along, but men were so evil that
they tried to kill the creator. Hence they were denied the gift of eter
nal life, which was, instead, conferred on those animals which renew
their skins, such as snakes, lizards, and beetles.
The belief among primitive men is widespread that, in the early days
of the world, men were immortal because they possessed the ability to
periodically cast their skins. Among other aborigines, the belief in im
mortality has been traced to the doctrine of lunar sympathy; for it was
thought that men passed through states of growth and decay, and of life
and death, corresponding in a sense to the phases of the moon. Men
died but were thought after three days to arise from the grave to a new
life, this belief being based on the three-day interval between disap
pearance of the old moon and the appearance of the new one. In a
myth current among the Caroline Islanders, we learn that men in the
good old days did not die; or, more precisely, although they did die, on
the last day of the waning moon, they again came to life on the appear
ance of the waxing moon; but an evil spirit intervened and so arranged
things that, when men died, they remained dead. A similar story is
popularly related among the Upotos of the Congo, who believe that one day Libanza (God) summoned the people of the moon and the
earth to a conference. The moon people showed up on time, whereas
the earth dwellers arrived very late. So Libanza, addressing the inhabit
ants of the moon, said: "Because thou came to me at once, when I
called thee, thou shalt never die. Thou shalt be dead for but two days
each month and that only to rest thee; and thou shalt return with
greater splendor." When the tardy earthlings finally arrived, Libanza
became angry and said to them: "Because you came not at once to me
when I called you, therefore, you will die one day and not revive ex
cept to come to me." In some primitive myths concerning the origin of death, there is a
blend of the stories of the perverted message and the cast skin. Such a tale is told in Annam, where a legendary god named Ngoc sent a cour
ier from heaven to tell men that when they became old they should
change their skins and live forever, but that serpents on growing old
would die. When the heaven-sent messenger arrived on earth, he an
nounced: "When man is old, he shall cast his skin; but when serpents



") 7 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
arc old they shall die and be laid in coffins. " A nearby brood of snakes
first heard the announcement and threatened the messenger with vio
lence. "You must say it over again, " the reptiles insisted, "and just the
contrary or we will bite you." The frightened courier restated his mes
sage as follows: "When the serpent is old, he shall cast his skin; but
when man is old, he shall die and be laid in the coffin." And that is why
all animals die except the serpent, who, when old, casts his skin and
lives forever.
Among the Gallas of East Africa, there dwells a bird called the Hala
waka (the Sheep of God). This bird is recognized by its dark blue or
black color, with a white patch on each wing and a crest on its head. It
perches in treetops and emits wailing sounds somewhat like the bleat
ing of sheep. In days of old, we are told, God sent this bird to tell men
that they should not die; but that on growing old they would slip off
their old skins and renew their youth. And God even gave the bird a
crest to serve as a badge identifying him as a heavenly ambassador. The
bird started on his journey, but on the way observed a snake dining on
carrion. Stopping, the avian addressed the reptile: "Give me some of
the meat and blood and 1 will tell you God's message.' 1
The snake replied, "I don't want to hear it." But the bird was insist
ent, and the serpent reluctantly agreed to his proposition.
"The message is this," the reptile was told. "When men grow old
they will die, but when you grow old, you will cast your skin and
renew your youth." And that is why men and women grow old and
die, and serpents crawl out of their old skins and enjov a renewal of
life.
The myths of the origin of death have their greatest concentration in
the continent of Africa, and it is the opinion of Sir J. G. Frazer that the
Hebrew version of the myth was probably of African origin.
Even if the story should hereafter be found in a Sumerian version [Frazer
held], this would not absolutely exclude the hypothesis of its African origin,
since the original home of the Sumerians is unknown. . . . In favor of an
African origin of the myth, it may be observed that the explanation of the
supposed immortality of serpents, which probably formed the kernel of the
story in its original form, has been preserved in several African versions,
while it has been wholly lost in the Hebrew version; from which it is natu
ral to infer that the African versions are older and nearer to the original
than the corresponding but incomplete narrative in Genesis. (91, pp. 223
24)



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 123
The New Testament of the Christian Bible contains the alleged bi
ography of a virgin-born savior of the human race. The Christians, in
this case, as in many others, were anticipated by the pagans; for virgin
born gods who sacrificed themselves for the good of the race were quite
common in the myths and legends of the heathen nations of antiquity.
The Reverend Charles H. Vail, in a scholarly study, The World's Sav
iors, records the stories of miraculous births of fifteen other saviors, who
lived before the Christian era. The names of these world's saviors are
Krishna of India, Gautama of India, Horus of Egypt, Tammuz of
Babylonia, Mithra of Persia, Zoroaster of Persia, Quetzalcoatl of Mex
ico, Bacab of Yucatan, Huetzilopochtli of Mexico, Freyr of Scandina
via, Attis of Phrygia, Bacchus of Greece, Adonis of Syria, Yu of
China, Jesus.
"The belief in miraculous births was common to antiquity," the
Reverend Vail noted. "Nearly every nation had a festival at or near the
25th of December in honor of the birth of a Divine Being. These incar
nations were generally referred to as immaculate conceptions and the
persons thus born were called saviors. The advent of the saviors here
mentioned preceded the Christian Era, some of them by thousands of
years." (255, p. 33)
Gods and heroes, born of virgins, were quite common in olden
times, and the source of most, if not all of these divinities, seems to have
been Egypt. Rhys has an instructive discourse on this fact:
Horus was said to be the parthenogenetic child of the Virgin Mother,
Isis. In the catacombs of Rome, black statues of this Egyptian divine
Mother and Infant still survive from the early Christian worship of the Vir
gin and Child to which they were converted. In these the Virgin Mary is
represented as a black Negress and often, with the face veiled in the true
Isis fashion. . . . Statues of the goddess Isis with the child Horus in her
arms were common in Egypt, and were exported to all neighboring and to
many remote countries, where they are still to be found with new names
attached to them —Christian in Europe, Buddhist in Turkestan, Taoist in
China and Japan. Figures of the Virgin Isis do duty as representations of Mary, of Hariti, of Kuan-Yin, of Kwannon, and of other Virgin Mothers
of Gods. (206, pp. 115-16)
The birth of the sun was personified in ancient Egypt as Horus, the
son of Osiris and Isis, the god of light and the savior of the world. On
the inner walls of the holy of holies in the temple of Luxor, inscribed
about two thousand years B.C. by King Amenhotep III, the birth of



124 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Horus is pictured in four scenes, strikingly like the Christian represen
tations of the Annunciation and the Immaculate Conception of the
Virgin Mary and the Birth and Adoration of the Christ Child. These
four consecutive scenes as engraved on the walls of the temple of Luxor
are reproduced in Gerald Massey's Ancient Egypt (163, Vol. II, p.
757), and may be described as follows:
1. The Annunciation: The god Thoth is shown announcing to the
virgin the fact that she is about to give birth to a son.
2. The Immaculate Conception: The god Kneph (the Holy Spirit)
and the goddess Hathor are shown mystically impregnating the virgin
by holding crosses (the symbol of life) to the head and nostrils of the
mother-to-be.
3. The Birth of the Child God: The mother is seated on the mid
wife's stool and the newborn infant is held by attendants.
4. The Adoration: The infant Horus is displayed as receiving hom
age from gods and men, including the three kings or magi who are ten
dering him gifts. In this scene, the symbol of the cross again appears.
"In this picture," the Egyptologist, Samuel Sharpe, noticed, "we
have the Annunciation, the Conception, the Birth and the Adoration as
described in the first and second chapters of Luke's Gospel; and as we
have historical assurance that the chapters in Matthew's Gospel which
contain the miraculous birth of Christ are after additions not in the ear
liest manuscripts, it seems probable that these two poetical chapters in
Luke may also be unhistorical and borrowed from the Egyptian ac
counts of the miraculous births of their kings." (225, p. 19)
Herodotus has transmitted to us an account of a festival that was cel
ebrated annually in ancient Egypt to commemorate the descent into
Hades of King Rhampsinitus and his return from the underworld back
to earth. A feature of this ritual, which was apparently connected with
the Osirian cult, was the enveloping of a priest in a shroud by two
other priests, disguised as wolves. The two wolflike characters con
ducted the shrouded one to a temple of Isis outside the city and there
left him. The two priests in wolves' clothing, who represented the two
divine guides of the dead, later returned and led the priest in the
shroud, who enacted the role of King Rhampsinitus, back to the city.
On his return, the mantled priest brought with him a napkin, suppos
edly given to him by the goddess Isis in Hades. It seems that incidents
from this Egyptian ceremony were incorporated into the Gospel of
Saint John, for we read there of visitors to the tomb of Jesus beholding
a napkin and a shroud. They also saw two celestial beings (angels).



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 125
A learned scholar from Scotland, Arthur Findlay, published in 1939
an unusual book with the title The Psychic Stream: The Source and
Growth of the Christian Faith. It is a large book, 1,200 pages in all, but
the reading of it is easy, despite the length. Findlay devotes considera
ble space to what he calls the Savior-God Religions, and he asserts: "At
least thirty savior-gods are known to history who were believed by
their followers to have suffered death as a sacrifice for sin and who
were elevated by them to be god-men on earth and saviors and media
tors in heaven." (79, p. 305) Out of the thirty savior-gods, Arthur
Findlay selected the five which he considered to be the most important
of the lot for extensive discussion. The names of these ancient gods are
Bel of Babylonia, Osiris of Egypt, Prometheus of Greece, Mithra of
Persia, Krishna of India.
The story of the savior-god Bel of Babylonia is of great value to stu
dents of comparative religion, for here we have a documentary source
of unquestioned authenticity in the form of an ancient cuneiform tab
let, now in the British Museum. Findlay secured an English translation
of this ancient record, which we will shortly reproduce.
The beliefs surrounding Bel [Findlay declares] were similar to those
which came, at a later date, to surround Jesus. This we know because there has been discovered engraved on baked clay the program of the drama of
the arrest, trial, death and resurrection of Bel which was in use at the time when Bel was worshipped in Babylon. This clay tablet is accepted by Bab
ylonian scholars as the program used by the priests at the drama depicting
the death scenes of the life of Bel. Its discovery was made by Professor
Zimmern and this is of comparatively recent date. When I called to discuss
it with the curator of the Babylonian Section of the British Museum, I
found that the translation I shall give is accepted as a list of parallel instances
found both in the story of Bel and of the Christ. Zimmern deduced the inci
dents of the story of Bel from ritual texts which seem to describe a primi
tive kind of religious play. . . . The tablet is believed to have been pro
duced about two thousand years before the Christian era, and it was
evidently used by the priest who acted as the announcer at the drama. He would have the tablet before him and before each scene would announce
what was about to take place. (79, pp. 220-21)
The English translation of the cuneiform record referred to above is
reproduced here: (1) Bel is taken prisoner; (2) Bel is tried in the Hall
of Justice; (3) Bel is smitten; (4) Bel is led away to the mount; (5) with
Bel are taken two malefactors, one of whom is released; (6) after Bel
has gone to the mount the city breaks out into tumult; (7) Bel's clothes



126 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
arc carried away; (8) Bel goes down into the mount and disappears
from life; (9) a weeping woman seeks him at the gate of burial; (10)
Bel is brought back to life.
We can look backward four thousand years and picture what hap
pened among the Bel worshipers of Babylon. In Findlay's words: "We
can imagine them sitting in a great amphitheater, such a one as was so
common in those days. The stage was before them and its roof was the
dome of heaven. Thousands of people could thus be accommodated
and, from the program, it is evident that it was situated on the side of a
small hill. It was in all probability built at the foot of one of the slopes
of this hill, which curved inwards. On the top of the hill may have been
a grove." (79, p. 223)
Before the multitude assembled at the theater, the actors took their
places on the stage. The service would be started by the singing of a
psalm similar to the Psalms of David in the Old Testament. Following
one or more psalms, a priest would recite the appropriate prayer for the
occasion. Then the announcer, holding a copy of the program, would
arise and read out in a loud clear voice: "Scene 1 —Bel is taken pris
oner."
An actor representing Bel, the Babylonian Christ, was seen on the
stage. Other actors dressed as soldiers would arrest the savior-god. As
the prisoner was led away by the squad of soldiers, the announcer again
rose up and called out: "Scene II — Bel is tried in the Hall of Justice."
At this point the scene of a trial was enacted. A judge was present,
and witnesses .testified for and against the prisoner, who was found in
nocent but sentenced to death anyway as in the similar case of Jesus in
the Gospels. After the sentencing of the victim, the next scene was
called out: "Scene III — Bel is smitten."
This scene showed the jeering at and the baiting of the prisoner after
the sentence of death had been passed. The next announcement would
be: "Scene IV —Bel is led away to the mount."
The actor impersonating the victim was led away by guards to the
sacred grove atop the hill. Then the announcing priest read: "Scene
V —With Bel are taken two malefactors, one of whom is released."
Actors representing the two criminals were seen on the stage and,
after a trial, one was found guilty, the other innocent. The guilty vic
tim was condemned to death and the innocent one released.
Although the death scene of savior Bel was a part of the Babylonian
Mysteries, this was not shown in the amphitheater. This fact is ex
plained by Arthur Findlay as follows:



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 127
The program does not contain a scene of the god's death. This may be because it took place on a hill where he was hung on one of the trees in the
sacred grove, or crucified, or slain on an altar, and so could not be enacted
on the stage. By now, the theater is empty and everyone has climbed to the
top of the hill to witness the death scene. As the actor, taking the place of
Bel, and the one representing the malefactor, are not actually killed, it may
be that the death the savior-god actually suffered was not enacted. This is
unlikely and it is more probable that the tablet which has been found referred only to the performance in the amphitheater, which accounts for the death scene not being included thereon. They were heavy to carry and would not be brought away from the theater. After the scene, when the
two malefactors appeared and one was sent after Bel to be sacrificed, the
people would know that, for the time being, the performance in the theater was over. For that reason, and because the death scene was not taking place
in the theater, it is not engraved on the tablet. (79, p. 227)
After the death scene, the audience would return to the theater and
the announcer would declare: "Scene VI —After Bel has gone to the
mount, the city breaks into tumult."
In this scene the disorderly mob was shown rioting and screaming to
exemplify the tumult that took place in the city.
The next scene was then announced: "Scene VII —Bel's clothes are
carried away."
In this scene Bel's body had been returned from the mount and was
seen on the stage by the multitude. His clothes were removed and his
corpse was prepared for burial. The priest then announced the next
act: "Scene VIII—Bel goes down into the mount and disappears from
life."
The stage being near the side of a hill, a tomb was dug and the body
of Bel was placed therein. The priest officiating at the funeral would
deliver the appropriate eulogy of the dead god. The announcement of
the subsequent scene would be: "Scene IX—A weeping woman seeks
him at the gate of burial." The weeping woman, perhaps the mother, wife, or lover of the dead
savior, added a dramatic touch to this ancient mystery play. Then fol
lowed the climax, when the announcer read: "Scene X — Bel is brought
back to life."
The moving story of what happened in the last act of the Babylonian
Passion Play and its effect on later religions has been vividly recon
structed by Arthur Findlay:
We can imagine the enthusiasm and excitement this announcement would cause. The people rise and there is thunderous noise and shouting.



128 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Then comes a hush and they reseat themselves awaiting in eager expect
ancy the denouement of this great drama. During the silence, the stone
which has been pushed up against the tomb is seen to move and slowly it is
pushed aside. Out of it comes Bel in his burial clothes. As he emerges from
the tomb, the audience rises and shouts in its frenzy till all are hoarse. The
great drama has reached its climax. Their god has re-appeared to them,
death has been conquered, and he has secured for all life in the hereafter. As
the actor could not re-appear as a spirit as did Bel after his sacrifice, the re
appearance had to be a physical one, just as the Christian drama depicts
Jesus having left the tomb as a human being. . . . This great religious serv
ice has never been forgotten. It was copied by the Greeks and is still per
formed in memory of Christ. It has been preserved for us throughout the
Christian era in the Four Gospels. The Christian dramatist made such
changes in the details as were required so that people should believe that it
was an historical event which happened in Jerusalem and that the actors
were those who were believed to have been the disciples of Jesus. (79, pp. 230-31)
We have already discussed the myths and rites of Osiris, but Findlay
has brought to our attention certain facts not discussed at length by the
standard authorities on the history and religion of ancient Egypt.
The Pharaoh Seti I (1450-1395 B.C.), first ruler of the 19th Dy
nasty, caused to be erected at Abydos a fine temple dedicated to the
worship of Osiris. This impressive structure, still standing, contains
chapels, halls, and seven principal sanctuaries. The walls are embel
lished with reliefs in color and are fine examples of the Egyptian sculp
tor's art. Directly behind this temple is situated the Osireon, a large sub
terranean hall, reachable by an underground passage. This under
ground structure was carved out of sandstone, and the roof is supported
by large granite piers. This hall contained an artificial island in the
midst of a body of water. On the island was a hill upon which the sun
god was imagined to stand and who, at sunrise, touched the land with
his life-giving beams. This hill symbolized the resurrection with the
rising sun overcoming the devil of darkness and hence symbolizing the
victory of life over death. Beyond this hall may be seen a sarcophagus,
known as the false tomb. This tomb was originally supposed to be the
burial site of Osiris, but the initiates of Egyptian Mysteries knew that
the body had been interred elsewhere. In the ancient passion play were
scenes of the arrest, trial, death, burial, and resurrection of Osiris. In
one scene the onlookers would observe the body, supposedly that of
Osiris, being buried in the tomb. The next act showed the opening of



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 129
the tomb, which was seen to be empty. The next, and final, scene
showed Osiris again alive, following his resurrection from the dead. In
ancient Egypt, there were two bodies of religious doctrines: the exo
teric, known to the common people, and the esoteric, which was known
only to the sacerdotal caste. The meaning of {he false tomb has been so
well explained by Findlay that we give it in his own words:
So that the people could realize that Osiris had conquered the grave, it
was necessary for the tomb to be empty. It was the only way to impress
them with the fact that the Lord had risen. It was impossible for the audi
ence to see, lying in the tomb, the dead body of Osiris and his spirit-body
standing beside it. So the only way this event could be dramatized was by showing the tomb as empty. This symbolism conveyed to the people the
idea of resurrection from the dead, and it was perpetuated, when this tem
ple was erected, by the empty rock tomb in which it was assumed that the
body of Osiris had been laid. It was there no longer because he had con
quered the grave. We can now understand the mystery of the empty tomb related in the Gospel narratives. The writers made use of the Egyptian symbolism taken
from their passion play and told the story just as it happened on the stage, of
the corpse being put into a rock tomb and not being there when the disci
ples visited the grave. . . . Now that we know that the Gospel record of the empty tomb was probably taken from the Egyptian passion drama, all
our difficulties disappear and what appears to be absurd and impossible be
comes understandable. (79, p. 248)
Another great Pagan Christ was Krishna of India; for it is recorded
in the sacred books of India that Krishna was born of the virgin Devaki;
that his nativity was heralded by a star; and that, though of royal line
age, he was born in a cave.
According to the Apocryphal Gospel, "Protevangelion," a work at
tributed to James, the brother of Jesus, the Christian savior was also
born in a cave.
At the time of Krishna's birth, the cave was mysteriously illumi
nated. Likewise, at the birth of Jesus, there was such a great light in the
cave that the eyes of Joseph and the midwife could not bear it. ("Prote
vangelion," Chapter XIV, Verse 11. See 3, p. 35.)
The infant Krishna spoke to his mother immediately after his birth.
"Jesus spake even when he was in the cradle, and said to his mother:
'Mary, 1 am Jesus, the Son of God, that Word which thou didst bring
forth according to the declaration of the Angel Gabriel to thee, and my
Father hath sent me for the salvation of the world.' " ("The First Gos



130 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
pel of the Infancy," Chapter I, Verses 2 and 3. This Gospel is also in The Apocryphal New Testament [3].)
Krishna was born while his foster father, Nanda, was in the city to
pay his tax to the king.
Jesus was born while his foster father, Joseph, was in the city to pay
his tax to the governor.
King Kansa sought the life of the Indian Christ by ordering the mas
