sacre of all male children born during the same night as was Krishna.
This is a parallel to the story of the slaughter of the innocents or
dered by King Herod as recounted in the Gospel according to Mat
thew, II, 16.
Nanda was warned by a heavenly voice to flee with the infant
Krishna across the Jumna River in order to escape from King Kansa.
Joseph was warned by a voice in a dream to flee into Egypt with the
Christ-child so as to escape from King Herod. In the city Mathura, which was his birthplace, Krishna performed
many miracles.
Jesus, while in Egypt, lived in Matarea, a town near Hermopolis,
and while residing there wrought numerous miracles.
Krishna was the second person of the Hindu Trinity, which consisted of (1) Brahma, (2) Vishnu, and (3) Siva. Krishna was the human
incarnation of Vishnu.
Jesus Christ is considered to be the second person of the Christian
Trinity, according to the Bible.
Krishna is said to have been a crucified Christ. He is pictured in In
dian art as hanging on a cross with arms extended. Dr. Thomas Inman
states: ''Krishna, whose history so closely resembles our Lord's, was
also like him in his being crucified." (Ancient Faiths, Vol. I, p. 41 1, by Thomas Inman, M.D., cited by T. W. Doane in 69, p. 186) Krishna, while hanging on the cross, was pierced by an arrow.
During his crucifixion, Jesus was pierced by a spear.
The light of the sun was blotted out at noon on the day of Krishna's
death.
The sun is said to have been darkened from the sixth to the ninth
hour on the day of the crucifixion of Christ. This darkness of the Pas
sion of Christ called forth a commentary from the historian Edward Gibbon that has become famous, and we append it below for the
thoughtful consideration of the reader:
But how shall we excuse the supine inattention of the Pagan and philo
sophic world to those evidences which were presented by the hand of Om



MYTHS AM) MYSTKRIKS: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 131
nipotence, not ro their reason, but to their senses? During the age of Christ,
of his apostles, and of their first disciples, the doctrine which they preached
was confirmed by innumerable prodigies. The lame walked, the blind saw,
the sick were healed, the dead were raised, demons were expelled, and the
laws of Nature were frequently suspended for the benefit of the church.
But the sages of Greece and Rome turned aside from the awful spectacle
and, pursuing the ordinary occupations of life and study, appeared uncon
scious of any alteration in the moral or physical government of the world.
Under the reign of Tiberius, the whole earth, or at least a celebrated prov
ince of the Roman Empire, was involved in a preternatural darkness of
three hours. Even this miraculous event, which ought to have excited the
wonder, the curiosity, and the devotion of mankind, passed without notice
in an age of science and history. It happened during the life time of Seneca
and the elder Pliny, who must have experienced the immediate effects or
received the earliest intelligence of the prodigy. Each of these philosophers
in a laborious work, has recorded all of the great phenomena of nature,
earthquakes, meteors, comets and eclipses, which his indefatigable curiosity
could collect. Both the one and the other have omitted to mention the
greatest phenomenon to which the mortal eye has been witness since the
creation of the globe. A distinct chapter of Pliny is designed for eclipses of
an extraordinary nature and duration; but he contents himself with describ
ing the singular defect of light which followed the murder of Caesar, when,
during the greatest part of a year, the orb of the sun appeared pale and
without splendor. This season of obscurity, which cannot surely be com
pared with the preternatural darkness of the Passion, had been already cele
brated by most of the poets and historians of that memorable age. (97, pp. 200-2)
Krishna descended into hell to raise the dead before returning to the
abode of the gods; and on his return from the infernal regions he
brought with him two boys, whom he restored to their parents on
earth.
In the New Testament Apocrypha, we read of Jesus Christ descend
ing into hell and leading back to earth a parry of saints, among them
two sons of the High Priest whom the Christ restored to life on earth.
For derails of the story of the Descent into Hell of Jesus Christ, the in
terested reader is referred to the apocryphal "Gospel of Nicodemus."
It is related of Krishna that he rose from the grave and ascended
bodily to the celestial realms in the presence of a multitude of specta
tors.
A similar story is told concerning Jesus Christ in the New Testa
ment.



132 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
In Indian art, Krishna is depicted as a man of black complexion, and
the name Krishna literally means the black.
In early Christian art, Jesus is almost invariably represented as black
skinned. "In the pictures and portraits of Christ by the early Chris
tians," Kersey Graves asserted, "he is uniformly represented as being
black. And to make this the more certain, the red tinge is given to the
lips; and the only text in the Christian Bible, quoted by orthodox
Christians as describing his complexion, represents it as being black.
Solomon's declaration i am black, but comely, Oh ye daughters of Je
rusalem' (Solomon, I, 5), is often cited as referring to Christ. Ac
cording to the Bible itself, then, Jesus Christ was a black man." (101, p.
56) In discussing the black complexion of Krishna, John M. Robertson
noticed, "He thus in the first place comes into line with the black
deities of other faiths, notably the Osiris of Egypt, to say nothing of the
black manifestations of Greek deities and of the Christian Jesus." (208,
p. 146) Robertson then posed the question: "Why, then, is Krishna, in
particular, black?" and gave the following answer:
A reasonable presumption is that he was a demon for the Aryan invaders, as
being a god of the aborigines, who figure generally on the side of the Krish
nas or black demons. Again, if Dr. Frazer be right as to the priority of the
idea of a vegetation god in cults commonly associated with the sun, Krishna
may have been primarily such a god, and as thus associated with the earth,
may have been black—the explanation of Dr. Frazer for the blackness of Demeter and Osiris. Or, he may have been black merely as a god of the
black skinned natives. (208, p. 148)
Among the Christian nations of Europe, even unto our own times,
we notice the prevalence of statues and pictures of the black Christ.
Mithra, a Persian sun god, was virgin-born on December 25th. His
earliest worshipers were shepherds, and he was accompanied in his
travels by twelve companions. The Mithraists recognized a weekly
sabbath day and celebrated the Eucharist by eating wafers embellished
with a cross. The great Mithraic festivals were the Birth (Christmas)
and the Resurrection (Easter). During the Mithraic Easter rites, the
presiding priest announced: "Be of good cheer, sacred band of Initiates,
your god has risen from the dead." (Origine de Tous les Cultes by
Charles F. Dupuis, cited by W. Williamson in 276, p. 57)
When the cult of Mithra was in its prime, a magnificent festival was
celebrated annually atop Mount Orontes. This pomp and pageantry



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 133
were elegantly described by Lydia M. Child in a three-volume work,
The Progress of Religious Ideas Through Successive Ages:
Perhaps no religious festival was ever more splendid than the annual Sal
utation of Mithra, during which forty days were set apart for thanksgiving
and sacrifice. The procession to salute the god formed long before the rising
of the sun. The High Priest was followed by a long train of the Magi, in
spotless white robes, chanting hymns and carrying the sacred fire on silver
censers. Then came three hundred and sixty five youths in scarlet, to repre
sent the days of the year, and the color of fire. These were followed by the
Chariot of the Sun—empty, decorated with garlands, and drawn by superb white horses harnessed with pure gold. Then came a white horse of mag
nificent size, his forehead blazing with gems in honor of Mithra. Close be
hind him rode the king in a chariot of ivory inlaid with gold, followed by his royal kindred in embroidered garments and a long train of nobles, riding
on camels richly caparisoned. This gorgeous retinue, facing the east, slowly ascended Mount Orontes. Arrived at the summit, the High Priest assumed
his tiara, wreathed with myrtle, and hailed the first rays of the rising sun
with incense and prayer. The other Magi gradually joined him in singing
hymns to Ormuzd, the source of all blessing, by whom the radiant Mithra
had been sent to gladden the earth, and preserve the principle of life. Fi
nally, they all joined in one universal chorus of praise, while the king, prin
ces and nobles prostrated themselves before the orb of day. (45, Vol. I, pp. 272-73)
There are two principal types of savior gods recognized by hierolo
gists: vegetation gods and sun gods. The vegetation theory was bril
liantly presented by Sir James G. Frazer in The Golden Bough, and by
Grant Allen in The Evolution of the Idea of God. This viewpoint has been summarized by Dr. David Forsyth:
Many gods besides Christ have been supposed to die, be resurrected and
ascend to heaven. This idea has now been traced back to its origin among
primitive people in the annual death and resurrection of crops and plant life generally. This explains the world-wide prevalence of the notion. Among still more primitive tribes, as Grant Allen showed, it is not yet understood
that sown corn sprouts because of the spring sunshine and they attribute the
result to divine agency. To this end, they are accustomed at seed time to
kill their tribal god—either in human or animal form—and scatter the flesh
and the blood over the sown fields. They believe that the seeds will not
grow unless the god is sacrificed and added to them in this manner. When,
therefore, the crop appears, they never doubt that it is their god coming to
life again. It is from this erroneous belief of primitive tribes that Christian
ity today derives its belief in Christ's death and resurrection. (83, p. 97)



134 MAN, GOD, AM) CIVILIZATION
According ro rhe advocates of the solar myth theory, the ancient
crucified saviors were personifications of the sun; and their life stories
were allegories of the sun's passage through the twelve constellations of
the zodiac. The Roman scholar, Macrobius (fourth century), has been
called the father of rhe solar myth theory. In his famous work, Saturna
lia, he discussed the practice in pagan temples of representing the gods
at different ages; and he stated: "These differences of age refer to the
sun which seems to a babe at the Winter Solstice, as the Egyptians rep
resent him in their temples on a certain day; that being the shortest
day, he is then supposed to be small and an infant.
This theory was not original with Macrobius, who probably obtained it from the Egyptian priests who were accustomed to explain
their myths by means of the principles of astronomy. The solar theory
has been neglected in recent years, so we shall give it some attention.
In Egypt, three thousand years ago, the birthday of the sun god was
celebrated on the 25th of December, since it was the first day to no
ticeably lengthen after December 21st (the day of the Winter Sol
stice). At the midnight hour, on the very first minute of the 25th of
December, the birthday of the sun was commemorated. At that time,
the sun was in the zodiacal sign Capricorn, which was then known as
the Stable of Augeas; so the infant sun god was said to have been born
in a stable. Shining bnghrlv on rhe meridian was Sinus (the Star from
the East); while rising in the east was Virgo (the virgin), the line of the
horizon passing through her center. To the right of Sirius was Orion
(the great hunter), with three stars in his belt. These stars lie in a
straight line, and point at Sirius. In ancient times they were known as
the Three Kings. We meet them in the Gospels as the Three Wise Men,
or Magi. In rhe zodiac on the interior of rhe dome of the Temple of
Denderah, the constellation Virgo was pictured as a woman wirh a
spike of corn in one hand; and on the adjacent margin, the virgin is an
notated by a figure of his wirh Horns in her arms.
Bur ir is well known as a matter of history [Edward Carpenter observed]
rhar rhe worship of Isis and Horus descended in rhe early Christian cenru
nes ro Alexandria, where ir rook rhe form of rhe worship of the Virgin
Mary and rhe Infant Savior, and so passed into the European ceremonial.
We have therefore rhe Virgin Mary connecred by linear succession and de
scenr wirh rhar remore zodiacal clusrer in rhe sky! ... A curious confirma
rion of rhe same asrronomical connecrion is afforded by rhe Roman Catholic
Calendar. Eor if this be consulred, ir will be found rhar rhe fesrival of rhe
Assumption of rhe Virgin is placed on rhe 1 5rh of Augusr, while rhe fesrival



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 135
of the birth of the Virgin is dated the 8th of September. At the present day,
the zodiacal signs (owing to precession) have shifted some distance from
the constellations of the same name. But at the time when the zodiac was
constituted and these names were given, the first date obviously would sig
nalize the actual disappearance of the cluster Virgo in the sun's rays—i.e.,
the Assumption of the Virgin into the glory of the God—while the second
date would signalize the re-appearance of the constellation, or the Birth of the Virgin. The Church of Notre Dame at Paris is supposed to be on the
original site of a Temple of Isis; and it is said (but I have not been able to
verify this myself), that one of the side entrance^—that, namely, on the left
in entering from the north (cloister) side—is figured with the signs of the
Zodiac, except that the sign Virgo is replaced by the figure of the Madonna
and Child. (41, pp. 32-3 3)
At the winter solstice (Christmas), the sun is at its southernmost
point in the celestial sphere. After that time, it begins to travel north
ward along the ecliptic; and then at the vernal equinox (Easter), it
passes over the celestial equator. This passing over of the sun from the
south to the north of the equator was the origin of the festival of the
Passover; for, when the sun reached the equinoctial point and crossed
or passed over the equator three thousand years ago, it was situated in
Aries, the Ram, or Lamb; so the lamb became the symbol of the god.
Here we have a clue to the origin of the Passover Lamb, which has
been widely regarded as a representation of the crucified Christ.
Should the reader consult such books as The World's Sixteen Crucified
Saviors by Kersey Graves (101), and Bible Myths and Their Parallels in
Other Religions by T. W. Doane (69), he will find the stories of sev
eral crucified pagan sun gods. The meaning of this solar crucifixion has
been explained in a scholarly fashion by L. G. Ry lands:
For in an astronomical chart, the sun is apparently crucified upon the in
tersecting lines of the Equator and the Ecliptic at the moment of his descent into the lower hemisphere, the hemisphere of darkness and death; and is so
again at the moment of his resurrection into the hemisphere of light and
life; while the period of transit is three days. At the time when the myth of
the death of the sun god originated, the sun, being in the constellation Aries
at the Spring Equinox, was identified with the Ram. That is the Lamb
which had been slain from the foundation of the world. The custom of dress
ing the paschal lamb in the shape of a cross is referrable to the same myth.
(218, p. 217)
In The World's Sixteen Crucified Saviors, Kersey Graves gave a list of
these ancient savior gods with the dates of their reputed deaths. The list



136 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
is as follows: Krishna of India (1200 B.C.), Sakia of India (600 B.C.),
Tammuz of Syria (1160 B.C.), Wirroba of the Telingonese (522
B.C.), lao of Nepal (622 B.C.), Hesus of the Celtic Druids (834
B.C.), Quetzalcoatl of Mexico (587 B.C.), Quirinius of Rome (506
B.C.), Prometheus of Greece (547 B.C.), Thulis of Egypt (1700
B.C.), Indra of Tibet (725 B.C.), Alcestos of Greece (600 B.C.), Attis
of Phrygia (1 170 B.C.), Crite of Chaldea (1200 B.C.), Bali of Orissa
(725 B.C.), Mithra of Persia (600 B.C.).
Another book dealing with the careers of the ancient savior gods is
The World's Saviors by the Reverend Charles H. Vail (255). The Rev
erend Vail gives a list of twenty-three savior gods who died and arose
from the tomb. The names of these ancient gods, as given by the Rev
erend Vail, are Krishna, Wittoba, Indra, Bacab, Quetzalcoatl, Pro
metheus, Mithra, Bacchus or Dionysus, Baldur, Samheim or Bal-Sab,
Tien, Aesculapius, Marsyas, Artemes, Melkarth, Sandan, Hyacinth,
Marduk, Osiris, Tammuz or Adonis, Attis, Bel Merodach, Jesus.
For details concerning the list of ancient saviors named above, the
reader should consult 255, pp. 81-135.
Vegetation cults, it seems from the evidence at hand, are older than
the stellar, lunar, and solar cults, but were later merged with them. In
the primitive sacrifice rites of vegetation gods, the victim, it is believed,
was originally the king or chief of the tribe or clan; and it was thought
by ancient men that the prosperity of the group depended on the well
being of the ruler. If the king became old and feeble, his subjects con
cluded that the nation or tribe would suffer a similar decline; so the
king, who was regarded as a god in human form, was sacrificed and re
placed by a younger and more vigorous man. After much passage of
time, the son of the king was substituted in the sacrificial rite and,
being the offspring of divinity, he was called the son of the god. At a
still later period, a condemned criminal was chosen in the place of the
royal victim. This culprit was given regal honors for a time, then put to
death. He was generally slain, while bound to a sacred tree, with arms
outstretched in the form of a cross. After being entombed, he was be
lieved to rise from the dead within three days—the three-day period
representing the return of vegetation. The question naturally comes to
mind: Why three days? The answer is that the three-day period is
based on the three-day interval between the old and new moons. (See
85, p. 29.) The belief is still widespread among the superstitious that an
intimate connection exists between the phases of the moon and the
growth of crops.



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 137
The ancient Chaldean historian, Berosus, has recorded the details of
a religious festival celebrated annually by the Babylonians and known
as the Sacaea. The duration of the fete was five days. For that length of
time, servants and masters exchanged places in society, the servants
giving orders and the masters obeying them. The king temporarily ab
dicated the throne and a mock king, called Zoganes, reigned in his
stead. But after five days, the mock king was dethroned and scourged
and then either hanged or crucified. "The victims of the human sacri
fices," Weigall noted, "were generally crucified or else killed and then
hung on a tree until the evening. In this regard, it is interesting to notice
that in the Acts, the writer mistakenly speaks of Jesus as having been
slain and then hanged on a tree as though this were a common phrase
coming readily to his mind; and the word hanged is frequently used in
Greek to denote crucifixion." (263, pp. 77-78)
Stellar and solar symbolism have profoundly affected the Christian
religion. In the Apocalypse we read of the four beasts and the four
horsemen. Taken literally, the narrative is devoid of meaning, but
when we recognize the beasts as zodiacal constellations and the horse
men as planets, we get a much clearer conception of the matter. In
Revelation, Chapter IV, Verse 7, we read, "The first beast was like a
lion and the second beast like a calf, and the third beast had a face as a
man and the fourth beast was like a flying eagle." These symbolic ani
mals were the constellations situated at the four cardinal points of the
zodiac five thousand years ago. They were Taurus the Bull (Vernal
Equinox), Leo the Lion (Summer Solstice), Scorpio the Scorpion (Au
tumnal Equinox), and Aquarius the Waterman (Winter Solstice). The
reader will notice that, in the Bible, the Eagle has been substituted for
the Scorpion.
According to Godfrey Higgins, "The signs of the zodiac, with ex
ception of the Scorpion which was exchanged by Dan for the Eagle,
were carried by the different tribes of the Israelites on their standards
and Taurus, Leo, Aquarius and Scorpio, or the Eagle, the four signs of
Reuben, Judah, Ephraim and Dan, were placed at the four corners
the four cardinal points—of their encampment, evidently in allusion to
the cardinal points of the sphere, the equinoxes and solstices, when the
equinox was in Taurus." (119, Vol. II, p. 105) As to the four horsemen and their steeds, we note the following:
1. The first horseman is a conqueror armed with a bow and wearing
a crown, and riding a white horse. This is the planet Venus.



138 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
2. The second horse is of the color red, and astride it is a warrior
with a sword. This is the planet Mars.
3. The third horse is black and his rider holds aloft a pair of bal
ances. This is the planet Saturn.
4. The fourth horse is of a pale complexion, pale green or blue
green, and on his back rides Death. This is the planet Mercury.
The ancient Babylonians built their temples in seven stages, each of
a different color, representing the sun, the moon, and the five planets
visible to the naked eye. The lowest stage was black and was dedicated
to Saturn. Next was an orange stage for Jupiter, then a red stage for
Mars, a gold-plated stage for the sun, a white or yellow stage for
Venus, a blue-green stage for Mercury; and a silver-plated stage for the
moon. The colors of the four apocalyptic horses point to their origin in
the astrological lore of Babylonia. Many incidents of the Gospel stories
can be explained as astronomical myths. We read in the Bible of Satan
leading Jesus to the mountaintop. The Devil has been represented in
Jewish and Christian art and folklore in the form of a goat, and we see
Satan in medieval paintings with the hooves, horns, and tail of a goat.
The Greek god Pan was part goat, and is pictured as leading Zeus to
the mountaintop. In ancient Babylonia, the Goat was the emblem of the
zodiacal constellation Capricorn. The sun reached its lowest point in the
celestial sphere in that constellation, after which it began to climb to
ward its highest point. So the goat god was imagined to lead the sun
god toward the highest point in the sky, figuratively called the moun
taintop. In Greek mythology we read of the savior Dionysus riding
upon two asses, which later were changed into celestial constellations.
In the New Testament, Jesus is described as riding into Jerusalem
upon two asses; i.e., upon an ass and colt, the foal of an ass. (Matthew,
XXI, 5-7) In ancient Babylonia, the symbol of the zodiacal constella
tion Cancer, in which the sun reached the highest point of its apparent
path, was the Ass and Foal.
In the Biblical book of Genesis, Chapter XLIX, we find that the
twelve zodiacal constellations were assigned to Jacob. "Among the
early Jews," Professor Harding shows, "each of the constellations of
the zodiac seems to have been identified with one of the children of
Jacob." (113, p. 276) Jacob referred to Reuben as "boiling over as water." This identifies him with Aquarius. Simeon and Levi are called
"the brethren," the twin stars Castor and Pollux, in Gemini. Judah,
"the lion's whelp," is obviously Leo. Dan, described as "a serpent in



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 139
the way," is Scorpio. Napthali, "a hind let loose," is Capricorn. Gad is
Aries, leader of the flock, for, according to Jacob, "a troup shall press
upon him; but he shall press upon their heel." Asher was a weigher of
bread, and he became Libra, symbolized by a scale beam. Issachar, "a
strong ass crouching down between the sheep folds," has been equated
with Taurus. Zebulon, who "shall dwell at the beach of the sea," is
none other than Pisces. Dinah, the only daughter of Jacob, was as
signed to Virgo. Joseph, because "his bow abodes in strength," is the
archer, Sagittarius. Benjamin, the youngest son of Jacob, became Can
cer. The sons of Jacob, as identified with the zodiacal constellation in
their proper order, were Gad (Aries), Issachar (Taurus), Simeon and
Levi (Gemini), Benjamin (Cancer), Judah (Leo), Dinah (Virgo),
Asher (Libra), Dan (Scorpio), Joseph (Sagittarius), Napthali (Capri
corn), Reuben (Aquarius), Zebulon (Pisces).
The signs and constellations of the zodiac were an integral part of an
ancient system of symbology of worldwide scope. They were not just
idle tales, conjured up by primitive storytellers.
We err in my opinion [states Sir D'Arcy W. Thompson] if we fail to
recognize in this antiquated symbolism a deeper intention. Cancer was domus Lunae, and the Crab is associated with the Moon of Consentia, Te
rina, etc., with the lunar Diana of the Ephesians, and with various other im
ages of the lunar goddess. Cancer was exaltatio Jovis, and the Crab was pe
culiarly associated with the Bird of Jove in the coinage of Agrigentum,
while the Aselli individual stars of the same constellation are mythologically
associated with the same god. . . . Cancer rose with Sirius and there are dog-headed representatives of both Mercury- A nubis and Luna-Hecate. Cancer is in the neighborhood of Corvus and Crater, and on coins of Mende the Ass is figured with the Raven and the Cup. Cancer rose opposite to Aguila and Delphinus which set soon afterwards, and moreover, set
precisely as the Dolphin rose; the Crab or the Ass is associated with the Eagle on the coins of Agrigentum and Motya, and with the Dolphin on the coins of Motya and Argolis. Cancer rose as Pegasus set, and the Crab is figured with the Horse on coins of Agrigentum. Cancer rose as the Southern Fish set, and the Crab and Ass are figured with the Fish on coins
of Agrigentum and Cyzicus. (Transactions of the Royal Society of Edin
burgh, Volume XXXIX)
Since the zodiacal symbolism looms so largely in the ancient myths and mysteries, we are emboldened to make a few remarks on the origin
and meaning of the world's oldest picture book. The zodiac is an imagi
nary band encircling the celestial sphere, and it stretches eight degrees



140 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
on each side of the ecliptic, whi^h is the apparent path of the sun. This
zodiac is divided into twelve equal sections, each corresponding to one
month. The zodiacal signs and constellations were originally the same,
but due to the precession of the equinoxes, each sign moves westward
into the next constellation in about 2,155 years. A sign, therefore,
makes a complete circuit of the heavens in about 26,000 years. The
constellations of the zodiac have the following names: Aries, the Ram
or Lamb; Taurus, the Bull; Gemini, the Twins; Cancer, the Crab; Leo,
the Lion; Virgo, the Virgin; Libra, the Balance; Scorpio, the Scorpion;
Sagittarius, the Archer; Capricorn, the Goat; Aquarius, the Water
Carrier; Pisces, the Fishes.
The following speculations on the origin of the celestial menagerie
are as accurate as available evidence affords. The constellations of the
Lamb, the Bull, and the Twins were star groups through which the sun
passed in the spring, in which time of the year occurred the seasons of
sheep raising, ploughing, and goat breeding. The Twins were origi
nally the two kids, since the young of goats are frequently born two at
a time. The Crab was so called because the sun reached its northern
most point in that constellation and then returned toward the south,
figuratively moving backward like a crab. The Lion was the star group through which the sun moved in midsummer, when its heat was most
potent, and hence was compared with the most powerful of the beasts.
The Virgin symbolized the harvest season when the young girls were
sent out to glean in the fields. The Balance was the constellation in
which the sun moved when day and night were equal, as if they were
weighed in a balance. The stars of the Scorpion were hidden by the
sun during the season of unhealthy weather and of plagues, which were
imagined to strike like a scorpion. Stars called the Archer reigned over
the hunting season, when the hunter shot game with bow and arrow.
In the constellation of the Goat, the sun reached the lowest point in its
course, after which it began to climb to the north again, as the wild
goat climbs to the summit of the hill. The Water Carrier marked the
position of the solar orb during the rainy season. The stars of the Fishes
constituted that star group through which the sun passed when the
fishing season was at its height.
The zodiacal constellations and signs discussed above were standard
ized by the ancient Greeks, but the Greek zodiac was not the most an
cient type of this stellar symbolism. There were older zodiacs known
to the Ethiopians, Egyptians, Babylonians, Hindus, Persians, Chinese,



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 141
etc., and the symbolic figures were different, as will be shown as we
proceed.
The Egyptian Sphinx, a colossal statue sacred to the sun god Horus,
was erected about six thousand years ago. It has the body of a lion
emblematic, no doubt, of the entrance of the sun into the zodiacal sign
of the Lion around 4000 B.C. The temple of the Sphinx was con
structed in the form of a cross and symbolizes North, South, East, and
West, the cardinal points of the celestial sphere: that is, the two sol
stices and the two equinoxes. It seems that originally there were only
four signs of the zodiac: the quarter signs at the cardinal points of the
sphere. George St. Clair, a talented student of astral mythologic lore
has made some illuminating comments on the original four signs of the
zodiac, from which we quote the following:
About 6000 years ago, the Spring Sun would be entering Taurus and the
four quarter signs would be the Bull, the Lion, the Scorpion and the Wat
erman, though some of these signs might be otherwise named. In memory
of that early arrangement—which in many ways left its mark—devices on
rings were, for example, a scorpion, a lion, a hawk, and a cynocephalus ape.
From the four quarters, we pass to the twelve signs. Between each two
quarter signs two other signs were inserted. The planisphere of the temple
of Denderah shows four gods supporting the heavens at the four quarter
points, corresponding to the Bull, the Lion, the Scorpion and the Water
man; and shows eight other divinities in pairs, one on either side of each pil
lar, making up the 12. (219, pp. 136-37)
The four gods holding up the heavens at the cardinal points of the
celestial sphere were the four sons of the sun god Horus, and their names were Amset, Hapi, Tuamutef, and Gebhsennuf These com
pass-point deities were easily recognized by their characteristic fea
tures: Amset had the head of a man, Hapi possessed the head of an ape,
Tuamutef was fitted out with the head of a jackal, Gebhsennuf bore on
his shoulders the head of a hawk.
The four gods holding up the skies are called Cherubim in the Bible.
"As for the likeness of their faces, they four had the face of a man and
the face of a lion, on the right side; and they four had the face of an ox
on the left side; they four had the face of an eagle." (Ezekiel, I, 10)
These celestial creatures were said to have been seen by Ezekiel in a vi
sion while in Babylon, and they were obviously the four corner con
stellations of the Chaldean zodiac. The Man was Aquarius, the Lion
was Leo, the Ox was Taurus, and the Eagle corresponded to Scorpio.



142 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The Egyptians, Chaldeans, and Greeks believed in certain star gods,
called decans. These decans were actually belts of stars extending
across the sky, the risings of which followed each other by ten days.
Dr. Wilhelm Gundel of Geissen, a noted authority on ancient astron
omy, in 1937 wrote a monumental work treating of the decans and
their stars, with the title Dekane und Dekansternbilder, which was pub
lished by J. J. Augustin at Gluckstadt and Hamburg. The book was
not translated into English, but it was reviewed in the English scientific
journal Nature by an eminent naturalist. We are pleased to cite a pas
sage from that most informative review:
When the year (of 360 days) had been divided into its twelve months
and put under the zodiacal signs, each of the twelve was again divided into
three parts often days each; and these thirty-six decans had their thirty-six
gods or rulers or dynasts, watchers of the hours (horoscopes), servants or
messengers of the greater gods or of Horus himself in his holy name,
spelled with thirty-six letters; each had his own face, which might chance to
be (in Egypt), the face of a bull or of an ibis, or of an eagle, or of a man. These were the angels, and archangels, the demons and arch-demons of
Hellenistic writers. Each had his own name; in mummy cases and papyri,
in obscure and fragmentary works like Hermes Trismegistus, or the Testi
monium Salomonis, in Celsus and Firmicus, the traditionary learning of
men like Kircher, Salmasius or Scaliger, we find the names of Chont-har,
Chont-chre, Siket and the thirty-three others, and all kinds of variants and
corruptions. Egyptian they were in the beginning, but the true form and
meaning of many are long lost. (245)
The British Orientalist, Robert Brown, Jr., in his Researches into the
Origin of the Primitive Constellations of the Greeks, Phoenicians, and
Babylonians, argued for an Euphratean origin of the zodiac, but we be
lieve the Nile Valley has a better claim for that honor. In one of his
major works, Sir Wallis Budge made the incredible statement that,
"The Egyptians borrowed their knowledge of the signs of the zodiac
together with much else, from the Greeks, who had derived a great
deal of their astronomical lore from the Babylonians." (32, Vol. II, p.
312) This opinion, to put it mildly, was quite preposterous. A fitting
rejoinder was made by Dr. Albert Churchward:
Why the knowledge of all this was old in Egypt before the Babylonians
even existed or knew anything about it. The Egyptians had worked out all
the architecture of the heavens and their priests had carried the same with
them to all parts of the world—not only the Northern heavens but the



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 143
Southern, as well. Probably rhey worked out the South before the North,
and the Druids and the Mayas and the Incas knew it all from the priests of
Egypt, the earliest probably thousands of years before the Babylonian na
tion existed. The Babylonians copied and obtained all their knowledge from
the Egyptians, and we are surprised that Dr. Budge should write that they
borrowed from the Greeks; they were old and degenerating in decay be
fore the Greek nation was born! Well may he say that "it is a subject of
conjecture at what period the Babylonians first divided the heavens into
sections, etc.," because they never did; what they knew they borrowed ei
ther direct from the Egyptians or Sumerians—the latter obtained it from
Egypt. It was the ancient Egyptians who mapped out the heavens into 12
divisions in the North, 12 divisions in the South, and 12 in the center, mak
ing 36 in all, and the 12 signs of the zodiac. ... It is very well to say that
"whether the Babylonians were themselves the inventors of such origins
i.e. (the zodiac), or whether they are to be attributed to the earlier non-Se
mitic Sumerian inhabitants of that country, cannot be said,"—and when he
states that "the Greeks borrowed the zodiac from the Babylonians, and
then the Greeks introduced it into Egypt, probably during the Ptolemaic
period," it appears to us that Dr. Budge must have left that part of "The
Gods of the Egyptians" to be written by one of his assistants, who knew
nothing about the history of the past. (48, pp. 212-13)
In the Open Court magazine for August, 1906, there was a scholarly
article by the editor of that journal, Dr. Paul Cams, with the title "Zo
diacs of Different Nations." One illustration accompanying this article
displays Egyptian and Greek zodiacs side by side in the photograph of
a late Roman Egyptian plaque. The dual symbols are listed below:

An example of an earlier Egyptian type of zodiac was given by Ger
ald Massey in his famous book Ancient Egypt, from which we cite the
following passage:
Taking the same order in which the signs on the ecliptic are read today
when Aries has become Princeps Zodiaci, we can identify at least a dozen
of the deities of Egypt with the twelve signs.
1. The ram-headed AMEN with the Constellation ARIES 2. OSIRIS, the Bull of Eternity with TAURUS 3. The SUT-HORUS twins with the GEMINI 4. The beetle-headed KHEPER-PTAH with the sign of the BEETLE, later, CRAB
