Many learned Christian scholars do not believe that Jesus had any
idea of starting a new religion or of establishing a church; they believe
that the real founder of Christianity was Saint Paul. And yet, we read
of Jesus referring to Peter as the rock on which the church was to be
built. Saint Peter was also popularly represented as the gatekeeper of
heaven. The name Peter comes from the Greek word Petra, which
means Rock. This may be a pseudonym, since he was also referred to as
Simon called Peter; that is to say, he may have been named Simon, and
was called the Rock because of some trait of character, just as General
Stonewall Jackson was so called because he stood up against the enemy
like a stone wall. It is interesting to note that there was a popular Se
mitic god named Simon and that the Egyptian god Petra was repre
sented as being the doorkeeper of heaven, the earth, and the under
world. In any case, as the stories of slain and resurrecting gods are
traced backward into the dim and distant past, we finally come to Af



MYTHS AND MYSTERIES: PAGAN AND CHRISTIAN 145
rica. One of the oldest religious celebrations of the ancient Egyptians
was the Sed Festival. Sir Flinders Petrie explained it as follows:
A special festival of the identity of the king with Osiris seems to have
been celebrated every thirty years, and a greater festival of the same nature every one hundred and twenty years. These periods are the lapse of a week
and a month in the shifting calendar. The festival was called the Sed or tail
feast, as marking the end of a period. From the various representations, it
has been gathered that at stated times, the king was killed to prevent his old
age impairing the fertility of the country, an African belief. (188, p. 41;
Vol. 1 1 of Herbert Spencer's Descriptive Sociology, published by Williams
and Norgate, London, 1925)
The earliest religion of Egypt has been traced back to central Africa:
The oldest structure of the people [again citing Petrie] was that which
resembled the African in beliefs and practices. There is a large body of cus
toms, especially those concerning the dead, which are closely alike in an
cient Egypt and modern Central Africa. In this stratum, probably preced
ing 10,000 B.C., animal worship was usual; so strong was the primitive
influence that this remained in practice down to the Roman age. The
source of this was a sense of kinship of men and animals. ("The Gods of Ancient Egypt," in 1 1 1, p. 667)
The same high authority, Sir Flinders Petrie, further asserted that
"the religion, like the population of Egypt, was always being mixed by
successive migrations of invaders. The old African ideas which under
lay it all still survive in Central Africa." (Ibid., p. 678)
In closing this chapter, we propose to discuss very briefly the my
thology connected with numbers. In a fascinating study, Amulets and
Talismans, Sir Wallis Budge has a chapter entitled "Theories About
Numbers and Their Mystic and Sacred Character." In gathering data
on the ancient myths and rites discussed in this chapter, we found that
the number forty seemed to be of considerable importance. However,
Budge did not find forty to be an important number. "Forty seems to
be nothing more than a round or general number," opined Dr. Budge,
and then he gave a few examples: "The Israelites ate manna 40 years
and wandered in the desert 40 years; Solomon, Jehoash and Joash each
reigned 40 years; Moses was on the mount 40 days, Jonah preached in Nineveh 40 days; our Lord fasted for 40 days and remained in the
tomb 40 hours, etc." (29, p. 440) The true significance of the number forty had been grasped by an



146 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
American scholar, Ernest Busenbark, we found. According ro this
careful investigator of ancient customs and myths:
At an extremely early date, a connection had doubtless been observed between the 28-day cycle of the moon and the menstrual cycle of women, and between changes of the moon and ocean tides. To people of the East, marriage and childbirth have always been the supreme duty and goal of
women, and the moon, who they believed controlled or regulated fecundity
and the generation of life, became known as the Great Regulator. Some
significances may also have been seen in the fact that the human foetus is
fully developed in 7 months, or the gestation of both women and cows takes
place in 280 days, or ten 28-day months. This period also represents 40 weeks, which may explain why, among ancient people, the number 40 is
habitually associated with periods of temptation, trial, hardship, and pain for
40 days, weeks, months, or years. For example, we have the 40 days and
nights of the Deluge, 40 years of wandering in the wilderness by the Israel
ites, 40 days and nights of fasting by Moses on Mt. Sinai; 40 days and
nights of Christ in the wilderness; 40 days between the resurrection of
Christ and his final disappearance from the earth; 40 days of mourning for the death of Jacob; 40 days of fasting by Elijah on Mount Horeb; 40 days in
which Ezekiel bore the iniquity of the House of Judah; 40 days of sacrifice
in the old Persian Salutation of Mithra; 40 nights of mourning in the Mysteries of Persephone; 40 days of mourning by the Babylonians before the
celebration of the festival of the Descent of Ishtar, which corresponds to the
Christian Lenten period of 40 days from Ash Wednesday to Easter. (38, pp. 18-19)
Many of the themes touched upon in this chapter call for further dis
cussion, and that will be undertaken in the chapters on the origin and
evolution of civilization.



CHAPTER EIGHT
The Twilight of the Gods
IT was mentioned in our chapter on the origin of magic and religion
that Sir James George Frazer has presented impressive evidence in his major work, The Golden Bough, that human thought in its long and ar
duous evolution has passed through three phases: the Age of Magic, the
Age of Religion, the Age of Science.
During the Age of Magic, men tried to bend the recalcitrant forces
of nature to their wills. In other words, they tried to coerce the natural
forces of the world. After much exercise of trial and error, over vast
stretches of time, the keener intellects became convinced that magic
was not adequate in practice or valid in theory. Then they imagined
supernatural beings behind the veil of nature who could be persuaded
to serve mankind if properly conciliated; and this outlook on the world
ushered in the Age of Religion. In time, the acutest thinkers among
humankind, by means of observation, experimentation and reason, ar
rived at the idea of natural law. The transition from the Age of Reli
gion to the Age of Science has been vividly portrayed by Frazer in ma
jestic prose tinged with poetry:
From one department of nature after another, the gods are reluctantly or
contemptuously dismissed, and their provinces committed to certain ab
stract ideas of ethers, atoms, molecules and so forth, which, though just as
imperceptible to human senses as their divine predecessors, are judged by
prevailing opinion to discharge their duties with greater regularity and dis
patch, and are, accordingly, firmly installed on the vacant thrones amid the
general applause of the more enlightened portion of mankind. Thus, instead
of being peopled with a noisy bustling crowd of full-blooded and pictur
esque deities, clothed in the graceful form and animated with the warm pas
sions of humanity, the universe outside the narrow circle of our conscious
ness is now conceived as absolutely silent, colorless and deserted. The
147



148 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
cheerful sounds which we hear, the bright hues which we see have no exist
ence, we are told, in the external world: the voices of friends, the harmonies
of music, the chime of falling waters, the solemn roll of ocean, the silver
splendor of the moon, the golden glories of sunset, the verdure of summer
woods, and the hectic tints of autumn—all these subsist only in our own
minds, and if we imagine them to have any reality elsewhere, we deceive
our selves. In fact, the whole external world as perceived by us is one great
illusion: if we gave the reins to fancy we might call it a mirage, a piece of
witchery, conjured up by the spells of some unknown magician to bewilder
poor ignorant humanity. Outside of ourselves, there stretches away on
every side an infinitude of space without sound, without light, without
color, a solitude traversed in every direction by an inconceivably complex
web of silent and impersonal forces. That, if I understand it aright, is the
general conception of the world which modern science has substituted for
polytheism.
When philosophy and science by their combined efforts have ejected
gods and goddesses from all the subordinate posts of nature, it might per
haps be expected that they would have no further occasion for the services
of a deity and that having relieved him of all his particular functions, they
would have arranged for the creation and general maintenance of the uni
verse without him by handing over these important offices to an efficient
staff of those ethers, atoms, corpuscles, and so forth, which had already
proved themselves so punctual in the discharge of the minor duties en
trusted to them. Nor, indeed, is this expectation altogether disappointed. A
number of atheistical philosophers have courageously come forward and as
sured us that the hypothesis of a deity as the creator and preserver of the
universe is quite superfluous and that all things came into being or have ex
isted from eternity without the help of any divine spirit, and that they will
continue to exist without it to the end, if end, indeed, there is to be. (88, pp.
213-14)
There has been considerable controversy in recent years as to
whether there is an inherent conflict between religion and science; and
there is a sizable literature on the subject. Perhaps the outstanding
work on the struggle between superstition and science is A History of
the Warfare of Science with Theology in Christendom (270) by Professor
Andrew Dickson White, the first president of Cornell University. Re
ligion has held the allegiance of many members of the human race for
ages, because it promised mankind power over the outside world; but
this promise religion could not realize. However, science has achieved
that control over the hostile forces of nature which religion sought but
was not able to bring about.



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 149
Quincy Howe, editor of Living Age, asked: "Is it not more likely
that the lowbrows will follow the highbrows away from religion than
that the reverse process will occur?" (Cited by V. F. Calverton, in 40,
p. 265.) The institutions of orthodox Christianity seemingly no longer
attract men and women of superior intelligence. Ellsworth Huntington
and Leon F. Whitney made a survey of the religious affiliations of men
and women listed in Who's Who in A?nerica, and they found that, of
every 100,000 Unitarians in the nation, 1,185 were sufficiently emi
nent to be listed in Who's Who, whereas there were only eight Luther
ans and seven Catholics listed therein. The decay of religion is particu
larly evident in the decline in the quality of men entering the Christian
ministry as a profession. As lately as three hundred years ago, many of
the best minds could be found among the clergy; and even in the eight
eenth and nineteenth centuries some first-rate intellects were attracted
to the ministry; but that is not so today.
About a hundred years ago in the United States, the outstanding col
leges and universities were affiliated with various denominations of the
Christian churches. A trend toward secularization was started by Ezra
Cornell, a philanthropist who desired to promote a system of higher ed
ucation free from clerical control. So Cornell established Cornell Uni
versity at Ithaca, New York. The trend toward secularization became
popular, and many other institutions followed suit. John D. Rockefel
ler, Sr., was a devout Christian of the Baptist persuasion; and he do
nated a large amount of money to the founding of the University of
Chicago for the specific purpose of training ministers for the Baptist
church. Today that university is almost completely secularized; and,
though it does have a theological school, the training of clergymen is
not an important part of its educational program. It is a well-known
fact that religion is no longer front-page news. In a modern daily news
paper, the most important news is displayed on the front page; slightly
less important news goes on page two. The relatively unimportant
news appears way back near the want ad section; and that is where you
find the sermon page in the Monday morning editions of the great
metropolitan dailies. Even the uneducated masses are largely indifferent
to religion. In the elegant eighties and the gay nineties of the nine
teenth century, Colonel Robert G. Ingersoll, the benevolent agnostic,
gave stirring antireligious lectures to large and enthusiastic audiences.
Outstanding Christian clergymen were outraged, and they preached
sermons against Ingersoll. In those days, both the lectures of the Colo



150 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
nel and his ministerial opponents were prominently featured by the
outstanding newspapers. Today, the public interest tends toward prob
lems of immediate concern, and hence religious controversy is no
longer big news.
Though the apologists for religion are willing to concede that their
beliefs are based primarily on faith, they do not reject entirely the em
ployment of reason; for they claim to prove by the use of the unaided
reason the validity of the God idea and the doctrine of immortality.
Near the end of the eighteenth century, a Christian theologian, Arch
deacon William Paley, published a book entitled The Evidences of
Christianity. His alleged proof of the existence of God became a Chris
tian classic. Paley, in this work, tells of how a European traveler lost his
watch on the beach of a lonely island in the South Seas. A savage came
along in due time and, finding the watch, picked it up. While gazing
thoughtfully at the timepiece, he engaged in metaphysical meditations
of great profundity. This savage noticed the skillfully arranged and in
tricate mechanism of wheels, levers, springs, and hands, and concluded
that they could not have assembled themselves fortuitously. It was ob
vious, he reasoned, that some man made the watch, as it certainly could
not have manufactured itself. Since complex machines like watches
must, of necessity, have been made by men, then the man must there
fore be more wonderful than the watch. If the watch could not produce
itself, then it was a certainty that man could not create himself. So
there must be someone greater than man who was responsible for his
existence. In other words, there must be a God. This argument when
presented to intelligent children frequently brings forth the question:
Who made God? Adults as a rule do not ask this question, since they
have been taught that such questions are not considered good form.
The stock orthodox argument in favor of belief in immortality is that
everybody has an instinctive desire to live forever and that the very ex
istence of this hope is valid evidence that immortality is a fact. It might
be argued on the other hand that, since most people fear death, this
very dread proves that death ends all.
The greatest asset of the sacerdotalists is the ignorance and credulity
of their clients. This was clearly shown by the crude religious propa
ganda circulated in the United States during World War II. There
was a popular slogan abroad stating: "There are no atheists in the fox
holes." We were told that the Good Lord allowed a large number of
Christian American soldiers to be trapped in foxholes where the heathen Japanese raked them with machine-gun fire and dropped bombs



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 151
on them. Why God exposed His followers to dire peril in that way and
kept the atheists in places of safety was not explained. Bergen Evans, in
his entertaining book, The Natural History of Nonsense (77), called at
tention to the unconscious irreverence of other war propaganda of the
same period. For instance, a book was written by an officer of the Air
Force with the title God Is My Co-Pi lot. God could not be dispensed with, but he was given the subordinate position of co-pilot.
A few years back, numerous orthodox apologists attempted to re
store the waning prestige of Christian theology by citing the testimony
of certain eminent scientists and scholars in favor of religion. The men
most frequently mentioned were Sir James Jeans, Sir Arthur Edding
ton, Professor Albert Einstein, Professor Henry Fairfield Osborn, and
Professor Robert A. Millikan. Not one among them accepted the doc
trines of traditional religion. Most of these men, it is true, were avow
edly religious, but they professed a very liberal type of religion which
was in no way connected with the traditional creeds of the various
churches. The late H. G. Wells gently poked fun at Sir James Jeans
for declaring that, since the universe seems to be based on mathematical principles, then God must be a Great Mathematician. Sir James
further stated that the appropriate symbol of his mathematical God should be the mathematical unit of the system of Imaginary Numbers.
This unit is the number \Zzl (the square root of minus one). Wells
seems to have thought that Jeans was joking, for it seemed to him that any God symbolized by the imaginary unit would be equally imaginary. But imaginary numbers, as any student of mathematics or engi
neering will testify, are by no means actually imaginary; so Sir James
Jeans may not have been joking.
Professor Isaac Asimov, in one of his popular science books, tells an
amusing story of how, when a student in college, he got into an argu
ment with a professor on the question as to whether imaginary num
bers existed or not. We shall let the professor tell his own tale, for his
style is inimitable:
When I was a mere slip of a lad and attended college, 1 had a friend with whom I ate lunch every day. His 11 A.M. class was in sociology, which I
absolutely refused to take, and my 11 A.M. class was calculus, which he as
steadfastly refused to take—so we had to separate at eleven and meet at
twelve. As it happened, the sociology professor was a scholar who did things in the grand manner, holding court after class was over. Consequently, when my calculus lecture was over, I had to enter the sociology room and wait
patiently for court to conclude.



152 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Once, I walked in when the professor was listing on the board his classi
fication of mankind into the two groups of mystics and realists, and under
mystics he had included mathematicians along with the poets and theologians. One student wanted to know why.
"Mathematicians," said the professor, "are mystics because they believe
in numbers that have no reality."
Now, ordinarily as a non-member of the class, I sat in the corner and
suffered in silent boredom, but now, 1 rose convulsively and said, "What numbers?"
The professor looked in my direction and said, "The square root of
minus one. It has no existence. Mathematicians call it imaginary, but they
believe it has some sort of an existence in a mystical way."
"There's nothing mystical about it," 1 said, angrily, "the square root of minus one is just as real as any other number."
The professor smiled. "Come, young man, hand me the square root of
minus one piece of chalk."
But I raised my voice, "I'll do it, I'll do it. I'll hand you the square root of
minus one piece of chalk, if you hand me a one 'half piece of chalk.' "
The professor smiled again, and said, "Very well," broke a fresh piece of
chalk in half and handed me one of the halves. "Now for your end of the
bargain."
"Ah, but wait," I said, "you haven't fulfilled your end. This is one piece
of chalk you've handed me, not a one half piece." 1 held it up for the others
to see. "Wouldn't you all say this was one piece of chalk? It certainly isn't
two or three."
But, by now the professor had lost his equanimity altogether and his final
argument was unanswerable. He said, "Get the hell out of here." I left
(laughing) and thereafter waited for my friend in the corridor. (4, pp. 73
74)
Giordano Bruno, in the sixteenth century, declared: "Man is no
more than an ant in the presence of the infinite." This did not please
the ruling Church-State setup, so Bruno was silenced by being burned
at the stake. But the progress of science was not halted. A century
later, the Age of Newton arrived and, although Newton was a devout
Christian, his discoveries were destructive of religious beliefs.
This viewpoint has been well expressed in a popular work on the
role of mathematics in Western culture, from which we cite the fol
lowing excerpt:
The Newtonian era created celestial mechanics but destroyed heaven,
the seat of God and the eventual dwelling of privileged human souls. God lost not only his home but his importance. To Descartes, it was already



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 153
clear rhar God, the omnipotent, could not abolish extension or the laws of
motion. Newton, along with Descartes, credited God with the act of crea
tion but restricted his daily functions. Huygens and Leibniz curtailed God's
role even more. They, too, credited Him with the initial act of creation, at
which time He established the mathematical order of the universe. There
after, however, His active relations with it ceased. The universe was stable;
there was no room for whim or chance. By this masterly mathematical
achievement, even the corrective measures formerly required of God were made unnecessary and God was deprived of one more duty. It was not long
before God, Himself, was completely dispensed with, for Hume attacked
causality, and, consequently, the need for a creator, or Prime Mover, of
the universe. The world became an eternal, infinite, self-moving machine
which existed before and would exist after insignificant man, serving no ap
parent purpose except perhaps to delight the mathematician who was
slowly, but surely uncovering the controlling principles. Events took place
not because God ordered them, individually, and after due consideration,
but rather because they were predetermined by fixed, already existing
mathematical laws. (135, pp. 258-59)
Archdeacon Paley's watch argument does not carry much weight
today. An American literary man, the late Colonel Rupert Hughes,
adopted a variant of Paley's contention and then showed that his exam
ination of the same problem led to a conclusion definitely anti-theistic.
In a brilliant essay, Colonel Hughes compared the creation of the uni
verse by God with the invention of the Ford motorcar by Henry Ford.
The reader was asked to ponder the question as to how the first Ford
car originated. We know that such a complex machine could not have
just happened. A reasonable view would be that the Ford auto was a
product of the inventive genius of Henry Ford. But could Mr. Ford
single-handedly have produced his famous Model T? Let us examine
this question carefully. A Ford, or any other automobile, requires
pneumatic tires made of rubber. This calls for rubber plantations and tire factories manned by many hands. The engine can be manufactured
and assembled only by a multitude of workers in iron, steel, and other
metals, and the same goes for the chassis and body of the car. The glass
for headlights and windshields also necessitates a considerable labor
force. A motorcar must have fuel, which is a product of an extensive
oil-producing industry, which also employ large numbers of men as la
borers, technicians, engineers, and so on. So we see that the Ford car was created by the combined efforts of myriads of people. Colonel
Hughes contended that, if one man could not create an automobile,



154 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
was it logical to assume that only one God could create a world or a
universe? Let us assume that one omnipotent God did make the world.
Harking back to Paley, a watch is a wonderful mechanism, and we
know that something more wonderful made the watch—in other
words, a man. Man could not create himself, so there must have been a
more wonderful being, namely a god. But how can we stop here?
Where did God come from? Was he created by a super-god or by a
syndicate of higher gods, and so on, and so on.
Colonel Ingersoll argued cogently that the god-idea explained noth
ing. We may ask: How did the universe or the world come about? The
answer is that we do not know; it is a mystery. We have one mystery
on hand already. Then we invoke another mystery called God to ac
count for a mysterious universe. Now, we have two mysteries to deal
with. This is clearly a violation of the law of parsimony. When one is
asked whether or not he believes in God, the question may be regarded
as so ambiguous as to have no meaning at all. For there are at least two
types of gods recognized in, the modern world. There is the personal
God of the theologians and the nonpersonal God or gods of the philos
ophers. When Professor Albert Einstein was asked if he believed in
God, he replied that he believed in the God of Spinoza. It is well
known that Spinoza believed that God was just another name for Na
ture. Disbelievers in supernaturalism are called agnostics or atheists; but
what, precisely, do these epithets mean? Here the opinions of Professor
J. B. Bury are worthy of thoughtful consideration:
The agnostic holds that there are limits to human reason, and that theol
ogy lies outside those limits. Within those limits lies the world with which
science (including psychology) deals. Science deals entirely with phe
nomena and has nothing to say to the nature of the ultimate reality which
may lie behind phenomena. There are four possible attitudes to this ulti
mate reality. There is the attitude of the metaphysician and theologian, who
are convinced not only that it exists, but that it can be at least partly
known. There is the attitude of the man who denies that it exists; but he
must be also a metaphysician for its existence can only be disproved by
metaphysical arguments. Then there are those who assert that it exists but
deny that we can know anything about it. And finally, there are those who
say that we cannot know whether it exists or not. These last are agnostics in
the strict sense of the term, men who profess not to know. The difference
between an agnostic and an atheist is that the atheist positively denies the ex
istence of a personal God; the agnostic does not believe in it. (36, pp. 213-14)



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 155
The classic statement of the agnostic position was given by Leslie
Stephen in an essay, "An Agnostic's Apology," published in 1876 in
the Fortnightly Review, from which the following is an excerpt:
Why, when no honest man will deny in private that every ultimate
problem is wrapped in the profoundest mystery, do honest men proclaim in
pulpits that unhesitating certainty is the duty of the most foolish and igno
rant? We are a company of ignorant beings, dimly discerning light enough
for our daily needs, but hopelessly differing whenever we attempt to de
scribe the ultimate origin or end of our paths; and yet, when one of us ven
tures to declare that we don't know the map of the universe as well as the
map of our infinitesimal parish, he is hooted, reviled and perhaps told that
he will be damned to all eternity for his faithlessness. (36, pp. 215-16)
Thomas Paine, in his famous book The Age of Reason, repeatedly
declared his belief in the existence of God, and yet Paine was charged
by the orthodox with being an atheist. Paine did not believe in the per
sonal god of the religions; he was therefore, not a theist but an atheist
He did believe in the God of the philosophers, which was not con
ceived as personal; so that Paine was actually a deist; his God was natu
ral rather than supernatural.
When organized Christianity was in its prime, it crushed heresy by
killing the heretics. Nowadays, it lacks this power, so it resorts to ostra
cism and other forms of harassment. This has posed a problem for anti
clerical skeptics. Since a frontal attack on organized superstition has
small chance of success, many critics of orthodoxy have adopted various
subterfuges. Voltaire, in his lifelong battle against the tyranny of
church and state, used the weapon of ridicule with telling effect. When
in the next century the English historian, Winwood Reade, openly at
tacked the foundations of Christianity, his enemies retaliated by mak
ing life miserable for him. Reade, in 1872, published his most famous book The Martyrdom of Man. He held a rather low opinion of the pop
ular religion of his time, and he did not hesitate to criticize it adversely.
In discussing the popular theism of his day, he wrote the following:
This brings us to the character of the Creator. We must beg to observe
again that we describe not the actual Creator, but the popular idea of the
Creator. It is said that the Creator is omnipotent and also that he is benevo
lent. But one proposition contradicts the other. Again, either sin entered
the world against the will of the Creator, in which case he is not omnipo
tent, or, it entered with his permission, in which case it is his agent, in
which case, he selects sin, in which case he has a preference for sin, in



156 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
which case he is fond of sin, in which case he is sinful. It is certain that the
feelings of the created have in no way been considered. If, indeed, there
were a judgment day, it would be for man to appear at the bar not as a
criminal, but as accuser. What has he done that he should be subjected to a
life of torture and temptation? God might have made us all happy and he
has made us all miserable. Is that benevolence? God might have made us all
pure and he has made us all sinful. Is that the perfection of morality? If I be
lieved in the existence of this man-created God, of this divine Nebuchad
nezzar, I would say, "You can make me live in your world, O Creator, but you cannot make me admire it; you can load me with chains, but you can
not make me flatter you; you can send me to hell-fire, but you cannot ob
tain my esteem. And if you condemn me, you condemn yourself. If I have committed sins, you invented them, which is worse. If the watch you have
made does not go well, whose fault is that? Is it rational to damn the wheels
and springs?" (203, pp. 416-17)
Mark Twain, the great American writer, read The Martyrdom of
Man, and liked it. The passage cited above particularly appealed to
Twain, and he wove a famous story around it. Here we allude to The
Mysterious Stranger, one of Mark Twain's greatest stories. Like many
other adventure stories, the plot calls for a hero and a villain. In The
Mysterious Stranger, Satan is the hero and God is the villain. The au
thor, thinking that the publication of the story would do him no good
in his lifetime, stipulated that his publisher should not issue the tale
until after his death. Mark Twain's publisher was the well-known firm
of Harper and Brothers. So, after the death of the author, The Mysteri
ous Stranger was published by Harper's as a juvenile classic. The book
was printed in large type with attractive color plates and became
widely popular.
The setting of this story was a sleepy little Austrian village, and the
time was the winter season of the year 1 590. The name of the village
was Eseldorf (English translation: Jackassville). The adventures began
when three village boys were playing in some neighboring woods and
along came a handsome young man, the Mysterious Stranger. The
three boys were named Theodor, Nikolaus, and Seppi; and the narra
tive is put into the mouth of Theodor, who is imagined to have written
down in later years the memories of his boyhood escapades. The Stranger was a handsome young man attired in new clothing of good
quality. He told the boys that his name was Philip Traum, and that he
was a magician. The kids naturally asked him to do some tricks, and he
gladly complied with their requests. Philip, of course, was the old



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 157
Devil, and his tricks were impressive. In the words of Theodor: "He
made a tiny toy squirrel our of clay, and it ran up a tree and sat on a limb overhead and barked down at us. Then he made a dog that was
not much larger than a mouse and it treed the squirrel and danced
about the tree, excited and barking and was as alive as any dog could
be. It frightened the squirrel from tree to tree and followed it up until
both were out of sight in the forest. He made birds out of clay and set them free and they flew away singing."
The Stranger, after gaining the confidence of the boys, told them
that he was an angel, and that his real name was Satan, but that, while
he was in Eseldorf, he would use the alias Philip Traum. Theodor re
called: uWe had seen wonders this day; and my thoughts began to run
on the pleasure it would be to tell them when I got home, but he no
ticed those thoughts and said: 'No, all these matters are a secret among
us four. I do not mind you trying to tell them if you like, but I will pro
tect your tongues and nothing of the secret will escape from them.'
After taking a short vacation, Satan rejoined his three youthful friends and announced that he would make them all invisible and show
them some interesting sights. There was a local dungeon with a torture
chamber, and to that place they were whisked in a state of invisibility.
The scene is described by Theodor:
We were in the torture chamber, Satan said. The rack was there and the
other instruments, and there was a smoky lantern or two hanging on the walls and helping to make the place look dim and dreadful. There were
people there—and executioners—but as they took no notice of us, it meant that we were invisible. A young man lay bound, and Satan said he was sus
pected of being a heretic, and the executioners were about to inquire into it.
They asked the man to confess to the charge, and he said he could not, for
it was not true. Then, they drove splinter after splinter under his nails and
he shrieked with pain. Satan was not disturbed, but I could not endure it
and had to be whisked out of there. I was faint and sick, but the fresh air revived me and we walked toward my home. I said it was a brutal thing.
Satan replied sharply:
No, it was a human thing. You should not insult the brutes by such a
misuse of that word; they have not deserved it. It is like your paltry race
always lying, always claiming virtues it hasn't got, always denying them to the higher animals, which alone possess them. No brute ever does a cruel
thing. When a brute inflicts pain, he does it innocently; he does not inflict
pain for the pleasure of inflicting it—only man does that. . . . Are you feeling better? Let me show you something.



158 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Then the Devil sailed through the air with his young companions to
a village in France. Theodor relates what they saw: "We walked through a great factory of some sort, where men and women and little
children were toiling in heat and dirt and a fog of dust; and they were
clothed in rags and drooped at their work, for they^were worn and half starved, and weak and drowsy."
The boys wondered at the spectacle, and Satan explained: The proprietors are rich and very holy; but the wage they pay to these poor brothers and sisters of theirs is only enough to keep them from dropping dead with hunger. The work hours are fourteen per day, winter and
summer—from six in the morning till eight at night—little children and all.
And they walk to and from the pigsties which they inhabit—four miles
each way, through mud and slush, rain, snow, sleet, and storm, daily, year in and year out. They get four hours of sleep. They kennel together, three
families in a room, in unimaginable filth and stench; and disease comes and
they die off like flies. Have they committed a crime, these mangy things? No. What have they done that they are punished so? Nothing at all, except getting themselves born into your foolish race. You have seen how they treat misdoers there in jail; now you see how they treat the innocent and
the worthy. Is your race logical? Are these ill-smelling innocents better off than that heretic? Indeed, no; his punishment is trivial compared with theirs. They broke him on the wheel and smashed him to rags and pulp after we left, and he is dead now, and free of your precious race; but these
poor slaves here—why they have been dying for years and some of them will not escape from life for years to come.
A
little girl named Lisa from one of the poor families of Eseldorf died, and the following tragedy was enacted at the cemetery:
At the graveyard, the body of little Lisa was seized for debt by a carpenter to whom the mother owed fifty groschen for work done the year before. She had never been able to pay this and was not able now. The carpenter took the corpse home and kept it four days in his cellar, the mother weeping and imploring about his house all the time; then he buried it in his brother's cattle yard without religious ceremonies. It drove the mother wild with grief and shame, and she forsook her work and went daily about the town, cursing the carpenter and blaspheming the laws of the emperor and the church, and it was pitiful to see. Seppi asked Satan to interfere, but he said the carpenter and the rest were members of the human race and were
acting quite neatly for that species of animal. He would interfere if he found a horse acting in such a way, and we must inform him when we came across that kind of horse doing that kind of a human thing so that he could stop it. We believed this was sarcasm, for, of course, there wasn't any
such horse.



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 159
Theodor and his companions had an enjoyable time hobnobbing
with the Mysterious Stranger. In the words of Theodor: "It was won
derful, the mastery Satan had over time and distance. For him, they did
not exist. He called them human inventions and said they were arti
ficialities. We often went to the most distant parts of the globe with
him and stayed weeks and months and yet, were gone only a fraction
of a second, as a rule. You could prove it by the clock."
After returning from one of these incursions into space and time,
Philip and the boys returned to the village just in time to see a nice old
lady, suspected of being a witch, hanged and stoned to death by a mob
of her fellow citizens. The sad story was told by an eyewitness, the boy
Theodor:
She came flying down, with the howling and cursing mob after her and
tried to take refuge in houses, but the doors were shut in her face. They
chased her more than half an hour, we following to see it, and at last, she
was exhausted and fell and they caught her. They dragged her to a tree and
threw a rope over the limb and began to make a noose of it, some holding
her, meantime, and she crying and begging, and her young daughter look
ing on and weeping, but afraid to say or do anything. They hanged the
lady, and I threw a stone at her, although in my heart I was sorry for her;
but all were throwing stones and each was watching his neighbor, and if I
had not done as the others did it would have been noticed and spoken of.
Satan burst out laughing.
A
little later on, after the mob had dispersed, Theodor looked at the
Devil with a hurt expression on his face and Satan, reading his
thoughts, said: "Yes, I was laughing at you, because in fear of what
others might report about you, you stoned the woman when your heart
revolted at the act—but I was laughing at the others, too."
"Why?" the lad inquired.
"Because their case was yours," the Stranger replied.
"How is that?" Theodor asked.
"Well, there were sixty-eight people there, and sixty-two of them
had no more desire to throw a stone than you had."
"Satan!" the boy indignantly shouted in reply.
Then the Prince of Darkness really sounded off:
Oh it's true. I know your race. It is made up of sheep. It is governed by
minorities, seldom or ever by majorities. It suppresses its feelings and its be
liefs and follows the handful that makes the most noise. The vast majority



160 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
of the race, whether savage or civilized, are secretly kindhearted and shrink
from inflicting pain, but in the presence of the aggressive and pitiless mi
nority, they don't dare to assert themselves. Think of it! One kindhearted
creature spies upon another and sees to it that he loyally helps in iniquities
which revolt both of them. Speaking as an expert, 1 know that ninety-nine
out of a hundred of your race were strongly against the killing of witches
when that foolishness was first agitated by a handful of pious lunatics in the
long ago. . . . And yet, apparently everybody hates witches and wants
them killed. Some day a handful will rise up on the other side and make the
most noise—perhaps even a single daring man with a determined front will
do it—and in a week all the sheep will wheel and follow him, and witch
hunting will come to a sudden end.
Monarchies, aristocracies and religions are all based on that large defect
in your race—the individual's distrust of his neighbor, and his desire for
safety's or comfort's sake to stand well in his neighbor's eye. These institu
tions will always remain, and always flourish and always oppress you,
affront you, and degrade you, because you will always be and remain slaves
of minorities. There was never a country where the majority of the people
were in their secret hearts loyal to any of these institutions.
Look at you in war—what muttons you are, and how ridiculous! There
has never been a just one, never an honorable one—on the part of the insti
gator of the war. The loud little handful as usual will shout for the war. The
pulpit will, warily and cautiously, object at first; the great, big, dull bulk of
the nation will rub its sleepy eyes and try to make out why there should be
a war, and will say earnestly and indignantly, l it is unjust and dishonor
able, and there is no necessity for it." Then the handful will shout louder.
And now the whole nation—pulpit and all—will take up the war cry and
shout itself hoarse and mob any honest man who ventures to open his
mouth; and presently, such mouths will cease to open. Next, the statesmen
will invent cheap lies, putting the blame upon the nation that is attacked,
and every man will be glad of those conscience-soothing falsities, and will
diligently study them, and refuse to examine any refutations of them; and
thus, he will by and by convince himself that the war is just and will thank
God for the better sleep he enjoys after this process of grotesque self-decep
tion.
Finally, one day, the Mysterious Stranger appeared to Theodor
when alone and announced that this was his final visit to Jackassville.
Before departing, Satan made a farewell speech in which he marveled
at the strange God worshiped by Christendom:
A God who could make good children as easily as bad, yet preferred to make bad ones; who could have made every one of them happy, yet never



THE TWILIGHT OF THE CODS 161
made a single happy one; who made rhem prize rheir birrer life, yet stingily
cut it short; who gave his angels eternal happiness unearned, yet required
his other children to earn it; who gave his angels painless lives, yet cursed
his other children with biting miseries and maladies of mind and body; who
mouths justice and invented hell, mouths mercy and invented hell, mouths
Golden Rules and forgiveness multiplied by seventy times seven, and in
vented hell; who mouths morals to other people and has none himself; who
frowns upon crimes, yet commits them all; who created man without invi
tation, then tries to shuffle the responsibility for man's acts upon man, in
stead of honorably placing it where it belongs, upon himself; and finally,
with altogether divine obtuseness, invites this poor abused slave to worship him!
After delivering this last tirade, Old Nick vanished into the atmos
phere, and thus made his final departure from the little village of Jack
assville.
When one is discussing the existence of God, the word "God"
should be defined; for the God of the theologians is anthropomorphic,
whereas the God of the philosophers is nonpersonal; and that makes all
the difference in the world. The atheist rarely gets a fair hearing in re
ligious controversy. He is generally denounced as being antireligious;
but this is not necessarily so. The Buddhist religion is devoid of the
God idea, so Buddhists are atheists; but no one can show that Bud
dhism, with its millions of believers, is not a religion. The late I). M.
Bennett was an avowed atheist, yet he considered himself a religious
man; for he was both a Spiritualist and a Theosophist.
There is a story of how the French scientist and mathematician,
Pierre Simon Laplace, presented Napoleon with a five-volume set of
his Mecanique Celeste [Celestial Mechanics]. Napoleon asked him how
it was that he could write a work on the system of the universe and not
once mention the name of God. Laplace replied: "Sir, 1 have no need
of that hypothesis." Napoleon was only joking, because he was a skep
tic himself. Like many highly placed unbelievers, Napoleon found no
need of religion for himself, but thought it was a good thing for the common people. "What is it," Napoleon asked, "that makes the poor
man think it quite natural that there are fires in my palace while he is
dying of the cold? That I have ten coats in my wardrobe while he goes
naked? That at each of my meals enough is served to feed his family for
a week? It is simply religion, which tells him that in another life, I shall
be only his equal and that he actually has more chance of being happy
there than I. Yes, we must see to it that the floors of the churches are



162 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
open to all, and that it does not cost the poor man much to have prayers said on his tomb." (Cited by Professor George A. Dorsey in 71, pp. 940-41) Professor Dorsey commented on the above remarks of Napo
leon as follows: "Are press, big business, and politics in the same boat
as that notorious skeptic, Napoleon, who left religion alone because it
paid?"
In the early part of the present century, Andrew Carnegie, a well
known philanthropist, was endowing public libraries, and making pres
ents of pipe organs to various churches. One of Carnegie's friends, knowing that he was an atheist, asked him why he was giving away or
gans to churches; and the philanthropist replied that the sermons in
most churches were so dull that he hoped the sounds of the pipe organs would drown them out. Many men of wealth and high social position
have no use for religion, but consider it bad taste to openly admit it.
Andrew Carnegie was an exception; he was a freethinker and never denied it. Among Carnegie's close friends were Colonel Robert G. Ingersoll and Dr. Moncure Daniel Conway.
There is an interesting story relating to the friendship between Con
way and Carnegie. In the year 1904, a man called on the steel magnate
and requested financial aid for "God's work." Much to his dismay,
Carnegie replied: "I don't believe in God." As a result of this incident,
a prominent educator told a representative of the Philadelphia North American on May 2, 1904: "Andrew Carnegie is an infidel." We learn from George E. Macdonald, for many years editor of The Truth Seeker,
that:
The ironmaster's disbelief, like Mark Twain's, took the form of irreverence. He perpetrated in that year 1904 a practical joke on Dickinson Col
lege, Carlisle, Pennsylvania, a Methodist institution. The college had lately
lost one of its buildings by fire and the mind of its president, Dr. George E. Reed, turned to Carnegie as the possible source of a contribution to erect a
new one. Dickinson chanced to be the institution that, in 1852, had bestowed the prefix reverend on Carnegie's admired friend, Moncure D. Conway; and so, notwithstanding Dr. Conway had dropped the ministerial
title, turned infidel and was, at the time, in Rome as a delegate to the International Freethought Congress, Mr. Carnegie told the man who solicited his money in behalf of the Methodist seat of learning that he would subscribe fifty thousand dollars toward a new building if they would call it
Conway Hall. The trustees consenting, the hall was built and named accordingly. The Truth Seeker chortled with unholy glee to see an institution founded in Calvinism in 1783 by John Dickinson and taken over by the Methodists one hundred and twenty years later, pay this distinguished
honor to a living Freethinker. (160, Vol. II, p. 246)



THE TWILIGHT OK THK CODS 163
The orthodox religionists managed to hold their own as long as they
could prevent scientists and scholars from getting a fair hearing; but
when the colleges and universities became secularized, they began to
lose ground rapidly. Only a few years ago, liberal clergymen lost their
pulpits for saying openly what they believed. The Reverend Dr. Harry
Emerson Fosdick was ousted by the Presbyterian Church, but that
caused him no hardship: Through the good offices of John D. Rocke
feller, Jr., he was called to the pulpit of the Riverside Church in New
York City. Bishop William Montgomery Brown of the Protestant Episcopal Church was unfrocked after announcing that he had become
a Darwinian Evolutionist and a Marxian Socialist. A short time later,
the Reverend Mr. Brown was installed as a Bishop in the Old Catholic
Church, an institution that did not regard his views as heretical. The
Christian churches have become so liberalized in recent years that a few of them even welcome atheists as members. This new movement is
known as Christian Atheism. This may sound outlandish to many, but
nevertheless it is a fact. A timely commentary on this situation has been
made by David Tribe, President of the National Secular Society of
l>ondon:
The novel situation has arisen that, while many freethinkers still describe
themselves as agnostics, some theologians are opting for atheism—Christian
atheism. The adjective makes it respectable. In The New Reformation
(1965), the Bishop of Woolwich took the logical next step from his Honest
to God (1963) to ask: "Can a truly contemporary person not be an atheist?"
In the United States, under the influence of freethinking, Friedrich
Nietzsche's "God is Dead," ... a number of modernist theologians, notably, Thomas J. J. Altizer of Emory University, William Hamilton of Col
gate Rochester Divinity School and Paul Van Buren of Temple University
have formed the Death of God school of Christian Atheists. The lack of
anxiety, indeed the relief, of these liberal churchmen comes from realization
of what freethinking atheists have always believed: in the Bishop of Wool
wich's words, "God is intellectually superfluous, emotionally dispensable
and morally intolerable." (252, p. 64)
William Robert Miller is a liberal Christian layman, who has written
a thoughtful book, with the title Goodbye, Jehovah, and we believe that
what he has to say is important:
When Edison invented the electric light, there were more people with
degrees in theology than in the sciences. Today the reverse is true. Factual
knowledge looms larger than in earlier centuries. Modern man believes in

electricity because it works, not because he sees in it anything supernatural

it would not occur to him to look to God for an explanation. Hence, whatever we may wish to believe about God, much of our outlook on life is functionally atheistic. Despite nineteen centuries of a Gospel of the brotherhood of man, white Christians, until recently entertained notions of racial superiority which have been exploded only under the impact of genetics, scientific anthropology and other kinds of fact finding. In a generation or two, the jaunty slogan of free, white and twenty-one has been overtaken by freedom now and black power. . . . The man of today is constantly assailed with images of a
better life; unlike his ancestors, he has little reason to be content with his lot
or grateful to God just to survive. (172, pp. 7-8)
Time-Life Books publishes many works dealing with issues and problems of contemporary interest. Notable among such publications is
Can Christianity Survive? by the staff of Time, edited by Joe David Brown. One chapter of this book is entitled 'is God Dead?" There we
read: "The young theologians who are leaders in today's death of God
group—principally, Thomas J. J. Altizer, 40, of Emory University;
William Hamilton, 43, of New College, Sarasota, Florida, and Paul
Van Buren, 43, of Temple University—also believe that God is indeed
absolutely dead, but propose to carry on without God." (26, p. 19)
Dr. Thomas Altizer, for several years Associate Professor of Bible
and Religion at Emory University, Atlanta, Georgia, has written a
book with the challenging title, The Gospel of Christian Atheism. The
book is scholarly and not easy to read, but Dr. Altizer honestly states
what he believes: "True, every man today who is open to experience
knows that God is absent, but only the Christian knows that God is
dead, that the death of God is a final and irrevocable event, and that
God's death has actualized in our history a new and liberated human
ity." (2, p. Ill)
Dr. Paul M. Van Buren, Associate Professor of Religion at Temple
University, has enjoyed a fine educational background. After gradua
tion from Harvard University, he studied at the Episcopal Theological
school, also in Cambridge, Massachusetts, and he then worked for his
doctorate at the University of Basel, Switzerland, where he studied
under Karl Barth. He was ordained to the priesthood of the Episcopal
Church in 195 1. When asked how his ideas fit in with the other Death
of-God theologians, Van Buren answered: "The death of God for me
is that he never existed. I think all of us are atheists in the traditional
sense. But Hamilton and I believe that the image of Christ is central for
Christianity." (26, pp. 26-27)

We have mentioned the opinions of Altizer and Van Buren, and now we must present the position of Dr. Hamilton. According to Mil
ler:
William Hamilton of New College in Sarasota, Florida, has forcefully insisted on the historical death of God, asserting not simply that God does not exist but that the God who was once alive is now dead. Dietrich Bonhoeffer stated that the philosopher's metaphysical God had ceased, in our
time, to be tenable. Hamilton . . . presses the point further to include the transcendant God of the Bible, but neither he nor most of the other death of God theologians has attempted a systematic explanation of how or when God died. (172, p. 143)
The question of how and when God died is discussed in a recent book Excerpts from the Diaries of the Late God, selected and edited by
Anthony Towne (250). Towne, a talented poet and journalist, states in his preface:
After the unfortunate death of the late God in the autumn of 1965, it was generally accepted that the process of divine revelation had also come to its end. Dead gods, some declared, tell no tales. This hasty conclusion has proved to be without foundation. The deeply lamented Supreme Being, it
turns out, characteristically foresighted, had scrupulously kept throughout his protracted reign, meticulous diaries of the events attendant upon it. It would be the height of presumption on my part to speculate as to why I, of all miserable sinners, should have been chosen as the vehicle for these, the very last of ultimate revelations. . . . Certainly, no one ought, on account of this unique burden, impute to me any merit beyond the ordinary,
if that. More pertinent, surely, is the sad happenstance that it fell to me to compose, for The New York Times and Motive magazine, the obituary of God, a document included at the end of this book for the benefit of readers
who superstitiously skip that fascinating page of the estimable Times and which contains a full report of the circumstances surrounding that most grievous of deaths and the reaction of the world to it. 1 can only suppose that my obituary, inadequate though it humanly had to be, somewhat
pleased the powers that be—or should I say: the powers that remain? I sug
gest, in other words, that the incumbent authorities hoped from me in edit
ing and selecting from God's diaries the same objectivity and good taste I strove to impart to the obituary. To that modest standard I have, in any
event, pledged myself. (250, pp. 1-2)
The document referred to by Towne in the passage cited above is
headlined as follows:
coo is dead in Georgia—Eminent Deity succumbs during Surgery Succession in Doubt as all Creation Groans. LBJ Orders Flags at Half

Special to the New York Times, Atlanta, Ga., Nov. 9—God, creator of
the universe, principal deity of the world's Jews, ultimate reality of Christi

The cause of death could not be immediately determined, pending an au

According to Towne's book, both houses of Congress met in Wash
ington and promptly adjourned on November 9, 1965, after passing a
joint resolutionexpressing "grief and great respect for the departed spirit
ual leader. " Senator Wayne Morse, Democrat of Oregon, objected,
holding that the resolution violated the principle of separation of church
and state, but was overruled by Vice President Hubert Humphrey,
who said that "this is not a time for partisan politics."
From Independence, Missouri, ex-President Harry S Truman was
quoted as saying: "I'm always sorry to hear somebody is dead. It's a
damn shame." In Gettysburg, Pennsylvania, former President Dwight
D. Eisenhower authorized the release of the following statement: "Mrs.
Eisenhower joins me in heartfelt sympathy to the family and many
friends of the late God. He was, I always felt, a force for moral good in
the universe. Those of us who were privileged to know him admired
the probity of his character, the breadth of his compassion, the depth of
his intellect. Generous almost to a fault, his many acts of kindness to
America will never be forgotten. It is a very great loss indeed. He will
be missed."

Near the end of the obituary is the following note: "(Next Sunday's
New York Times will include, without extra charge, a 24-page full

of God's long reign, the circumstances of his sudden and untimely

Towne concludes the obituary with the following announcement:
"There has been as yet no statement from Jesus, but a close associate,
the Holy Ghost, has urged prayer and good works. He also said that it
is the wish of the family that in lieu of flowers, contributions be made
to the Building Fund for the Cathedral of St. John the Divine in New
York City so that the edifice may be finished." (250, p. 108)
An interesting question to consider is: How did religious doctrines
which had survived for ages finally reach their nadir in the middle of
the twentieth century? It seems to us that the man who had the clearest
insight on this question was the late Dr. Paul Carus. He was a distin
guished philosopher, the author of many worthwhile books, and the ed
itor of two scholarly magazines, The Open Court and The Monist. His
finest book was The History of the Devil and the Idea of Evil from the
Earliest Times to the Present Day (42). The author of that work seemed
to think that religion was losing ground among intelligent people, be
cause after such people began to disbelieve the existence of the Devil,
the next step was to doubt the existence of God. After all, if you be
lieve in both a God and a Devil, you can thank God for all the good
things in the world and then blame the Devil for all that is bad. But
once the Devil is dismissed as a myth, then God becomes responsible
for the evil as well as the good. "While the idea of God has received
much attention from philosophers and progressive theologians," Dr.
Carus observed, "its counterpart, the dark figure of the Evil One, has
been much neglected. And yet, the Devil is, after all, a very interesting
personality, grotesque, romantic, humorous, pathetic, nay, even grand
and tragic. And if we have to declare that the idea of God is a symbol
signifying an actual presence in the world of facts, should we not ex
pect that the idea of the Devil also represents a reality?" (42, p. 5)
Good is good only because there is evil, and God is God because there is
a Devil. As evil is not mere negation, so the figure of Satan in religion is not
an idle fancy. . . . According to the account in the second chapter of Gen
esis, Satan is the father of science, for he induced Eve to make Adam taste
of the fruit of knowledge, and the Ophites, a Gnostic sect, worshiped the



168 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
serpent for that reason. Satan produces the unrest in society, which, in spite
of many inconveniences, makes the world move onward and forward; he is
the patron of progress, investigation, and invention. Milton's Satan is a
grand character, a noble souled rebel, who would rather undergo an eter
nity of torture than suffer humiliation. Consider but the fact that, taking the
statements of his adversaries alone, the Devil is the most trustworthy person
in existence. He has been cheated by innumerable sinners, saints, angels,
and (according to various old church legends) even by the good Lord him
self; and yet he has never been found wanting in the literal and punctilious
fulfillment of all his promises; and all the bad experiences he has had in the
course of millenniums have not in the least lowered his character. His mere
word is honored as the holiest oath, or as the best signature verified with
seals and legal witnesses. The instances are rare in which it is known that
persons with whom he has had business transactions have requested him to
sign a contract, to give a pledge, or to show any proof that he would honestly abide by his word; his honesty was never doubted by anybody. And,
mind you, it is not the Devil who boasts of his integrity, but this is the con
clusion at which we arrive from the evidences adduced by his enemies. The
Devil is the father of all misunderstood geniuses. It is he who induces us to
try new paths; he begets originality of thought and deed. He tempts us to
venture into unknown seas for the discovery of new ways to the wealth of
distant Indias. He makes us dream of and hope for more prosperity and
greater happiness. He is the spirit of discontent that embitters our hearts,
but in the end, often leads to a better arrangement of affairs. In truth, he is a
very useful servant of the Almighty, and all the heinous features of his char
acter disappear when we consider the fact that he is necessary in the econ
omy of nature as a wholesome stimulant to action and as the power of re
sistance that evokes the noblest efforts of living beings.
God, being the All in All, regarded as the ultimate authority for conduct,
is neither evil itself nor goodness itself, but, nevertheless, he is in the good,
and he is in the evil. He who has eyes to see, ears to hear and a mind to per
ceive, will read a lesson out of the very existence of evil, a lesson which, in
spite of the terrors it inspires, is certainly not less impressive, nor less di
vine, than the sublimity of a holy life; and thus it becomes apparent that the
existence of Satan is part and parcel of the divine dispensation. Indeed we
must grant that the Devil is the most indispensable and faithful helpmate of
God. To speak mystically, even the existence of the Devil is filled with the
presence of God. (42, pp. 484-88)
Colonel Robert Ingersoll was once asked what he would do to improve the world, were he endowed with supernormal power, and he
answered, "The first thing 1 would do, would be to make health catch
ing instead of disease." A laudable sentiment, we think, but such a



THE TWILIGHT OF THE GODS 169
project does not seem possible. This brings to mind the story of the
meeting of an American Christian missionary with a pagan African
chief. "Chief," the missionary said, "you should embrace my religion
because my God can do anything." The chief answered: "That I can
not believe. I would like to have your God place two mountains next to
each other without having a valley between them."
The future of religion does not seem very bright at present. In the
early ages of the world, men and women worshiped many gods; then
the gods began to die out, until only one was left; and now we are told,
not by infidel scientists, but by Christian theologians, that the one sur
viving deity has died. But let us not despair. We might well take heed
of some advice left to us by Colonel Ingersoll, who said: "Let us put
another O in GOD, and make it GOOD, and let us take the D out of DEVIL and make it EVIL."



CHAPTER NINE
The Nature of Civilization
THE shelves of libraries contain numerous ponderous tomes treating
of the history of civilization. The writers of these works are presuma
bly learned men, possessed of profound knowledge. However, they
have not been notably successful in transmitting their lore to the unini
tiated. One large defect of these so-called authorities is that they rarely,
if ever, get around to telling us what civilization is. Some sort of answer
to the question, What is civilization?, we think, would be of considera
ble value to the reader. On page one, Volume I, of his voluminous The
Story of Civilization, Dr. Will Durant tells us: ''Civilization is social
order promoting cultural creation," which is all very well as far as it
goes, but it does not go far enough. Dictionaries are as a rule not very
helpful when a question of this sort arises; for it does not help us much
to be informed that: "Civilization is a state of being civilized." So we
shall have to attempt to formulate a scientifically accurate definition of
civilization.
To begin, let us say that civilization is a form of culture; so first we
must define culture. Culture, to speak precisely, is patterned behavior,
and may be described as the organized behavior which the individual
person learns either through training or by imitation from other mem
bers of his or her social group. Culture consists of all forms of human
behavior except those which apes and mankind share in common.
Among behavior patterns common to apes and men are impulses to
ward mating and parenthood; the tendencies to show off, to attack oth
ers when angry, and to take flight when frightened; and the desire for
companionship. These are noncultural activities. The forms of behavior
restricted to humanity are truly cultural activities and may be denoted
as the growing of crops, the domestication of animals and plants, the art
170



THE NATURE OF CIVILIZATION 171
of cooking, the weaving of clothes, and the use of language (which may
be defined as the expression of definite ideas by means of the larynx,
lips, and tongue) for the purpose of mutual communication.
Now we should be able to give a satisfactory definition of civiliza
tion, for civilization is nothing more or less than literate culture. A bare
definition would be of little value, but the following brief but lucid ex
planation from the pen of an outstanding anthropologist is illuminating:
A society is civilized only if it contains scholars and scientists. The
scholar consolidates and clarifies the knowledge which has already been ac
quired and hands it on to the scientist, who, thus provided, proceeds to experiment and thus to the increase of knowledge. Without the torch of
learning, the scientist is reduced to groping in the dark, and without the
scientist to use and test the results of his learning, the scholar sinks into a
barren pedantry. Thus scholarship and science, in the widest sense of these
terms, are the warp and woof of civilization. And the scientist, no less than
the scholar, is dependent upon the written word; not only must he be able
to use the learning of the scholars, but he must be able to record the results
of his own investigations. Since, then, civilization depends upon scholarship
and science, and these depend upon writing, civilization can only arise
where the art of writing is known. (104, pp. 3-4)
Any discussion of the nature of civilization involves the idea of prog
ress; so we must have some conception of what constitutes progress. In
the absence of progress, we experience its negative counterpart, retro
gression; so this fact must also be taken into account. Progress means
the increase of knowledge, whereas retrogression is another name for
the loss of knowledge. Since knowledge is just another name for
science, we may say that a civilization is progressive when science
flourishes within its framework. On the other hand, in a culture where
science is on the decline, we have retrogression or regress. The out
standing feature of culture is its dynamism. So civilization, being a
form of culture, cannot remain static; it must change by either going
forward or slipping backward, for it cannot just stand still. In the jun
gles of Asia, Africa, and Central America, explorers have discovered
ruins of great palaces and temples in places as far apart as Angkor,
Pagan, Borobudur, Zimbabwe, Palenque, and Uxmal. These lost cities
were once centers of civilizations which declined and finally expired.
This fact suggests the question: Why do civilizations wane and disappear? A popular theory advanced by Oswald Spengler in his The De
cline of the West is that nations, like individuals, are born; then pass



172 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
through stages of infancy, youth, maturity, and senility; and eventually
die. As the old civilizations pass off the stage of history, they are suc
ceeded by younger and more vigorous cultures. These, rekindling the
torch of progress, pass it on to the future. Henry Thomas Buckle, in his
History of Civilization, argued that civilization began in southern lands
but improved in quality as it traveled northward. Greek and Roman
cultures were greater than that of Egypt because Greece and Rome
were situated further north than Egypt; and the English people who
lived even further north than the Greco-Romans produced the greatest
of all cultures. Buckle, being a patriotic Englishman of the mid-nine
teenth century, did not follow his hypothesis to its logical conclusion.
For this line of reasoning would lead us to expect that the Eskimos of
the Arctic regions should have reached the very apex of civilization,
dwelling as they do so close to the North Pole. We are told, by those
who regard progress as an innate feature of human societies, that civili
zation is here to stay; for whenever an old civilization gives up the
ghost there is always a new one ready to carry on. This speculation,
plausible though it may seem to its advocates, has nothing to recom
mend it from a pragmatic angle. For, as Lord Raglan observed: "The builders of Babylon and Borobudur, of Uxmal and Zimbabwe and
many another great city of the past, were succeeded not by people who
could revive and carry forward their civilization, but by the savage, the
jungle or the desert." (194, p. 26) Sir Edward Tylor in his classic
Primitive Culture suggested that a study of the decline and fall of various cultures of the past would produce knowledge which would be of
great value to students of the history of civilization. No such compre
hensive study of past cultures, such as that recommended by Tylor, has
as yet been undertaken; but Lord Raglan has made a short survey of
the subject in Chapter IV of How Came Civilization? and we shall at
tempt a brief discussion of this and other evidence. The ancestors of the
present-day inhabitants of Polynesia owned seagoing canoes and
ranged over the vast reaches of the Pacific Ocean establishing them
selves in places so far apart as New Zealand, Hawaii, and Easter Island.
Some modern Polynesians still possess seaworthy canoes, but they
rarely venture out of sight of land and no longer engage in long sea
voyages. Easter Island became famous on account of its ancient giant
stone statues. It is believed that these statues were constructed by the
ancestors of the modern inhabitants of the island, but the modern
Easter islanders have lost the art of carving stone. And there have been
other cultural losses; for when Captain Cook visited the island in 1774,



THE NATURE OF CIVILIZATION 173
the natives built canoes which they used in fishing. They now fish by
swimming, canoe-building having among them become a lost art. An
cient wooden plaques on Easter Island contain picture writing similar
to ancient Asiatic scripts, whereas the present dwellers can neither
create nor understand such inscriptions. The Maoris of New Zealand
were, in times past, proficient carvers in jade, but this is now a long lost
art among them. Professor Dixon notes that "the Polynesians in their
eastward drift into the Pacific, lost textiles, pottery, metal-working and
gave up the use of the bow." (68, p. 280)
Similar examples of retrogression have been observed among the
Melanesians. Among the people of the New Hebrides, as late as the
seventeenth century, the craft of the weaver was in a flourishing condi
tion; whereas today the art of weaving has been entirely lost. Likewise,
pottery manufacture has expired in many parts of the same region.
Certain arts and crafts were the monopolies of particular families in
Melanesia. When these families died out, the skills were lost to the
tribes. It was in this way that the art of boat-building became extinct in
the Torres Islands. Similarly, pottery-making became a lost art in
Santo, and the same fate befell weaving in Ureparapara. The Papuans
are still acquainted with the knowledge of weaving fabrics with the hand loom, but this art is declining among them, even where European
influence was absent.
In Africa, it has been noticed by W. D. Hambly that "in time past,
the Cape Bushmen, also some Hottentots, made pottery, but this is
now a lost art." (109, p. 332) In Rhodesia are the remains of ancient gold mines, some of them 1 50 feet deep. These mines were worked by
Africans in the past, but their present-day successors know nothing of
mining, lacking even the candles and lamps without which these mines
could never have been worked. The ancient people of Zimbabwe, in
Mashonaland, were miners of iron on an extensive scale, since relics of
smelting furnaces have been found in the ruins, but this skill is now
only a memory of a distant past. "How far back this civilization dates it
is difficult to say," asserted Professor DuBois, "a great deal depending
on the dating of the Iron Age in South Africa. If it was the same as in
the Mediterranean regions, the earliest limit was 1000 B.C.; it might,
however, have been earlier, especially if, as seems probable, the use of
iron originated in Africa. On the other hand, the culmination of this
culture has been placed by some as late as the Middle Ages." (73, p.
74) In Uganda, Kenya, and Tanzania are extensive remains of a system
of graded roads, and terraces which were used for cultivation; but few



174 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
of the present inhabitants of this area make any use of the terraces. In
West Africa, remains of megalithic structures have been found among
peoples with a primitive type of culture, which shows that the ancestors
of these peoples possessed a higher type of culture in the past than is
extant in these areas at the present day.
In the Americas and in the northern part of Asia may be observed
instances of cultural regression. The Eskimo culture is not as well de
veloped now as it was in the past; for harpoon heads, discovered on the
sites of ancient villages in northern Alaska are considerably more elabo
rate and complex than the modern ones of the same area. Formerly,
these people built houses of stone and earth. Though the practice is not
yet extinct, it is in a state of decline. New houses of this type are rarely
built, and the old houses have largely ceased to be repaired. The Pueblo
Indians of the United States are now restricted to the northern parts of
Arizona and New Mexico; whereas about a thousand years ago, the
Pueblo culture covered all the territory now embraced by Arizona,
New Mexico, Utah, and a part of Texas. For a time, this region en
joyed prosperity and a fairly high level of culture; but, even before con
tact with Europeans, this culture disintegrated and the original inhabit
ants were replaced by tribes with a lower culture level, such as the
Apache. In the arts of building construction and pottery-making, the
Pueblo Indians have displayed a continuing record of regression. The
Incan culture of Peru and that of the Mayans of Yucatan were in an
advanced state of decay long before Columbus visited America. The
culture of the Mound Builders of the Ohio and Mississippi Valleys be
came completely extinct in pre-Columbian times. The Ostiak, who
now dwell along the Arctic shores of western Siberia, are believed to
have lived in times past much further to the south, in the region of the
Altai, and to have possessed a knowledge of metal working which was
lost in their northern migration.
Scientists and scholars who accept the theory of progress hold that
any human society, if left alone, tends to improve itself in a cultural
sense; but all objective evidence is against this position. "What the facts
suggest," Lord Raglan observed, "is that the natural state of man is a
state of low savagery, and that towards that state he always tends to re
vert whenever he is not checked, or forced in the opposite direction, by
that unexplained, but highly artificial, localized and spasmodic process
which we know as the progress of civilization." (194, p. 38) The prog
ress of Western civilization, during the last three or four hundred
years, has blinded many students of culture to the fact that only an un



THI. NATURK OK CIVILIZATION 175
rclcnring struggle can maintain a civilization. To advance the same re
quires an even greater effort. On purely theoretical grounds, progress
is not essential to the maintenance of civilization. We can imagine a so
ciety which neither lost old knowledge nor acquired new intelligence
in the various arts and sciences, and we can think of this dead-level of
culture surviving for ages. But, in practice, this could not happen. For
the scholars of this hypothetical society would, in time, specialize in
certain branches of knowledge, and the decline of neglected fields of
science would eventually lead to the retrogression of the entire culture.
Inevitably, there would be famines, plagues, wars, invasions, fires,
floods, earthquakes, and other disasters, which would lead to the loss of
libraries, records, scientists, and scholars. In its essential nature, civiliza
tion is dynamic, which is to say that it is always in a state of flux. A civ
ilization may slip backward; but to go forward, it has to be pushed.
In the study of the origin and evolution of culture, there are two
main schools of thought: the Evolutionary School and the Diffusionisr
School. The evolutionists believe that all the various civilizations origi
nated independently and then went through certain stages of cultural
change. The diffusionists, to the contrary, believe that from one origi
nal culture all the other civilizations evolved. Modern anthropologists
who were considered adherents of the evolutionary school were Lewis
H. iMorgan, Sir E. B. Tylor, Sir J. G. Frazer, and Professor Clark
Wissler. The outstanding advocates of diffusionism were Sir Grafton
Elliot Smith, Professor W. J. Perry, and Ford Raglan. Since the next
chapter treats of the origin of civilization, some of the ideas mentioned
in this chapter will be more fully elaborated and elucidated.



CHAPTER TEN
The Origin of Civilization
THE essential characteristic of civilized people is that they live in
cities; but before men could construct cities, they had to have possessed
