hard metal tools. For countless millennia, before the dawn of the Age
of Metals, the only tools were those made of wood and stone. The ear
liest tool users were anthropoid apes, and the cleverest among these
tool-using apes evolved into the earliest type of humanity. Man
branched off from the apes about twenty-five million years ago, and he
has been civilized, we believe, for fewer than twenty-five thousand
years. Human culture may be divided into three periods: savagery, bar
barism, and civilization. The American anthropologist, Lewis Henry
Morgan, has traced this evolution of culture from savagery to civiliza
tion in the third chapter of Part I of his famous book, Ancient Society.
This scheme has been adversely criticized by some anthropologists, but
is still largely accepted by other students of human culture. According
to Morgan, the stages of savagery and barbarism were broken down
into three sub-periods each. These, with the period of civilization, give
us seven ethnical stages:
1 Lower Status of Savagery. This stage of human culture began
with the origin of humanity and ended with the acquisition of a fish
subsistence and of a knowledge of the use of fire. Men and women of
these early days were food-gatherers, subsisting on fruits and nuts. The
origin of articulate speech belongs to this period.
2. Middle Status of Savagery. This phase of human development
commenced with the acquirement of a fish subsistence and the art of making and using fire, and ended with the invention of the bow and
arrow. In this period, the human race left its original habitat and spread
out over the earth.
3. Upper Status of Savagery. This culture stage began with the in
176



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 177
vcnrion of the bow and arrow and terminared with rhe invention of the
art of pottery. This closes the period of savagery.
4. Lower Status of Barbarism. This sub-period began with the manu
facture of pottery and ended in the Old World with the domestication
of animals, and in the New World with the cultivation of maize and
other plants by irrigation, and the use of adobe, brick, and stone in the
building of houses.
5. Middle Status of Barbarism. This stage extended from the domes
tication of animals, irrigation, and brick and stone architecture to the
invention of the process of smelting iron ore.
6. Upper Status of Barbarism. It began with the manufacture of iron
implements and ended with the invention of a phonetic alphabet and
the use of writing in literary composition.
7. Status of Civilization. This period began with the use of a pho
netic alphabet and the art of producing literary records, and continues
to the present time.
Some modern anthropologists would prefer a different alignment of
the stages of the evolution of early human culture. A good example of this scheme has been outlined by Professor George Thomson:
The surviving tribes of modern times have been assigned to the fol
lowing categories in virtue of their predominant mode of food-production: Lower Hunters (food-gathering and hunting); Higher Hunters (hunting
and fishing); Pastoral (two grades); Agricultural (three grades). The Higher Hunter hunting grade is distinguished from the lower by the use of
the bow in addition to the spear. In the second pastoral grade, stock-raising
is supplemented by agriculture. Of the three agricultural grades, the first is
characterized by garden tillage, the second by field tillage, and in the third
field tillage is supplemented by stock-raising. This classification is, of
course, an abstraction. The categories are nor mutually exclusive. Thus, food gathering is maintained among the higher hunters and hunting
throughout the pastoral and agricultural grades, but in each case with di
minishing importance. Nor do they constitute a strict chronological se
quence. Stock-raising and tillage have been universally preceded by hunt
ing and that, in turn, by food gathering, but the relation between stock
raising and tillage is variable, being largely determined by geographical fac
tors. Some parts of the world are rich in domestic animals, others in cultivable plants. Others, again, are rich in both, and in these, the two modes of production may have been combined from the beginning in the form of
mixed farming. (248, p. 9)
The career of man up to the end of the New Stone Age was dis
cussed in a previous chapter. Following the stone ages, we learn from



178 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the discoveries of the archaeologists that there were three metal ages in
the following sequence: Copper, Bronze, and Iron. According to Pro
fessor Robert Lowie, the Copper Age may be traced back to 4000
B.C., the Bronze Age started about 3000 B.C., and the Iron Age had
its beginning about 2000 B.C. Let us say frankly that this scheme is of
dubious validity; for central Africa, Polynesia, Finland, northern
Russia, southern India, Australia, North America, and Japan bypassed
the Copper and Bronze Ages and made the transit directly from stone
to iron. Bronze, even in cultures where iron was unknown, seems to
have been a luxury metal, restricted to aristocrats, kings, and priests.
The commoners, therefore, had to get along with implements of wood
and stone. The Iron Age was the key factor leading to the genesis of
civilization. The bearing of the discovery of iron smelting on the origin
of civilization was brilliantly stated by Morgan, as follows:
When the barbarian, advancing step by step, had discovered the native
metals, and learned to melt them in the crucible and to cast them in moulds;
when he had alloyed native copper with tin and produced bronze; and
finally, when by a still greater effort of thought, he had invented the fur
nace, and produced iron from the ore, nine-tenths of the battle for civiliza
tion was gained. Furnished with iron tools, capable of holding both an edge
and a point, mankind was certain of attaining to civilization. The produc
tion of iron was the event of events in human experience, without a parallel,
and without an equal, beside which all other inventions and discoveries
were inconsiderable, or at least, subordinate. Out of it came the metallic
hammer and anvil, the axe and the chisel, the plow with an iron point, the
iron sword; in fine, the basis of civilization which may be said to rest upon
this metal. The want of iron tools arrested the progress of mankind in bar
barism. There, they would have remained to the present hour had they
failed to bridge the chasm. It seems probable that the conception and the
process of smelting iron ore came but once to man. It would be a singular
satisfaction could it be known to what tribe and family we are indebted for
this knowledge, and with it for civilization. (177, pp. 42^-3)
Evidence garnered since Morgan's day seems to point to Central Af
rica as the place of origin of the Iron Age. The American anthropolo
gist, Professor A. F. Chamberlain, expressed himself on this question in
the following words:
The question of the origin of the art of iron smelting is now being
treated in detail by ethnologists and, while general agreement has not been
reached, the mass of evidence so far disclosed has convinced eminent men



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 179
of science like Boas and Von Luschan rhar the smelting of iron was first discovered by the African Negroes, from whom, by way of Egypt and Asia Minor, this art made its way into Europe and the rest of the Old World
(44).
In his capacity of visiting Professor of Anthropology to Atlanta Uni
versity, Franz Boas made the following declaration:
It seems likely that at a time when the European was still satisfied with rude stone tools, the African had invented or adopted the art of smelting
iron. Consider for a moment what this invention has meant for the advance
of the human race. As long as the hammer, knife, saw, drill, the spade, and the hoe had to be chipped out of stone, or had to be made of shell or hard
wood, effective industrial work was not impossible, but difficult. A great progress was made when copper found in large nuggets was hammered out into tools and later on shaped by melting, and when bronze was introduced;
but the true advancement of industrial life did not begin until the hard iron
was discovered. It seems not unlikely that the people who made the marvelous discovery of reducing iron ores by smelting were the African Negroes. Neither ancient Europe nor ancient Western Asia, nor ancient China knew the iron, and everything points to its introduction from Africa. At the
time of the great African discoveries toward the end of the past century, the
trade of the blacksmith was found all over Africa, from north to south, and from east to west. With his simple bellows and a charcoal fire, he reduced the ore that is found in many parts of the continent and forged implements
of great usefulness and beauty. (Atlanta University Leaflet No. 19, cited by W. E. B. DuBois in 74, pp. 114-15)
The Belgian anthropologist, Professor Emil Torday, traveled widely
in Africa, and he made the following statement in regard to the antiq
uity of iron culture in that continent:
That iron could be discovered by accident in Africa seems beyond doubt,
if this is so in any other parts of the world, 1 am not competent to say. 1 will only remind you that Schweinfurth and Petherick record the fact that in the northern part of East Africa smelting furnaces are worked without artificial air current and on the other hand, Stuhlmann and Kollmann found near Victoria Nyanza that the natives simply mixed powdered ore with
charcoal and by introduction of air currents obtained the metal. These simple processes make it simple that iron should have been discovered in East or Central Africa. No bronze implements have ever been found in black
Africa; had the Africans received iron from the Egyptians, bronze would
have preceded this metal and all traces of it would not have disappeared.
Black Africa was, for a long time, an exporter of iron, and even in the
twelfth century, exports to India and Java are recorded by Idrisi.



180 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
It is difficult to imagine that Egypt should have obtained it from Europe
where the oldest find (in Hallstadt) cannot be of an earlier period than 800
B.C., or from Asia, where iron is not known before 1000 B.C., . . . while
iron beads have been only recently discovered by Messrs. G. A. Wain
wright and Bushe Fox in a predynastic grave, and where a piece of this
metal, possibly a tool, was found in the masonry of the great pyramid.
(Professor Emil Torday, in the Journal of the Royal Anthropological Insti
tute, Volume XLIII, pp. 414-15)
That iron tools were employed in the building of the Great Pyramid
was stated as a fact by Herodotus; and on statements of fact we may
safely rely on the judgment of the Father of History (who also might
properly be called "the Father of Anthropology"). In the opinion of
Professor Haddon, "The writings of Herodotus [C. 480-425 B.C.] are
a veritable storehouse of information from the highest civilizations
down to the veriest savagery, and his work has lost none of its freshness
or value through lapse of time. As a matter of fact, modern investiga
tions, carried out in the areas treated of by him, more frequently
confirm and amplify rather than refute his statements." (103, pp. 100
1) August Mencken, a civil engineer of many years' experience, wrote
a fine little book on the construction of the Great Pyramid, and in this
work he expressed the opinion that "little is known about the tools
used, but the cutting points of stone picks and the edges of chisels were
probably made of steel." (170, p. 56) Certain modern writers state
dogmatically that implements of iron and steel were unknown in an
cient Egypt, but this position is certainly erroneous, and the evidence is
nor lacking to show that the contrary opinion is the true one. More
than a century ago, an Egyptologist of high repute made the following
statement:
Iron and copper mines are found in the Egyptian desert, which were worked in old times; and the monuments of Thebes, and even the tombs about Memphis, dating more than 4000 years ago, represent butchers
sharpening their knives on a round bar of metal attached to their apron,
which from its blue color, can only be steel; and the distinction between the bronze and iron weapons in the tomb of Rameses III, one painted red, the
other blue, leaves no doubt of both having been used (as in Rome) at the same periods. In Ethiopia, iron was much more abundant than in Egypt, and Herodotus states that copper was a rare metal there; though we may
doubt his assertion of prisoners in that country having been bound with fet
ters of gold." (275, Vol. II, p. 155)



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 181
We shall have more to say about this subject in the next chapter.
Among the prerequisites of civilization is an adequate food supply.
It, in turn, depends upon the domestication of certain plants and ani
mals. A stable civilization demands a cultivated plant with a high yield
of storable food, and a draft animal for carrying heavy loads and pulling
carts and plows. Besides this, av dependable animal or plant source of
fibcii ii> needed. 1 he chief available plants are the cereals, potatoes, and
soya beans; and these vary considerably in nutritional value. Maize, in
contrast to wheat or oats, is very deficient in Vitamin B5. A people de
pending largely on a maize diet would be subject to attacks of pellagra,
a skin disorder brought on by a deficiency of Vitamin B5 in the food
supply. "This is probably one reason," declared Professor Haldane,
"why the maize civilizations of Central America never reached the
level of the wheat, barley and rice civilizations of the Old World."
(107, p. 54)
Professor Vavilov and a party of Russian scientists have followed the
modern varieties of wheat back to two primitive groups of wheat, each
of which has been traced to a center of origin. One center was located
in Abyssinia, and the other in or near southeastern Afghanistan. The
Ethiopian center is thought to have been the original source of the agri
culture upon which the civilization of Egypt was based. The Afghan
center is regarded as the primitive source of the Mesopotamian and In
dian wheats, and of the principal varieties now grown in Europe and
North America. Other important food plants have been traced back to
these African and Asiatic centers. Hard wheat, barley, and coffee are of
Abyssinian origin. Soft wheat, turnips, rye, perhaps carrots and some
types of beans, lentils, cotton, and flax have been traced back to the Af
ghan center. Rice may have originated in northeastern India and vari
ous fruit trees in Persia. Evidence uncovered in Neolithic tombs in
Egypt show that barley, rye, millet, flax, and wheat were cultivated in
the Nile Valley before the dawn of civilization. Animal husbandry also
flourished in the same region, for short-horned cattle, sheep, and goats
were bred and the donkey was domesticated and employed as a beast of
burden. Attempts were likewise made to tame the antelope, deer, ga
zelle, and ibex. The establishment of agriculture on a large scale and
the extensive development of animal husbandry, together with the ac
quisition of metal tools, enabled ancient peoples to settle permanently
in villages; and these innovations induced a marked division of labor be
tween the sexes. The women specialized in the rearing of children,



182 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
cooking, spinning, weaving, and truck gardening; while the men went
in for hunting, fighting, tending of livestock, and the sowing and plow
ing of the fields. In these early days of agriculture, the land was the
common property of the clan or tribe; likewise, the first herds of
domesticated animals were owned collectively. But with the increase of
productivity, it was found expedient on grounds of efficiency periodi
cally to allot plots of land to individual cultivators, and this led in time
to the institution of private property. In a similar fashion, cattle, too,
became recognized as private property; although the pastures, as a rule,
continued to be held in common ownership by the group. Private
property did not turn out to be an unmixed blessing; for intertribal
conflicts over land and cattle led to warfare. This, in turn, brought
about slavery. Large-scale farming and the tending of herds and flocks
involved a lot of hard labor; so, when a powerful tribe subdued a
weaker foe, it became customary for the victors to reduce their van
quished foes to the status of servitude. It was not only more humane
but also more economical to exploit the labor of the conquered enemy
rather than to take his life! Although in its origin slavery may have
been an improvement over murder or cannibalism, in the long run, as
the annals of history show, it became a great curse to the human race.
The birth of civilization brought about profound changes in human so
ciety, such as the passage from primitive communism to private prop
erty and slavery, from magic to religion, from food gathering to agri
culture, from mother right to father right, and from relative peace to
chronic warfare. An interesting feature of the transition from barba
rism to civilization was the decline of the matriarchal family and the
rise of the patriarchal family.
When tribes of various localities formed the habit of assembling pe
riodically at a common meeting place for purposes of trade, worship,
and deliberation on problems of mutual interest, the city resulted. The
tribal chief of greatest power and influence became the ruler of the
city-state; and these early rulers were generally priest kings, and estab
lished their headquarters in temples, which were not only places of
worship but also warehouses. In later times, as trade and commerce de
veloped, and metal currency was invented, the temple became the first
bank. The necessity of keeping records in the process of operating the
early temple and city administrations was a powerful factor in the ori
gin and development of the art of writing, one of the key foundations
of civilization. As to just where and when civilization commenced,
there is no agreement among the recognized authorities. "All we can



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 183
say," Lord Raglan suggested, "is that its founders must have possessed
all that there then was of knowledge, but at the same time, must have
succeeded in preserving or freeing themselves from that subservience
to the past which presents all savages and indeed, the great majority of
the human race, from originating anything." (194, p. 182) In the con
siderable literature on the history of civilization, there is much discus
sion concerning the bearing of environment and race on cultural ori
gins. A well-known example of this type of literature is a multivolumed
work entitled A Study of History, by Professor Arnold J. Toynbee.
We are told in this study of history that "the Minoan civilization was a
response to the challenge of the sea, the Egyptian civilization, a re
sponse to the challenge of the Nile." (251, p. 438)
This is a fair statement of the environmental theory of the origin of
civilization. This hypothesis may still have some advocates among his
torians, but it has no defenders of any consequence among anthropolo
gists. It is a truism to say that good harbors make a maritime civilization
possible, but we have no evidence that they produce such a culture.
Professor Roland B. Dixon, of Harvard University, has carefully stud
ied the role of environment in the building of cultures, and he reached
conclusions diametrically opposite to those of Toynbee:
A well indented coastline with many harbors and inlets, tends, it is said,
to develop seafaring life, and numerous striking instances of people with
maritime cultures living on such coasts are pointed out. Bur many equally
striking exceptions exist, as, for example in Tasmania, where with just such
a coastline, the people were virtually destitute of any form of craft. Again,
islands are said often to develop as centers of maritime culture, with far
reaching trade and commerce. The instances of Crete, the British Isles and
Japan are cited in confirmation of this. It is true that Crete was 2 striking example of such development; it is not so today. The two other cases must
be also accepted with reserve, for England did not become a maritime
country until Elizabethan times, when economic influences external to it
self and unrelated to its insular character, were instrumental in bringing about the change. Japan's development as a maritime and commercial state
is really only a matter of the last fifty years, and has depended more on her
far reaching adoption of European culture than on the insular character of
her environment. (68, pp. 16-17)
The importance of the environment is not being denied. We merely
point out that it acts as a limiting agent rather than a determining one.
The late J. M. Robertson directed our attention to the fact that pri
mary civilizations arise in territories favorable to the regular production



184 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
of an abundant food supply, and lying inland, so as nor to be exposed to
piratical incursions. Robertson observed that a fertile coast land could
be defended only by a strong community. (See 209, Chapter IV.) On
the other hand, the protection of the environment must not be so ex
treme as to produce complete cultural isolation. What is demanded is
adequate protection, but not paralyzing isolation. The true role of the
environment as a factor in the origin of cultures was illustrated by An
drew Reid Cowan in an examination of the civilization of Egypt:
The population had perhaps almost nothing to fear from the nomad; less
probably in the Nile Valley than in the Tigris-Euphrates system; causing Egypt perhaps to be won earlier than Mesopotamia. There is almost no nourishment for man in the unmitigated desert surrounding Egypt. The sea
in ancient times was a sufficient barrier and it was only in Greek times that
it was effectively penetrated. (53, pp. 46-47)
The idea that certain varieties of the human race have a highly de
veloped capacity for building civilization, while others possess this tal
ent in a lesser degree, or lack it altogether, is a complete fallacy, having
no foundation in fact. Professor Toynbee has adopted this fallacy in
toto. He argues that, up to our own time, twenty-one civilizations have
flourished in various parts of the world; and we are told that: "The
Black Race has not helped to create any civilization, while the Polyne
sian White Race has helped to create one civilization, the Brown Race two, the Yellow Race three, the Red Race and the Nordic White Race,
four apiece, the Alpine White Race, nine, and the Mediterranean
White Race, ten." (251, p. 234) This sort of propaganda may be rel
ished by historians as a class, but anthropologists and archaeologists will
reject it; for they know better. We do not propose to dwell on all of
the numerous errors in Toynbee's statement—that would be a waste of
time and space—but we should like to note the absolutely false asser
tion that the black race has made no creative contribution to civiliza
tion.
Herr Eugen Georg, an able German historical scholar, disagreed
with the professor absolutely on this point. He declared:
A splendid era of black seems to have preceded all the later races! There must once have been a tremendous Negro expansion, since the original masters of all the lands between Iberia and the Cape of Good Hope and
East India were primitive and probably dwarfed black men. We have long
had proof that a primitive Negroid race of pigmies once lived around the Mediterranean. Blacks were the first to plow the mud of the Nile, they



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 185
were the dark skinned, curly haired Kushires. Blacks were masters of Su
meria and Babylon before it became the country of the four tongues. And in India, the kingdom of the Dravidian Monarchs, the black and godless
enemies existed until the period of written history. Many thousands of
years before Christ, great cultures bloomed in the rank, rich valleys of the
Yang-tse-kiang, the Ho, Indus, Euphrates, Nile and Congo rivers, while
Oceania, Central America, the highlands of the Andes were centers of
human settlement. As monumental as if planted on earth by the gods them
selves, the Egyptian culture gleamed in iridescent holiness, sustained by the
despotic will of omnipotent Pharaohs. (96, pp. 44-45)
A similar opinion was advanced by Professor Ernest Albert Hooton,
anthropologist of Harvard University:
Archaeology has brought to light, on the banks of the Niger in West Af
rica, in the upper part of the Nile Valley and in Rhodesia, extensive monu
mental remains of great and extinct civilizations which seem to have been Negro or Negroid. The Negroes have been for an indeterminate period of time very skilled iron workers and may have developed for themselves the
knowledge of smelting iron ores. A large share of the responsibility for the
great civilization of India must be assigned to Negroes, since there is un
questionably a very strong Negroid strain in the Indian population. (120, pp. 591-92)
Herodotus, who spent two years traveling in Egypt in the fifth cen
tury B.C., had no hesitation in assigning the ancient Egyptians to the
black race. H. G. Wells noted: ''Herodotus remarked upon a series of
resemblances between the Colchians and the Egyptians." (266, p. 184)
But Wells could not find the space to actually cite the remarks of the
Father of History. In commenting on the Colchians, Herodotus com
pared them with the Egyptians:
There can be no doubt that the Colchians are an Egyptian race. Before I heard any mention of the fact from others, I had remarked it myself. My own conjectures were founded first on the fact that they are black skinned
and have woolly hair, which certainly amounts to but little, since several
other nations are so too; but further, and more especially, on the circum
stance that the Colchians, the Egyptians, and the Ethiopians are the only
nations who have practiced circumcision from the earliest times. I will add a further proof to the identity of the Egyptians and the Colchians. These two
nations weave their linen in exactly the same way, and this is a way entirely
unknown to the rest of the world; they also in their whole mode of life, and
in their language, resemble one another. (117, Book II, p. 1 14-15)



186 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The descendants of these ancient Colchians still survive in the Ab
khazian Autonomous Republic, which is in the southern part of the So
viet Union, and their land is often referred to as the Black Soviet. (An
interesting account of these exotic people and their land may be found
in 212, p. 291.)
Some authorities have held that the civilization of Egypt is the oldest
of the river valley cultures of the Old World; whereas others awarded
that priority to Western Asia, or India. It has also been suggested by
certain students that, since all these cultures possess certain similar
traits, they may have all evolved from an older common civilization.
There has also been considerable disagreement concerning the proper
racial classification of the original founders of civilization; some authori
ties assigning them to the caucasoid or white race and others to the ne
groid or black race, or to an intermediate mixed brown race. So we
shall have to discuss briefly the controversial question of the classifica
tion of the so-called races of man.
The early modern classifications of the varieties of the human species
were geographical in nature. The famous naturalist, Linnaeus ( 1 707
1778), listed four races according to continent: European (white), Af
rican (black), Asiatic (yellow), and American (red). Blumenbach, in
1775, added a fifth type, the Oceanic (brown) race. In geography texts
of fifty years ago the races of mankind were classified as Ethiopian
(black), Caucasian (white), American (red), Mongolian (yellow), and
Malayan (brown). The French naturalist, Baron Cuvier, in 1800
traced all varieties of men back to Ham, Shern, and Japheth, the three
sons of Noah. After that day, race classification developed into a con
test. By 1873, Haeckel had discovered, or invented, no fewer than
twelve distinct races of mankind, and this was only a starter. A few
years later, he had uncovered twenty-two more races, bringing the
grand total of human types up to thirty-four. Deniker, in 1900, hit the
jackpot by postulating the human species as existing in the form of six
grand divisions, seventeen divisions, and twenty-nine races. Despite all
this industry among anthropological taxonomists, there is as yet no
agreement on the proper classification of racial types. Professor Franz
Boas of Columbia University decided that the human race consisted of only two sub-types; and this scheme has been admirably outlined by
Professor George A. Dorsey:
Open your atlas to a map of the world. Look at the Indian Ocean—on
the west, Africa; on the north, the three great southern peninsulas of Asia;



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 187
on the east, a chain of great islands terminating in Australia. Wherever that
Indian Ocean touches land, it finds dark-skinned people with strongly de
veloped jaws, relatively long arms and kinky or frizzly hair. Call that the
Indian Ocean or Negroid division of the human race.
Now look at the Pacific Ocean—on one side, the two Americas; on the
other, Asia. (Geographically, Europe is a rail to the Asiatic kite.) The abo
riginal population of the Americas and of Asia, north of its southern penin
sula, was a light skinned people with straight hair, relatively short arms and a face without prominent jaws. Call that the Pacific Ocean or Mongoloid
division. (72, pp. 44-45)
Professor Alfred L Kroeber decided that the peoples of Europe have
bleached out enough to merit classification as a distinct racial group. This would add a European or Caucasoid division to the Negroid and
Mongoloid types recognized by Professor Boas. This tri-racial classi
fication has found widespread acceptance. The best statement of this
system was given by Professor Barnes: lw The yellow race is not only
yellow in color, but also has a very round brachyccphalic head and very
straight hair, round in cross section. The black race stands at the oppo
site extreme, with a very long dolichocephalic head and curly hair, flat
in cross section. Between these two extremes falls the white race with
an intermediate head form and wavy hair, elliptical in cross section."
(8, pp. 21-22) Orientalists have formed two schools of thought concerning the
location of the world's earliest civilization. The Panegyptians claim pri
ority for the Nile Valley; whereas the Panbab) lonians make a similar
claim for Mesopotamia. But some experts have held that an earlier
common culture would be a more reasonable hypothesis. The fol
lowing opinion was expressed by a distinguished Orientalist: "It would be wrong to say that the Egyptians borrowed from the Sumerians or the Sumerians from the Egyptians, but it may be submitted that the lit
erati of both peoples borrowed their theological systems from some
common but exceedingly ancient source. The similarity between the
two companies of gods is too close to be accidental/ 1
(31, p. 155) A pioneer American Egyptologist advanced the following view:
In both Babylonia and Egypt, the convenient and basic number (360), of fundamental importance in the division of the circle, and therefore, in geography, astronomy and time measurement, had its origin in the number of days in the year in the earliest known form of the calendar. While its use seems to be older in Egypt than in Babylonia, there is no way to determine
with certainty that we owe it exclusively to either of these two countries. A
common origin older than cither is possible. ( 1 7, pp. 69-70)



188 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
"It may be," to circ an English scholar, "as some indeed suspect, that
the science which we see ar the dawn of recorded history was not
science at its dawn, but the remnants of the science of some great and
as yet untraced civilization. Where, however, is the seat of the civiliza
tion to be located? 1
' (224, p. 15)
A number of scientists and scholars in ancient and modern times
have concluded that the world's first civilization was the creation of a
people known as the Ethiopians. The name Ethiopian we owe to the
ancient Greeks; for when these Hellenes first came in contact with the
dusky inhabitants of Africa and Asia, they called them the burnt faces.
In Greek, the word for burnt was Ethios, and the word for face was
ops; so ethios plus ops became Ethiopian. The Greeks thought that these
peoples developed their dark complexions because they lived closer to
the sun than the fairer inhabitants of Europe. We have been told by a
well-informed modern authority:
The fame of the Ethiopians was widespread in ancient history. Herod
otus describes them as "the tallest, most beautiful and long-lived of the
human races," and before Herodotus, Homer, in even more flattering lan
guage described them as "the most just of men; the favorites of the gods."
The annals of all the great early nations of Asia Minor are full of them. The
Mosaic records allude to them frequently; but while they are described as
the most powerful, the most just, and the most beautiful of the human race,
they are constantly spoken of as black, and there seems to be no other con
clusion to be drawn than that at that remote period of history, the leading
race of the Western World was a black race. (146, p. 221)
We learn from the works of Homer and Herodotus that all the peo
ples of the following territories were considered to be Ethiopians: The
Sudan, Egypt, Arabia, Palestine, Western Asia, India.
Today, we think of the Ethiopians as being exclusively an African
people, but in ancient times the case was quite different. An illuminat
ing discourse concerning this matter has been presented by a recog
nized modern authority on Ethiopian history, which we are pleased to
quote:
It seems certain that classical historians and geographers called the whole
region from India to Egypt, both countries inclusive, by the name of Ethiopia, and in consequence they regarded all the dark skinned and black peoples who inhabited it as Ethiopians. Mention is made of Eastern and West
ern Ethiopians, and it is probable that the Easterners were Asiatics and the Westerners Africans. In the present work which 1 have called A History of



THE ORIGIN OK CIVILIZATION 189
Ethiopia, I have made no attempt ro describe the history of that large por
tion of the earth's surface which the Greeks called Kthiopia, bur only rhar
comparatively small section of it which is today named, both by large num
bers of Orientals and by Europeans generally, Abvssinia, and also the coun
try of Kush, which is now known as Nubia. . . . The idcntificarion of
Kush with Abyssinia under the name of Kthiopia made bv the rranslarors of
the Erhiopic version of the Bible in the 5th (or 6rh) century has, for many
centuries been accepted by the Abyssinians. And to this day, the Abyssin
ian, in reciting Psalm KXVIII (V. 3 1 ), says "Kthiopia shall make her hands reach unto God."
During the preparation of this work I have been driven to the conclusion
that the Kthiopians whose manners and customs have been so fully de
scribed by Herodotus, Diodorus, Strabo, Pliny and others, were not Abvs
sinians at all, but the natives of Upper Nubia, and the Island of Meroe, and
the Negroes and Negroid peoples who inhabited the hot, moist lands which
extend from Southern Abyssinia to the Kquator. The hieroglyphic inscrip
rions of rhe VI, XII and XVIII Dynasties prove rhar caravans Travelled
from Egypr ro rhe counrries round abour rhe Blue Nile, and ro regions
much furrher ro rhe sourh, bur rhere is no menrion in rhem of any counrry
which can be idenrified wirh Abyssinia proper. In facr, rhe Egyptian in
scriprions cannor be said ro yield any direcr informarion abour rhe real
Abyssinia, or irs peoples, and even rhe Nubian and Meroiric inscriprions
rhrow very lirrle lighr upon rhe hisrory of rhe period in which rhey were
wrirren. From rhe cuneiform inscriprions we can expecr no informarion
abour Abyssinia, rhough borh rhe Assyrians and rhe Hebrews knew of rhe
existence of Kush, and that it lay to the south of Egypt. (33, Vol. I, pp. vii
and viii)
The ancient Greek bard, Homer, spoke of the Ethiopians as divided
into two groups and dwelling "at the ends of the earth, towards the set
ting and the rising sun." This passage has been variously interpreted.
Some students have held that it merely referred to the Ethiopians south
of Egypt, as being divided by the Nile River, some living along the
west bank and others on the east bank. Other students explained the
Homeric reference as relating to the east and west coasts of Africa with
the Ethiopians dwelling on both coasts, and divided only by the vast
extent of the African continent. Another opinion was offered by Epho
rus, and has been transmitted to posterity by Strabo, that all the south
coasts of both Africa and Asia were inhabired bv Ethiopians, and that
the African Ethiopians and their Asiatic brothers were separated by the
Arabian Gulf, which separated the two continents. The traditions con
cerning King Memnon lead us to suspect a close cultural connection



190 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
between the Ethiopians of Africa and those of western Asia, for ac
cording to Hesiod, Pindar, and Aeschylus, Memnon, king of Ethiopia,
was the son of a Cissian woman, and the Cissians were an Asiatic peo
ple; and we learn from The History of Herodotus that Memnon was the
founder of the city of Susa, the ancient capital of Elam. We have been
told that he led a combined army of Susianians (FJamitcs) and Ethiopi
ans to the assistance of King Priam (his father's brother) during the
Trojan War, and that after distinguished military conduct, he perished
in one of the battles before Troy. This same Memnon was claimed as a
king by the Nilotic Ethiopians, and was identified with the Pharaoh
Amenhotep 111 by the Egyptians. There is a tradition that his expedi
tion started from the African Ethiopia and proceeded by way of Egypt
to its destination. Palaces said to have been erected by King Memnon
have been reported from both Egypt and Elam, and it has been said
that a tribe called Memnones dwelt in the vicinity of Meroe. Memnon
hence unites both the eastern and western Ethiopians, and we think he
is best regarded, not as an historical personage, but as a culture hero,
personifying the ethnic identity of the two groups of Ethiopians. Fur
ther proof could be adduced of the Greek belief in an Asiatic Ethiopia,
stretching from Arabia to India, along the shores of the Indian Ocean,
were it necessary. On two occasions, Herodotus mentioned the Ethio
pians of Asia, whom he carefully distinguished from those of Africa;
and Ephorus, as we have already noted, extended the Ethiopians along
the entire coasts of the lands bordering on the Indian Ocean.
The traditions of the ancient Armenians are in harmony with those of the Greeks.- According to the Armenians, the name of Kush, or
Ethiopia, applied to Persia, Media, Elam, and Aria, or the whole region
between the Indus and the Tigris rivers. Moses of Khorene, an Arme
nian historian, identified King Belus of Babylon with Nimrod, and he
held that Nimrod was a grandson of Kush (Ethiopia), and a son of
Mizraim (Egypt). In this way, a close kinship was shown between the
Ethiopians of Africa and Asia.
We should not overlook the ancient Hebrew tradition as preserved
in Genesis, where we are informed that the sons of Eiam were Kush, Mizraim, Phut, and Canaan; and the Kush begat Nimrod, and the beginning of his kingdom was Babel, and Erech and Akkad and Kalneh
in the land of Shinar. Here an ancient Babylonian kingdom is traced to
a Kushite or Ethiopian origin, and is culturally connected with
Mizraim (Egypt), Phut (Central Africa), and Canaan (Palestine). The
plain meaning of these traditions, as we see it, is that these various



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 191
groups of peoples were all regarded as children of Ham, and rhar the
earliest people of Babylonia were a branch of the Kushites, or Ethiopi
ans, connected in some degree with the Egyptians, Canaanites, and
Libyans, but still more closely akin to the people who anciently dwelt
along the banks of the Upper Nile. When the early inhabitants of
Chaldea are said to have belonged to the same racial group as the dwell
ers upon the Upper Nile, the question suggests itself as to which were
the indigenous inhabitants and which were the colonists? Were the
shores of the Persian Gulf the original home of the Kushites, from
whence they spread eastward and westward, in one direction to Elam,
Persia, and on to India; and in the other, to Arabia and the East African
coast? Or shall we surmise that the migration proceeded in only one di
rection; and that the Kushites, starting from the country south of
Egypt, sent out colonies to the south coast of Arabia, and from there
into the lands bordering on the Persian Gulf, occupying Surner and
Elam, and thence spreading through Kerman and Mekran to the re
gions bordering on the Indus River? Something may be said in behalf
of either hypothesis.
The Biblical genealogy of the sons of Ham derives Nimrod from
Kush, not Kush from Nimrod. This tradition, as given in the tenth
chapter of Genesis, shows the Hamites as starting from Africa (the
home of the children of Ham), in two different migrations: one
through southern Arabia to Mesopotamia and the other from Egypt
through the land of Canaan into Syria. The antiquity of civilization in
the Nile Valley, which preceded by ages that of the plain of Shinar, is a
persuasive argument in favor of these early migrations having been
from west to east; and the monuments and traditions of the Euphratean
peoples themselves present indications of an East African origin of their
culture. It seems most probable that the people represented in Genesis by the legendary hero, Nimrod, and among the Greeks by the eponym
of Belus, migrated from East Africa by way of Arabia to the Tigris-Eu
phrates Valley, in times prior to recorded history. In 1854, a well
known German scholar, Baron Bunsen, in the third volume of his Phi
losophy of Universal History, declared that Nimrod, the legendary fa
ther of the Babylonians, was in no way related to Kush (Ethiopia). It is
true, said the Baron, that they had come into Mesopotamia from Af
rica, and so, having come from the land of Kush, they were called
Kushites. But the expression was purely geographical. From a racial
standpoint they were akin neither to the Egyptians nor the Ethiopians.
This conclusion was vigorously contested by the English Orientalist,



192 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
General Sir Henry Rawlinson, on both archaeological and philological
grounds. His findings were well presented by his celebrated brother,
Canon George Rawlinson of Canterbury, as follows:
A laborious study of the primitive language of Chaldea, led him (Sir
Henry Rawlinson) to the conviction that the dominant race in Babylonia at
the earliest time to which the monuments reached back was Kushite. He
found the vocabulary of the primitive race to be decidedly Kushite or Ethi
opian, and he was able to interpret the inscriptions chiefly by the aid which
was furnished to him from published works on the Galla (Abyssinian) and Mahra (South Arabian) dialects. He noted moreover, a considerable resemblance in the system of writing which the primitive race employed, and that
which was established from a very remote date in Egypt. Both were picto
rial; both to a certain extent symbolic; both in some instances used identi
cally the same symbols. Again, he found words in use among the primitive Babylonians and their neighbors and kinsmen, the Susianians, which
seemed to be identical with ancient Egyptian or Ethiopic roots. The root
hyk or hak, which Manetho interprets as king, and which is found in the well known Hyksos or Shepherd Kings, appeared in Babylonian and Susianian royal names under the form of Khak, and as the terminal element
which is its position also in royal Ethiopic names. The name Tirkak is com
mon to the royal lists of Susiana and Ethiopia, as that of Nimrod is to the
royal names of Babylon and Egypt. The sun god is called Ra in Egyptian, and Ra was the Kushite name of the supreme god of the Babylonians. . . .
The author of Genesis unites together as members of the same ethnic fam
ily the Egyptians, the Ethiopians, the Southern Arabians and the primitive
inhabitants of Babylon. Modern ethnology finds, in the localities indicated,
a number of languages, partly ancient, partly modern, which have common
characteristics, and which evidently constitute one group. Egyptian, an
cient and modern, Ethiopic, as represented by the Galla, Agau, etc.,
Southern Arabian (Himyaric and Mahra), and ancient Babylonian, are dis
covered to be cognate tongues, varieties of one original form of speech.
(201, pp. 212-14)
The people that Canon Rawlinson and his brother called primitive
or Proto-Chaldeans are by modern authorities named Sumerians. In
another work, Canon Rawlinson describes them in the following terms: "This element . . . was predominantly Kushite. ... In Su
siana (Elam), where the Kushite blood was maintained in tolerable pu
rity, there was, if we may trust the Assyrian remains, a very decided
prevalency of a Negro type of countenance. The head was covered
with short crisp curls; the eye was large, the nose and mouth nearly in
the same line, the lips thick." (200, Vol. II, p. 500)



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 193
So much propaganda of a racist nature has enrcred into the writings
of some of the best modern scholars that the reader, or student, must
constantly be on the watch for it. For instance, Professor Charles Sei
gnobos of the University of Paris wrote a scholarly book entitled His
tory of Ancient Civilization. In the second chapter of that work, we are
told: "Almost all civilized peoples belong to the white race. The people of
other races have remained savage or barbarian, like the men of prehistoric
times. "And immediately following the above announcement we learn:
"It is within the limits of Asia and Africa that the first civilized peoples
had their development-the Egyptians in the Nile Valley, the Chaldeans in
the plains of the Euphrates. They were people of sedentary and peaceful
pursuits. Their skin was dark, the hair short and thick, the lips strong. No
body knows their origin with exactness and scholars are not agreed on the
name to give them {some terming them Cushites, others Hamites). Later,
between the 20th and 25th centuries B.C. came bands of martial shep
herds who had spread over all Europe and the west of Asia—the Ary
ans and the Semites." (222, p. 17)
We shall meet with more of this combination of double-think and
double-talk as we proceed in considering the origin of civilization. A
sounder appreciation of the ancient Ethiopian contribution to early civ
ilization was given by an English anthropologist who spent most of his
life in the Orient. We refer to Major General J. G. R. Forlong, and
the following extract is from his major work, Rivers of Life:
It was undoubtedly Kushites who rendered possible the Aryan advance,
and who played the part of a civilizing Rome thousands of years before
Roma's birth. It was their vast mythology and strange legends that passed as
Lord Bacon wrote "like light air into the flutes of Grecians, there to be
modulated as best suited Grecian fancies." Indeed, it is manifest from many
old writings, that it was their tales, myths, traditions and histories that lay at
the base of the Western World's thought and legendary lore. These so impressed all subsequent races and entered so deeply and minutely into all Aryan mythologies that many writers now think Aryans can only claim to have added to the superstructure and complexion of Ethiopian myths and
mythical history; and let us remember that active Aryan life and mythologies began at least 3000 years B.C. when high Asia . . . , becoming too
cramped for this race, they were pressing southward to India and Ariana and to the west generally. Then and there must Aryans have met with
Ethiopian civilization, as did Semites, when these began to group them
selves into nations about a thousand years later, or say, 2000 B.C. They
were all builders on old Kushite foundations. (82, Vol. II, pp. 403-4)



194 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The ancient culture which we have discussed in this chapter has in
spired a considerable literature; but we believe the most comprehensive
study of this old civilization was that pursued by Professor W. J. Perry
of the University of Manchester. His most important works are The
Megalithic Culture of Indonesia, Manchester, 1918; 77?^ Children of the
Sun: A Study in the Early History of Civilization, London and New
York, 1923, 2nd edition, 1926; The Growth of Civilization, London,
1924, 2nd edition, 1926; and 77?^ Pr'nnordial Ocean, London, 1935.
Perry has called this ancient cultural complex the Archaic Civilization,
and its most important elements, we are convinced, are the following
features: (1) the practice of agriculture by means of irrigation; (2) the
carving of stone images, and the employment of stone in constructing
pyramids, dolmens, rock-cut tombs, and stone circles; (3) metal work
ing; (4) rne manufacture of pottery; (5) the practice of making
mummies; (6) the worship of the Great Mother Goddess; (7) rites in
volving human sacrifice, connected with agriculture and the cult of the
Great Mother; (8) the worship of the heavenly bodies, such as stars,
planets, the moon, and the' sun; (9) a ruling class split into two divi
sions; (a) one branch of the ruling class connected with the sky-world
and claiming kinship with the gods and engaging in incestuous unions;
(b) the other branch of the ruling class associated with the underworld;
(10) the survival of the Totemic complex; (11) the dual organization;
operative in the social structure and in political affairs; (12) the preva
lence of the institution of Mother-Right, in which descent in the fe
male line is connected with the succession of kings in the female line;
(13) the presence of the institution of Divine Kingship, especially in
Africa.
The archaic civilization has been traced back to ancient Egypt by
Elliot Smith and Perry; and they have contended that this culture in
ancient times spread from the Nile to Crete and western Asia. Then,
after traveling through South Asia to Indonesia and the islands of the
Pacific, it finally appeared in South and Central America, culminating
in the civilizations of the lncas, the Mayas, the Aztecs. Other anthro
pologists of the difTusionist school, such as Lord Raglan and Professor
W. H. R. Rivers, seem to favor an Asiatic origin of the archaic civiliza
tion. That all the later cultures of the world can be traced back to an
older common civilization we are firmly convinced. We have also been
convinced by abundant and impressive evidence that this original early culture was far more ancient than those of Egypt and Mesopotamia.



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 195
The ancient Egyptians, as we have already noted, were considered by
the scholars of Greece and Rome to be Ethiopians. The Ethiopian
theory of the origin of civilization has been given favorable considera
tion by such outstanding scholars as Count Yolnev, Sir 1 lenry Rawlin
son, Professor A. H. L Heeren, General J. G. R. Forlong, Gerald
Massey, Dr. Albert Churchward, and fast, but not least, Eadv Lugard. The monumental works of Gerald Massey have not received the
careful study they so eminently deserve. Massey was distinguished in
his day as a social reformer, poet, philologist, and Egyptologist. His
outstanding publications are A Book of the Beginnings, two volumes,
1881; The Natural Genesis, two volumes, 1883; and Ancient Egypt, two
volumes, 1907. In his later years of research, Massey was joined by Dr.
Albert Churchward, whose principal works are The Signs and Symbols
of Primordial Man, 2nd edition, London, 1913, and The Origin and Ev
olution of the Human Race, London, 192 1
Most academic authorities dismiss the scholars named above as
visionaries, but there are a few independent thinkers who argue that
the minority party has a good case, and that it deserves a hearing. We
should like to recommend to the attention of the reader a remarkable
book, Maps of the Ancient Sea Kings: Evidence of Advanced Civilization
in the Ice Age, by Professor Charles H. Hapgood, F.R.G.S. This book
is scholarly and well documented, and among the conclusions ex
pressed in that work are the following examples: kC The idea of a simple
linear development of society from the culture of Paleolithic (Old
Stone Age), through the successive stages of the Neolithic (New Stone
Age), Bronze and Iron Ages must be given up. . . . We shall now as
sume that some 20,000 or more years ago, while paleolithic peoples
held out in Europe, more advanced cultures existed elsew here on the
earth and that we have inherited a part of what they once possessed,
passed down from people to people. " (1 12, pp. 193-94)
In studying the history of ancient nations, such as Egypt in the Nile
Valley, and of Sumer in western Asia, the student finds certain ques
tions arising which cannot readily be put aside. For these old nations
seem to have no beginning. They are fully developed when they first
appear on the stage of history; and after that the pattern seems to be
that of a decline and fall. This point will be dealt with in some detail in
our next chapter, which will treat of the evolution of civilization. The
orthodox scholars in the fields of anthropology and history, when faced
with embarrassing questions, either ignore them or refer to the ques



196 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
tioners as visionaries or crackpots. But the critics of scientific orthodoxy
refuse to be shouted down; and they cannot all be dismissed as idle
dreamers.
Scholars such as Massey and Churchward have adduced impressive
evidence that both man and civilization were cradled in Africa; but
their labors have been mainly disregarded for obvious reasons. We live
in a culture today where even the educated classes have been taught
that Africa is the Dark Continent, that the peoples of Africa were and
are savages and that they have not made any worthwhile contribution
to human advancement. This outlook, of course, is a vicious and un
warranted caricature of reality, but it enjoys the sanction of powerful
vested interests. The African origin of the human race has been so well
established by the discoveries of Broom, Dart, Leakey, and others that
their critics have more or less been silenced. The African origin of civi
lization is still challenged. Although Egypt is an African country, there
are still scholars who claim that the Egyptians were not an indigenous
African people. Yet, the facts are not in dispute. This accurate state
ment was made by Gerald Massey as far back as 1881: "Egypt is often
called Kam, the black land, and Kam does signify black; the name
probably applied to the earlier inhabitants whose type is the Kam or
Ham of the Hebrew writers." (164, Vol. I, p. 4)
"It will be maintained in this book that the oldest mythology, reli
gion, symbols, language, had their birthplace in Africa, that the primi
tive race of Kam came thence, and the civilization attained in Egypt
emanated from that country and spread over the world. The most rea
sonable view on the evolutionary theory ... is that the black race is
the most ancient, and that Africa is the primordial home." (164, Vol. I,
p. 18) "The Hebrew scriptures, among their other fragments of ancient
lore, are very emphatic in deriving the line of Mizraim from Ham or
Kam, the black type coupled with Kush, another form of the black.
They give no countenance to the theory of Asiatic origin for the Egyp
tians. In the Biblical account of the generations of Noah, Mizraim is
the son of Ham, i.e., of Kam, the black race." (164, Vol. I, p. 33)
The peoples designated as Kushites or Kamites by Massey were
called Nilotic Negroes by Dr. Churchward, in his pioneer work The
Origin and Evolution of the Human Race. In tracing the ancient inhab
itants of Egypt back to the Great Lakes region of Africa, he was able to
obtain the endorsement of recognized authorities, such as Professor C.
G. Seligman and Sir E. A. Wallis Budge. "The prehistoric native of



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 197
Egypt, both in the old and new Stone Ages, was an African," according to Budge, and "there are many things in the manners and cus
toms and religions of the historic Egyptians, . . . that suggest that the
original home of their prehistoric ancestors was a country in the neigh
borhood of Uganda and Punt." (30, pp. 21-22) In this same little book
we are also told that the most ancient symbolism of Egypt was of un
doubted African origin: "These symbols are found painted on pots at
Nakadah and are supported on standards fixed in the prehistoric boats;
many of them reappear in the names of gods in the historic period, and
they form the oldest Egyptian hieroglyphs known. They are very im
portant as showing that the earliest attempts to write in Egypt were
made by native African Egyptians." (30, p. 42)
The diffusionists, in tracing civilization back to an African origin,
have a good case, but there is one important link which is missing in
their chain of reasoning. In the works of Smith, Perry, Lord Raglan,
and Dr. Churchward, we are given a convincing picture of how the ar
chaic civilization spread across Asia to the islands of the South Pacific and eventually to the New World. Churchward is most emphatic in
tracing the beginning of civilization to the Nilotic Negroes of East and
Central Africa, and of its diffusion from that part of the world to Asia
and America by way of the Indian and Pacific Oceans. The peoples of West Africa Churchward called True Negroes, and he dogmatically
declared that none of these peoples ever left the African continent. If
the East African Nilotics could convey civilization to the Americas by
way of southern Asia and the Pacific Islands, why could not the West
Africans take the much shorter route across the Atlantic Ocean?
An attempt was made to answer this question by Professor Leo Wiener of Harvard University, in his three-volume work Africa and
the Discovery of America. A further contribution was made in this di
rection by Wiener in the publication of his Mayan and Mexican Ori
gins. In these well-documented studies, Professor Wiener claimed that
West African mariners and merchants made more than fifty voyages
across the Atlantic to the New World in pre-Columbian times. On
this topic we shall have more to say in the next chapter.
Where facts are in short supply, speculation runs rife. This is exem
plified by the hypotheses of the lost continents of Lemuria, Mu, and
Atlantis. Lemuria is thought to have been an ancient continent in the
middle of what is now the Indian Ocean; Mu was a similar continent in
the Pacific Ocean; and Atlantis was, of course, in the Atlantic Ocean midway between Africa and South America. All these continents be



198 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
came lost by sinking bencarh the waters of the various oceans. The
Atlantean hypothesis has spawned a vast literature, whereas the other
lost continents have never captivated the public fancy. It is true that
Colonel James Churchward wrote a series of books on the lost conti
nent of Mu, but, since these works were more akin to science fiction
than sober science, we may dismiss them as literary curiosities. But
Atlantis caught on. We have been informed by a contemporary writer:
"Atlantis was a fable invented by Plato for the edification of his stu
dents. His Greek contemporaries quite properlv regarded it as such
and wasted no time trying to find out where it was and why it sank to
the bottom of the sea, and what happened to the Atlantians. ,,
(51, p.
252) We are inclined to think that Cohen's relegation of Atlantis to
the realm of fable is correct, but the Atlantis story has captivated so
many scientists and scholars of distinction and has given rise to such an
extensive literature that we must take some notice of it.
Colonel Alexandre Braghine, the French explorer, was convinced
that the ancient Ethiopians of Africa were in some way connected with
the lost continent of Atlantis. The Greek historian, Proclus, told of the
visit of Krantor to the temple of the goddess Neith, in the ancient
Egyptian city of Sais. The priests of the establishment showed Krantor
columns of hieroglyphs relating to the history of Atlantis. Proclus cited
as another source of his know ledge of Atlantis The Ethiopian History of
Marcellus. Skylax of Karyanda, a Carian navigator, who explored the
Indus River region of India for the king of Persia in the sixth century
B.C., also recorded that Phoenician mariners carried on a lively trade
with Ethiopians of the island of Cernc, beyond the Pillars of Hercules,
in the Atlantic Ocean. At a later time, Diodorus Siculus specifically
stated that the western region of Ethiopia was inhabited by Atlanteans.
According to Georg, the Atlanteans were Ethiopians, for he has de
clared:
The new age that began after the disappearance of Atlantis was marked
at first by the world wide dominance of Ethiopian representatives of the black race. They were supreme in Africa and Asia, . . . and they even
infiltrated through Southern Europe. But they were expelled from this lat
ter place after a thousand years of bitter warfare between the races and on
slaughts by the invaders from the north. The memory of the great advance
of the blacks has been almost completely lost; only in the dread of dragons

for the dragon painted on the insignia of the kings was carried in the van
of the black armies— was a final vestige of the inter-racial wars preserved.
(96, p. 121)



THE ORIGIN OK CIVILIZATION 199
Professor Leo Frobenius made a careful studv of rhe Atlantis ques
tion. He concluded that there never was an island named Atlantis in
the ocean between Africa and the Americas; but his studies convinced
him that there was an ancient Atlantean culture located on the coast of
West Africa. For in Yorubaland he unearthed ruins of palaces and
beautiful statuary; and he heard among the Yorubans legends of an an
cient royal city and its palace with golden walks which in the distant
past sank beneath the waves. Frobenius found Poseidon's castle not in
the mid-Atlantic, but in Yorubaland, on the West African coast: "Yo
ruba, with its channeled network of lakes on the coast and the reaches
of the Niger; Yoruba, whose peculiarities are not inadequately depicted
in the Platonic account—this Yoruba, I assert, is Atlantis, the home of
Poseidon's posterity, the Sea God by them named Olokun; the land of
peoples of whom Solon declared: 'They had even extended their lord
ship over Egypt and Tyrrhene.' " (93, Vol. I, p. 345)
This great Africanist also detected cultural links between the Yoru
bas of West Africa and the Mayas of Central America. "I cannot
finish," he wrote, "without devoting a word or two to a certain symp
tomatic conformity of the Western Atlantic civilization with its higher
manifestations in America. Its cognate features are so striking that they
cannot be overlooked and as the region of Atlantic African culture is
Yoruba, ... it seems to be a present question, whether it might not be
possible to bring the marvelous Maya monuments, whose dates have
been deciphered by our eminent American archaeologists, into some
prehistoric connection with those of Yoruba." (93, Vol. I, p. 348) (It
may be of interest to the reader to consult 139, pp. 32-33. See espe
cially the map of the world according to Herodotus, on page 33, where
Atlanteans are shown as dwelling on the West African coast, and
hence on the shore of the Atlantic sea.)
The literature on Atlantis runs into thousands of titles, in numerous
languages, so only a few of the most scholarly works on the subject can
be mentioned here. The late Lewis Spence, of Scotland, an outstanding
anthropologist, wrote a series of books on the Atlantis question. Proba
bly the best of the lot was his History of Atlantis. Atlantis: The Antedi
luvian World by Ignatius Donnelly, an American, was published in
1882. A revised edition, edited by Egerton Sykes, F.R.G.S., was pub
lished as recently as 1949. The Shadow of Atlantis by the Frenchman,
Colonel Alexandre Braghine, is, in our opinion, one of the best of the
books on the subject. Professor Leo Frobenius wrote a series of books



200 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
with the title Atlantis in German, but the work has not to date been
translated into English. The speculations of Raquena and LePlongeon
are noted here, for whatever they are worth. Professor Rafael Raquena,
a Venezuelan archaeologist, believed that Atlantis was colonized from
ancient Egypt and that these Egyptians established other colonies in
the New World before the submergence of Atlantis. Professor Au
gustus LePlongeon, a French archaeologist who finally settled in
Brooklyn, New York, carried on extensive explorations in Yucatan. In
1874, LePlongeon uncovered the ruins of a palace in Chichen Irza,
which he named Prince Con's Palace. In these ruins the professor
found pictographs and inscriptions which he claims to have deciphered.
The result of these finds was a book, Queen Moo and the Egyptian
Sphinx, in which LePlongeon gave his interpretations of the inscrip
tions along with reproductions of the pictographs. Mrs. LePlongeon,
who assisted her husband in his labors, wrote a little book entitled
Queen Moo's Talisman. The story as given in these works runs briefly
as follows: About eleven thousand years ago, two brothers, princes of
Yucatan, sought the hand of the ruling monarch of the realm, Queen
Moo, in marriage. The brothers were named Coh and Aac, re
spectively, and Prince Coh was the successful suitor. This enraged
Prince Aac, who thereupon stabbed his brother fatally with a stone
knife. Then Aac attempted to force Queen Moo to marry him. The
queen, rather than submit, decided to flee to Atlantis. On reaching the
coast, she learned that Atlantis, overwhelmed by earthquakes, had
sunken below the waves of the ocean. So the queen embarked for Af
rica and ended her journey in Egypt. In that country she was hailed as
queen and elevated to the throne. Then she caused the Sphinx to be
erected as a memorial to her dead husband. Is it possible, or even proba
ble, that the great Sphinx with its Ethiopian physiognomy was set up as
a memorial to an ancient Mayan prince? The story so charmed Mrs.
LePlongeon that she set it to poetry as follows:
Where flows the river Nile, The queen found rest; There once again her days
With peace were blessed. Upon that soil where Welcome frank was found, Did Moo a giant Sphinx from Out of the ground
Cause to arise, and



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 201
Thus Coh's fame renew?
Did she immortalize
Her consort true? (143, p. 65)
A young man in New England published an article in 1879 with the
title "Atlantis Not a Myth." The purpose of this essay was to show
that the Mayas were a surviving branch of the ancient inhabitants of Atlantis. In 1885, this young man, Edward Herbert Thompson, went
to Central America and then spent forty years carrying on explorations
in that area. Up to the time of his death in 1935, he still held that the
Mayas were direct descendants of the ancient Atlanteans. Another
American archaeologist who succumbed to the lure of Atlantis was Dr.
J. A. Mason of the Field Museum of Chicago. In 1922, Dr. Mason was
engaged in excavating a site near Santa Mataa in the province of
Magdalena, United States of Colombia. There the ruins of an ancient
city were unearthed. The site is about forty miles from the Caribbean
coast. Mason's report to the Field Museum declared: "There must have
been a tremendous population here at one time, as the whole country is
covered with house sites." No official estimate as to the age of the ruins
was published, but the museum authorities announced that the discov
ery of this old Colombian city "heightens the probability that the con
tinent of Atlantis, said to have been swallowed by the ocean, and lying
midway between South America and Africa, actually existed/' (Cited
by Maynard Shipley in 226, p. 16)
A few more words must be said on the contentious question of the
races of man before closing this chapter. Speaking scientifically, there is
only one race, the human race, which the biologists classify as genus
homo, and species sapiens. The several varieties of the human species
are subspecies of one original group. In the literature of history and the
social sciences we read of all sorts of races that have been invented by
well-meaning enthusiasts with a case to prove. We read of the Hamit
ic, Semitic, and Aryan races, which is all nonsense, for these names
refer to groups of languages and have no connection with race. Even
worse, we read of the exploits of the German, French, and English
races. In works relating to the role of Africa in world history, we see
this process carried to such an extreme pitch that it reduces itself to
utter absurdity. A few examples will demonstrate this opinion. In the
latter part of the last century, a brilliant young lady in England, Miss
Flora Louisa Shaw, decided to become a journalist. She became a colo
nial correspondent for the London Times and traveled extensively in

Africa. She also became rhe wife of Lord Lugard, who was Governor General of Nigeria, ar rhe rurn of rhe century, and is hence widely known in rhe world of lerrers as Lady Lugard. From her pen came a
very able hisrorical work, A Tropical Dependency: An Outline of the
Ancient History of the Western Sudan with an Account of the Modern Set
tlement of Northern Nigeria. In discussing rhe early history of rhe
Sudan, Lady Lugard refers ro rhar extensive area as Negroland, as is
shown in rhe following passage:
When rhe hisrory of Negroland comes ro be wrirren in derail, ir may be found rhar rhe kingdoms lying towards rhe easrern end of rhe Sudan were
rhe home of races who inspired, rarhcr rhan of races who received rhe tradi

grees and 1 7 degrees, territories in which are found monuments more ancient than the oldest Rgyprian monuments. If this should prove to be the
case, and the civilized world be forced to recognize in a black people the fount of its original enlightenment, it may happen that we shall have to re
vise entirely our view of the black races and regard those who now exist as
the decadent representatives of an almost forgotten era, rather than as the
embryonic possibility of an era yet to come. (146, pp. 17-18)
Lady Lugard writes learnedly of rhe grear ancicnr and medieval
kingdoms and empires of rhe Sudan, or rhe land of rhe blacks, also

Sudanic peoples of the past were, in her opinion, not Negroes. In a dis
