The passage cited above brings ro mind a remark by the German an
thropologist Ratzel: "Trie hideous Negro type, which the fancy of ob
servers one saw all over Africa, is really to be seen onlv as a sign in
front of tobacco shops." (199, Vol. 11, p. 313)
An interesting article, "The Negro Enigma," by Professor M. I).
W. Jeffreys, was published in the West African Rercieu\ September,
1951. The following extracts are from that article:
The Black Belt, anthropologically speaking, is that area on the earth's
surface that comprises the dark skinned races. . . . The black belt extends
from Africa via India, to Melanesia and Australia. In this great arc the posi
tion of the Negro is the great enigma. At the two ends, or horns, are people
who are Negroes, but in the center there are none. The center is occupied
by a dark skinned race, the Hindu, but he offers no difficulty. He belongs
to the same race as the European, namely, the Caucasian. . . . The Cauca
sian comes from an old human stock —a stock that is today called modern
man. . . . The Swanscombe skull found in Great Britain is dated 250,000
years, and is our stock, not Negro. The skeletal remains dug up by the Leakeys in East Africa are us, not Negro. Boskop man, found in the Cape,
is dated 50,000 years, and falls into our group, not that of the Negro. There
are no Negro skulls of any antiquity. . . . The two Grimaldi skulls, one of
a woman, the other of a boy, are not Negro skulls. ... So the enigma
deepens: all the evidence points to the Negro being a comparatively recent
race and here is the old Caucasian race in a continuous stretch from Britain
to India. . . . Now in Africa, there is a continuous evidence, unlike any
where else on the globe, of man's uninterrupted occupation of the earth for
close on a million years. Africa is thus today accepted by many scientists as
the cradle of the human species. Thus, in Africa, from the Old Stone Age to modern times, Modern Man is the tool maker. Nowhere is the Negro,
unlike the Bushman, associated with these stone age cultures.
According to Professor Jeffreys, the so-called Negro type is an
enigma even in Africa. In a recent valuable study, // Began in Babel, a
German Scholar, Herbert Wendt, devotes several pages to a discussion
of the question: Where did the Negro come from? We are told by the
author that: "Even in the wisest and most detailed anthropological and
ethnological works, no answer will be found to this question, though
there are all kinds of hypotheses, suppositions and questionable indica
tions." (269, p. 368) Wendt directs our attention to southern Asia and Oceania, and con
cludes that they were not the original home of the Negroes. Then,
turning to a consideration of Africa, he tells us:



204 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The African Negroes are of even more "Negroid" type than the dark
races of Asia and the South Seas. Their skin color is generally darker, their
hair woolier, their lips more prominent—in short, their whole appearance is
more "Negro like" than that of a Tamil or a Papuan. Accordingly they
must be the pure, original Negroes, and the black race must have had its
birth in Africa. But apart from one or two exceptions, there is no proof of
this. All primitive Africans, ignoring Neanderthal or even older human
types, belong broadly speaking to European humanity. . . . Then if there
are no primeval Negroes in the dark continent, how did the Negroes come
to Africa? (269, pp. 368-69)
Herbert Wendt finally decided that the word race has lost its mean
ing in Africa, and that we should speak instead of cultural group. "It is
enough to say here," he concludes, "that there is after all no real Negro
civilization, but that Africa is a mirror reflecting the most varied human
cultures— just like Eurasia and America." (269, p. 377)
In contrast to the opinions cited above, it is only fair to consider the
views of Sir Harry Johnston, who has been widely recognized as an au
thority on Africa and its peoples:
Whether the African Negro was the first human colonizer of Africa or
was preceded by a more brutish or more generalized type, such as the Gal
ley-Hill man, is not yet known to us. But from the little we possess in the
way of fossil human remains and other evidence, it seems probable that
every region of Africa, even Algeria and Egypt, once possessed a Negro
population. . . . In Egypt a dwarfish type of Negro seems to have inhabited
the Nile Delta.some 10,000 years ago; and big black Negroes formed the population of Upper Nubia and Dongola as late as about 4,000 years ago.
(129, p. 5)
Though contending that Negroid types lived in Africa in very early
times, Johnston was convinced that some part of southern Asia, possi
bly India, was the original home of this particular branch of the human
species. On this topic, he wrote as follows:
There is a strong underlying Negroid element in the mass of the Indian
population and in the southernmost part of the great peninsula there are
forest tribes of dark skin and strikingly Negro physiognomy, with frizzled
or wooly hair. ... In the more eastern among the Malay Islands—espe
cially in Buru, Jilolo and Timor—the interior tribes are of obvious Negro
stock. Still more marked is this in the case of New Guinea, and most of all
in the Bismarck Archipelago and northern Solomon Islands. In these last
the resemblance of the natives to the average Negro of Africa is most strik



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 205
ing, although the distance is something like 8,000 miles. Negro affinities ex
tend east of the Solomon Archipelago to Fiji and Hawaii, and south to New
Caledonia, Tasmania, and even New Zealand. On the other hand, Africa
for many thousand years has been obviously the chief domain of the Negro.
(129, p. 3)
It should be apparent to the reader at this point that, if Johnston
knew whereof he spoke, then the opinions expressed by Jeffreys and
Wendt must lack all validity, and vice versa. It is of no profit to ask
where the Negro came from, or to pontificate on the Negro Enigma, if
we do not beforehand define the term Negro; and this, as far as we are
aware, has never been done. In Greco-Roman times the various peo
ples of Africa were known as Ethiopians. Europeans of the Middle
Ages called all Africans Moors. In modern times certain African peo
ples were designated as Negroes. If you ask what is the difference be
tween a Kushite, a Hamite, and a Negro, you can expect no concord
ant conclusion, for the so-called authorities on the races of mankind
have never told us just what branch of the human species should be
classified as Negroes. As an example of this sort of confusion, we refer
to a study by Sir Grafton Elliot Smith. In this work, he discussed at
length the question of the racial origins of the ancient Egyptians; and
started off as follows: "Not a few writers, like the traveler Volney in
the eighteenth century, have expressed the belief that the ancient
Egyptians were Negroes, or at any rate strongly Negroid. In recent
times, even a writer so discriminating as Ripley usually is, has given his
adhesion to this view, which I consider to be the most serious blot on
his most valuable and interesting memoir 'The People of Europe.'
(234, p. 37)
The writers referred to above by Professor Smith were the French
Orientalist Count Constantine Francis Volney and the American an
thropologist, Professor William Z. Ripley. Count Volney, in his fa
mous book 77?^ Ruins of Empires, in a reference to the African Ethio
pia, wrote: "There a people, now forgotten, discovered, while others
were yet barbarians, the elements of the arts and sciences. A race of
men now rejected from society for their sable skin and frizzled hair,
founded on the study of the laws of nature, those civil and religious sys
tems which still govern the universe." (258, pp. 16-17) In a footnote, Count Volney cites the Greek writer Lucian as
saying: "The Ethiopians were the first who invented the science of the
stars, and gave names to the planets. Not at random, and without



206 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
meaning, bur descriptive of rhe qualities which they conceived them to
possess; and it was from them that this art passed, still in an imperfect
state, to the Egyptians." Volney, commenting on the opinion of Lu
cian, asserted: "It would be easy to multiply citations upon this subject;
from all which it follows, that we have the strongest reasons to believe
that the country neighboring to the tropic was the cradle of the scien
ces, and of consequence that the first learned nation was a nation of
blacks; for it is incontrovertible, that, by the term Ethiopians, the an
cients meant to represent a people of black complexion, thick lips and
woolly hair." (258, p. 17)
We now turn back to the opinions of Elliot Smith, who stated:
"Within recent years, many scholars have advocated the view that
there is a large element of the Negro in the composition of the Proto
Egyptian population, and Ripley, apparently as the result of a misun
derstanding of Sergi r
s views, boldly states that the Egyptians and the
whole Mediterranean race are descendants of Negroes." (234, p. 79)
Smith freely admitted that the ancient Egyptians were not members of
the white race, and he felt equally certain that they were not classifia
ble in the black race; so he listed them under the label of the brown
race. "There can be no doubt that in respect to many features," ac
cording to Sir Elliot, "the brown and black races present many points
of similarity. . . . That there is no close affinity between the two races
is shown by an analysis and comparison of the intimate structure of the
bodies of representative individuals. In the texture of bone, the archi
tecture of the skull, the nature of the asymmetry of the body and the
character of the variations—in these and many other respects there is
evidence of the profound gap that separates the Negro from the rest of
mankind, including the Egyptian." (234, pp. 79-80) Assuming that
there actually is a profound gap between the so-called Negro and the
rest of the human race, one would expect Professor Smith to present
some sort of proof to validate his hypothesis; but this objective evidence
he entirely failed to produce.
The fact is that the stereotype of the so-called Negro race corre
sponds to no reality in the world of objective phenomena. At the pres
ent time, in our own United States of America, anyone reputed to have
an African ancestry, however remote, is supposed to be a Negro. Now,
let us carry this thesis to its logical conclusion. Since there is over
whelming evidence that the human race originated in Africa, then all mankind has an African ancestry. Hence, all men must be Negroes.



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 207
To carry the argument a srep further, according to the theory of evolu
tion, the human species possesses a common ancestry with the anthro
poid apes; therefore, we are all apes. If the reader thinks this is far
fetched, we beg leave to illustrate our position by relating the true
story of an incident of the recent past. Soon after the United States be
came involved in World War II, an open-air political meeting was held
in the Harlem section of New York City. In the crowd attending the
affair was a gentleman visiting the city, and next to him stood a mem
ber of the staff of the local Y.M.C.A. The two men became engaged in
conversation, and the visitor asked the Y.M.C.A. clerk to point out to
him the celebrities seated on the platform from which the meeting was
being conducted. The Y.M.C.A. man addressed the out-of-towner as
he pointed to politicians on the platform:
"Sir! Observing from left to right, you will notice a short, stout,
dark-complected man; that is Mayor LaGuardia. Seated next to him, you will see another man of swarthy complexion; and he is Commis
sioner Newbold Morris. They may look like colored men from here,
but they are, in fact, both white men. Now, on the other side of the
platform you will observe the presence of two men of very fair com
plexion. The one at which I point is Congressman A. C. Powell, Jr., and the gentleman seated next to him is Mr. Walter White, the Sec
retary of the National Association for the Advancement of Colored
People. Though these two gents look like white men, they are actually
Negroes."
Any word that can be used so ambiguously as Negro should be dis
carded, since, for descriptive purposes, it is totally devoid of meaning.
But getting rid of this word is not going to be easy; for false ideas are
not cast off readily, even after being conclusively discredited. As an
example of how absurdities are perpetuated, we cite an amusing anec
dote from the pen of the late Lord Russell:
Give me an adequate army, with power to provide it with more pay and
better food than falls to the lot of the average man, and I will undertake,
within 30 years, to make the majority of the population believe that two
and two are three, that water freezes when it gets hot and boils when it gets
cold, or any other nonsense that might seem to serve the interest of the
state. Of course, even when these beliefs had been generated, people would
not put the kettle in the refrigerator when they wanted it to boil. That cold
makes water boil would be a Sunday truth, sacred and mystical, to be pro
fessed in awed tones, but not to be acted on in daily life. What would hap



208 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
pen would be that any verbal denial of the mystic doctrine would be made illegal, and obstinate heretics would be frozen at the stake. No person who
did not enthusiastically accept the official doctrine would be allowed to
teach or to have any position of power. Only the very highest officials, in
their cups, would whisper to each other what rubbish it all is; then they
would laugh and drink again. This is hardly a caricature of what happens
under some modern governments. (217, p. 95)
That branch of the human race which may be described as black or
dark brown is customarily labeled Negroid, meaning Negro-like. If
Negro is disallowed, then Negroid will also have to go, for, if the Negro
is a mythical concept, then how can anything be likened to a nonexist
ent entity? But if we get rid of the term Negro, what will we put in its
place? This is a fair question, and we come forth with a suggestion. In
ancient times, black men were called Ethiopians; and why cannot we
today, following the usage of modern anthropology, refer to members
of the black race as Ethiopoids, rather than Negroids.
Let us repeat that, in antiquity, all black men were regarded as Ethi
opians. Today we are told by certain scientists and scholars, who
should know better, that down through the ages, there have been two
groups of black people; one, known as Kushites or Ethiopians, highly
civilized and progressive; and another called Negroes, uncivilized, and
who never rose above savagery and barbarism to the level of civiliza
tion. In the middle of the last century, an American scholar, Professor
John William Draper, wrote a two-volume History of the Intellectual
Development of Europe. In the first edition of this work, the author di
gressed from his main theme, and presented a melancholy picture of
the sad plight of certain natives of what was then called the dark conti
nent. He mused on how the benighted denizens of the West African
coast gazed for ages on the mighty Atlantic Ocean, vaguely wondering
what lay on the other side of it, but were not clever enough to build
boats and embark on voyages of exploration. To the credit of Draper,
this nonsense was eliminated in the revised edition of his book. Evi
dently he learned that discoveries had been made which showed that
Africans had somehow made their way to the New World in pre-Co
lumbian times.
These so-called Negroes, sometimes called pure Negroes or true Ne
groes, never actually existed, of course. For no group of people, who
over periods of thousands of years just stood still in a state of suspended
animation while the rest of mankind advanced, could possibly have sur



THE ORIGIN OF CIVILIZATION 209
vived. Evolution does nor operate thar way. So we musr conclude that whar has been called the Negro is a myrh. This type of mythology has
led to great tragedy in the past, and even in the present its baleful in
fluence is still in our midst. For example, consider the following senti
ments of a contemporary scientist as transmitted by Lord Raglan:
''Professor R. B. Cattell . . . concludes that savages, including the
whole Negro race, should on account of their low mentality and un
pleasant nature, be painlessly exterminated." (196, p. 4) Lord Raglan,
to his credit, dissents from such an outlook. "Nobody with any knowl
edge of savages would agree with these views," he noted. "Real savages
show many differences, both racially and individually, but in general
they are pleasant and kindly people with plenty of common sense
which they apply whenever they are not inhibited by their traditional
beliefs. There is no evidence that savages in general are inferior to Eu
ropeans in innate mental capacity, though, as among Europeans, there
are marked individual differences." (196, p. 4)
Fortunately, Africans for the most part have rejected this Negro
mythology. The late Professor William L. Hansberry was for many
years chairman of the History Department of Howard University.
After retirement, the professor was appointed to the directorship of a
school for historical research connected with an African university.
Professor Hansberry on one occasion, in pursuit of his studies, visited
an African scholar in the Congo. On introducing the professor to some
friends, the African gentleman mentioned that Professor Hansberry
hailed from the United States of America and was known in that land
as a Negro. This little group of Africans told Dr. Hansberry that they
took a special interest in making his acquaintance, since he was the first
Negro that they had ever seen.
We must now move on to our next topic, the evolution of civiliza
tion, which will be surveyed in the next chapters.



CHAPTER ELEVEN
The Evolution of Civilization:
Ethiopia and Egypt
PROBABLY the most ambitious attempt to subject the rise and fall of
civilizations to scientific scrutiny was made by the Egyptologist, Sir
Flinders Petrie, in a little book entitled 77?^ Revolutions of Civilization.
Petrie studied the history of Egypt over a period of seven thousand
years, in which he claimed to have detected the rise and fall of civiliza
tion several times over. Then he compared European civilization from
the days of ancient Crete to the present time with the culture cycles of
ancient Egypt. This study tended to show a definite cultural pattern, in
which can be traced the rise, progress, and decline of civilizations.
"Every civilization of a settled population," Petrie observed, "tends
to incessant decay from its maximum condition; and this decay con
tinues until it is too weak to initiate anything, when a fresh race comes
in and utilizes the old stock to graft on, both in blood and culture. As
soon as the mixture is well started, it rapidly grows on the old soil and
produces a new wave of civilization. There is no new generation with
out a mixture of blood, parthenogenesis is unknown in the birth of na
tions." (189, p. 114)
After an old civilization has declined to its minimum condition, a
fresh cultural wave ensues, and then the process makes a new start, and
the revitalized culture begins to rise to a new maximum state. Every
invasion of an old civilization by a new people results in from four hun
dred to six hundred years of autocratic rule; and during this period,
there is a gradual improvement in the arts of life. For another four hun
dred to six hundred years in which oligarchy prevails, the arts flourish.
Then there is a final phase of about four hundred years of democracy,
and during this time, the arts decay. "When democracy has attained
210



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 211
full power," we are told, "the majority without capital necessarily eat
up the capital of the minority and the civilization steadily decays until
the inferior population is swept away to make room for a fitter people."
(189, p. 124) Petrie contended that the arts have always followed each
other in a definite succession, sculpture being the first to rise and
decay, followed successively by painting, literature, and mechanics.
This scheme of Petrie's undoubtedly has value, but his generalizations
appear to be too sweeping.
Professor Charles H. Hapgood, of the University of New Hamp
shire, has made a special study of ancient maps and has published his
findings in Maps of the Ancient Sea Kings. In Chapter VIII of that
work, entitled U A Civilization That Vanished," he made the following
statement of opinion:
The evidence presented by the ancient maps appears to suggest the exist
ence in remote times, before the rise of any of the known cultures, of a true
civilization, of a comparatively advanced sort, which either was localized in
one area but had worldwide commerce, or was, in a real sense, a worldwide
culture. This culture, at least in some respects, may well have been more
advanced than the civilizations of Egypt, Babylonia, Greece and Rome. In
astronomy, nautical science, map making and possibly ship-building, it was
perhaps more advanced than any state of culture before the 18th century of
the Christian Era. (1 12, p. 193)
Professor Hapgood does not draw any conclusions as to what people
or to what part of the world this ancient culture could plausibly be
traced. A German historian of a century ago, Professor A. H. L.
Heeren, also speculated on the place of origin of the world's earliest
civilization, and he had no hesitation in locating it among the African
Ethiopians:
In Nubia and Ethiopia, stupendous, numerous and primeval monuments
proclaim so loudly a civilization contemporary to, aye, earlier than that of
Egypt, that it may be conjectured with the greatest confidence that the arts,
sciences and religion descended from Nubia to the lower country of
Mizraim; that civilization descended the Nile, built Memphis and finally
sometime later, wrested by colonization the Delta from the Sea. (Historical
Researches: African Nations by Arnold Hermann Ludwig Heeren, cited by Lady Lugard in 146, pp. 220-21)
In commenting on the above passage from Heeren, Lady Lugard ob
served: "The people of Ethiopia colonized to the north and west.



212 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Amongst their colonies to the north, one of the most important was Thebes. Thebes and Meroe together founded the colony Ammonium in the western desert, and through Thebes the religion of Meroe was
carried into Lower Egypt. It was at a much later period, about 1500 B.C., that Egypt returned upon Meroe and conquered it." (146, p.
221) As to the influence of the ancient Ethiopians in the Sudan, Lady Lu
gard offered the following evidence:
In corroboration of the view that the trade and influence of Meroe may have extended farther west than has as yet been ascertained by modern explorations, I may mention a fact told me by Zebehr Pasha, when, during his confinement at Gibraltar in 1886, he related to me the history of the foundation of his ephemeral empire in the Bahr-el-Ghazal. It was that, having
occasion to act as the military ally of a certain native king Tekkima, whose territory lay somewhere south and west of the spot marked upon modern
maps as Dem Suleiman or Dem Zebehr—that is, presumably about 8 degrees north and 25 degrees east, he was informed that he had to fight against magicians, who habitually came out of the earth, fought, and then
disappeared. A careful system of scouting disclosed to him the fact that they came from underground and when, after cutting off their retreat and conquering them, he insisted on being shown their place of habitation, he found it to be deeply buried in the sand, a wonderful system of temples, "far finer" to use the words in which he described it, "than modern eyes have seen in the mosques of Cairo and Constantinople." It was, he said, such work of massive stone as was done by the great races of old. Through this underground city of stone there ran a stream, and by the stream his na
tive antagonists lived in common straw native huts. "Were your people, then," he asked them, "a nation of stone cutters?" And they said "Oh, no! This is not the work of our forefathers but our forefathers found it here and we have lived for many generations in these huts." Whether this accidental discovery of unknown monuments may yet be repeated further west and links be established in a continuous chain of ancient civilization reaching from the Red Sea to the country west of Chad,
or whether the civilizations of the western and the eastern ends of the fer
tile belt of the Sudan were in fact separated from one another by a sea of which the waters of Chad are but the disappearing trace is, however, a
question which, interesting as it is, becomes ... a question rather of detail
than of principle.
If there was no connection by the south, there certainly was connection by the north, by means of which the early inhabitants of the Hausa States may have been brought under the same influences of civilization which spread from Ethiopia to ancient Egypt and thence to Europe and North Af
rica. (146, pp. 225-26)



ETHIOPIA ANi) EGYPT 213
Several Egyptologists have defended the idea that the ancestors of
the ancient Egyptians originally came from Asia; but there never was
any evidence to substantiate this position; and the only reason it was
adopted was that it was the fashion to believe that no African peoples
were capable of developing a great civilization. An American scholar,
GeofFry Parsons, in a well-documented historical study, referred to the
culture of Egypt as "genuinely African in its origin and development."
(182, p. 154)
The Greek historian Diodorus Siculus, who lived in the first century
B.C., wrote a world history in forty books. He utilized good sources
and recorded them accurately. In Book III is a valuable account of the
Ethiopian origin of Egyptian culture, from which we cite the following
extracts:
Now the Ethiopians, as historians relate, were the first of all men and the
proofs of this statement, they say, are manifest. For that they did not come
into their land as immigrants from abroad but were the natives of it and so
justly bear the name of autochthones (sprung from the soil itself), is, they
maintain, conceded by practically all men. . . .
They say also that the Egyptians are colonists sent out by the Ethiopians,
Osiris having been the leader of the colony. For, speaking generally, what
is now Egypt, they maintain, was not land but sea, when in the beginning the universe was being formed; afterwards, however, as the Nile during the
times of its inundation carried down the mud from Ethiopia, land was grad
ually built up from the deposit. . . . A*id the larger parts of the customs of
the Egyptians are, they hold, Ethiopian, the colonists still preserving their
ancient manners. For instance, the belief that their kings are gods, the very
special attention which they pay to their burials, and many other matters of
a similar nature, are Ethiopian practices, while the shapes of their statues
and the forms of their letters are Ethiopian; for of the two kinds of writing
which the Egyptians have, that which is known as popular (demotic) is
learned by everyone, while that which is called sacred (hieratic), is under
stood only by the priests of the Egyptians, who learn it from their fathers as
one of the things which are not divulged, but among the Ethiopians every
one uses these forms of letters. Furthermore, the orders of the priests, they
maintain, have much the same position among both peoples; for all are clean
who are engaged in the service of the gods, keeping themselves shaven, like
the Ethiopian priests, and having the same dress and form of staff, which is
shaped like a plough and is carried by their kings who wear high felt hats
which end in a knob at the top and are circled by the serpents which they
call asps; and this symbol appears to carry the thought that it will be the lot
who shall dare to attack the king to encounter death-carrying stings. Many



214 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
other things are told by them concerning their own antiquity and the col
ony which they sent out that became the Egyptians, but about this there is
no special need of our writing anything.
We must now speak about the Ethiopian writing which is called hiero
glyphic among the Egyptians, in order that we may omit nothing in our
discussion of their antiquities. Now it is found that the forms of their letters
take the shape of animals of every kind, and of the members of the human
body, and of implements and especially carpenter's tools; for their writing
does not express the intended concept by means of syllables joined one to
another, but by means of the significance of the objects which have been
copied and by its figurative meaning which has been impressed upon the
memory by practice. For instance, they draw the picture of a hawk, a croc
odile, a snake, and all of the merrtLers of the human body—an eye, a hand, a
face, and the like. Now the hawk signifies to them everything which hap
pens swiftly, since this animal is practically the swiftest of winged crea
tures. . . . And the crocodile is a symbol of all that is evil, and the eye is the
warder of justice and the guardian of the entire body. And as for the mem
bers of the body, the right hand with fingers extended signifies a procuring
of livelihood, and the left with the fingers closed, a keeping and guarding of
property. The same way of reasoning applies also to the remaining charac
ters, which represent parts of the body and implements and all other things;
for by paying close attention to the significance which is inherent in each
object and by training their minds through drill and exercise of the memory
over a long period, they read from habit everything which has been writ
ten. (67, pp. 89-97)
It has truly been observed that Egypt is the gift of the Nile, for this
seat of an ancient culture is six hundred miles long, and is hemmed in
by two ranges of barren limestone hills, which alternately approach and
retreat from each other, maintaining between them an average breadth
of seven miles. In the north they spread our and finally disappear, giv
ing way to a marshlike plain which extends to the Mediterranean Sea.
As we go southward up the Nile, the limestone hills are replaced by
those of granite; and these hills finally approach each other until they
almost touch. Through this gorge the river, during the season of inun
dation, rushes into the valley and runs northward to the sea. In the
winter and spring, the river rolls along sluggishly through a dry and
dusty plain; but in the summer all this changes; for then the waters
begin to move swiftly. The river first turns red in color, then later it
takes on a green hue, and eventually the Nile overflows its banks and
floods the valley to the bases of the hills. The whole valley becomes a
huge lake from which the villages rise like islands, being in fact built on



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 215
artificial mounds. The sources of the Nile are two great lakes located
deep in the heart of Africa: Lakes Victoria and Albert. As the clouds
laden with moisture pass over equatorial Africa, they release their wa
ters. This results in a rainy season often months' duration. The deluge
finds its way into the great lakes, and they discharge their excess wa
ters, which flow northward as the river Nile. As this stream flows
through a thirsty land, the parched soil drinks it up, but the waters con
tinue to come forth, and so push the river across a great desert stretch
ing from the latitude of Lake Chad to the Mediterranean Sea. The
river Nile owes its existence to the Great Lakes, but the inundation is
produced by a separate cause, in which the lakes do not participate. In
between the Nile and the mouth of the Arabian Gulf are the highlands
of Ethiopia, which rise toward the heavens thousands of feet above sea
level. Clouds floating in from above the Indian Ocean are intercepted
by these mountains as they travel northward. The clouds release their
burden of moisture, and during the rainy season two rivers rush
through their arid beds and roar into the Nile. The great river moves
northward between rocky walls as it traverses the Nubian Desert, and
when the lowlands of Egypt are reached, it overflows its banks. The
two Ethiopian tributaries make respectively important and unique con
tributions. The waters of the White Nile are crystal clear; but the At
bara and Blue Nile system brings along a rich black silt from the Abys
sinian highlands. This sediment finally settles over the land of Egypt as
a rich topsoil. When the waters recede, the farmers scatter their seed
over the fertile land and then wait for the solar energy to bring forth
their crops. But for the White Nile, the rivers of Ethiopia would be ab
sorbed by the thirsty desert; and without the Ethiopian waters, the
White Nile would be a barren water course. The river is generated by
the equatorial rains; the land, on the other hand, is produced by the
precipitation of the tropical rains on the slopes of the Ethiopian moun
tain pile. u ln that fair Egyptian valley," Winwood Reade noted, "fat
tened by a foreign soil, brightened by eternal sunshine, watered by ter
restrial rain, the natives were able to obtain a year's food in return for a
few days' toil, so were provided with a wealth of time which is essential
for a nation's growth." (203, p. 3)
The Edfu Text is of prime importance as a documentary source on
the early history of the Nile Valley. This famous inscription, found in
the 'Temple of Horus at Edfu, gives a valuable account of the origin of
the Egyptian civilization. According to this venerable record, civiliza



216 MAN, COD, A"ND CIVILIZATION
rion was brought from the south by a band of invaders led by King
Horus; and the followers of Horus were called the Blacksmiths on ac
count of their possession of implements made of iron. Sir Wallis Budge
argued that Horus and his blacksmiths came originally from western
Asia, bringing with them a religion based on the worship of the sun.
But this cannot have happened, since, as Dr. Robert Brirfault cogently
observed, there was no solar cult in western Asia in those early days.
This ancient culture has been traced back to Somaliland, though it
quite possibly had its origin on the shores of the Great Lakes of Central
Africa. In Somaliland, ruins of buildings have been found constructed
with dressed stone, and displaying an architectural style similar to that
of earliest Egypt. Professor Arthur G. Brodeur in The Pageant of Civi
lization asserted that the ancestors of the South Egyptians came origi
nally from this region, that they entered the Nile Valley by way of
Nubia and brought with them a well-developed civilization, and that
this migration took place long before 5000 B.C. The fact that these an
cient Africans possessed tools and weapons of iron should call forth no
surprise, for in the magazine Natural History, September-October,
1932, there appeared an article by the Italian explorer, Nino del
Grande, entitled "Prehistoric Iron Smelting in Africa," in which the
author described his discovery of an iron-smelting furnace in Northern
Rhodesia, of an antiquity of from five thousand to six thousand years.
We are told by some anthropologists that the earliest tribes of Egypt
were Hamites of Kushite origin; that is to say, they were of Ethiopian
ancestry and spoke an Hamitic type of language. According to Sir
Harry Johnston:
The Dynastic Egyptians were not far distant in physical type from the
Galla of today, but they had perhaps some element of the proto-Semite; and
their language, which is still rather a puzzle to classifiers, though mainly
Kushite in its features, exhibited early in its history the influence of Semitic
speech and no doubt absorbed into itself elements of the Libyan sister,
which it perhaps found already extending to the valley of the Nile. The Dynastic Egyptians evidently concentrated themselves in the narrow strip
of fertility along the banks of the Nile, not colonizing very markedly the
Red Sea coast lands. By about 8000 years ago, they had become the conquerors and rulers of Lower and Upper Egypt. The inhabitants of Egypt were thenceforth a people in which Hamitic (Libyan-Kushite), Semitic, Nilotic and even Sudanese-Negro elements were fused. (Cited by Dr. W.
E. B. DuBois in 73, p. 24)



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 217
Johnston referred to the Hamites as: "That Negroid race which was
the main stock of the ancient Egyptian, and is represented at the pres
ent day by the Somali, the Galla, and some of the blood of Abyssinia
and of Nubia, and perhaps by the peoples of the Sahara Desert." (132,
Vol. II, p. 473)
The Greek historian, Herodotus, observed that Egyptian women
went into the market place and engaged in trade, while their husbands
stayed at home and sat at the loom; and that sons were not compelled to
support their parents, but that daughters were obliged to do so,
whether they chose to or not. Diodorus Siculus, in referring to the ma
triarchal character of the Egyptian royal family, noted a similar state of
affairs among the commoners. "Among private citizens," he stated,
"the husband, by the terms of the marriage agreement, appertains to
the wife and it is stipulated between them that the man shall obey the
woman in all things." (Cited by Dr. Robert Briffault in 19, p. 279)
These customs seemed strange to the Greeks, but they were normal
features of African societies. Dr. Briffault has discussed this point with
characteristic lucidity:
The social features of prepatriarchal society have sometimes survived
under conditions of advanced civilization. This happened notably in Egypt.
Down to the time when a dynasty of Greek rulers sought to introduce for
eign usages, the conservative society of the great African kingdom, which
has contributed so largely to the material and intellectual culture of the
Western World, never lost the lineaments of a matriarchal social order.
. . . The functions of royalty in ancient Egypt were regarded as being
transmitted in the female line. While every Egyptian princess of the Royal
House was born a queen and bore the title and dignities of the office from
the day of her birth, a man only acquired them at his coronation, and could
do so only by becoming the consort of the royal princess. . . . Those fea
tures of the constitution of Egyptian royalty are not singular. They are sub
stantially identical with those obtaining in all other African kingdoms. (19, pp. 274-75)
Sir Gaston Maspero estimated that the ancient Egyptians first en
tered upon the stage of history around eight thousand to ten thousand
years B.C. (See Maspero's The Dawn of Civilization, 162, p. 44.) This
estimate we do not regard as excessive, for the Great Pyramid was
erected about six thousand years ago, and the Sphinx is believed to be much older. Massey noted: "There is a stone in the museum at Boulak
consecrated to the memory of a noteworthy transaction. We learn



218 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
from it rhar in the time of Khufu of the fourth dynasty, and founder of
the Great Pyramid, that the Sphinx and its recently discovered temple
not only existed, but were then in a state of dilapidation, and it is re
corded in the inscription that he restored them." (164, Vol. I, pp. 9
10) The basis of Egyptian chronology is the lost History of Egypt by the
historian Manetho of Sebennytus. The Pharaoh Ptolemy Philadelphus,
in the third century B.C., commissioned Manetho to write an authori
tative history of Egypt from the earliest times up to his own day. The
greater part of his history was lost in the destruction of the Alexan
drian Library, but among the few surviving fragments are Manetho's
lists of the kings of Egypt. This compilation divides the rulers of Egypt
into thirty dynasties. Modern Egyptologists have grouped the dynasties
into the following periods: The Old Kingdom (Dynasties I—VI), The Middle Kingdom (Dynasties XI-X1V), The Empire (Dynasties XVII1-XX), The Saite Age (Dynasty XXVI).
In the XXVllth Dynasty, the country was overrun by the Persians.
Since that time, Egypt has rarely been free from foreign rule. It should
be noticed that there were interludes of chaos between the Old and Middle Kingdoms, between the Middle Kingdom and the Empire, and
between the Empire and the Saitic Age. In the study of the history of
Egypt we have a choice of two chronological schemes. There is a short
chronology compiled by Professor James Henry Breasted and a long
one constructed by Sir Elinders Petrie. Both lists are given below for
comparison: - DATES B.C.

Duncan Macnaughron of Edinburgh, Scotland, has made an exhaustive study of Egyptian chronology and has embodied the results of his
researches in his valuable book A Scheme of Egyptian Chronology

etho passes all critical examination wherever it can be tested. We are
convinced that this is so, and hence, in treating of the history of Egypt,
we are dating events according to the long chronology. We have the
highest respect for the scholarship of Professor Breasted, but his short
chronology in our opinion is now quite obsolete. Macnaughton com

We have been advised by Macnaughton that "none of these esti
mates ever gained universal acceptance/' Sir Wallis Budge made the
following statement in 1914: "At the present time the dates proposed
by Egyptologists for the reign of Menes are 5869, 5702, 5613, 5004,
4400 and 3315 B.C. ... the earliest date that has been proposed by
any Egyptologist is, in my opinion, far more likely to be correct than
such a date as 3315 B.C." (161, p. 6)



220 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
The first king of Dynasty I was Aha Mena, or Menes. This Pharaoh
ascended the throne in 5776 B.C., according to Manetho, and reigned
for sixty-two years. Before the time of Menes, there were many petty
kings ruling over small territories in Upper and Lower Egypt. One
tribe in Lower Egypt had sixty kings prior to the reign of Menes. The
tribal totem of this kingdom was the hornet, and the royal crown was a
low red cap with a high peak at the back. Further south was the realm
of the reed kingdom, whose regal headdress was a tall white crown. Still
more distant in regions to the south were two hawk kingdoms; and
these domains were in time united by marriage and conquest. The fa
ther of Menes ruled over the united kingdom of the reeds and hawks of
Upper Egypt and, by conquest, he annexed the hornet kingdom of
Lower Egypt. When Menes took over the kingship he combined the
red and white crowns into the famous double crown of United Egypt.
Even in predynastic times the Egyptians had reached a high level of
civilization; for they imported gold, silver, tin, lead, iron, hematite,
emery, galena, turquoise, obsidian, serpentine, lapis lazuli, coral, and
tortoise shell; and with these materials produced beautiful and useful
works of art which were to call forth the wonder and admiration of
later ages. Egypt's first Golden Age was inaugurated by an invasion
from Ethiopia. According to Petrie: "A conqueror of Sudani features,
founded the Third Dynasty and many entirely new ideas entered the
country. This new movement culminated in the vast schemes of
Khufu, one of history's most dominating personalities. With him the
lines of Egyptian growth were established; and the course of events be
came the subject of the written record. ("Modern Discovery of the
Unknown Past" by Sir W. M. Flinders Petrie in 1 10, p. 112)
The great achievement of the reign of Khufu was the building of the
Great Pyramid—a structure celebrated in the annals of history as one
of the Seven Wonders of the Ancient World. The classic account of
the building of the Great Pyramid was related by Herodotus. From
that narrative, as translated by Canon George Rawlinson, we quote the
following extracts:
The Pyramid itself was twenty years in the building. It is a square 800
feet each way, . . . built entirely of polished stone, fitted together with the
utmost care. The stones of which it is composed are none of them less than
30 feet in length. . . . After laying the stones for the base, they raised the remaining stones to their places by means of machines formed of short
wooden planks. . . . There is an inscription in Egyptian characters on the



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 221
pyramid which records the quantity of radishes, onions and garlic con
sumed by the laborers who constructed it; and 1 perfectly well remember
that the interpreter who read the writing to me said that the money ex
pended in this way was about 1,600 talents of silver. If this, then, is a true
record, what a vast sum must have been spent on the iron tools used in the
work, and on the feeding and clothing of the laborers, considering the
length of time the work lasted, which has already been stated, and the addi
tional time—no small space I imagine—which must have been occupied by the quarrying of the stones, their conveyance, and the formation of the un
derground apartments. (118, p. 125)
The reader will have noticed that the Father of History specifically
referred to the iron tools used by the pyramid builders. Some modern
authorities deny that the Egyptians of the Pyramid Age possessed iron
implements. Professors King and Hall, of the Department of Egyptian
and Assyrian Antiquities, British Museum, have discussed this question
in their Egypt and Western Asia, to which the reader is referred.
The Abbe Thomas Moreux, eminent French astronomer, Director
of the Observatory of Bourges, produced a remarkable book The Mys
terious Science of the Pharaohs. In that work, he argued that the Great
Pyramid was used as a vault for the preservation of scientific instru
ments, and of standard weights and measures, rather than as a tomb. In
place of a sarcophagus, there was a granite slab, which served as stand
ard of measurement. The length of this slab was said to have been one
ten-millionth of the distance from either of the poles to the center of
the earth. This invariable distance, only recently determined by mod
ern scientists, is the basis of the metric system of measurement. The
distance from each pole to the center of the earth has been calculated as
3,949.79 miles. If this figure is correct, then the circumference of the
earth, measured as a great circle passing through the poles, is 24,817.32
miles. Abbe Moreux felt certain that these facts were known to the as
tronomers of ancient Egypt about six thousand years ago. The Sume
rians and Chaldeans of Mesopotamia were no mean students of astron
omy, but their best estimate of the circumference of the earth was
24,000 miles. The height of the Great Pyramid, at the time of its con
struction, was one-billionth of the distance from the earth to the sun
unit of measure not accurately established in modern times until 1874.
Abbe Moreux noted that this pyramid was oriented within one-twelfth
of a degree, a very accurate precision, and that the parallel of longitude
passing through the pyramid traverses the most land and least sea of
any in the world—a fact which also applies to the parallel of latitude



222 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
passing through the structure. In the north side of the Great Pyramid is
the entrance to an underground tunnel which is bored through 350 feet
of solid rock at an angle of 26 degrees, 1 7 minutes to the horizon.
Alpha Draconis, or Thuban, was the pole star about 3440 B.C., and for
several hundred years before and after that date. At its lower culmina
tion, when 3 degrees, 42 minutes from the pole, this star shone down
the underground tunnel. The ascending passage runs off from the un
derground tunnel of the pyramid at the base-line level; and the ascend
ing passage leads into the grand gallery. Both the ascending passage
and the grand gallery are inclined to the horizon at an angle of 26 de
grees and 17 minutes, the same as the inclination of the underground
tunnel, but in the opposite direction. These passages seem to have
served two purposes. Firstly, they enabled the builders to orient the
base and the lower layers of the pyramid up to the king's chamber in a
true north-south line. Secondly, the passages were so arranged that the
grand gallery could serve as the equivalent of the equatorial telescope of
a modern astronomical observatory.
The English astronomer, Richard A. Proctor, in his Problems of the
Pyramids presents much convincing evidence tending to show that the
Great Pyramid was used as an astronomical observatory. "The sun's
annual course round the celestial sphere," according to Proctor, "could be determined much more exactly than by any gnomon, by observa
tions made from the great gallery. The moon's monthly path and its
changes could have been dealt with in the same effective way. The ge
ometric paths, and thence the true paths of the planets, could be deter
mined very accurately. The place of any visible star along the zodiac
could be most accurately determined." (Cited by Samuel Laing in 138,
p. 56).
The triangular area of each of the four sides of the pyramid equals
the square of the vertical height. The added lengths of the four sides of
the square base bear to the vertical height the same proportion as that of
the circumference of a circle to its radius. This involves the mathemati
cal constant II (Pi). The numerical value of II, calculated to four deci
mal places, is 3.1416. The lengths of the base sides of the pyramid
added together would give us 3,055.16 feet. Its height is 486.23 feet.
Let us double the height, which gives us 972.46 feet. Now divide
3,055.16 by 972.46, and our answer comes out as 3.1416. Now let us
take the pyramidal height, 486.23 feet, and translate it into miles.
There are 5,280 feet in a mile, so we divide 486.23 by 5,280, and our
answer is 0.0921 mile. If we multiply 0.0921 by 1,000,000,000, we ob



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 223
tain 92,100,000 as an answer. In miles, this figure gives us the approximate distance of the earth from the sun. The average diameter of the earth has been calculated to be about 7,913.333 miles. This is changed
into feet by multiplying it by 5,280. (7,913.333 X 5,280 =
41,792,398.24 feet.) Now we divide this last figure by 20,000,000, and the resulting 2.09 feet is one twenty-millionth of the average diameter of the earth. In the words of an eminent mathematician, Professor
Aaron Bakst: "We are led to think that the Egyptian priests knew more about astronomy, the year, and the diameter of the earth than we
ever suspected." (6, p. 105) The length of each side of the square py
ramidal base measured 365% sacred cubits, which is equivalent to the
length of the year in days. The two diagonals of the base contained 25,824 pyramid inches, a good approximation of the number of years in
the precessional cycle. Professor Piazzi Smyth, a famous astronomer of the last century, made extensive measurements of the Great Pyramid. His results were summarized by Dr. Alfred Russell Wallace, in an ad
dress before the British Association for the Advancement of Science at
Glasgow in 1876, as follows:
1. That the pyramid is truly square, the sides being equal, and the angles
right angles;
2. That the four sockets on which the first four stones of the corners
rested are truly on the same level;
3. That the directions of the sides are accurate to the four cardinal
points;
4. That the vertical height of the pyramid bears the same proportion to
its circumference at the base as the radius of a circle does to its circumfer
ence.
Now all these measures, angles, and levels are accurate, not as an ordinary surveyor or builder could make them, but to such a degree as requires the very best modern instruments and all the refinements of geodetical sci
ence to discover any error at all. In addition to this, we have the wonderful
perfection of the workmanship in the interior of the pyramid, the passages and chambers being lined with huge blocks of stone fitted with the utmost
accuracy, while every part of the building exhibits the highest structural
science. (260, p. 117)
A talented young man from England landed in Egypt in 1881. This
individual, William Matthew Flinders Petrie, at that time only twenty
seven years of age, was soon to establish Egyptology on a truly scien
tific basis.
Petrie was an extraordinary man [we learn from a recent commentator] even among the other eminent Victorians. Though he never attended any



224 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
institution of higher learning, he made the pompous holders of a university
degree look like fumbling amateurs; and in due time, he was given honor
ary titles from dozens of academies and was appointed to England's first Chair of Egyptology, at the University of London. A man of enormous in
dependence of mind, he lacked the disposition to deal with officialdom and
often made scathing comments that, of course, reached the ears of his asso
ciates. Unfortunately, he was in need of official assistance, which at best, was precarious. That he managed at all was due to extreme austerity and
economy. (65, p. 99)
Petrie's first major undertaking in the archaeological field was a
study of the Great Pyramid, which kept him engaged for nearly two years. The young scientist was overwhelmed by the amazing crafts
manship displayed by the ancient builders. "The laying out of the base
of the Great Pyramid of Khufu," Petrie noted, "is a triumph of skill; its
errors both in length and in angles, could be covered by placing one's
thumb on them." But inside the structure he found some odd mistakes.
"After having levelled the casing so finely, the builders made a hundred
times the error in levelling the shorter length of the king's chamber, so
that they might have done it far better by just looking at the horizon.
After having dressed the casing joints so beautifully, they left the face
of the wall in the grand gallery rough chiselled." (Sir Flinders Petrie,
cited by Robert Silverberg in 227, p. 30) Petrie could only conclude
that an architectural and engineering genius had commenced the proj
ect and had died before the job was completed. His successor appar
ently was a man of lesser stature, who was both careless and inept. The
Pharaoh Khufu has been pictured by certain modern historians as a
cruel and remorseless tyrant who employed slave labor to erect his co
lossal pyramid; but this opinion is most certainly erroneous, for slavery
was practically unknown in ancient Egypt. "It seems that on the
whole," to cite a modern authority, "slavery never attained the serious
and infamous proportions that it had in Greece, or in Italy. The serf
age, which probably continued throughout the history, prevented the
requirements of slave labor on large estates. It was a mild and compara
tively harmless obligation, which did not prevent ability from rising,
and it saved the land from the ruin which slavery brings." (190, p. 25)
Some of the cinema spectaculars turned out by Hollywood studios
have given an entirely false picture of life in ancient Egypt. It is to the
credit of the English Egyptologist, Sir Arthur Weigall, that he has
made a protest against this type of propaganda. We have been assured by Weigall that:



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 225
The kindest race of the ancient world was that of the Egyptians; and if
we direct our course to the Nile Valley of three or four thousand years ago,
we shall find conditions there so humane that instances of pharaonic cruelty such as we have been shown in some recent film dramas will be seen at
once to reflect the mentality of the producer and not that of the people he
has thought to portray. The Egyptians were seldom consciously cruel; they
did not grind men to pulp under the wheels of their juggernauts, they did
not lash their laboring slaves, they did not torture people, they did not cal
lously inflict death upon anybody—in fact, they never did any of the things
of this sort which the film producers are at such pains to present to us. (262,
p. 58)
Weigall was Inspector General of Antiquities for the Egyptian gov
ernment for several years and had a wide and sound knowledge of the
ancient mores of the Egyptian people. For more details, the reader is
referred to the work cited above. "The Egyptians, indeed," Sir Arthur
concluded, "were tenderhearted to an astonishing extent; and that
being so, let me urge the film producer, should he feel impelled to wal
low for a while in the sink of iniquity, to leave out of the picture these
humane dwellers on the banks of the Nile. The bestial, half naked,
hairy chested executioner, beloved of Hollywood, did not exist in
Egypt." (262, p. 63) The building of the Great Pyramid was a skillfully organized project. One hundred thousand men were employed, three months at a
time, in transporting the rocks to the construction site. This was during
the season of the inundation, when there was no other work to be done.
Ten years were spent in constructing the great causeway, over which
the stones were hauled, and in preparing the site, and leveling and hol
lowing out the underground tunnel and chamber. The actual construc
tion required an additional twenty years. Sir Flinders Petrie has given
us a vivid impression of how this vast engineering enterprise was ac
complished:
This time would imply that a gang of eight men (about as many as could
work on one block) could move ten stones from the quarry across the Nile, up the causeways and have raised into place within three months. This
would be quite possible with good organization. There are several cause
ways besides the main one, still visible on the desert, and they must have been closely packed by working gangs to get up the thousand blocks every
day during the working season. Of course, there were also highly skilled
masons necessary for the thirteen acres of finely jointed casing, and the in
ternal parts; the barracks for these are still visible and would hold at the out



226 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
side 4000 men, who would live there continuously. If half of them were en
gaged on the casing, each man would have to prepare accurately and fit in
place one casing block every three weeks, or rather, a gang of three men
doing a block in a week. This is also a reasonable result. Of course, the
great blocks at the base would take far longer and the small courses would
be done in perhaps half the time for each stone. . . . Much nonsense has
been written about the oppression of the people, their tears and groans.
With the splendid organization evident in the work, the people must have
been well managed, and there was no hardship, whatever, in carrying out
the work. Each man might have been levied twice in his lifetime; he would
be just as well off there as at home, for he could do nothing during the in
undation. All that was necessary was to transport a couple of hundredweight of food with him, which he would eat there instead of at home. The
immense gain to the people was the education in combined work and tech
nical training. (190, pp. 25-27)
An unusual feature connected with the orientation of the Great Pyr
amid was studied by Colonel Braghine, which we let him present in his own words:
A detailed study of the structure will convince any investigator that the
wealth of mathematical, geometrical and astronomical data concealed
within it is not accidental, but has been produced intentionally after numer
ous and complex calculations, made by somebody possessing an astounding
amount of knowledge. . . . Not the least interesting detail concerning the
orientation of the pyramid is the following: the reflection of the sun rays
from the sides of the pyramid indicate almost exactly the equinoxes and sol
stices and therefore, the sowing time. The northern side of the pyramid is
lighted at sunrise for some moments during the period from the spring equinox to the autumn equinox. During the remainder of the year, the
southern side is lighted from sunrise till sunset. This phenomenon fixes the
moment of the equinoxes within 12 hours. When the stone facing was intact, this phenomenon of the missing shadows must have been still more pronounced and was noticed by the ancients. The Latin poet Ausonius
writes: "Quadrata cui in fastigo cono surgit et ipsa suas consumit puramis umbras" ("The pyramid itself swallows the shadow born on its summit"). This phenomenon has now been explained by Professor Pochan, who dis
covered that the northern and southern sides of the pyramid are not true
planes, but dihedral angles of 1 79 degrees, 50 minutes. Thus in plain speak
ing, the sides in question have been hollowed out to the extent of 64 centi
meters, insuring a rapid disappearance of the shadow of the sunrise at the
equinoxes. (14, pp. 237-38)



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 227
Among the great inventions of the Egyptians were the alphabet,
paper, ink, and the pen. The earliest paper was made of thin strips of
papyrus reed, pasted together. Ink was made from vegetable gum and
soot mixed with water. By dipping a pointed reed in the ink, the Egyp
tian scribe wrote messages on the papyrus sheets. Writing was a basic
invention, and its influence on the evolution of civilization was pro
found. As Breasted has truly asserted: ''The invention of writing and of
a convenient system of records on paper has had a greater influence in
uplifting the human race than any other intellectual achievement in the
career of man. It was more important than all the battles ever fought
and all the constitutions ever devised." (15, p. 45)
Another great Egyptian invention was the calendar. The earliest
Egyptians measured time by observing the phases of the moon. The
month (the interval between two new moons) was the unit of time
measurement. Unfortunately, the lunar month varies in length from
twenty-nine to thirty days, and hence the moon calendar is cumber
some and inaccurate. The lunar year of 354 days falls short of the solar
year by eleven days. Such a calendar can be made to work only by in
tercalating an extra month at suitable intervals; and the Egyptians met
this difficulty by devising an accurate calendar. It was noticed by them
that the dog star Sothis, or Sirius, rose heliacally (before sunrise) on
July 19th, at the beginning of the inundation (the annual overflow of
the Nile). One of the earliest fixed dates in recorded history is believed
to have been July 19, 4236 B.C., for on this date, it is said, our calendar
was born. This calendar consisted of twelve months, each of thirty
days' duration. To this total of 360 days were added five extra days at
the end of the year. Each thirty-day month was divided into three
weeks of ten days each, and there were three seasons recognized, each
of four months. The seasons bore the following names: (1) Inundation,
(2) Seed Time, (3) Harvest.
The Egyptian calendar is given below, and, for the benefit of the
reader, the months of our own Gregorian calendar are correlated:
Egyptian Gregorian
INUNDATION
Thoth End of June Paophi Athyr Khoiak
End of July End of August End of September



228 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
SEED-TIME
Tybi End of October Menchir End of November Phamenoth End of December Pharmouthi End of January
HARVEST
Pachons End of February Payni End of March Epiphi End of April Mesori End of May
The astronomers of ancient Egypt knew that the true length of the
years was close to 365 4 days and that the calendar could have been im
proved by inserting an extra day every four years. King Ptolemy Euer
getes issued a decree in 238 B.C. ordering that every fourth year
should be of 366 days' duration, but the Egyptians were opposed to the
institution of leap year, so they just ignored the royal decree. This ven
erable calendar, established in Egypt around six thousand years ago, has
descended down the stream of history to our own day. It was nearly
ruined by a couple of Roman politicians, who were bent on getting
their names perpetuated in the calendar. Julius Caesar, for instance, is
commemorated by the month July. Then Augustus Caesar insisted on
having the month of August in the calendar to perpetuate his name.
Not being satisfied with thirty-day months, these imperial personages
made July and* August into thirty-one-day months at the expense of
February, which was reduced to twenty-eight days. As a result of this
calendrical jugglery, we now have November, which was originally the
ninth month, showing up as the eleventh, and December, originally the
tenth month, winding up as the twelfth. The division of the circle into
360 parts may have also been an Egyptian invention. This point was
discussed by Breasted in a lecture, which we are pleased to quote:
It is certain that the length of the stellar year as recorded by successive
sunrise reappearances of Sirius was at first roughly established by the Egyp
tians as 360 days. As early as the fourth thousand-years B.C., that is, well
back of 3000 B.C., we find this 360-day year divided into 36 decads of 10
days each, for grouping the constellations along the celestial equator. This
appearance of a circle of 36 decads in the fourth millennium B.C. is highly
significant. It is certainly the oldest appearance of a circle of 360 divisions.
The Sumerian sexagesimal system, in which 60 appears as a numerical unit



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 229
(called sussu), is without doubt enormously old; and in all probability arose
from the length of the year— 360 days—by dividing it into six parts. ("The
Beginnings of Time—Measurement and the Origins of Our Calendar," a
lecture delivered by Professor James Henry Breasted at New York Univer
sity, May 16, 1935)
Breasted thought that the sexagesimal system of circular measure
ment was known earlier in Egypt than in Babylonia, but conceded that
it might have been derived by both the Egyptians and the Sumerians
from some more ancient common culture. (See 1 7.)
Before taking leave of Egypt's first Golden Age, we must say some
thing about Imhotep, the architect who designed the first pyramid.
This truly great man was vizier to King Zoser of Dynasty III (5345
5307 B.C.). He was also an astronomer, and held the post of Chief
Physician to the sovereign. In later days, he was deified, becoming the
God of Medicine. Known to the Greeks as Imouthes, he was metamor
phosed into their own Aesculapius. "A temple was erected to him near
the Serapeum at Memphis," we have been told by Breasted, "and at
the present day, every museum possesses a bronze statuette or two of
this apotheosized wise man, the proverb-maker, physician and architect
of Zoser. The priests who conducted the rebuilding of the Temple of
Edfu under the Ptolemies claimed to be reproducing the structure
formerly erected there after plans of Imhotep; and it may, therefore,
well be that Zoser was the builder of a temple there." (16, p. 95)
Egypt's first Golden Age ended in 4163 B.C. when the death of
King Neterkere ended the Vlth Dynasty. From 4163 to 3554 B.C.,
chaos prevailed in the Nile Valley, and this intermediate period covers
Dynasties VII through X. Often several rulers claimed the throne at
one time. This period of confusion was ended by a Theban nobleman
named Intef, who became the first king of Dynasty XI, which began
the Middle Kingdom. Intef I was succeeded by Intef II, who, in turn,
was followed by five Mentuhoteps. Mentuhotep V was so feeble that
he was dethroned by Amenemhet I, who established Dynasty XII.
This monarch was a man of outstanding ability. An inscription of his
age states that "he restored that which he found ruined; that which a
city had taken from its neighbor; while he caused city to know its
boundary with city, establishing their landmarks like the heavens, dis
tinguishing their waters according to what was in the writings, investi
gating according to that which was of old, because he so greatly loved
justice." (Cited by Robert Silverberg in 227, p. 9)



230 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Under Sesostris I (3 373-3327 B.C.) a period of expansion ensued,
and the boundaries of Egypt were extended into the Kingdom of Kush
in the south. The armies of Amenemhet II invaded the Sinai Peninsula
and, under Sesostris III, the conquest was extended into Syria. Since
the dollar has a tendency to follow the flag, the Egyptian merchants
followed in the wake of the armies, and the country benefited from the
resulting foreign commerce. In 3184 B.C., Amenemhet IV died leav
ing no heir to the throne. This created a problem, for in Egypt, as in
most African kingdoms, the kingship was inherited through the
daughter of the monarch. That is to say, the new queen transmitted the
regal prerogatives to her husband, who became the next pharaoh. The
kingly office passed from father to son-in-law; and in order to preserve
the royal succession within the family, the custom was to have the old
est son of the king marry his oldest sister, through whom the right of
sovereignty was transmitted. This made the son of the king also his
son-in-law, and gave him the right of succession to the throne. Amen
emhet IV had followed the prescribed procedure by marrying his sister
Sebeknefrure. Since he had died childless, the queen was privileged to
select the next king, inasmuch as her husband would, by law, inherit
the crown. Queen Sebeknefrure, it was assumed, would marry a The
ban noble and elevate him to the throne, but instead she married a com
moner from lower Egypt. The result of this action was a state of civil
war, for the Theban nobility refused to have a northerner from the
Delta as their king; and the ensuing civil strife lasted for about one
hundred years. Kings of Dynasty XIII ruled from Thebes, and a rival
ruler of Dynasty XIV occupied the throne at Memphis; while both
monarchs claimed the succession to Dynasty XII. The armies of both
regimes fought each other up and down the valley with neither side
being able to gain a victory. Now, while this senseless civil war was
going on, the country was invaded by nomads from Asia, known as the
Hyksos, or Shepherd-Kings. First, they conquered Lower Egypt, then
moved up the river and captured Thebes; and these foreign invaders dominated Egypt during the XVth, XVlth, and XVIIth Dynasties, a
time span of about 1 50 years.
While these alien kings controlled the land, certain Theban nobles
traveled southward to Kush and organized an underground liberation movement. The Hyksos were finally expelled from Thebes by an
army led by the Pharaoh Sekenenre, who ended his career on the field
of battle. He was followed in the kingship by the Theban monarch,
Ahmose I, who ascended the throne in 1709 B.C. This king led his



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 231
army to the north, liberated Memphis, and drove the Hyksos out of
Egypt into the desert of Sinai. Thus was the beginning of Dynasty XVI11, which inaugurated the time of the New Kingdom. The new
line of kings erected palaces and temples at Thebes which were among the wonders of the world and were heralds of a new Golden Age in the
Nile Valley. Under Thutmose I (1662-1628) Egyptian imperialism
reached its zenith. An admirable summary of this achievement has been
given by Professor Breasted:
Egypt had now become the controlling power in the far reaching group
of civilizations clustering in and about the eastern end of the Mediterra
nean, the center, perhaps the nucleus of the civilized world of rhat day. . . .
Seated astride both the intercontinental and interoceanic highway, Egypt
was building up and dominating the world of contiguous Africa and Eura
sia. Traditional limits disappeared, the currents of life eddied no longer
within the landmarks of tiny kingdoms, but pulsed from end to end of a great empire, embracing many kingdoms and tongues, from the Upper
Nile to the Upper Euphrates. The wealth of Asiatic trade circulating through the eastern end of the Mediterranean which once flowed down the
Euphrates to Babylon, was thus diverted to the Nile Delta, long before
united by canal with the Red Sea. All the world traded in the Delta mar
kers. ("Zenith of Egyptian Power" by James Henry Breasted, in 37, Vol.
II, p. 88)
The true glory of the age culminated in the reign of Amenhotep 111 (1538-1501 B.C.). Timber was imported from Syria, and large
seaworthy ships were built. With this fleet the Egyptian mariners and
merchants sailed down the East African coast and traded with the peo
ple of Punt, from whom they imported cargoes of ebony, ivory, ostrich
feathers, spices, and gold. To the south, they extended their sway over
the Kushites of Nubia, and in the north, they overwhelmed a confederation of foes in a battle at Har-Meggiddo (Armageddon). The domain
of Amenhotep III extended from the confines of Rush to the plains of
Shinar; and all these territories were so well organized and so fairly
governed as to greatly enhance their productive potential. Thebes ex
panded into a mighty metropolis with walls nine miles in circumfer
ence. In the suburbs, elegant mansions were erected for the nobility,
some of them containing fifty or sixty rooms. Their walls were deco
rated with fine paintings. They were fitted out with costly and attrac
tive inlaid furniture, and adorned with beautiful vases and skillfully
carved ornaments and utensils of bronze, ebony, and ivory. Along the
river front, superb temples were built by order of the king and were



232 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
linked together by impressive avenues of sphinxes. Surrounding the
mansions and temples were tree-shaded boulevards and gardens of
flowers, while the adjacent landscape was enhanced by a series of lakes.
Since the horse had been introduced into Egypt by the Hyksos, an im
proved system of transport developed: Bigger and better roads were
constructed, and Egyptian gentlemen traversed the highways in swift horse-drawn chariots.
The death of Amenhotep III, in 1 501 B.C., ended a brilliant reign of
thirty-six years and five months. He was succeeded by his son, the boy
Amenhotep IV. Queen Ti, mother of the young king, acted as regent
until her son reached his majority. The new monarch inaugurated a re
ligious revolution which brought him fame and, at the same time, ended the Golden Age established by his father. The young pharaoh betook himself from Thebes and established a new city, Tell el Amarna, as his capital. He repudiated the numerous gods of the Egyptian pantheon, and recognized only one god, Aton, whose symbol was the flaming disk of the sun; and he even renamed himself Ikhnaton (devoted to Aton). The nomarchs (provincial governors) had warned him that hostile armies were gathering on the borders and planning to in
vade the nation, but he heeded them not. The priests of the disestab
lished cults finally rebelled and Ikhnaton, the Heretic king, died ignominiously, probably poisoned by his enemies; and the Aton cult was abolished. On the enthronement of his youthful son-in-law, Tutankha
men, the capitol was moved back to Thebes, and the old religious cults were restored to power. The career of King Tut was ended by his
early death, at the age of seventeen years. Then the throne was seized by a priest named Eye, whose reign brought ruin to the country; for,
during his brief rule, nearly all the foreign territories annexed by the great kings of Dynasty XVIII were lost. Harmhab, a general from the North, ascended the river and toppled the usurper Eye from the
throne, and brought the period of decline to an end. The monotheistic Aton cult was crushed in Egypt, but it did not
perish entirely; since there was a young priest Moshe (Moses), who
had received his theological education at the Temple of Heliopolis, and who became a disciple of Ikhnaton. When Atonism was suppressed in Egypt, Moses led a group of heretics out of the country and reestab
lished the cult in Palestine. The Egyptian historian, Manetho, is our
authority for saying that Moses was an Egyptian priest. His opinion was endorsed by Strabo, who wrote: "Moses, who was one of the Egyptian priests, taught riis followers that it was an egregious error to
represent the deity under the form of animals, as the Egyptians did, or



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 233
in the shape of man, as was rhe practice of the Greeks and Africans.
... It is for this reason, that, rejecting every species of images or idols, Moses wished the deity to be worshipped without emblems and according to his proper nature; and he accordingly ordered a temple wor
thy of him to be erected." (Strabo, Geography, Book 16, cited by Count Volney in 258, pp. 150-51) Moses, as we know, did not enjoy an unqualified success in his promulgation of the new religion, since
many of his followers still recognized the old gods.
But in vain did he proscribe the worship of the symbols which prevailed in lx)wer Egypt and Phoenicia: as Volney observed, "for his god was, nevertheless, an Egyptian god invented by those priests of whom Moses had
been the disciple. ... In vain did Moses wish to blot from his religion
everything which had relation to the stars; many traits call them to mind in
spite of all he has done. The seven planetary luminaries of the great candle
stick; the twelve stones, or signs in the Urim of the high priests; the feasts
of the two equinoxes (entrances and gates of the two hemispheres); the cer
emony of the lamb (the celestial ram, then in the fifteenth degree); ... all
remain as so many witnesses of the filiation of his ideas, and of their extrac
tion from the common source. (258, pp. 149-51)
The Temple of Amen-Ra at Karnak, erected in 3700 B.C., was one
of the glories of ancient Egypt. "The temple built at Thebes," Profes
sor DuBois noted, "had a relief representing the four courts of the four
quarters of the Nilotic world: Dedun, the great God of Ethiopia, repre
sents the south; Sopd, the eastern desert; Sebek, the western desert; and
Horus, the north." (75, p. 137) Of all the solar temples of old Egypt,
this one was indubitably the most impressive. Sir Norman Lockyer,
one of the great astronomers of modern times, has given us a fine de
scription of this ancient temple in his excellent book The Dawn of Astronomy: A Study of the Temple Worship and Mythology of the Ancient
Egyptians, which we quote:
The solar temple of Amen-Ra at Karnak is the finest Egyptian solar temple which remains open to our examination. It is beyond all question the most majestic ruin in the world. It covers about twice the area covered by
St. Peter's at Rome, so that the whole structure was of a vastness absolutely unapproached in the modern ecclesiastical world. It is one of the most soulinspiring temples which have ever been conceived or built by man. There is a sort of stone avenue in the center giving a view towards the northwest, and this axis is something like five hundred yards in length. The whole ob
ject of the builder of the great temple at Karnak was to preserve that axis
absolutely open, the point being that the axis should be absolutely open
straight and true. The axis was directed toward the hills on the west side of



234 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the Nile where are located the tombs of the kings. From the entrance py
lons the temple stretches through various halls of different sizes and details
until at last at the extreme end what is called the Sanctuary, Naos, Adytum, or Holy of Holies is reached. The end of the temple at which the pylons are
situated is open, the other closed. Every part of the temple was built to sub
serve a special object, viz. to limit the light which fell on its front into a nar
row beam and to carry it to the other extremity of the temple into the sanc
tuary so that once a year, when the sun set at the solstice, the light is passed
without interruption along the whole length of the temple finally illumi
nating the sanctuary in most resplendent fashion and striking the sanctu
ary wall. We find that a narrow beam of sunlight coming through a narrow en
