trance some five hundred yards away from the door of the Holy of Holies
would, provided the temple were properly oriented to the solstice, and pro
vided the solstice occurred at the absolute moment of sunrise, or sunset, ac
cording to which the temple was being utilized, practically flash into the
sanctuary and remain there for about a couple of minutes, and then pass
away. . . . The magnificent burst of light at sunset into the sanctuary
would show that a new true solar year was beginning. (Sir Norman Lockyer in The Dawn of Astronomy, cited by William Tyler Olcott in 1 80, pp. 274-77)
The Grand High Priest of the Temple of Amen-Ra in the year 500 B.C. was a dark and handsome man named Mesocharis. He was about
sixty years of age, possessed of superior intellect and vast learning. He
was preparing to celebrate the festival of the New Year, and was stand
ing in the rear end of the world's largest temple, in a room which was
an exact cube in form; for its length, height, and width were all equal
to each other. He was clad in fine linen of royal purple color, which
was decorated with cloth of gold. On his head was a lofty mitre,
adorned with sparkling jewels and the insignia of his high office. He
wore on his breast a plate, ten inches square, set with twelve gem
stones arranged in four rows, each containing three stones each. These
precious stones were symbols of the zodiacal signs, and they were the
following: sardius, topaz, carbuncle, emerald, sapphire, diamond, lig
ure, agate, amethyst, beryl, onyx, jasper.
Each of these stones of the oracular breastplate was set in a socket of
gold, with the diamond located in the center position and representing
the constellation of Leo the Lion, since therein the summer solstice oc
curred. The waist of the priest was circled by an abnet, a girdle woven
from the finest linen, and dyed with the colors blue, purple, and scarlet.
His ephod, or tunic, was also an embroidered coat of fine twined linen
adorned with the figures of stars, gods, men, the sun, and animals.



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 235
Over his shoulders rested a leopard skin, his feer were clad in jewel-be
decked sandals, and he carried in his right hand a wand fashioned of
wood and gold. Mesocharis, as Grand High Priest, ranked second only
to the reigning pharaoh. He was descended from a long line of priests
going as far back as the Illrd Dynasty. In the temple library were his
torical data, genealogies of preceding priests, records of festivals and of
astronomical phenomena, especially those pertaining to the zodiac.
When the sun apparently crosses the celestial equator in March and
September, at the time of the equinoxes, it sets exactly in the west, if
the center of the sun crosses the equator at the moment of sunset. The
distance measured on the horizon to the north or south of the equator is
called amplitude, and is measured in degrees, minutes, and seconds of
arc. At the moment of sunset, the distance of the sun's center from the
equator may be found with precision with modern instruments. After
the apparent equatorial crossing, the sun moves a certain distance
northward, then halts for a moment at maximum amplitude, then re
treats again toward the equator. The ancient Temple of Amen-Ra had
its central aisle directed toward the point where the sun set at the very
moment when its center was at the greatest possible distance from the
celestial equator, in summer, in the northern half of the celestial sphere.
This solar temple faced to the northwest, and a straight line drawn
from the position of the center of the sun at the summer and winter
solstices would traverse the axis of this temple. This imaginary line was
in the exact plane of the orbit of the earth; and extended from the
tropic of Cancer to that of Capricorn. Since this temple was con
structed for the specific purpose of garnering the light of the sun in the
sanctuary at sunset of the summer solstice, its northwest end was open,
whereas its southeast end was walled up completely. From time imme
morial, a high priest had officiated at the New Year ceremonial in the
temple, but in the year 500 B.C. something unusual happened, and the
Grand High Priest Mesocharis faced a crisis in his career. The story of
this incident has been told so well by Professor Edgar L. Larkin that
we give it in his own words:
For centuries, a high priest of Egypt had entered the Holy of Holies
once each year on the day of the summer solstice to behold the rays from
the setting sun, the sun of Egypt, come stealing into the dark and silent
sanctuary. For when the tiny pencil appeared on a polished reflecting sur
face in the darkened room, that supreme moment was the instant of New Year. This New Year's day was of far more importance to the Egyptians
than it is to us, for on an average, during thousands of years, the Nile began
to rise on the solstitial day, the day of days. From lethargy, a nation wak



236 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
ened into activity in a day. Agriculture began from Philae to Memphis; and
the entire country soon put on garments of living green. . . . The day of
the summer solstice was to the Egyptians as great as the winter solstice
Christmas—is to northern races.
On this solstice, however, the High Priest came an hour earlier than
usual to his beloved sanctuary. The first and entirely usual act of Meso
charis was to make the thin opening in the door of the Holy of Holies nar
rower than before. The mark on the rear wall, where the central ray from the sun had fallen for more than 3000 years was inspected and the dust re
moved. During the day, he had seen that all apertures along the axis of the
temple were in order and that no side light disturbed the straight band from
the sun. Then the multitude was admitted. . . . The sun approached the
horizon. Nearer it declined, and finally the critical moment arrived. The
tension in the minds of the people was intense. The priest opened wide the
door, held a diamond in the beam, flashed the light to all parts of the temple,
and gave his annual blessing to the waiting thousands, and proclaimed the
glad New Year. The people, with a mighty shout, rushed into the streets
with trumpets, proclaiming "New Year, New Year, the Nile, the waters,
joy and happiness!" The great festival was in progress and good will
reigned throughout the entire nation.
Everybody was happy except Mesocharis, the august High Priest of
Egypt, one of the most exalted dignitaries in the world. His mind was a tu
multuous sea, a tempest raged within. Dismay, alarm, and fear, a nameless
kind of dread filled his very being with crushing power. The central solar ray
did not fall on the ancient mark in the wall of Egypt's Holy of Holies; but to
one side, towards the north! (141, pp. 232-35)
Mesocharis was sorely troubled, for he knew that the pharaoh would
call for some sort of explanation of this unusual happening, and the
problem utterly baffled him. He summoned to Thebes the priests of the
solar temples in Memphis, Abydos, and Heliopolis for a conference,
and in due time they came. They had also noticed that the sun's rays
missed their mark, but could not figure out the reason why. Pinotem, a
priest from Memphis, suggested that they visit the king in a body and
tell him what had happened to the light of Egypt on New Year's day.
The assembled priests, headed by Mesocharis, approached the throne
of the reigning monarch and related to him the bad news. The pharaoh
was astounded and asked that they meet with him again on the third
day for further discussion of the problem.
What then happened is best told in the words of Professor Larkin:
Every nation of antiquity from Gaul to India had esoteric mysteries, se
cret societies, whose members were received by solemn rites of initiation.



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 237
The chief or grand lodge of Egypt (also the chief one in the world), was
due to meet in the great Labyrinth in Arsinoe, on the day of the first full
moon after the summer solstice. On the third day, the four priests came to counsel with their monarch. The approaching Grand Lodge was mentioned, of which the king was Grand Master and must preside, while Meso
charis himself was Grand Warden. Meines, priest of the solar temple at
Heliopolis, proposed that inquiry be made of all priests and delegates from the temples of Egypt and from temples in foreign countries to find whether
their temples were turning. Hikten, priest of the nome of Abydos, agreed
to the plan. Meanwhile, it was decided that this shifting of the solar rays
should nor be revealed to the people and by no means, to the army, save to
its commanders who were initiates into the mysteries.
When the full moon appeared, the grand Labyrinth was a scene of ani
mation. Hierophants, princes, scholars, literary men, and the most intellec
tual men of the world filled the enormous rooms; traversed the mysterious
subterranean ways and corridors, and held feasts of intellect amid surround
ings of culture and refinement. Members of the mysteries of Isis and Osiris
came from Ellora, Persepolis, Susa, Eckbatana, Nineveh, Babylon, Palmyra, Jerusalem, Tyre, Sidon, Athens, Dodona, Eleusis, Samothrace, Corinth and Rome. Many candidates from the temples in these world centers were
waiting to receive initiatory rites in the Labyrinth. When all business had
been transacted, the subject of the shifting of solar temples was broached by
the king. Pent-up excitement at once broke forth. Every priest present from every temple in the cities mentioned had noticed the mystery. No one
could even suggest a cause.
Priests of many other types of religion were present, also Hierophants
were in attendance who presided over sacrifices and rites in vast temples
orientated towards the larger stars. Temples in all parts of the world, of
porphyry, marble and onyx, whose axes were directed to the stars, Sirius,
Canopus, Aldebaran, Vega, Arcturus and others, were declared to be turning their Holy of Holies away from the tiny beams. Consternation filled
the minds of all. They realized that they were in the presence of an appall
ing awe-inspiring mystery. Could the gods be displeased with all mankind? And were all hierarchies and kingdoms to end? Were the gods preparing to
destroy man and the earth? (141, pp. 239-41)
We can readily understand the puzzlement of the ancient Egyptian
priests when faced with these baffling astronomical problems. The star
temples had been giving the Egyptian priests trouble long before the
building of solar temples. They had experienced the shifting positions
of the stars, but that the movements of the sun-god Ra should become
unpredictable was something that they could in no way explain. Olcotr
noticed that: "An extremely interesting feature of Egyptian temple
orientation, although it only pertains to temples dedicated to star wor



238 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
ship, is found in the fact that the precession of the equinoxes necessi
tated an alteration of the axis of the temple at long intervals to accom
modate the change of direction of the star to which the temple was
originally oriented. At Luxor and many other places in Egypt, there
are to be seen evidences of this change, a- later axis having been con
structed to meet the new requirements/' (180, p. 277) George St.
Clair argued that, though the Egyptian astronomers were acquainted
with the precession of the equinoxes, they were ignorant of its cause
and perplexed by the change it produced in the declination of the stars.
"It dislocated their catalogs and the havoc which it wrought seemed to
be so entirely mischievous that it could only be attributed to powers in
imical to the divine order. To this notion we owe many of the myths."
(219, p. 43) We have noted that the Temple of Amen-Ra at its building in 3700
B.C. was oriented to the plane of the earth's orbit. But the plane of the
earth's orbit has a motion of its own, not connected with the precession
of the equinoxes, and this was a mystery that the ancient astronomers
did not even suspect. According to Professor Edgar L. Larkin: "Long
before the temple was erected, this plane had been slowly moving
nearer to the plane of the earth's equator; and is still, and will for sev
eral thousand years to come. It will stop and slowly retrace its steps,
thus rocking to and fro like a pendulum, to a distance of one and one
half degrees on each side of its middle position. The cause is the in
fluence of attraction and motions of all the planets, Jupiter, Saturn, etc.,
on the earth." (141, p. 241) Professor Larkin, in the early years of the
present century, was Director of the Lowe Observatory on Echo
Mountain, in the Sierra Madre Range of California. He was a distin
guished astronomer and wrote many scientific articles for The Open
Court, a quality monthly magazine published in Chicago and edited by
Dr. Paul Carus, the well-known philosopher. Dynasty XIX began with the reign of Hormhab (1454-1395 B.C.),
but the great ruler of this dynasty was Rameses II (1394-1328 B.C.).
This king in his sixty-six-year reign conquered extensive territories in
western Asia and built colossal temples in the Nile Valley. In Ethiopia,
Rameses was worshiped as a god; for among the Kushites, he erected
six temples dedicated to Amen, Ra, and Ptah. In discussing these tem
ples, Breasted stated that "in all of them, Rameses was more or less
prominently worshipped, and in one, his queen, Nefretiri, was the pre
siding divinity. Of his Nubian sanctuaries, the great rock temple at
Abu Simbel is the finest, and deservedly the goal of modern travellers



ETHIOPIA AND EGYPT 239
in Egypt." (16, pp. 373-75) Rameses III (1230-1199 B.C.) of Dy
nasty XX was also a great ruler. He was succeeded by eight other
kings, all named Rameses, but none of them could be called great.
From 1075 to 714 B.C., a time known as Egypt's 3rd Intermediate
Period, there were four dynasties: XXI, XX11, XXIII, and XXIV, with some of the rulers being of Libyan and Ethiopian origin. Sheshonk I
(926-905 B.C.), a Libyan, led an army into Palestine, captured Jerusa
lem, and looted King Solomon's Temple. Then, in 761 B.C., Piankhi,
the Ethiopian, ascended the throne of Egypt, and rarely after that date
was Egypt under the rule of native monarchs. Piankhi began his career
as king of Kush and, from his palace in Napata, watched with misgiv
ings the tribute of gold, cattle, and soldiers which his country was obli
gated to send to King Orsokon III of Egypt. Finally, deciding to end
this state of affairs, he fitted out a large fleet, raised a mighty army, and
descended the river to lay siege to Hermopolis. The local ruler, King
Namlot, surrendered the city to the Ethiopian conqueror. From an in
scription of Piankhi, we read: "Hermopolis threw herself upon her
belly and pleaded before the king. Messengers came forth and de
scended bearing everything beautiful to behold; gold, every splendid
costly stone, clothing in a chest, and the diadem which was upon his
head; the uraeus which inspireth fear of him, without ceasing during
many days." On visiting the royal stables, Piankhi was saddened by the
malnutrition suffered by the horses during the siege of the city, so he
said to the former ruler of the place: "By God! 1 swear it grieves me
more that these horses have been starved than any other mischief that
you, Namlot, have caused." Then he proceeded to another city, and delivered this ultimatum:
"You suicidal, silly and miserable creatures! If an hour passes without
your opening your gates to me, you are dead men; and as it would be
painful to me to have to kill you, do not close the door upon life and do
not court death in this manner."
The garrison surrendered the city, and not one life was lost. On ap
proaching another city, Piankhi sent a message to the enemy, saying:
"Open your gates and you shall live; close them and you shall die."
The garrison surrendered, and Piankhi entered the city and held a
service in the local temple. The Kushite conqueror, continuing down
the river, next took Memphis. Then he crossed the Nile to Heliopolis
and was met there by King Osorkon III of Bubastis, who yielded his
regal powers to the Ethiopian monarch. On returning to Napata, Pian
khi had a granite stele set up in the temple of Amen and, on the four



240 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
sides of this monument, he described his successful campaign in detail.
This ancient record possesses intrinsic merit and has been praised by a
modern Egyptologist as follows:
It displays literary skill, and an appreciation of dramatic situations which
is notable, while the vivacious touches wound here and there quite relieve it of the arid tone usual in such hieroglyphic documents. The imagination en
dures the personages appearing here more easily with life than those of any
other similar historical narrative of Egypt; and the humane Piankhi espe
cially, the lover of horses, remains a man far removed from the conventional
companion and equal of the gods who inevitably occupied the exalted
throne of the pharaohs in all other such records. (16, p. 456)
The XXIVth Dynasty consisted of one Bocchoris, who, according
to Manetho, reigned only six years (720-714 B.C.). The XXVth Dy
nasty has been called the Kushite or Ethiopian Dynasty; and it began in
714 B.C. when, according to Herodotus: "Egypt was invaded by a vast
army of Ethiopians, led by Sabacos, their king." (117, p. 129) This
ruler, better known as Shabaka (714-702 B.C.), undertook the con
quest of Lower Egypt, and there established Ethiopian supremacy. He
then started an Asiatic campaign by waging war on the Assyrians. In
that campaign, his army escaped defeat only because the Assyrian hosts
were afflicted by a plague and were forced to retreat. This king was
followed by Shabataka, who, after reigning twelve years, was slain by
Taharka, who seized the crown and set up his capital at Tanis. The
reign of this pharaoh was marked by prosperity and cultural advance.
Sir Arthur Weigall has referred to this period as "that astonishing
epoch of nigger dominion." (262, p. 222) There is a tradition that Ta
harka led expeditions as far as the Strait of Gibraltar, but in the end he
failed to contain his enemies. The country was faced by an invasion
from Assyria, and Taharka fought the Assyrians until age forced him
to turn over the reins of government to Tanutamen; but the new king
could not halt the invading foe, and Ashurbanipal's Assyrian armies as
cended the Nile and captured and looted the great city of Thebes. The
Assyrians, however, couldn't hold Egypt, so they soon retreated to
their own land. The next dynasty, the XXVIth, lasted from 684 to 525
B.C. After that, no native Egyptian king ruled over the Nilotic domin
ions.
The last great African potentate to reign over Egypt, Ahmose II
(569-525 B.C.), was of Libyan ancestry; and this king, whom the
Greeks called Amasis, was a great statesman. However, he failed to



KT HI OP I A AND EGYPT 241
save his country from foreign domination. The best soldiers of the
Egyptian army had years before deserted to the Ethiopian king at
Meroe, and Amasis had to depend on Libyan and Greek mercenaries
for defense against foreign invasion. Being an able diplomat, he man
aged to maintain peace in the realm, but after his death, early in 525
B.C., the kingship fell to Psamtik III. A few months later, the land of Egypt was overrun by the armies of King Cambyses of Persia. Thus was ushered in Dynasty XXVII. This was followed by four puppet
dynasties, under Persian control, lasting until 332 B.C., when the Nile
land succumbed to Alexander the Great. His successors, the Ptolemies,
held on until 30 B.C., when Egypt became a province of the Roman
Empire. From Menes to Amasis was almost five thousand years, and
the record of Egyptian civilization under her native rulers was highly
creditable. The achievements of this African culture have been well summarized by a contemporary German scholar:
The insurgent Amasis stood at the end of Egypt's history, but at its be
ginning lay the Nile mud, which came from the interior of Africa, spread itself over the annually flooded river valley and proved to be an excellent fertilizer. And even in the Neolithic Age, men were sowing wheat and bar
ley there. These Neolithic children of the Nile were East Hamitic Afri
cans, related to the present day Galla, Somali and Masai in East Africa; the
later Egyptians were their descendants. The language of the Egyptians was an East Hamitic dialect as is spoken today by the natives between the
Upper Nile and the Masai Steppes. Egyptian skeletons, statues and countless pictures of Egyptians in their temples and monuments show the same
racial characteristics as the Nubians and the Nilotic tribes, the brown
skinned hunters of the Steppes and the savannah husbandmen of the Sudan.
Therefore, Egypt was a great kingdom created by Africans. . . . Of Afri
can inspiration are the pyramids, the golden burial chambers, the statues,
plastic arts, temple friezes and other great Egyptian works of art. The
Sphinx is an African monument, the hieroglyphs are an African script and
Ammon, Isis and Osiris are African gods. So great was the achievement of
the Africans in the Nile Valley that all the great men of ancient Europe
journeyed there—the philosophers Thales and Anaximander, the mathema
tician Pythagoras, the statesman Solon and an endless stream of historians and geographers whose works are all based on Herodotus' outstanding de
scription of Egypt, to which the second volume of his history entirely was
devoted. (269, p. 58)



CHAPTER TWELVE
The Evolution of Civilization:
Western Asia
WE now leave Egypt for a while and pay a brief visit to the land of the
Two Rivers. The ancient inhabitants of Mesopotamia are sometimes
called the Chaldeans, but they were latecomers to the area. Preceding
them were the Sumerians, Akkadians, Babylonians, and Assyrians.
The earliest civilization was that of the Sumerians. They are described
in the Assyrio-Babylonian inscriptions as a black-faced people and are
shown on the monuments as beardless and with shaven heads. The
later Semitic Babylonians are depicted on the monuments with beards
and long hair. Around 4000 B.C., the Sumerians had attained a high
level of civilization in the plains of Shinar. They tilled the soil, prac
ticed irrigation, erected cities, reared cattle, and invented the system of
cuneiform writing which they bequeathed to their Semitic successors.
The Sumerians achieved such excellence in the various arts and scien
ces that none of the future cultures of Mesopotamia ever rose to their
level. "Babylonian science," as Dr. Briffault pointed out, "was exactly
as far advanced in the nebulous dawn of Sumerian culture as it was
nearly four thousand years later when the Greeks came to gather up its
crumbs. Not a single aspect or feature of the Babylonian civilization
shows in the course of the thousands of years of its supremacy the
slightest indication of advance or development." (20, p. 49) But we
must say that the Chaldean stargazers made a creditable use of their
Sumerian scientific heritage, for they were very able astronomers.
Shortly before 500 B.C., the astronomer Naburiannu calculated the
length of the solar year with great precision. His result was 365 days, 6
hours, 15 minutes, and 41 seconds. His figures made the year only 26
minutes and 55 seconds too long. Kidinnu, the last great Chaldean as
242



WKSTKRN ASIA 243
tronomer, about 500 B.C. predicted eclipses so accurately that modern
astronomers only reached his level of precision within the last hundred
years. The British Museum houses a Babylonian calendrical tablet,
showing the month as divided into four weeks, each of seven days' du
ration. This was a lunar calendar, with a month of twenty-eight days,
with seven days for each of the four phases of the moon: new moon,
first quarter, full moon, and last quarter. Every seventh day was a Sha
battu (Sabbath), or day of rest. Hence, we can trace our seven-day
week, Sabbath day included, back to the ancient peoples of Mesopota
mia. Attempting to trace the Sumerians back to their origin presents a
problem that has arrested the attention of a number of anthropologists
and archaeologists. Professor Dorsey thought that the original Sume
rians were Elamite immigrants. "If they lived today," he surmised, "we
should probably call them Arabs; but if they lived in Afghanistan, we
should call them Afghans. Perhaps they were prehistoric Mongols."
(71, p. 315) If the Sumerians were a colony of Elamites, as Dorsey sug
gested, then they were not likely to have been prehistoric Mongols.
More likely they were of Ethiopic origin. "The Elamites of Mesopota
mia," according to Johnston, "appear to have been a Negroid people
with kinky hair, and to have transmitted this racial type to the Jews
and Syrians. There is a curliness of the hair together with a Negro eye
and full lips in the portraiture of Assyria, which conveys the idea of an
evident Negro element in Babylonia." (130, p. 27) The opinions of
Johnston are supported by archaeological evidence; for Reginald S.
Poole, of the British Museum staff, compared Assyrian portraits from
the monuments with those of the Babylonians and Elamites and con
cluded: "The Assyrians themselves are shown to have been of a very
pure type of Semites, but in the Babylonias, there is a sign of Kushite
blood. . . . There is one portrait of an Elamite king on a vase found at
Susa; he is painted black and thus belongs tc the Kushite race." (Cited
by A. C. Haddon, in 103, p. 6) Dr. H. R. Hall, of the Department of
Egyptian and Assyrian Antiquities of the British Museum, was con
vinced by his own studies that the Sumerians were Dravidian migrants
from the Indus Valley. Dr. Hall's opinions are worthy of careful con
sideration, and extracts from some of his conclusions are given below:
The Sumerian culture springs into our view ready made. . . . We have no knowledge of the time when the Sumerians were savages; when we first
meet with them, in the fourth millennium B.C., they are already a civilized,
metal using people, living in great and populous cities, possessing a compli



244 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
cared system of writing and living under rhe government of firmly estab
lished civil and religious dynasties and hierarchies. . . . The ethnic type of
the Sumerians, so strongly marked in their statues and reliefs, was as
different from those of the races which surrounded them as was their lan
guage from those of the Semites, Aryans, or others; they were decidedly
Indian in type. The face type of the average Indian of today is no doubt
much the same as that of his Dravidian race ancestors thousands of years
ago. . . . And it is to this Dravidian ethnic type of India that the ancient Sumerian bears most resemblance, so far as we can judge from his monu
ments. . . . And it is by no means improbable that the Sumerians were an
Indian race which passed, certainly by land, perhaps also by sea, through
Persia to the Valley of the Two Rivers. It was in the Indian home (perhaps
the Indus Valley), that we suppose for them that their culture developed.
(108, pp. 172-74)
In our opinion, Dr. Hall failed to prove his case. From the evidence
presented, we might just as rightly conclude that the Sumerian culture
preceded that of the Dravidians, or that both cultures stemmed from an
earlier common civilization. About 340 B.C., the Babylonian historian
Berosus wrote a history of his people in Greek. We learn from frag
ments of this work which have survived the ruins of empires that the
founders of the Sumerian culture came from the south in ships and in
troduced the arts of agriculture, reading, writing, metalworking, "and
all things that make life better." A legendary ruler named Oannes and
his seafaring companions brought civilization to Mesopotamia, and
these first Sumerians, according to Berosus, were "black-faced for
eigners." The ^traditions preserved by Berosus seem to ring true; for
though Sumerian civilization was buried under waves of Akkadians,
Amorites, Babylonians, Assyrians, Hittites, Arameans, Kassites,
Medes, Persians, and Hellenistic Greeks, the Sumerians managed to
survive; for their descendants still live on, as a dark-skinned group,
among the populations of Hither Asia. Later invaders absorbed the cul
ture brought from the south by the legendary Oannes. Their arts and
crafts, their inventions, their scripts, and much else were absorbed by
their successors in the land between the rivers. Babylonia, Assyria, the
Hittite kingdom, Mitanni, Phoenicia, and Israel built on the founda
tions laid by the Sumerians. In the code of laws framed by King Ham
murabi of Babylonia, we are told: "Anu and Bel called me, Hammurabi
the exalted prince, the worshipper of the gods; to cause justice to pre
vail in the land, to destroy the wicked, to prevent the strong from op
pressing the weak, to go forth like the sun over the black faced race, to
enlighten the land and to further the welfare of the people/' The Su



WESTERN ASIA 245
merian stories of their origins definitely point to Egypt and Ethiopia as
their original homeland. This point of view was well presented by
Gerald Massey in a lecture, "The Hebrew and Other Creations Fundamentally Explained," from which we quote:
According to ancient tradition, the culture of Chaldea was brought to
that country by a Fish Man who rose up in the first year from that part of
the Red or Erythraean Sea, which borders upon Babylonia. The original of
this type can be identified in Ea the fish god, deity of the house of the deep
and divinity of wisdom. Whence came Ea, then, by the Red Sea? Lepsius
says from Egypt—so says Egypt herself. . . . It is at least suggestive to find
that the name and nature of Ea, the oldest Akkadian form of the One God,
may be so fully explained by the Egyptian Ua (later Ea) for the one, the
one alone, isolated as the only one; also the thinker and the captain of the
boat. It should be premised that the Egyptian U preceded the letter or sound of E, hence Ua = Ea. The Egyptian Ua, which passed into Ea, also
appears in the Akkadian Ua for the Supreme One the sole Lord or Chief.
. . . Ea was the deity of the deep, and Ua = Ea is Boat and Captain both.
. . . Ea is the wise god, the thinker and instructor; and Uaua (Egyptian)
means to think, consider, meditate. (165, p. 31)
Massey was certainly on the right track, for from the earliest times
Sumer, Egypt, and Ethiopia seem to have been in touch with each other. Early Sumerian texts refer to Dilmun, Magan, and Meluhha.
Where were these places? Professor W. J. Perry has devoted some
study to this question, and we shall let him speak for himself:
Dilmun was the first settlement that was made by the god Enki, who was
the founder of Sumerian civilization. He was the god of the first Sumerian
city of Eridu, situated on what was then the head of the Persian Gulf. Enki
is said to have come up from the Persian Gulf, and to have taught the Su
merians their civilization. . . . Of late years a considerable amount of dis
cussion has taken place with regard to the localization of Dilmun, Magan and Meluhha, which are always mentioned together, as places noted for
their ships and dates. Magan was famous among the Sumerians as a place whence they got diorite and copper, Meluhha as a place whence they got
gold, Dilmun has been identified with some place or other in the Persian
Gulf, perhaps the Bahrein Islands. ... In a late inscription of the Assyri
ans, it is said that Magan and Meluhha were the archaic names for Ethiopia
and Egypt, ... for this reason, it has been concluded by some scholars that Magan and Meluhha were actually Egypt and the place called by the Egyp
tians the land of Punt. . . . The two countries therefore, can well have
been the Magan and Meluhha of the Sumerians. Mention is also made, in



246 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
early Sumerian inscriptions, of a Magan pig, which certainly was an Egyp
tian pig; and this is another reason for identifying Magan with Egypt. (186, pp. 60-61)
Anthropologists of the evolutionary school deny any cultural con
nection between Egypt and Sumer in the early days of their history,
but their case fails for lack of evidence. On the contrary, we know that
there was a close affinity between the religions of Egypt and Mesopo
tamia. In Egyptian myth, the underworld was named Osiris—in As
syrian, Asar; the goddess Hathor corresponded with Ashtoreth, and Isis
with Istar. Sir Wallis Budge in his fine study The Gods of the Egyptians
has an interesting chapter entitled "The Oldest Company of the Gods
and the Creation." The oldest company of the gods in Egypt consisted
of four gods and four goddesses, and each god was paired with his fe
male counterpart. In the Assyrio-Babylonian Seven Tablets of Creation,
we find a similar company of gods described, being eight in number,
and divided into four pairs. Budge was convinced that the close corres
pondence of the two groups of godly companies was not due to chance.
The Seven Tablets of Creation [he noticed] contain several Assyrianized
forms of ancient Sumerian words, a fact which proves the original traditions
incorporated in the work must be of Sumerian origin, and must have been
formulated in remote antiquity. It is surprising therefore, to find so much
similarity existing between the primeval gods of Sumer and those of Egypt,
especially as the resemblance cannot be the result of borrowing. . . . And
we are therefore driven to the conclusion that both the Sumerians and the early Egyptians derived their primeval gods from some common but exceedingly ancient source. The similarity between the two companies of
gods seems to be too close to be accidental. ... Be this as it may, it is cer
tain that the company of primeval gods, which, as we have seen, was com
mon to the Sumerians and Egyptians, was quite different from the companies of gods of which Osiris and Ra-Tem were the heads in Egypt, and also from those which were formed in Babylonia and Assyria when these
countries were inhabited by Semitic populations. (32, pp. 290-91)
The myths, legends, and traditions of the Sumerians definitely point
to Africa as the original home of the Sumerians. The first Sumerian re
mains were unearthed in the middle of the nineteenth century by
Hincks, Oppert, and Rawlinson. Sir Henry Rawlinson called these
people Kushites. The name Sumerian we owe to Professor Jules Op
pert, a Franco-German Orientalist. Rawlinson anticipated Perry by
tracing the Sumerians back to Ethiopia.



CHAPTER THIRTEEN
The Evolution of Civilization: India
MEN of almost every shade of color are found in the great subconti
nent of India, but the early inhabitants of this region were black. Their
descendants may be observed in central India today. They are distinguished by Ethiopoid features, curly hair, and a dusky complexion. Their ancient forebears were described by Professor Lynn Thorndike
as "short black men with almost Negro noses." (249, p. 227) Dr. Will Durant referred to them as "a dark skinned, broad nosed people whom, without knowing the origin of the word, we call Dravidians." (76, p.
396) The first great civilization of India was established by the Dra
vidians in the Indus Valley. There they built large cities, the principal ones being Mohenjo Daro, Chanhu'Daro, and Harappa. These cities were well constructed. Mohenjo Daro was two square miles in area,
with regularly laid-out main and side streets, lined with attractive twostory brick houses. Bathrooms were a common feature, and they were
fitted out with runaway drains leading to brick sewers laid under the streets. This culture reached its peak about 3000 B.C. These people
had domesticated cattle, sheep, and elephants; cultivated cotton and wheat; built boats and wheeled carts; and became skilled workers of bronze and iron. They even discovered a process for making iron rustproof. Erich Von Daniken recently noted: 'in the courtyard of a tem
ple of Delhi there exists . . . a column made of welded iron parts that has been exposed to weathering for more than 4000 years without showing a trace of rust. In addition, it is unaffected by sulphur or phos
phorus. Here we have an unknown alloy from antiquity staring us in
the face." (54, p. 94) That the Dravidians possessed high skill in the
mining and fabricating of iron and steel implements is a fact not subject
to dispute.
The Megalith builders in India [Perry observed] made one great discov
ery. They found lying about on the surface in great quantities, in Hydera
247



248 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
bad and elsewhere, iron of such a quality that it practically constituted a
natural steel. In fact, it was of this iron that Damascus blades were made.
. . . The craft of iron working was, in time, carried further east, so that we
find the people of the East Indian Archipelago, which may be called Indo
nesia for short, working iron in Borneo, Celebes and elsewhere, they hav
ing, in the case of Celebes, learned the craft from wonderful strangers who
built megalithic monuments, and therefore belonged to the archaic civiliza
tion. (185, p. 108)
The Indus Valley civilization was in a way unique, for these people
seem not to have had priest kings. The largest building in Mohenjo
Daro was not a temple or palace, but a public bath. In Hammerton's
Wonders of the Past, there is an instructive study by S. G. Blaxland
Stubbs, Wonder Cities of Most Ancient India. We have been informed
by Stubbs:
The early Aryan literature of India, the hymns of the Rigveda, which, it
is commonly agreed, date from about 1000 B.C., speak of the people whom
the proud Aryan invaders found in India as black skinned barbarians, or
slaves. But Aryan pride of race has received something of a shock from the
archaeological investigations carried out by Sir John Marshall, and more re
cently by Dr. E. Mackay in the valley of the Indus. Here, ample evidence
has been found of a race whose complex civilization and high culture were
equal, and in some respects superior to those of early Mesopotamia and
Egypt. ("Wonder Cities of Most Ancient India" by S. G. Blaxland Stubbs,
in 111, pp. 659-66)
The Indus civilization is still a puzzle to the archaeologists, for they
have no clue as to its origin. In prehistoric times, primitive village
dwellers lived in the Indus area and points further north. Then sud
denly great cities appeared. By whom were they built? If the builders
were migrants, from whence did they come? As Robert Silverberg has
observed: "A civilization as complex as that of the Indus Valley does
not spring full blown out of nowhere. But that is exactly what appears
to have happened at Mohenjo Daro and Harappa. They are cities built
from scratch." (227, p. 93) Another puzzling feature of this ancient
culture is the system of writing, which seems to have been a picto
graphic script of about two hundred different signs. For many of these
signs (about a hundred) are almost identical with those used by the
mysterious inhabitants of Easter Island, situated two thousand miles
west of Chile in the Pacific Ocean. "All sorts of fantastic theories have
been spawned about this resemblance. Most serious scholars, though,



INDIA 249
regard the coincidence as just that: pure chance. They note that the
Indus script was lost from sight 3500 years ago—at least two thousand
years, possibly three thousand, before the earliest known use of the
Easter Island script. There is no way to explain the resemblance except
as a coincidence." The foregoing quote is the opinion of Robert Silver
berg (227, p. 102). A man who writes so charmingly is hard to disa
gree with, but here we must register dissent; this looks to us like an
other example of the diffusion of culture. Dragging in chance, or coin
cidence, in this case, begs the question. For there is plenty of evidence
that cultural diffusion was just as prevalent in the ancient world as it is
in our day. An interesting discussion of this fact has been presented by Eugen Georg:
For ages, the bond between Africa and the Far East (India, China, and
the Malayan district) has been amazingly close. A Suaheli colony flourishes on the west coast of India, and Madagascar is still the home of the Hova or Malays who immigrated more than a thousand years ago from Sumatra.
. . . Starting with Madagascar, the Oriental influence extends deep into
the heart of Africa. Some tribes in the western Sudan speak a dialect which shows undeniable relationship with the Chinese. The xylophones of inner
Africa are tuned to the same scales and absolute tones as are the Indo-Chi
nese xylophone. The prows of boats are painted black, white, red or green
by Negroes of Kamerun; and in details of form, color and style, they imi
tate the Knjallans of the Dyak of Borneo, whose black, white and red are an
original artistic element of mankind in the Pacific from the South Seas to Northwestern America. The colors assume mythical and magic values:
black is the color of night, fear and death; red that of blood and youth;
white that of light, the sun, life and joy. Astounding similarities, physical
and anthropological, are to be noted between certain tribes in West Africa and Melanesia. Both use bows of the same type, strung with the same mate
rial; both build rectangular huts with a gable roof. Almost identical ceremo
nies are employed in the initiations into secret fetichistic male societies.
Likewise the same masks, the same textiles made from bark, the same slit drums are found on the coast of Guinea and in Kamerun as in New Guinea, the Admiralty Islands and the New Hebrides. The Spaniard de
Rete early recognized such similarities; it was on account of them that he
called an island which he had discovered near Australia, New Guinea. (96,
pp. 100-1)



CHAPTER FOURTEEN
The Evolution of Civilization:
Crete and Phoenicia
THE ancient civilization of Crete is frequently referred to as the Mi
noan culture, being named after King Minos, an early legendary ruler.
The ancestors of the Cretans were natives of Africa, a branch of the
western Ethiopians, who dwelt in the grasslands of North Africa before
that area dried up and became a great desert. As the Saharan sands en
croached on their homeland, they took to the sea and, in Crete and
neighboring islands, set up a maritime culture. The archaeological
discoveries of Sir Arthur Evans have revealed to us the knowledge that
cultural influences from beyond the Mediterranean Sea were reaching
Crete before the rise of the Egyptian dynasties. By 1700 B.C. this civi
lization had reached its zenith; and from their capital at Knossus the
Sea Kings of Crete ruled over a region which had become the culture
nucleus of the eastern Mediterranean. The seat of these kings was the
Broad Palace of Minos, in Knossus. It was a huge structure, five stories
high, and spread out over an area of four acres. On the walls of its nu
merous rooms were beautiful frescoes and mosaics of high artistic
merit. In this palace were bathrooms with terra cotta bathtubs, fitted
with drains quite modern in construction. They were made of faucet
jointed pipes superior to anything known to the later Romans, and
were not equaled in modern times until the mid-nineteenth century.
The Palace of Minos contained a throne room, a chapel, storerooms,
living quarters, and recreational halls. There were no walls around the
city, so the island evidently enjoyed security from outside interference.
Throughout Crete were attractive and comfortable homes, well-con
structed ports, and fine paved roads. The Minoan mariners were domi
nant in the Aegean Sea, and carried on a lively trade with Egypt,
250



CRETE AND PHOENICIA 251
Sicily, and southern Italy. The people had ample leisure time, so they
went in for music, dancing, theatricals, athletics, and other forms of en
tertainment. The social organization was egalitarian, with women
enjoying the same rights and privileges as men. There was no powerful
sacerdotal caste; the gods were worshiped in caves or on hilltops or at
domestic shrines, but there were no temples. Around 1400 B.C. this
splendid culture was laid in ruins by an invasion of semi-barbarous
Greeks from the north; and upon the ruins of the Cretan culture the
Greeks in later days built their civilization. These Greeks were the
first civilized white folks.
The great mariners and merchants of the ancient world were the
Phoenicians, who adopted much of the culture of ancient Egypt, and
were instrumental in diffusing its elements to other nations. We first
hear of these people as dwelling on the shores of the Persian Gulf, and
have been told that they later colonized the land of Canaan, at the east
ern end of the Mediterranean. Hence they called themselves Canaan
ites. These ancient mariners manufactured a famous dye called royal
purple, which the Greeks named Phoenix. As a result, the makers of the
precious dye were nicknamed Phoenikes, or Phoenicians. Herodotus,
in Book VII, Chapter LXXXIX, of his famous History, stated: "The
Phoenicians, as they themselves say, anciently dwelt on the Erythraean
Sea (Indian Ocean); and having crossed over from thence, they settled
on the sea coast of Syria/' This opinion has been questioned by certain
modern scholars, but it has not been disproven. Professor George
Rawlinson, in an essay "On the Ethnic Affinities of the Nations of
Western Asia," expressed the opinion that: "The primeval Canaanites,
indeed, were of the race of Ham (i.e., they were Kushites), and no
doubt originally spoke a dialect closely akin to the Egyptian." (Cited
by John D. Baldwin in 7, p. 132) The Phoenicians carried on their in
dustrial and commercial operations from the cities of Aradus, By bios,
Sidon, and Tyre, situated at the eastern end of the Mediterranean Sea.
The port of Byblos was exporting timber to Egypt as far back as the
days of Dynasty III, when forty boats laden with cedars of Lebanon
sailed up the Nile. A stone tablet of the reign of the Pharaoh Snefru
reads: "We brought 40 ships laden with cedar trunks. We built ships
ofcedarwood. . . . We made the doors of the king's palace of cedar
wood." (Cited by Robert Silverberg in 227, p. 112) In later days,
when life in Canaan became hazardous, the Phoenicians expanded their
activities and established many colonies, the most important being Car



252 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
rhage, Urica, and Bizerre in North Africa, and Gadcs (Cadiz) in Spain
and Palermo in Sicily.
The city stare of Carthage, originally a colony of Tyre, was estab
lished on the North African coast before 800 B.C. This city, a great
metropolis of the ancient world, was destroyed by the Romans after the
final defeat of the Carthaginians, at the end of the Punic Wars. The
Phoenicians were great merchants and mariners. Not being warlike,
they neglected military affairs; and this finally led to their undoing.
For, as Professor Charles Seignobos pointed out:
To protect her colonies from the natives, to hold her subjects in check
who were always ready to revolt, a strong army was necessary. But the life
of a Carthaginian was too valuable to risk it without necessity. Carthage
preferred to pay mercenary soldiers, recruiting them among the barbarians
of her empire and among the adventurers of all countries. Her army was a
bizarre aggregation in which all languages were spoken, all religions prac
ticed and in which every soldier wore different arms and costume. There
were seen Numidians clothed in lion skins which served them as couch,
mounted bareback on small fleet horses, and drawing the bow with horse at
full gallop; Libyans with black skins armed with pikes; Iberians from Spain
in white garments adorned with red, armed with a long pointed sword;
Gauls, naked to the girdle, bearing enormous shields and a rounded sword
which they held in both hands; natives of the Balearic Islands, trained from
infancy to sling with stones or balls of lead. (222, pp. 78-79)
After losing Sicily to the Romans in 242 B.C., the Carthaginians
were faced with a rebellion of their Libyan vassals; and, to forestall
Roman aid to the rebels, they were forced to surrender the island of
Sardinia to the Romans. The Libyans were subdued by Carthaginian
arms, under the generalship of Hamilcar Barca in 238 B.C. Hamilcar
cherished a dream of restoring the ancient glories of Carthage, and of
recapturing Sicily and Sardinia; so the Second Punic War was waged
in Europe and Africa with Hannibal (a son of Hamilcar) leading the
Carthaginians, and Cornelius Scipio heading the Roman forces. A se
ries of blunders by Hannibal brought about the downfall of the Cartha
ginians at the battle of Zama, in 202 B.C. The Roman peace terms
were harsh; for Carthage was compelled to pay, over a period of fifty
years, an indemnity of 10,000 talents of silver, or about $20,000,000 in
modern currency. All the ships of war and elephants of Carthage were
confiscated by the Romans; and the establishment of new colonies or
involvement in foreign wars was forbidden, unless approved by Rome.



CRETE AND PHOENICIA 253
The great African city stare, despite all these setbacks, made a good re
covery and might have survived; but in the year 1 50 B.C., the armies of
King iMassanissa, of Numidia, an African ally of Rome, invaded Car
thaginian territory. The Carthaginians counterattacked in self-defense, and this led to the Third Punic War. The Romans, led by the evil
minded Roman Senator Cato, who plotted to crush Carthage under
any pretext, declared war on Carthage. This war, begun by Rome in
149 B.C. under the trumped-up claim that an old treaty had been vio
lated, raged for three years. In 146 B.C., the city of Carthage was cap
tured by the Romans and was completely destroyed by fire. This wan
ton destruction of the great African city by the Romans must be
regarded as one of the great crimes of history. For the Carthaginians
and their Phoenician forebears were culturally and intellectually supe
rior to the Romans. "They (the Romans) burned the great city of Car
thage," Professor Hapgood has stated, "their ancient enemy and their
incalculable superior in everything relating to science. The library of
Carthage is said to have contained about 500,000 volumes, and these,
no doubt, dealt with the history and the sciences of Phoenicia as a
whole." (112, p. 196)
The ships of the Phoenicians traded with ports on the shores of the
Indian Ocean, the Mediterranean, the Black Sea, and the Atlantic
Ocean. The Phoenician seafarers and traders operated tin mines in
Cornwall and swapped goods with the ancient Britons; their ships cir
cumnavigated Africa and crossed the Atlantic to America. The Phoe
nicians have been credited with the invention of the alphabet, but this
claim is not valid; for it has been shown by the researches of the Egyp
tologist Prisse d'Avennes that the alphabet was an Egyptian invention.
"It is sufficiently proved," as noted by a modern authority, "by the pa
pyrus in the Bibliotheque Nationale, Paris, actually the oldest book in
the world. It consists of eighteen pages written in black ink with a bold
round character, the prototype of the letters copied by the Greeks from
the Phoenicians and transmitted through the Latins to us." (80, p. 15)
In other words, the Phoenicians adopted the Egyptian alphabet, sim
plified it, and then diffused it among other nations.



CHAPT E R F I F T E E
The Evolution of Civilization:
Greece and Rome
THE Greeks are noted for their contributions to philosophy, but they
knew nothing of that study before coming under the influence of the
Egyptians. Aristotle conceded that his people obtained their first
knowledge of astronomy and mathematics from Egypt; and, according
to Diodorus Siculus, Egyptian priests possessed souvenirs of the visits of
Pythagoras, Democritus, Plato, and Eudoxus to their country. In
Egypt, Democritus studied astronomy; Pythagoras learned geometry,
the theory of numbers, and transmigration; and it was there that Solon,
Plato, and Lycurgus got their knowledge of law. Eudoxus, about 370
B.C., learned from the Egyptian astronomers the conception of the ce
lestial sphere. He carried it back to Greece and outlined upon it the ce
lestial equator; the ecliptic, and the most important constellations.
Thales of Miletus, of mixed Greek and Phoenician ancestry, was born
in the first half of the sixth century B.C., and he was the founder of
Greek science and philosophy. He was a merchant, traveler, philoso
pher, mathematician, astronomer, and an engineer. As a young man, he
was a student in Egypt, but he returned to Miletus in his old age.
The role of Egypt in civilizing the Greeks has been vividly depicted
by the English historian, Winwood Reade:
Egypt from the earliest times had been rhe university of Greece. It had
been visited, according to tradition, by Orpheus and Homer. . . . And
now every intellectual Greek made the voyage to that country; it was re
garded as a part of education, as a pilgrimage to the cradle land of their
mythology. To us, Egypt is a land of surpassing interest, but nevertheless,
merely a charnel house, a museum, a valley of ruins and dry bones. The
Greeks saw it alive. . . . They wandered in the mazes of the Labyrinth;
254



GREECE AND ROME 255
they gazed on the mighty Sphinx couched on the yellow sands with a tem
ple between its paws, they entered the great hall of Karnak, filled with col
umns like a forest and paved with acres of solid stone. . . . There are some
who ascribe the whole civilization of Greece and the rapid growth of Greek
literature to the free trade which existed between the two lands. Greece
imported all its paper from Egypt, and without paper, there would have
been few books. (203, pp. 60-61)
In material culture, the Greeks were inferior to the Orientals and
even to the Romans, and they showed progress in applied science only
in the Hellenistic era of Alexandria, which was more Oriental than
Hellenic. The great gifts of the Greeks were in the fields of free
thought, intellectual tolerance, and urbanity. They excelled in logic,
metaphysics, and theoretical science, but also made valuable contribu
tions to education, art, literature, and politics.
A blend of Greek and Oriental elements produced the Hellenistic
culture of Alexandria. Shortly before his death, Alexander the Great
decided to build a new city on the Mediterranean coast of Egypt.
When Alexander's domains were divided up, one of his generals,
Ptolemy, got Egypt as his share and then undertook the building of the
proposed city, which was named Alexandria. The new city was built
on a strip of land five or six miles long, and about two miles wide, be
tween the Mediterranean Sea and Lake Mareotis. In the harbor of
Alexandria, was the island of Pharos, on which was erected a splendid
lighthouse. It was built of marble and its pinnacle reached four hundred
feet into the air. From the summit shone a light, visible thirty miles out
at sea. This grand structure cost close to $1,000,000, which in those
days was a lot of money. The walls of Alexandria were about fifteen
miles in circumference, and underground cisterns were established ca
pable of storing an emergency fresh-water supply sufficient to sustain
the entire population for one year. The layout of the city was quite
modern, with streets running north-south and east-west, and crossing
each other at right angles. Canobic Street traversed the metropolis
from one end to another in an east-west direction of five or six miles. It
was over a hundred feet in width and was lined on both sides by a mar
ble colonnade. Another boulevard equally wide and stately ran in the
north-south direction. These avenues were lined with noble palms, and
were illuminated with lamps at night. The palaces, mansions, and pub
lic buildings were faced with white marble and polished granite. In
daytime, the glare of the sun was moderated by the use of veils and



256 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
curtains of green silk, which were everywhere in evidence. The mag
nificent tomb of Alexander the Great was located at the intersection of
the two main boulevards of the city. This mausoleum, which contained
the mummified remains of the deceased monarch, was surrounded by
gardens, fountains, and obelisks. The glory of Alexandria was the Mu
seum, commenced by Ptolemy Soter and completed by his son
Ptolemy Philadelphus. The outstanding feature of the Alexandrian
Museum, located in the Bruchion, the aristocratic quarter of the city
and adjoining the Royal Palace, was the Philadelphian Library. Its or
nate apartments were adorned with the finest statues and pictures of
the age, and housed about 400,000 volumes. Later, another library was
established in the Serapion (The Temple of Serapis), and this collec
tion grew to about 300,000 volumes. In the establishment of the Mu
seum (actually a university) and its famous libraries, Ptolemy Soter had
in mind three objectives: (1) The preservation of extant knowledge,
(2) The increase of such knowledge, (3) The diffusion of knowledge.
The first Ptolemy abdicated his throne in favor of his son Ptolemy II
(Philadelphus) in 285 B.C. During the reign of this second Ptolemy
(285-247 B.C.), Alexandria entered its Golden Age. The coronation
of Ptolemy Philadelphus was attended with great pomp and ceremony,
as the following account shows:
From morn to dusk of a mild November day, a stupendous procession
paraded broad, marble lined avenues. ... A large gold and ivory statue of
Dionysus rode in a chariot at the head of a vintage pageant which included
24 chariots containing gaily dressed Hindu ladies and drawn by elephants
and 80 chariots drawn by Asiatic antelopes, goats and wild asses. . . .
Statues of gods and kings rode in chariots of ivory and gold. In the royal box were a dozen of the Greek world's most famous scholars and poets; and
doubtless, they sat at the close in the specially built banquet hall, with mar
ble columns shaped like palms, the choicest paintings in the world, hangings
of Egyptian scarlet and Phoenician purple, and large gold vessels studded
with diamonds and rubies. The coronation cost, it is said, 600,000 pounds; and the gold crowns presented to the young king and queen by the cities of
the world were worth more than that in value. ( 1 50, pp. 66-67)
Ptolemy II appointed Callimachus to the post of Chief Librarian;
and one of his projects was a collection of literature embracing the sa
cred books of the Ethiopians, Elamites, Persians, Indians, Babylonians,
Assyrians, Phoenicians, Syrians, and Greeks. The chief librarian was
commissioned by royal authority to purchase whatever books he could



GREECE AND ROME 257
ar the expense of the government. All books brought into Egypt by
foreigners were taken to the library, where correct copies were made
by a corps of transcribers. A transcript was returned to the owner, and
the original was placed in the library. Owners of valuable books were
well compensated on the sale of such works. Ptolemy Euergetes bought
from a citizen of Athens certain works of Sophocles, Euripides, and
Aeschylus; and to the owner he returned transcripts of the books plus
an indemnity of about $15,000. The museum is sometimes called the
University of Alexandria, for it contained faculties of mathematics, as
tronomy, medicine, and literature. Connected with the museum were
botanical and geological gardens, an astronomical observatory, a chemi
cal laboratory and an anatomical dissection room. The scientists and
scholars of the establishment were especially celebrated in the fields of
mathematics, astronomy, and geography. One of the shining lights of
Alexandrian erudition was the mathematician Euclid, whose Elements
of Geometry was used as a textbook in the schools of the world for more
than two thousand years. The contemporary mathematician, Sir
Thomas Heath, has described Euclid's Elements as: "The greatest text
book of elementary mathematics that there has ever been or is likely to
be, a book which, ever since it was written twenty-two centuries ago,
has been read and appealed to as authoritative by mathematicians great
and small from Archimedes and Apollonius of Perga onwards." Ptol
emy Soter asked Euclid to simplify the study of geometry so that he
could learn it without too much mental effort. To this request, Euclid
replied, "In geometry there is no special path for kings." Another
mathematician of the Alexandrian school was Apollonius, who wrote
treatises on the conic sections. Also on the staff of the museum was Ar
chimedes, who was a mathematician, physicist, and an engineer. Era
tosthenesr astronomer and geographer, measured the curvature of the
earth for a distance of 480 miles from Alexandria to Syene, and then
calculated from the aforesaid data the circumference of the earth,
which he found roughly to be 24,000 miles. Other great astronomers of
Alexandria were Claudius Ptolemy, Hipparchus, and Sosigenes. The
last named revised the calendar at the behest of Julius Caesar, and
hence was responsible for the Julian version of the calendar. The mu
seum maintained a group of engineers and inventors. Ctesibius inven
ted a single-cylinder fire engine, and his pupil, Hero, improved the
same by giving it two cylinders. This same Hero invented a steam en
gine, which was a forerunner of 'the modern steam turbine. Water



258 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
clocks constructed by Apollonius and Cresibius measured time in the
Temple of Serapis. As a center of scientific research, the museum
flourished for about a hundred years, then went into a decline; whereas
the library was destroyed by fire in 48 B.C. when a Roman army led
by Julius Caesar captured Alexandria. The library was partly restored
by the Romans by importing books from foreign libraries, especially
that of King Eumenes of Pergamus. This second Alexandrian library,
which was added to the collection in the Serapion, was destroyed by a
mob of fanatical Christian monks in 389 A.C. The story of the de
struction of the library at Alexandria by Arab invaders in the seventh
century has no foundation in fact, since there was then no library for
them to loot. When Alexandria's period of progress had run its course,
the Romans took over and became the new custodians of civilization.
In a way, this was unfortunate, for the Romans lacked the intellectual
receptivity and mental acumen of the Greeks.
A good example of the Greek outlook on the world at its best was
noted by Win wood Reade, who pointed out:
The Egyptians laughed in the faces of the Greeks, and called them chil
dren when they talked of their gods of yesterday, and so well did their pu
pils profit by their lesson that they soon laughed at the Egyptians for believ
ing in the gods at all. Xenophanes declaimed against the Egyptian myth of
an earth-walking, dying, resuscitated god. He said that if Osiris was a man,
they should not worship him, and that if he was a god they need not lament
his sufferings. This remarkable man was the Voltaire of Greece; there had
been free thinkers before his time, but they had reserved their opinions for
their disciples. (203, pp. 78-79)
The Greeks had distinguished themselves in the realms of abstract
thought and pure science. In these endeavors, the duller- witted Romans were no match for the Greeks.
The Romans did make a valuable contribution to civilization, how
ever, in the domains of the industrial arts and the applied sciences, nor
should we overlook the imposing edifice of Roman law. The main de
fects of Roman civilization were slavery, militarism, and a bad fiscal
system. The Romans are not as bad as they have been pictured by their
critics. They had virtues as well as vices, and their good points should
not be overlooked. The free citizens of Rome in the imperial age were
probably better off than some free workingmen in the modern world.
Rome had slums, we know, but it also had the Forum, a finer civic cen
ter than that of any modern city of the Western world. The Roman



GREECE AND ROME 259
proletariat received a free supply of corn, and sometimes were given
free pork. They were also in receipt of a free and excellent water sup
ply, free schooling, and free medical service. Free entertainments were
available to the multitude in the Circus and the Amphitheater. For a
modest fee, the Roman citizen could patronize the public baths—huge
marble palaces with pools of hot and cold water, libraries, gymnasia,
and ornate halls. The Emperor Diocletian had a law passed guaranteeing the Roman worker a minimum wage. The Roman laborer
worked about 1 70 days per year. On the other 195 days, believe or not,
he enjoyed his holidays. At about 3:00 p.m. in imperial Rome, bells
rang, and the laborers ceased work for the day. "From this height of
freedom and enjoyment," McCabe declared, "the workers of the
Roman world were within less than a century of the establishment of
Christianity to be cast down to a level of sordid poverty, heavy toil, and
virtual slavery." (156, p. 17)
There is a considerable literature on the fall of Rome in which
theories are propounded attempting to explain the decline and fall of
that ancient empire. Most of these books are quite worthless; they spin
hypotheses, but explain nothing. Professor J. B. Bury, a leading mod
ern authority on Roman history, could find no good reason why Rome
fell, so he concluded that it was just a case of bad luck on the part of the
Romans. An American scholar, Edward Lucas White, wrote a book
with the title Why Rome Fell, and we think the work should be better
known than it is. In the introduction to his study on the fall of Rome,
White wrote:
This book is an attempt to answer the question: "Why did the Roman Empire, the worship of the Olympian Pantheon and the Greco-Roman civ
ilization and culture perish together?"
Its thesis is that, while there were many and potent negative, passive,
minor and contributory causes totally unconnected with Christianity,
Christianity, throughout its rise, growth, diffusion and success, as it was
manifestly the cause of the extinction of the worship of the Olympian Pan
theon, just as truly was the positive, active, major and paramount cause of
the ruin of the Roman Empire and of the annihilation of the Greco-Roman
culture and civilization. (271, p. ix)
For the details of the decline and fall of Rome, the reader is referred
to the considerable literature on the subject. Edward Gibbon's History
of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire is a well-known classic.
White's book is not as difficult to read as Gibbon's, and we recom



260 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
mend it to the reader if he has any interest in the Roman civilization.
Since Why Rome Fell is out of print and many readers may not be able
to find a copy of it even in a library, we give below extracts from that
work, in which the author shows the devastating effects of a great pesti
lence that swept over the Roman Empire in the second century of the
Christian Era:
The Great Pestilence of 166 was, in fact, the real death blow to Rome's
greatness and from it dates the decline of Roman power. It broke the tradi
tion of civilization and culture which had grown from its small beginnings
among the primitive Greeks and Etruscans more than two thousand years
before. ... Up to the year of the Great Pestilence, that civilization had
prospered, had produced a long series of generals, inventors, architects,
sculptors, painters, musicians, poets, authors and orators. Everywhere, men
had shown self confidence, capacity, originality, power and competence
and had achieved success for two thousand years.
The Great Pestilence of A.D. 166 so depleted the population of the Roman Empire that Rome never again advanced its boundaries.
. . . All classes of the people suffered, but most of all, the rich, the well
to-do, the cultured and educated classes of the towns and cities. And the
main point of difference between the Great Pestilence and the others which
had preceded it was the universality of its incidence. For two thousand
years, pestilences had occurred at intervals, but previously not everywhere
at once. If one country suffered, others did not; if half the Mediterranean
world, even, was devastated, the other half escaped. From immune regions, competence and capacity flowed into the ruined
areas and civilization was restored there. But the Great Pestilence left no
district unscathed. In six months, it killed off all the brains and skill, all the
culture and ingenuity in the Empire. There were so few capable men left in
any line of activity that the next generation grew up practically untaught.
The tradition of two thousand years was broken. In all the Mediterranean
world, until centuries later, descendants of the savage invaders developed
their new civilization on the ruins of the old, no man ever again made a
great speech, wrote a great book or play or poem, painted a good picture,
carved a good statue, or contrived a good campaign or battle. The brains of
the Roman world died that year; the originality of the whole nation was
killed at once; the tradition broke off.
What the pestilence was, whether Asiatic cholera, typhus or what else
does not matter. The fact of its virulence is beyond question. It ravaged the Empire from Nisibis in Upper Mesopotamia to the west coast of Wales, from the Rhine and Danube to the cataracts of the Nile. Every farm, ham
let and village suffered; in not one town did more than half the inhabitants
remain alive. Few cities escaped with so much as a third of their population



GREECE AND ROME 261
surviving. Famine accompanied the pestilence in all the western portions of the Roman world, and from famine perished many whom the plague had
spared. (271, pp. 292-93)
The barbarians began to overrun the western Roman Empire in the
early part of rhe fifth century, and by the end of that century Roman
civilization was in ruins. Europe then entered upon the long night of
the Dark Ages, which lasted for five hundred years (500-1 100 A.C.).
The barbarians have been blamed for the collapse of Roman culture,
but they cannot be held entirely responsible for the debacle. "The
Dark Ages," as Professor Thompson truly said, "were at least as much
due to the corruption of the Church as to the decay of Roman civiliza
tion or the barbarian invasions." (247, p. 74) The story of the Dark
Ages in Europe presents a chronicle of horrors almost without a parallel
in human history; and the saddest part of it is the story of the conver
sion of Europe to Christianity. "Could the full history of the conversion
of Europe to Christianity be written," said Dr. Briffault, "it would
present a tale of horror more appalling than that of the Christianization
of Spain by the Inquisition. The Christian religion has been imposed
upon the people of Europe in much the same manner as was imposed
on Mexico and Peru, in the course of whose conversion Las Casas esti
mates twelve million people perished, butchered, burnt alive and tor
tured." (20, p. 117)
The Church not only perpetuated slavery, but created it where it
had never existed under Roman Law. Bishop Ratherius of Verona, in
the tenth century, went so far as to say: "God has mercifully destined
those to slavery for whom He saw that freedom was not fitting." In the
treatment of slaves, the clergy were as brutal as lay masters. There are
cases on record showing that churchmen mutilated or starved their
slaves to death. Civilization was finally restored in Europe, but it was
not from any relics of Greco-Roman culture, buried amidst the ruins,
nor from the degenerate empire of Byzantium. The first light to pene
trate the long night of Europe's Dark Ages and to herald the dawn of a new day was from Moorish Spain. As McCabe has so well put it:
None of our modern sophistry redeems the squalor of Europe from the
fifth to the eleventh century. And it was again the dark skinned men of the
south who restored civilization. By the year 1000, Europe was reduced to a
condition which, if we were not Europeans, we should frankly call barba
rism, yet at that time, the Arabs had a splendid civilization in Spain, Sicily,
Syria, Egypt and Persia, and it linked on to those of India and China. We



262 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
write manuals of the history of Europe, or of the Middle Ages, and we
confine ourselves to a small squalid area (Russia and Prussia were not yet
civilized and Spain was Moorish), and ignore the brilliant civilization that
ran from Portugal to the China Sea. ( 1 54, p. 298)



CHAPTER SIXTEEN
The Evolution of Civilization:
The Arab-Moorish Culture
THE story of the medieval Arab-Moorish culture begins with the birth
of Mohammed in 571 C.E. (Christian Era); and it has been claimed by
his followers that his birth was heralded by a spectacular display of mir
acles. When he was about thirty years old, while engaged in religious
meditation in a cave on Mount Hara, it is said that the angel Gabriel
appeared to him, proclaiming him as Mohammed, the Prophet of God.
In Mecca, the new prophet was not popular. In fact, he was forced to
flee to Medina; and at that place, he got his start in the year 622. This
year, known as the Hejira (flight), marks the beginning of the Moham
medan Era. The doctrine of the Arabian prophet proved to be widely
popular; for the Arabian peninsula had been recently devastated by a
prolonged drought, and the restless tribes were quite ready to listen to
the voice of a new messiah. The essence of the Islam doctrine is very
simple: "There is no god but God (Allah), and Mohammed is the
prophet of God."
This very simplicity was an asset. Islam appealed to all classes of so
ciety. The doctrines of equality and brotherhood were approved by the
oppressed, the doctrine of conquest was hailed by the tribes of the
desert lands, city dwellers were pleased by Mohammed's encourage
ment of commerce, and the superstitious were delighted by ceremony
and ritualism. Large numbers of Jews and Christians were converted to
Islam, because both Moses and Jesus were recognized as prophets of
God. Mohammed recognized no opposition and boldly challenged all
dissenters, proclaiming: "I, the last of the prophets, am sent with the
sword. Let those who promulgate my faith enter into no argument or
discussion, but slay all who refuse obedience to the law. Whoever
263



264 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
fights for the true faith, whether he fall or conquer, will assuredly re
ceive a glorious reward." The head officer of the Islamic religion was
called the caliph, 2 word meaning Successor or Representative of Mo
hammed. On the death of the Prophet of Islam, his father-in-law, Abu
Beker, was elected as the first caliph. This caused dissension among the
faithful, for many held that the office should have gone to Mohammed's
brother-in-law, Ali. The partisans of Abu Beker claimed that the cal
iphate should be elective, whereas the advocates of Ali argued for suc
cession by heredity. The first group, the Sunnites, became dominant in
Arabia, Africa, and Turkey and Turkestan; whereas the second sect,
the Shiites, prevailed in India and Persia. But despite internal strife, the
successors of Mohammed successfully pursued a vigorous policy of
conquest. Six years after the death of the prophet, Asia Minor and
Mesopotamia had succumbed to the Islamic armies, and the conquest of
Persia and India soon followed. A century later, the Moslem dominions
stretched from Spain through North Africa, across Asia to China. The
first great dynasty of caliphs in the East were the Ommayads, the de
scendants of Omar, known as the St. Paul of Islam. This line of rulers
was followed by the Abbasids, descendants of Mohammed's uncle,
Abbas. Egypt became the seat of the Fatimite caliphate, and the holders
of the office were descendants of Mohammed's daughter, Fatima. The
Caliph Almansor (753-75) transferred the seat of the Eastern Caliph
ate from Damascus to the new city of Bagdad (founded in the year
750), and proceeded to transform the new capital into a great metropo
lis. Here he promoted the study of the sciences, especially astronomy,
and founded schools of law and medicine. His grandson, Harun-Al
Rashid, Caliph of Bagdad (786-809) of Arabian Nights fame, con
tinued this policy by ordering that a school should be attached to every
mosque in his realm.
The Golden Age of Bagdad was the caliphate of Al Mamun (813
32). At its zenith, the Bagdad caliphate extended from the Oxus River
to Tripoli and from the Arabian Desert to the Caucasus Mountains. Sir
Richard Burton, in his translation of the Arabian Nights, gave the fol
lowing fine description of Bagdad in its day of splendor:
Bagdad was the inheritor of the glory of Babylon, Seleucia, and Ctesi
phon. In the midst of the richest and most thickly peopled country of the Abbasid Empire, well watered by the Tigris and Euphrates and the count
less canals springing from them, surrounded by gardens, where the natural
advantages of the climate were heightened by every device that art could



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 265
suggest, closely connected with the outer world both by land and sea,
Bagdad may well have seemed an earthly paradise. ... A city within a city
inhabited by thousands of officials, the Caliph's palace spread its vast courts
on the west bank with the great mosque, surrounded by the quarters of all
the many trades which fed on the luxury of the court. On the east bank of
the Tigris was the fashionable quarter. . . . where the great family of the
Barmecides had their houses and gardens. Countless minarets shot up their
slender stems like tall rushes into the clear sky. (35, Vol. X, p. 167)
Even with Europe, there was a considerable Saracenic commerce.
Moslems and Russians traded goods at Astrakhan, Kiev, Sari, and the
Black Sea ports. The Russians sold the Saracens slaves, furs, hemp,
cordage, and lumber, and bought fruits, spices, perfumes, and other
luxuries from them. A Moslem traveler visited Germany during the
reign of Otto the Great (936-73) and saw spices and silks from eastern
lands on sale in the city of Mainz. The extent and volume of this com
merce have been revealed to us by the numerous coins containing Ara
bic inscriptions which have been found widely scattered in eastern,
northern, and central Europe. All told, more than thirteen thousand
coins bearing the stamp of Saracenic sovereigns have been discovered
in these areas.
The reader of this discourse has probably heard of the invasion of
Spain by the Moors of North Africa in the seventh century and of how
they ruled over the Iberian peninsula for about eight hundred years.
This phase of the history of civilization has been slighted by the writers
of textbooks on so-called world history, so we must briefly treat of it.
Following the destruction of the city of Carthage in 146 B.C., the
Romans established a group of five provinces in North Africa which
were known collectively as Africa Romana. The ancient Libyan inhab
itants of this territory became amalgamated with Phoenician, Greek,
and Roman immigrants, and the Romans called these North Africans
Barbari (barbarians), from whence we get the name Berber, so in me
dieval and modern times, these people have been called Berbers. The
Romans labeled these Africans barbarians, not because of any cultural
inferiority, but only because they possessed certain mores which
differed from those of the Romans. The Libyans and their Berber kins
men had a matriarchal type of social organization which was common
to all African societies, but which seemed somewhat odd to the Romans
of Europe.
In the first century B.C., Julius Caesar decided to build a new Car



266 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
thage on the site of the old city but, due to his untimely death, the plan
was actually carried out by Augustus Caesar. An interesting account of
this event has been given by Professor J. C. de Graft-Johnson, of the
University of Ghana, which we are pleased to quote:
