Carthage was rebuilt in the finest Roman architectural style. The build
ers worked under military discipline and soon the temples, palaces, baths,
theaters, high houses and market places sprang up on the old sites. A new
Forum was erected, the villas and lovely gardens of the Carthaginian sub
urb Megara reappeared. . . . Utica and Carthage were joined by a splendid
road in the true Roman style and blockhouses were built at intervals along
the route in order to protect the caravans against robbers. Other forts and
cities soon made their appearance and with the rebuilding of Carthage, the
prosperity of North Africa increased tremendously. The Carthage which
the Romans had destroyed was to be supplied with fresh, clear water con
veyed by a specially constructed aqueduct which brought the water from
ninety miles away. Julius and Augustus Caesar had in part atoned for the
sins of the past. (59, pp. 27-29)
In the last days of the Western Roman Empire, Spain was occupied
by the Vandals, where their tenure was insecure, since they were
being menaced by an incursion of the Visigoths. The Vandals, through
the good offices of Count Boniface, a Roman legate in North Africa,
were able to migrate to Africa Romana. The Vandals established a tyr
anny in that region that was destructive in the highest degree. We
learn from Professor de Graft -Johnson: "They found Africa flourish
ing and they ieft it desolate, with its great buildings thrown down, its
people reduced to slavery and the Church of Africa, so important in
those early days of Christianity, practically non-existent." (59, p. 56)
The Vandal despotism in Africa was ended in the year 533 when the Emperor Justinian of the Byzantine Empire sent an army under Gen
eral Belisarius to undertake the reconquest of Africa Romana. The Vandals were overcome, and North Africa, for nearly a hundred years
afterward, was under Byzantine rule. A contemporary writer, the
monk Salvian, has left us an interesting description of Carthage under
Byzantine sway:
Where are there more abundant treasures than with the Africans?
Where can we find more prosperous commerce shops better stocked? . . . Carthage, formerly the rival of Rome as regards power and warlike quality,
was she not afterward her rival in splendor, and in imposing majesty? Car
thage, the Rome of Africa, held in her bosom all the treasuries of the state;



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 267
here was the seat of government and all the institutes of the State; here
were schools for the liberal arts, audiences for philosophers, chairs for pro
fessors of all languages and for every branch of law. (Cited by J. C. de
Graft-Johnson in 59, p. 57)
The first incursion of the forces of Islam into Africa was in 640,
when Amru the Conqueror led his army into Egypt. The Saracen con
quest was aided by the Christians of Alexandria, who were enemies of
the Byzantine tyrant. These heretical Christians paid tribute to the ca
liph, repaired roads and bridges, and supplied provisions to the invading
hosts. After a siege of fourteen months, Alexandria capitulated to
Amru in the year 642. In a dispatch to the Caliph Omar, Amru enu
merated the glories of Alexandria and spoke of "its 4,000 palaces, 4,000
baths, 400 theaters, 1,200 shops for the sale of vegetables, and 40,000
tributary Jews."
The Saracens, after capturing Alexandria, almost immediately set
out to conquer the North African coast. General Abdullah, starting
from Memphis with an army forty thousand strong, traversed the
desert of Barca and besieged Tripoli; but the onset of a plague in his
forces compelled a retreat to Egypt. Twenty years later, another at
tempt was made to take over North Africa, when General Akbah led
an army from the Nile River to the Atlantic Ocean. Facing the Canary
Islands, the Saracen general rode his horse into the ocean and pro
claimed: "Great God! If my course were not stopped by this sea, I
would still go on to the unknown'kingdoms of the West, preaching the
unity of thy holy name, and putting to the sword the rebellious nations
who worship any other gods than thee." Early in the eighth century,
Caliph Alwalid of Damascus authorized his general, Musa, to under
take an invasion of Europe by way of Spain. The conquest of North Africa was completed in 708 when Musa subdued all of Morocco ex
cept Ceuta, which was under the rule of the Byzantine governor,
Count Julian. Musa could go no further until an army of Moors and
Berbers was organized and the opposition of Count Julian was over
come; and this task was accomplished by a Moor named Tarik. The
events leading up to the Moorish invasion of Andalusia have been in
terestingly related by Professor de Graft-Johnson:
Among the African chiefs converted to the Islamic faith during the Arab
invasion of Morocco was a great general known as Tarik. . . . Tarik was given the rank of general in the Arab army by Musa-ibn-Nusair. Musa later left Tarik in charge of Tangiers and made him governor of Mauri



268 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
tania. . . . The African, Tarik, now governor of Mauritania, entered into
friendly relations with Count Julian, governor of Ceuta. It was then that
Tarik discovered that Julian was on very bad terms with his master, Roder
ick, the Gothic king of Spain. Roderick, a profligate prince, had ravished
Julian's daughter, and Julian was looking for a way to avenge the dishonor
done to his family. Count Julian urged the African Tarik to invade Spain,
but the suggestion had to be carried out cautiously. Tarik accordingly informed Musa-ibn-Nusair, who had appointed him governor, that he in
tended crossing the straits to survey and examine the possibilities for an
invasion. (59, pp. 68-69)
This preliminary invasion was delegated ro Tarif, an officer in Tank's army. Four boats were borrowed from Count Julian, who is said to have accompanied Tarif; and an army of four hundred men and a
hundred horses were transported to Spain. The army landed at a place
later renamed Tarifa in honor of Tarif; and it was at the port of Tarifa
that the Moors later levied a certain tax, which, taking its name from
the town, became known as the tariff. Tarif and his small force plun
dered Algericas and neighboring towns and returned to Africa, with
their boats laden with spoils. This result was encouraging, so Tarik, in
71 1, led an army of twelve thousand in an all-out invasion of Spain. In
de Graft-Johnson's words:
Tarik crossed the straits and landed on the isthmus between an escarp
ment, then known as Mons Calpe, and the continent. . . . Realizing the immense advantage which Mons Calpe commanded, Tarik ordered a castle
to be built ortit so that he could retire there and hold out should he meet
with reverses in battle. Part of this castle is still to be seen today.
Tarik left a garrison at the foot of Mons Calpe (which the Africans re
named, in compliment to their general, Gebel Tarik—the Hill of Tarik
name which was subsequently corrupted by the Spaniards into Gibraltar). General Tarik and his African army surprised and captured several Spanish towns, among them Heraclea, which was only four miles from the rock of
Gibraltar. King Roderick soon heard about the invading army and he set
about gathering a huge force to oppose Tarik. After a series of skirmishes,
the two armies met near Xeres in Andalusia. The conflict was a bloody one,
but Tarik was victorious and soon became master of Spain. (59, p. 69)
There is a droll and fantastic legend concerning King Roderick. Ac
cording to this tale, prior to the Moorish invasion of Spain, King Rod
erick was visited at his palace in Toledo by two elderly men who
sought audience with him. These visitors were clad in white robes of



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 269
ancient design and wore girdles adorned with the zodiacal signs from which hung numerous keys. Addressing the monarch, the old men announced: "Know, O King, that in days of yore, when Hercules had set
up his pillars at the ocean strait, he erected a strong tower near to this
ancient city of Toledo, and shut up within it a magical spell, secured by
a ponderous iron gate with locks of steel; and he ordained that every
new king should set a fresh lock to the portal, and foretold woe and de
struction to him who should seek to unravel the mystery of the tower.
Now, we and our ancestors have kept the door of the tower from the
days of Hercules even to this hour; and though there have been kings
who have sought to discover the secret, their end has ever been death
or sore amazement. None ever penetrated beyond the threshold. Now,
O King, we come to beg thee to affix thy lock upon the enchanted
tower as all the kings before thee have done."
This message having been delivered, the aged pair departed. Roder
ick naturally wanted to find out the secret of the tower and so informed
his courtiers. His counselors warned him against such an endeavor and
told him that no one had ever entered the tower and returned to tell
about it; and that even Julius Caesar had not dared to enter the strong
hold. The royal advisers conveyed to the king the following poetic ad
monition:
Nor shall it ever ope, old records say,
Save to a king, the last of all his line,
What time his empire totters to decay,
And treason digs, beneath, her fatal mine, And high above impends avenging wrath Divine.
This, of course, did not hold back King Roderick who, surrounded
by a group of cavaliers, one day advanced on the tower, which stood on
a lofty rock, hemmed in by cliffs and precipices. The walls of this
tower were of jasper and marble, inlaid with designs which sparkled in
the rays of the sun. The entrance was by way of a passage cut through
stone, and was barred by a huge iron gate covered with rusty locks,
said to date back to the time of Hercules; and on each side of the gate
stood one of the two venerable men who had visited the audience
chamber of the king. For almost a day the two elderly guardians of the
gate, aided by the cavaliers of the king, turned keys in the rusty locks.
Slightly before sunset, the gate was opened and the royal party entered
the enchanted tower. Inside the gate was a hall, on the other side of
which was a door guarded by a gigantic bronze figure of terrible as



270 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
peer, which continuously swung a massive mace that smote the ground
nearby with mighty blows. On the breast of this monster robot King
Roderick saw the words "1 do my duty"; so the king appealed to the
animated image to permit him to pass, since he planned no sacrilege,
but only wished to solve the mystery of the tower. The figure then be
came still and, with uplifted mace, allowed the king and his compan
ions through the door into the second room. There they found them
selves in a chamber encrusted with precious stones in the midst of
which was a table, which had been placed therein by Hercules; and on the table was a casket upon which was engraved an inscription which
read: 'in this coffer is the mystery of the Tower. The hand of none
but a king can open it; but let him beware, for wonderful things will be
disclosed to him, which must happen before his death."
The king opened the coffer and on the inside was a piece of parchment folded between two plates of copper. On the parchment were
pictures of fierce warriors on horseback armed with bows and scimi
tars, and above them was theslogan: "Behold rash man, those who shall
hurl thee from thy throne and subdue thy kingdom." As the king and
his courtiers gazed at the figures on the parchment, they suddenly be
came aware of the sounds of battle. The figures of horsemen began to
move, and before them arose a vision of war:
They beheld before them a great field of battle where Christians and Moors were engaged in deadly conflict. They heard the rush and tramping
of steeds, the^blast of trump and clarion, the clash of cymbal, and the stormy din of a thousand drums. There was the flash of swords and maces
and battle axes with the whistling of arrows and the hurling of darts and
lances. The Christians quailed before the foe. The infidels pressed upon
them and put them to utter rout; the standard of the cross was cast down,
the banner of Spain was trodden under foot; the air resounded with shouts of triumph, with yells of fury and with the groans of dying men. Amidst
the flying squadrons, King Roderick beheld a crowned warrior whose back was turned towards him, but whose armor and device were his own, and who was mounted on a white steed that resembled his own war horse Ore
lia. In the confusion of the fight, the warrior was dismounted and was no
longer seen to be, and Orelia galloped wildly through the field of battle
without a rider. (The Conquest of Spain, Bonn's edition, pp. 378 ff., by
Washington Irving, cited by Stanley Lane-Poole in 140, pp. 18-19)
As the vision of the impending disaster came to an end, the king and
his attendants fled from the tower in a state of panic. By this time, the



THK ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 271
great bronze image had vanished, and the two old men were lying dead
at the entrance to the tower. The tower itself then burst into flame and
every stone of it was consumed by fire.
When King Roderick went forth to do battle with the Moors he led
a large army that outnumbered his foe six to one. He was clad in an
outfit of ornate armor and was covered by a magnificent canopy. But
the Africans quailed not; for, as General Tarik led them into battle, his
voice rang out: "Men, before you is the enemy and the sea is at your
backs. By Allah, there is no escape for you save in valor and resolu
tion." This electrified the Moorish and they shouted in reply: "We
will follow thee, O. Tarik," as they charged into the ranks of the
enemy. The battle raged for a week, but finally the African invaders broke through the Andalusian lines and King Roderick and his hosts
were routed, the king himself being killed in the battle.
The Islamic culture of the Middle Ages is customarily refer/ed to as
Arabic, but the fact is that the Arabs were a minority in the so-called
Arabic world, and their chief contribution was the Arabic language.
This matter has been judiciously explained by Professor de Graft
Johnson:
It was because the conquering army in Spain was largely made up of Af
ricans from Morocco that we hear such phrases as the Moorish invasion of
Spain, and why Shakespeare's hero Othello is a Moor, and why the word
blackamoor exists in the English language, a word which leaves no doubt as
to the color of the army of occupation in Spain and Portugal.
To begin with, there was an intolerant self-sufficiency among the Arabs which made the Koran the only source of learning and the only book worth reading. This made the development of science and true knowledge impos
sible, but the intolerance was soon to give way to a new wave of learning. The organization of education throughout the Moslem world began in the
eighth century and by the ninth, learned men in the schools of Cordova in
Spain were corresponding with the learned in Kairowan, Cairo, Bagdad, Bokhara and Samarkand. The Greek classics were rediscovered and Aristotle came into his own.
The museum at Alexandria, so long neglected, became the center of re
search and learning. Mathematics, medicine, and the physical sciences received fresh attention. The clumsy Roman numerals were soon ousted by
the Arabic figures which we use to this day, and the zero sign first came
into general usage. Arabic words like algebra and chemistry became univer
sal words. . . . The term Arabic we intend in a cultural rather than a racial
sense, for the Arabs did not believe in any herrenvolk theory and freely in
termarried with those they conquered. ... It was through Africa that the



272 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
new knowledge of China, India and Arabia reached Europe, and it was Af
rica which supplied the men who protected Moslem Europe or Spain from
attack, and thus made it possible for the new learning to take root and de
velop. (59, pp. 74-76)
After overrunning the Iberian Peninsula, the Moorish invaders
pushed on through to France, where they were repulsed with heavy
losses by an army of Franks, led by Charles Martel. After this setback,
they retired into Spain, and there laid the foundations of a new civiliza
tion. The country was incalculably enriched by their labors. They, for
instance, first established the silk industry in Spain; they were highly
skilled agriculturists; and they introduced cotton, rice, sugar cane,
dates, ginger, lemons, and strawberries into the peninsula. Abu Zacaria
and Ibn-al-Awam wrote learned works on animal husbandry and agri
culture. A translation of an agricultural treatise by Ibn-al-Awam
(twelfth century) was published in Spain as late as 1802 for the in
struction of Spanish farmers. Ibn Khaldun, another Moorish expert on
agriculture, wrote an important treatise on farming and worked out a
theory of prices and the nature of capital. The Moorish achievement in
hydraulic engineering was outstanding. The Caliph Abd-er-Rahman of
Cordova commissioned the construction of an aqueduct which con
veyed water from the mountains to the city through lead pipes. In
modern Spain, only minute vestiges remain of the extensive irrigation
systems constructed by Moorish engineers; who also built large under
ground silos for the storage of grain in cases of emergency. The min
eral wealth ofthe land was not disregarded, for copper, gold, silver, tin,
lead, iron, quicksilver, and alum were mined on a large scale. Up to the
twelfth century, the maritime commerce of the Saracens on the Medi
terranean was greater than that of the Christian nations, and hundreds
of ships were engaged in it. This extensive trade was a natural stimu
lant to manufacturing. The sword blades of Toledo in quality and ap
pearance excelled all others produced in Europe, and a factory not far
from Cordova turned out twelve thousand fine shields per year. In
Murcia, factories fabricated all sorts of iron and brass implements; the
tanneries of Cordova and Morocco City were the best in the world;
Almeria specialized in the production of sashes, famous for their fine
texture and brilliant colors; carpets were made in Teulala, and bright
hued woolens in Granada and Baza. The Moorish craftsmen also pro
duced glass and pottery, vases, mosaics, and jewelry.
Cordova was the wonderful citv of the tenth century; the streets



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 273
were well paved and there were raised sidewalks for pedestrians. At
night one could walk for ten miles by the light of lamps, flanked by an
uninterrupted extent of buildings. All this was hundreds of years before
there was a paved street in Paris or a street lamp in London. Cordova
with a population exceeding one million was served by four thousand
public markets and five thousand mills. Its public baths numbered in
the hundreds, when bathing in the rest of Europe was frowned upon as
a diabolical custom, avoided by all good Christians. Moorish monarchs
dwelt in sumptuous palaces, while the crowned heads of England,
France, and Germany lived in big barns, lacking both windows and
chimneys and with only a hole in the roof for the exit of smoke. Educa
tion was universal in Moslem Spain, being given to the most humble,
while in Christian Europe 99 percent of the populace was illiterate, and
even kings could neither read nor write. In the tenth and eleventh
centuries, public libraries in Christian Europe were conspicuous by
their absence, while Moslem Spain could boast of more than seventy,
of which the one in Cordova housed 600,000 manuscripts. Christian
Europe contained only two universities of any consequence, while in
Spain there were seventeen outstanding universities. The finest were
those located in Almeria, Cordova, Granada, Jaen, Malaga, Seville, and
Toledo. Scientific progress in astronomy, chemistry, physics, mathe
matics, geography, and philology in Mqslem Spain reached a high level
of development. Scholars and artists formed associations to promote
their particular studies, and scientific congresses were organized to pro
mote research and facilitate the spread of knowledge.
Other cities in Spain were scarcely less opulent than Cordova. The
Sheik Ash-Shakandi, a Moorish scholar of the early thirteenth century,
was widely traveled in Spain and Morocco, and he praised highly the
glories of several other great cities of the Moorish Empire. Almeria
came in for special commendation. It was a bustling seaport, and its
shipyard manufactured fine boats. This greatest of Andalusian marts
did a thriving business in the maritime exports and imports. Ships from
eastern lands brought to its docks the finest goods of India and China.
The Almerian silk factories specialized in the production of damasks
and brocades and of silver and gold tissues, a trade which employed
thousands of artisans. There were also factories turning out high-qual
ity glass, pottery, and hardware. "Almeria is an opulent and magnif
icent city," said the Sheik, "whose fame has spread far and wide. God
has endowed its inhabitants with various gifts, such as a temperate cli



274 MAN, 001), AND CIVILIZATION
mare and abundance of fruits; rhey are handsome, well-made, good na
tured, very hospitable, very much attached to their friends and, above
all things, very refined in their manners and very elegant in their dress.
Its coast is the finest in all the Mediterranean as well as the safest and
most frequented." The citizens of Almeria were the wealthiest in all
Andalusia and managed to create a big factory town without detracting
from its scenic beauty and charm. A picturesque river wound over the
landscape for forty miles, and along its banks were groves, gardens, and
orchards inhabited by songbirds who delighted with their harmonies
the ears of the citizens.
A brisk intellectual life flourished in all parts of the Islamic domin
ions, for caliphs of both the East and the West were, as a rule, en
lightened patrons of learning. Caliph Al Hakem of Cordova filled an
entire palace with books collected from all parts of the known world,
and the 600,000 volumes of the royal library were all carefully cata
logued. Caliph Al-Mamun of Bagdad imported hundreds of camel loads
of books into his metropolis, and he signed a treaty with the Emperor
Michael III of the Byzantine Empire in which he stipulated that an en
tire library in Constantinople should be given to him. In this library
was a rare literary treasure, the treatise of Claudius Ptolemy on the
mathematical structure of the heavens. The Caliph, an able astronomer
himself, had the work translated into Arabic under the title of "The
Almagest." Harun-al-Rashid was the founder of the University of
Bagdad, and the most celebrated professor of this institution was a Jew
ish scholar, Joshua Ben Nun. Harun was also a patron of the Medical
College of Djoridesabour in southern Persia, and graduate students in
medicine had to pass an examination set by the faculty of that school or
by that of the University of Bagdad before being licensed to practice
the healing art. The Caliph Al-Mamun appointed a noted Christian
scholar to the presidency of a college in Damascus. The same ruler commissioned Postmaster Ibn Khurdadbeh of Bagdad to make a geo
graphical survey of his realms, mapping all sea and land routes, with all
places accurately located in respect to latitude and longitude. The Sara
cens adopted the decimal system of numerical notation from the mathe
maticians of India, and this notation was perfected by Mohammed Ben
Musa Al-Khwarizmi in the ninth century. He also introduced zero as a
mathematical quantity. The same mathematician wrote the first text
book on algebra and was the author of a treatise on spherical trigonom
etry. The Moslem mathematicians solved quadratic and cubic equa
tions in algebra and made trigonometry into a science by substituting



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 275
sines and tangents for the chords of the Greeks. Omar Khayyam is
now remembered for his poetry, but in his own time he was best known as a mathematician and an astronomer. The Caliph Al-Mamun
(ninth century) determined the obliquity of the ecliptic and calculated
the circumference of the earth by sending out an expedition which
measured the length of a degree of latitude on the shore of the Red Sea.
We have been taught in popular school histories that Magellan estab
lished the fact of the rotundity of the earth by circumnavigating it in
1519. This popular opinion is false, for Moorish schoolteachers taught
geography from globes long before Magellan's birth. The Moorish
scientist El Idrisi (twelfth century) wrote a book on geography, and in
that work said: "What results from the opinion of philosophers, learned
men, and those skilled in observation of the heavenly bodies, is that the
world is round as a sphere, of which the waters are adherent and main
tained upon its surface by natural equilibrium. It is surrounded by air,
and all created bodies are stable on its surfaces, the earth drawing to it
self all that is heavy in the same way as a magnet attracts iron." (Cited
by Lady Lugard in 146, pp. 37-38) Avicenna of Bokhara (980-1037) was a philosopher, physician, and
geologist and wrote many scientific and philosophical treatises. His best
known works were The Utility and Advantage of Science, Health and
Remedies, Canons of Physic, Astronomical Observations, Mathematical
Theorems, The Arabic Language, The Origin of the Soul and the Resur
rection of the Body, An Abridgment of Euclid, Physics and Metaphysics,
An Encyclopedia of Human Knowledge (twenty volumes). Under the scepter of Abd-er-Rahman, the western caliphate ex
tended from the Pyrenees to the borders of the Sudan; and there was a
dual government, with courts in both Spain and Morocco. This state
was known as the Empire of the Two Shores, and its sovereign, Abd
er-Rahman, was a man of many talents and high character. We have
been reliably informed: u His meekness, his generosity and his love of
justice became proverbial. . . . He was fond of science and the patron
of the learned, with whom he loved to converse, spending those hours
that he stole from the arduous labors of the administration in literary
meetings, to which all the eminent poets and learned men of his court
were admitted." (146, p. 51) The Moors introduced the manufacture of gunpowder into Europe,
and then their enemies adopted this weapon and used it to drive them
back into Africa. After several centuries of dominance, the culture of
Islam finally faded away into decadence. The Saracens never recovered



276 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
from the conquests of the Seljuk Turks in the eleventh century and the Mongols in the thirteenth. The Mongols, under Hulagu Khan, cap
tured and sacked Bagdad in 1258, when they destroyed the great irri
gation system dating back to antiquity, and thus turned vast areas of
fertile land into a desert. The disintegration of the Cordovan caliphate
dates from the year 1031, when the internecine strife of the Moslems
made it possible for their Christian enemies to overpower them. By
1492, the Moors had lost all Spain except the kingdom of Granada; and
the Christians were finally united by the marriage of Ferdinand and Is
abella, which joined in peace the formerly hostile royal houses of Ara
gon and Castile. The consolidated Christian forces cut off" and block
aded the city of Granada for eight months. The Moorish monarch,
King Baobdil, eventually capitulated. They lingered in Spain for a
while, but by 1610, through expulsion and migration, a million of them
had returned to North and West Africa.
The word Moor literally means black, so the Moors were the black
people. The Frenchman, de Fontenelle, in 1686 in his book Conversa
tion on the Plurality of Worlds speculated on what kind of people inhab
ited the planet Venus. "I can tell from here," he said, "what the inhab
itants of Venus are like; they resemble the Moors of Granada; a small
black people, burned by the sun, full of wit and fire, always in love,
writing verse, fond of music, arranging festivals, dances and tourna
ments every day." (Cited by Robert Jastrow in 125, pp. 1 18-19)
The final undoing of the dusky Moors was their underestimation of
the menace of the fair barbarians to the north of the Pyrenees. A Mos
lem historian iri the eleventh century referred to these people as barbar
ians with big bellies, pale skins, long and lank hair, and declared:
"They lack keenness of understanding and clarity of intelligence, and
are overcome by ignorance and foolishness, blindness and stupidity."
Another Moorish scholar, Said of Toledo, in the thirteenth century,
spoke of this same group of people in the following words: "They are
cold of temperament and never reach maturity. They are of great stat
ure and of a white color. But they lack all sharpness of wit and penetra
tion of intellect." (Cited by J. B. S. Haldane in 106, p. 138) The ex
pulsion of the Moors from Spain was a serious setback to modern
civilization. One of the finest studies of the Moorish contribution to
world history was Stanley Lane-Poole's The Story of the Moors in Spain
(140). This work is now a very rare book, but the interested student
should have some acquaintance with it, so we give the gist of this excel
lent study in the words of its distinguished author:



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 277
The history of Spain offers us a melancholy contrast. Twelve hundred
years ago, Tarik the Moor added the land of the Visigoths to the long cata
log of kingdoms subdued by the Moslems. For nearly eight centuries, under her Mohammedan rulers, Spain set to all Europe a shining example of a civ
ilized and enlightened state. Her fertile provinces, rendered doubly prolific
by the industry and engineering skill of her conquerors, bore fruit an hun
dred fold. Cities innumerable sprang up in the rich valleys of the Guadelquivir and the Guadiana, whose names, and names only, still commemorate
the vanished glories of their past. Art, literature, and science prospered as
they then prospered nowhere else in Europe. Students flocked from France and Germany and England to drink from the fountain of learning which
flowed only in the cities of the Moors. The surgeons and doctors of Andalusia were in the van of science: women were encouraged to devote them
selves to serious study, and the lady doctor was not unknown among the people of Cordova. Mathematics, astronomy and botany, history, philoso
phy and jurisprudence were to be mastered in Spain and in Spain alone.
The practical work of the field, the scientific methods of irrigation, the arts
of fortification and shipbuilding; the highest and most elaborate products of
the loom, the graver and the hammer, the potter's wheel and the mason's
trowel, were brought to perfection by the Spanish Moors. In the practice of
war, no less than in the arts of peace, they long stood supreme. Their fleets
disputed the command of the Mediterranean with the Fatimites, while their
armies carried fire and sword through the Christian marches. The Cid him
self, the national hero, long fought on the Moorish side, and in all save education was more than half a Moor. Whatsoever makes a kingdom great and prosperous, whatsoever tends to refinement and civilization, was found in Moslem Spain. In 1492 the last bulwark of the Moors gave way before the crusade of
Ferdinand and Isabella, and with Granada fell all Spain's greatness. ... In
the land where science was once supreme, the Spanish doctors became
noted for nothing but their ignorance and incapacity, and the discoveries of Newton and Harvey were condemned as pernicious to the faith. Where
once seventy public libraries had fed the minds of scholars, and half a mil
lion books had been gathered at Cordova for the benefit of the world, such
indifference to learning afterwards prevailed that the new capital, Madrid,
possessed no public library in the eighteenth century, and even the manu
scripts of the Escurial were denied in our own day to the first scholarly his
torian of the Moors, though himself a Spaniard. The sixteen thousand looms
of Seville soon dwindled to a fifth of their ancient number; the arts and in
dustries of Toledo and Almeria faded into insignificance; the very baths
public buildings of equal ornament and use—were destroyed because clean
liness savored too strongly of rank infidelity. The land, deprived of the
skillful irrigation of the Moors, grew impoverished and neglected; the rich



278 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
est and most fertile valleys languished and were deserted; most of the popu
lous cities which had filled every district of Andalusia fell into ruinous
decay; and beggars, friars and bandits took the place of scholars, merchants
and knights. So low fell Spain when she had driven away the Moors. Such
is the melancholy contrast offered by her history. (140, pp. vii-ix)
Lane-Poole concluded his brilliant chronicle of the Moorish achieve
ment in Spain with this dolorous passage:
The Moors were banished; for a while Christian Spain shone like the
moon, with a borrowed light; then came the eclipse, and in that darkness
Spain has grovelled ever since. The true memorial of the Moors is seen in
desolate tracts of utter barrenness, where once the Moslem grew luxuriant
vines and olives and yellow ears of corn; in a stupid, ignorant population
where once wit and learning flourished; in the general stagnation and deg
radation of a people which has hopelessly fallen in the scale of nations, and
has deserved its humiliation. (140, p. 280)
The outstanding characteristic of the Arab-Moorish monarchs was
their urbanity and tolerance. Their relations with the most distant na
tions were extremely cordial. Our knowledge of the past has been
greatly enriched by the industry of Moslem travelers, historians, and
scholars. Early in the tenth century, mariners of Oman in the south of
Arabia sailed southward to trade with the Zanj (Swahili) of the East
African coast. In one of their boats was Al Masudi of Bagdad, a great
historian of his age, and a voluminous writer. Most of his works have
perished, but one great opus survives, his Meadows of Gold and Mines of
Gems. A complete French translation of this great narrative was pub
lished in Paris, in 1864, in nine volumes. The only English translation,
a one-volume abridgment, was published in London in 1841. There
was a brisk ivory trade in the tenth century between East Africa and
India and China. Masudi related how the tusks of ivory from the Zanj
country were first taken to Oman and there transshipped to India and
China. "In China," he noted, "the kings and their military and civilian
officers use carrying chairs of ivory. . . . Ivory is much prized in India;
there it is made into handles for the daggers known as harari or ham in
the singular, as well as for the hilts of curved swords. . . . But the
biggest use of ivory is in the manufacture of chessmen and other gam
ing pieces." (Meadows of Gold and Mines of Gems, Aloys Sprenger's
translation, cited in 7)
Besides the Zanj of Africa, Masudi wrote of another Zanj Empire in
Asia, centered in Indochina and spreading into Malaysia and Indonesia.



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 279
"India is a vast country," said Masudi, "having many seas and mountains, and borders upon the empire of ez-Zanij, which is the kingdom
of the Maharaj, the king of the islands, whose dominions form the fron
tier between India and China, and are considered as part of India." He
referred to the "splendor and high civilization" of these Asiatic Zanj
and stated that "the Maharaj is lord of the sixth sea and king of the
islands from which drugs and spices are exported and that the popula
tion and the number of the troops of his kingdom cannot be counted;
and the islands under his scepter are so numerous that the fastest sailing
vessel is not able to go round them in two years." (Meadows of Gold
and Mines of Gems, Aloys Sprenger's translation, cited in 7, p. 263)
According to Masudi, the people of this island empire were black. These Asiatic blacks were the Khmers, who were dominant in south
east Asia for six hundred years, and their center of culture was in Cam
bodia. It flourished during the years 800-1432. We have been informed by a contemporary investigator:
The Khmers began to develop their civilization about 2,000 years ago under the influence of traders from India and later from China. A Chinese
traveler of the third century A.D. reported: "The men are all ugly and
black. Their hair is curly. They go naked and barefoot." A later account of
the Khmer region, which the Chinese called Funan, said: "The people of
Funan make rings and bracelets of gold and vessels of silver." In the middle of the sixth century, Funan merged with another Khmer kingdom inland
known as Chenla to form a larger nation that called itself Kambujadesa—in
its westernized form, Cambodia (228, p. 137)
The Moorish historian Idrisi (1100-1166) referred to the superior
quality of Indian iron in one of his historical works. This Indian iron
was the famous wootz steel, fabricated in India from iron imported
from southeast Africa. The celebrated Damascus blades were made
from this steel.
When the first Portuguese explorers sailed up the east coast of Af
rica on the way to India they were surprised to find there a series of
seaport cities, such as Sofala, Mombasa, Kilwa, Malindi, and Zanzibar.
Kilwa was the greatest of these trading ports. In the year 1331 the fa
mous Moorish globe-trotter Ibn Batuta journeyed down the East Afri
can coast. Of all the places he visited, Kilwa impressed him most favor
ably. We give his impressions in his own words:
Then I set off by sea from the town of Mogadishu for the land of the
Swahili and the town of Kilwa, which is in the land of Zanj. We arrived at



280 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Mombasa, a large island two days' journey from the land of the Swahili.
The island is quite separate from the mainland. It grows bananas, lemons
and oranges. . . . The people do not engage in agriculture, but import
grain from the Swahili. The greater part of their diet is bananas and fish.
. . . We spent a night on the island of Mombasa and then set sail for
Kilwa, the principal town on the coast, the greater part of whose inhabit
ants are Zanj of very black complexion. . . . Kilwa is one of the most beau
tiful and well constructed towns in the world. The whole of it is elegantly
built. (92, p. 31)
Most of the Swahili trading ports were destroyed by Portuguese
vandals in the early sixteenth century, but the one emporium that man
aged to survive was Zanzibar. Famous for its spices, Zanzibar is an
island some four hundred miles south of the equator and about twenty
miles off the Tanzanian coast. In 1940, from the fourteen million clove
trees of Zanzibar, a crop of fifteen thousand tons of cloves was realized.
This was about 80 percent of the clove production of the entire world.
Zanzibar is one of those exotic places that has a charm all of its own.
We have learned from the statement of a resident that: "There are
worse places than Zanzibar, but not much worse. The place is so
windy that passengers on landing are blown up the beach right into the
Africa Hotel, which serves whiskey with lizards in it. You'll be sorry if
you visit Zanzibar, but you will be equally sorry if you do not. The
Island has a railroad that goes to a place called Boobooboo. It is of very
narrow gauge and has eight engines; one for every mile." (Cited by
Carveth Wells in 264, p. 158)
Ibn Batuta was born in Morocco in 1303, and starting in his youth
spent about thirty years traveling widely in Africa, Asia, and Europe.
In 1 349 he settled down in Fez, the capital of the kingdom of Morocco,
for a rest from his globe-trotting. The head of state of that nation was
at the time King Abu el Hacen, and Ibn Batuta thought him a good ad
ministrator, and regarded conditions of life in his kingdom as highly
commendable. His sentiments have been preserved for posterity by a modern commentator:
For reasons which he sets forth at length, his judgment was convinced
that the noble country over which its sovereign ruled was the best country and the best administered of all those that he had visited. Here he found the
conditions of life better than in any other country. Food was more plentiful,
varied and cheap; life and property were more secure, law was milder, jus
tice was more assured, charity more fully organized, religion more truly maintained, and literature, science and art more honored than in any other



THE ARAB-MOORISH CULTURE 281
center of civilization. He mentions in regard to the organized charities of
the country, that free hospitals were constructed and endowed in every
town of the kingdom. As regards the endowment of science and literature,
he describes the great College of Fez as having no parallel in the known
world for size, beauty and magnificence. He speaks of the deep interest
taken by the sovereign in all that related to science and literature, the very
considerable literary achievements of the sovereign himself, and of the gen
erous protection which he gave to all persons who were devoted to the
study of science. (146, pp. 74-75)
In the mid-fourteenth century, the Empire of Mali in the western
Sudan was an enlightened and prosperous state, and Ibn Batuta could
not resist the urge to pay it a visit; so on February 18, 1 352, he crossed
the Moroccan border and headed south for Mali. The Moorish traveler
crossed the frontier of Mali at Walata. Though noting the excessive
heat, Batuta liked Walata and its citizens. He noticed that foodstuffs
were good and plentiful, and that the citizens wore clothing of an at
tractive design. The women were charming and beautiful and, in Batu
ta's opinion, decidedly superior to the men. He pondered over their
somewhat odd customs—especially the practice of tracing kinship in
the female line, through a maternal uncle, and of inheritance passing
not from father to son, but from father to the son of a sister. This prac
tice was common in the Sudan, but Batuta recorded that the only other
place he had come across these customs was among the inhabitants of
Malibar in India. From Walata to Niani, the Malian capital was a jour
ney of twenty days. Along the highway, villages were numerous, trav
eling was safe, good food was plentiful and cheap, and among the flora
of the region were fruit trees which bore fruits resembling plums, ap
ples, peaches, and apricots. Anything needful during the journey could
be purchased at the roadside villages. Ibn Batuta walked into Niani just
in time to be present at the ceremony of the ending of the fast of Ra
madan. The prime feature of this celebration was the presenting of
arms to the Sultan. Squires presented to the ruler magnificent swords,
trimmed in gold, with scabbards of the precious metal, spears of gold
and silver, quivers of gold, and clubs of crystal. This was succeeded by
dramatic performances, with fencing, dancing, gymnastics, and the
recitation of comic poetry included; and which the Moorish visitor re
garded as first-rate entertainment. Lady Lugard in her fascinating book
A Tropical Dependency devotes a whole chapter to the adventures of
Ibn Batuta in Mali; and the feature of these happenings that arrested
her attention the most was the fact that some of the customs of Mali in



282 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the fourteenth century were almost identical with those of the contem
porary Aztecs of Mexico. In her own words:
Poets wearing masks and dressed like birds were allowed to speak their
opinion to the monarch. Ibn Batuta states that this practice was of great an
tiquity, long anterior to the introduction of Islam among these people. The
description which he gives in some detail can hardly fail to recall similar
practices inherited from the Tezcucans by the Aztecs, who in nearly the
same latitude on the American continent were at this very moment, in the
middle of the fourteenth century, making good their position upon the
Mexican plateau. . . . They had other customs which correspond to those
of the Sudanese. The Aztec crown was transmitted, like that of Mali, in
collateral descent, though in both kingdoms, exceptions to the rule oc
curred. The practice of keeping the sons of subject princes as a sort of hon
orable hostage at the court of the monarch was perhaps too general to be
worthy of special note, though it was also common to the two people. The
more terrible custom of propitiating the gods with human sacrifice which
was so extensively practiced by the Aztecs, was, it will be remembered,
only the other day brought to an end under British rule in Benin, and is
probably still practiced in less accessible portions of the Pagan belt. . . .
The custom of wearing the heads of animals as headdress, which was also
common to the Aztecs, was preserved among the Pagans of the West Afri
can coast at the time of the first occupation of the Gold Coast by the Portu
guese in 1481. (146, pp. 137-39)



CHAPTER SEVENTEEN
The Evolution of Civilization:
Ancient America
J. A. ROGERS, in his scholarly work Africa's Gift to America, cited Latin-American scholars on the ancient African influence in America. For example, C. C. Marquez is quoted as saying: "The Negro type is seen in the most ancient American sculpture. . . . Negroes figure fre
quently in the most remote traditions." From the Mexican historian,
Riva-Palacio, we have the following: "It is indisputable that in very an
cient times the Negro race occupied our territory (Mexico). . . . The
Mexicans recall a Negro god, Ixlilton,* which means black-face." Also, we have the testimony of Nicolas Leon, who declared: "The almost ex
tinction of the original Negroes during the time of the Spanish con
quest and the memories of them in the most ancient traditions induce
us to believe that the Negroes were the first inhabitants of Mexico."
In a little scholarly monograph, African Explorers of the New World,
Harold G. Lawrence expressed the following opinions:
That Africans voyaged across the Atlantic before the era of Christopher
Columbus is no recent belief. Scholars have long speculated that a great
seafaring nation which sent its ships to the Americas once existed on Afri
ca's west coast. . . . We can now positively state that the Mandingos of the
Mali and Songhay Empires and possibly other Africans, crossed the Atlan
tic to carry on trade with the Western Hemisphere Indians, and further
succeeded in establishing colonies throughout the Americas. (142, p. 2)
The Arab historian Abulfeda (1273-1332) described the world as
being spherical in shape and told of ships which had circumnavigated
* Ixlilton was the Aztec god of medicine and good health. The priests of this god were medicine men or shamans who specialized in the medical treatment of children. Do we have here an Aztec version of Imhotep, the deified Egyptian physician who became a god of medicine among the Greeks, under the name of Imouthes?

284 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the globe. We have documentary evidence of an African expedition to
the New World as late as the fourteenth century. In the magazine
West Africa, for Saturday, June 7, 1969, there was an article by Basil Davidson entitled: "Africans Before Columbus?" According to Davidson: "Columbus and other early European arriv
als in America came back with quite a bit of evidence, suggestive but
inconclusive, that black peoples from Africa had already reached those
shores. Various writers have pointed from time to time, over the past
twenty years and more, to the likely West African origins of these
black explorers, notably of that tribe of Almamys who were said to have settled in Honduras." The great West African potentate, Mansa Musa I of Mali (ca.
1312-1337), while visiting his friend the Caliph of Cairo in Egypt, became engaged in discussion with an Egyptian scholar, Ibn Amir Hajib. This conversation was recorded by the Egyptian annalist Al-Omari.
The account of this discourse, given below, was translated from the
Arabic by Basil Davidson. The dialog was between Mansa Musa and
Ibn Amir Hajib, and went as follows:
1 asked the Sultan Musa how it was that power came into his hands.
"We are," he told me, "from a house that transmits power by heritage. The ruler who preceded me (probably Mansa Mohammed) would not be
lieve that it was impossible to discover the limits of the neighboring sea (i.e., the Mali Empire's western and southwestern sea, the Atlantic). He wanted
to find out and persisted in his plan. "He had two hundred ships equipped and filled with men, and others in the same number filled with gold, water and supplies in sufficient quantity
to last for years. He told those who commanded them: 'Return only when you have reached the extremity of the ocean, or when you have exhausted your food and water.' They went away; their absence was long before any
of them returned. Finally, a single ship reappeared. We asked the captain
about their adventures.
" 'Prince,' he replied, 'we sailed for a long time, up to the moment when we encountered in mid-ocean something like a river with a violent current.
My ship was lost. The others sailed on, and gradually as each of them en
tered this place, they disappeared and did not come back. We did not know what had happened to them. As to me, I returned where I was and did not
enter that current.' "But the Emperor (Muhammad), did not want to believe him. He equipped two thousand vessels, a thousand for himself, and a thousand for water and supplies. He conferred power on me (Mansa Musa) and left with his companions on the ocean. This was the last time that I saw him and the others, and I remained absolute master of the empire." (56)



ANCIENT AMERICA 285
"Now it seems very probable indeed," Basil Davidson commented,
"that Musa said this to Ibn Hajib who told Al-Omari, a careful annal
ist, whose information about Mali rested on other eye witness accounts
by Egyptian scholars who had sojourned there."
Continuing speculations on this topic, Davidson finally concluded:
Those who believe that the sailors of Mali did in fact reach the Americas
have other evidence to adduce. They point out that ships sailing west from
Senegal would pass the Cape Verdes a little north of latitude 10 degrees
north and that, in doing so, they would sail into the steady westward
flowing equatorial current, and thence into the Antilles current as far as the
Gulf of Mexico. They also recall that Columbus, on his third voyage, had
information from the Cape Verde Islands that "canoes had been found
which start from the coast of Guinea and navigate to the west with mer
chandise." The view that Mansa Muhammad's expeditions were a figment of Mansa
Musa's imagination, or that, if they really did take place, they altogether
failed to reach the Americas, has thus to overlook a great many bits of and
pieces of evidence to the contrary. This evidence has been largely set aside
by historians, one cannot help feeling, because of some inherent preconcep
tion about the inability of Africans to navigate at sea. . . Medieval Mali
was no mere outpost of the civilized world of those times: on the contrary,
it was in touch with the eastern world of Islam through many travelers and
learned men, and the eastern world of Islam was very familiar with the sea.
Traveling was very much in the spirit of West Africa, then as later; and if
by land, why not by sea as well? (56)
Professor Paul Radin wrote learnedly on the various Amerindian
cultures, but the rise and fall of the Maya utterly baffled him. His
bewilderment was expressed as follows:
Their culture seems to have arisen suddenly and to have disappeared in
as mysterious a fashion. ... As early as the year 100 B.C., the hiero
glyphic system had already been fully developed. . . . Where and when
this hieroglyphic system developed, of that we have not the remotest idea.
The earliest hieroglyphic date is found on a little statuette and it is not until
two hundred years later that we find monuments with inscriptions and
dates. Then, without warning or preparation, we find enormous mounds
with elaborate temples built upon them, public squares, stelae and altars. No
excavations have ever revealed to us any civilization of a simpler nature
from which this elaborate culture could possibly have been developed. (193,
pp. 76-77)



286 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Fifty years ago, Professor Leo Wiener of Harvard University pre
sented his solution to the riddle of the genesis of early American civili
zations. His major work was the three-volume Africa and the Discovery
of America (1920-1922), and this was followed by Mayan and Mexican
Origins (1926). Since Wiener's profound researches clearly showed
that the ancient cultures of x'Xmerica were of African origin, his books
were either denounced or ignored. One of his Harvard colleagues, Pro
fessor Herbert J. Spinden, wrote an essay on "The Prosaic vs. the Ro
mantic School in Anthropology" in which he discussed Wiener's ideas
as follows:
Professor Wiener solves the riddle of old American civilizations with an
Arabico-Mandingo lexicon and derives everything of importance in the
New World from the highly civilized coasts of Gambia and Sierra Leone.
From brightest Africa came the principal American food plants, the Mayan
calendar and the Mexican religion. . . . The full splendor of his disarticula
tion is demonstrated in several books. One, freshly off the press, has 1 10
colored plates, and 16 plates in black and white, a veritable monument to misguided enthusiasm. ... It may be added that Professor Wiener swarms
his Negroes across the Atlantic in no less than fifty voyages before Colum
bus (241).
Professor Leo Wiener's three-volume work on Africa and the discovery of America was amply documented, but he failed to get a fair
hearing. His auditors had already decided that Africans were a bunch
of benighted savages, a people who had never entered the stream of his
tory. In a finat-chapter of conclusions, Wiener gave us the gist of his
three volumes, in the following words:
The presence of Negroes with their trading masters in America before Columbus is proved by the representation of Negroes in American sculp
ture and design, by the occurrence of a black nation at Darien early in the
XVlth century, but more specifically, by Columbus' emphatic reference to
Negro traders from Guinea, who trafficked in a gold alloy, guanin, of pre
cisely the same composition and bearing the same name as frequently re
ferred to by early writers in Africa. . . .
There were several foci from which the Negro traders spread in the two Americas. The eastern part of South America, where the Caribs are men
tioned, seems to have been reached by them from the West Indies. Another
stream, possibly from the same focus, radiated to the north along roads
marked by the presence of mounds, and reached as far as Canada. The chief
cultural influence was exerted by a Negro colony in Mexico, most likely from Teotinhuacan and Tuxtla, who may have been instrumental in estab



ANCIENT AMERICA 287
lishing the City of Mexico. From here, their influence pervaded the neigh
boring tribes and ultimately, directly or indirectly, reached Peru. That the
Negro civilization was carried chiefly by the trader is proved not only by
Columbus' specific reference, but also by the presence of the African merchant, the tangoman as Tiangizman in Mexico, hence the Aztec Tiangiz
(market), and by the universality of the blue and white shell-money from
Canada to La Plata, and the use of shells in the Peru-Guatemala trade.
(272, Vol. Ill, p. 365)
The reader has probably seen pictures of the huge stone heads show
ing African facial features, which have been unearthed in Mexico fre
quently in the recent past. The first of these stone heads was reported
by J. M. Melgar in 1869. This cabeza colosal, as the Mexicans called it,
was half buried, but enough of it was visible so that observers noted its
Ethiopian features, and the presence of a headdress resembling a foot
ball helmet. The site of this ancient monument was in the wilds of
Veracruz, not far from the Gulf of Mexico. Early in 1938, Dr. Matthew W. Stirling, Director of the Bureau of American Ethnology, a
branch of the Smithsonian Institution, of Washington, D.C., visited
the forests of Veracruz and took a look at the colossal head. The an
cient relic was located near the village of Tres Zapotes, and after a pre
liminary survey, Dr. Stirling decided that the area looked promising as
a site for archaeological exploration. On returning to Washington, he
organized an expedition sponsored jointly by the Smithsonian Institu
tion and the National Geographic Society. This expedition later in the
same year proceeded to Tres Zapotes and began unearthing the great
stone head. It turned out to be a solid block of basalt, six feet high and
eighteen feet in circumference, with its base attached to a platform of
crudely hewn stone. A member of Stirling's staff noticed the African
features of the stone face and the cap on the head resembling the train
ing headgear of a pugilist, and gave the cabeza colosal the nickname Joe
Louis. In exploring the area around the site, the expedition found a
stone containing a date. After deciphering the inscription, which was
in the dot and bar notation similar to that in use among the ancient
Mayans of Yucatan, they were able to arrive at a date which in our cal
endar would be November 4, 291 B.C., the earliest date found in the
New World up to that time. In 1939, Dr. Stirling moved his party to the island of La Venta, near
the coast of the Gulf of Mexico, in the state of Tabasco. A stone head
had been reported at that location by a Tulane University expedition in
1925, but it had not been excavated. Stirling's group, looking for one



288 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
head, had the good fortune of finding five. One stone head had a circumference of twenty-two feet and an estimated weight of at least
twenty tons. All these relics closely resembled the cabeza colosal of Tres
Zapotes.
In 1946, Dr. Stirling organized another headhunting expedition.
This time, the site chosen was the San Lorenzo Plateau, in the south
eastern part of Veracruz. At this spot, five more of the giant stone
heads were uncovered. The prize find of the lot was a stone head of
about thirty tons in weight, with a mouth over three feet wide and eyes
measuring almost two feet across; and it was appropriately labeled El
Rey (the king).
In the spring of 1967, another party of archaeologists, led by Profes
sor Michael D. Coe of Yale University, enjoyed a successful season,
marked by rewarding finds, such as another huge basalt head with the
typical African facial features. A reproduction of this particular stone
head was featured on the cover of Science Digest for September, 1967.
These relics, dug up by the expeditions led by Stirling and Coe, were
all remains of the ancient Olmec culture. This early American civiliza
tion flourished in the states of Veracruz, Tabasco, and Chiapas, and
seems to have been the chief source of the later Maya, Aztec, Toltec,
and Zapotec cultures. There was an interesting and instructive article
in Science Digest, from which we quote the following passage:
The first Olmec artifact to attract archaeologists turned up in a plowed field in 1902 not far from the Bay of Campeche in the Gulf of Mexico.
Sculpted in pale green jadette was a beautifully carved, fat, bald-headed In
dian priest about eight inches high. Undecipherable glyphs were incised on its stomach. Also on its stomach was a Mayan date. The date corresponded to 98 B.C., well before any known Maya civilization and in the wrong area of Mexico for Maya.
The long-count calendar, attributable at that time to the Maya, is a more
accurate calendar than the one we use today. It begins, for unknown reasons, in the year 3113 B.C. Closely correlated to astronomical observations, it can be read precisely for any day and year in the intermediate period and
transcribed into a date in terms of our calendar. It is a masterpiece of mathematical and astronomical knowledge, and it was the Olmecs, not the Maya, who developed it. [The Olmecs are sometimes called the People of the Jaguar, because in their numerous works of art, the jaguar motif is
prominent, and it is believed that the jaguar was an object of worship by the Olmecs.] (204, pp. 8-9)
The American scholar, William H. Prescott, when writing his
histories of the conquests of Mexico and Peru, speculated on the origin



ANCIENT AMERICA 289
of early American cultures. He had heard about the Olmecs, but con
sidered them as mythical. The pyramids of Teotihaucan were very an
cient, surmised Prescott, and he asked: "Who were the builders? Were
they the mythical Olmecs, whose history is lost in the mists of legend
and fable, or the peace loving and industrious Toltecs, about whom we
have scarcely more definite information? What happened to the tribes
that built them?" The pioneer Americanist, Professor Augustus Le
Plongeon, who studied Mayan ruins in Yucatan, published his Maya
Archaeology in 1885. In this work he recorded the tradition of an an
cient Olmec culture, which had preceded that of the Toltecs, and the
belief that these ancient people had come from the east in ships in order
to settle in the New World. Had LePlongeon had an open mind, he might have concluded that the Olmecs had come from Africa; but to
him, this would have been unthinkable; so he, of course, traced them
back to the lost continent of Atlantis.
The Olmec culture seems to have come to a sudden end about the
year 600 of our era. How this came about has been explained as fol
lows:
Judging by the conditions found by the archaeologists, it would appear
that at that time, the flatnosed people were destroyed by an alien race who
not only wiped out the others but devoted an enormous amount of time and
energy to defacing and destroying their idols, stonework, monuments and
anything else they could do away with. It would seem probable these in
vaders were from Palenque, for in the Temple of the Jaguars, there is a stucco panel showing the same type of figure wearing the Phrygian cap ex
actly like that shown on the pottery portrait head from San Lorenzo. A
similar portrait appears on a ceremonial axe of Totonac origin. Also the
dates agree. Dr. Stirling places the end of the flatnosed people's culture as
about 600 A.D. and archaeologists have dated the Palenque remains as
about 600 A.D. also. Whatever the truth may be, there is no question that the most ancient of
the Mexican higher cultures was that of the fat-faced, thick-lipped, flat
nosed people of the La Venta area in the State of Tabasco. (257, p. 100)
The African influence in ancient America is even now denied by
many, but the evidence is quite clear. This is supported by the fol
lowing remarks by a well-known explorer:
Hitherto, ethnologists imagined that Negroes appeared in the New World only during our own epoch, when they were imported as slaves; but the most recent researches demonstrate that they first came to America in a



290 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
period very remote. . . . I have seen a statuette of a Negro in the archaeological collection of Mr. Ernesto Franco in Quito (Ecuador): according to
the opinion of local archaeologists, this statuette is at least 20,000 years old. The autochthonous black races in America were either gradually mixed with the Indian ones, or became extinct, but in a very remote time, Negroes or Negroids were numerous in the New World. (14, p. 40)
Columbus has been called the discoverer of America, but he himself
made no such claim. In the words of a recent investigator:
Proof of this is evidenced by the fact that Columbus was informed by some men, when he stopped at the Cape Verde Islands off the coast of Af
rica, that Negroes had been known to set out into the Atlantic from the Guinea coast in canoes loaded with merchandise and steering toward the west. The same Christopher Columbus was further informed by the Indians of Hispaniola when he arrived in the West Indies that they had been
able to obtain gold from black men who had come from across the sea from the south and southeast. ... It must also be added that Amerigo Vespucci, on his voyage to the Americas, witnessed these same black men out in the
Atlantic returning to Africa. (142, p. 6)
In an earlier chapter, we referred to the four compass gods of the an
cient Egyptians. They were depicted as follows: Amset, who was Human-Headed; Hapi, who was Ape-Headed; Tuamutef who was Jackal-Headed; Gebhsennuf, who was Hawk-Headed.
These deities were also connected with certain utensils known as
Canopic Jars. For an explanation of this connection, we refer to Every
man's Dictionary of Non-Classical Mythology. Under the heading
"Canopic Jars," we read:
In the process of embalming as practiced in Egypt, the intestines and other internal organs were removed, cleaned, wrapped in linen with pow
dered spices, etc., and placed in jars called Canopic Jars. The name is said to have arisen from the old legend whereby Canopus, the pilot of Menelaus, was buried at Canopus, and there worshiped in the shape of a jar with small
feet, thin neck, swollen body and a round back. As far back as the sixth dy
nasty, these jars were dedicated to the four cardinal points, and from about the seventeenth dynasty they bore the heads of the sons of Horus (or of Osiris): Amset, Hapi, Duamutef, and Qebhsneuf, who also became gods of
the cardinal points, protecting the liver, lungs, stomach, and intestines respectively. Traces of a similar custom have been observed in America. For
details see Bacabs.
In the same dictionary, we read that the Bacabs were: "The Canopic gods of the Mayas, who were also the supporters of



ANCIENT AMERICA 291
the four corners of the earth. Their names, their cardinal points, and
their symbolic colors were as follows: Mulac, north, white; Cauac,
south, red; Kan, east, yellow; lx, west, black." (242)
In discussing the ancient Mayan religious rites, Professor Paul Radin
wrote: "The earth was held in place by four deities, the four cardinal
points. With each cardinal point was associated a definite color. It is
difficult to overestimate the significance of these deities. Indian ceremo
nialism is almost unthinkable without them." (193, p. 66) An almost
identical symbolic complex existed among the Aztecs, where the com
pass-point gods were called the four Sky-bearers. The following accu
rate description we owe to Lewis Spence:
The Mexicans divided the universe into 4 regions, governed by 4 gods,
who were supposed to uphold the skies at the 4 directions of the compass.
These compass directions were associated with various colors, red, white,
black, yellow. . . . These sky bearers were supposed to be spirits or gods of
the stars. . . .
They were usually symbolized by insects, particularly scorpions, spiders
and bees. They were also associated with the only 4 days on which the cal
endar could begin, Acatl, Tecpatl, Calli and Tochtli. (237, p. 48)
An American archaeologist, Professor J. Eric Thompson, wrote a
scholarly essay, "Sky Bearers, Colors, and Directions in Maya and
Mexican Religions." For comparison he listed the Mayan and Mexican
compass gods, and the two lists are reproduced below:
Names Colors Directions
MAYA Kan Red East
Muluc White North lx Black West Cauac Yellow South MEXICAN Acatl Red? East Tecpatl White North Calli Black? West Tochtli Yellow South
The divinities are described as: "Gods who bore the weight of the
heavens and were at the same time associated with world directions,
and by extension, with their respective colors, are known to have
played an important role in Maya and Mexican religious belief and cer
emony." (246, p. 211)

The symbolic similarities of the sky gods of both the Maya and
Mexican systems point clearly to a common origin, as the tabulation
below displays.
MAYA: 4 Sky Bearers
1 Created before the sun or the flood;
2. Associated with directions;
3. Associated with colors;
4. Associated with the wind;
5. Associated with the rain;
6. Patrons of agriculture;
7. Associated with shells, turtles, spiders, and possibly bees.
MEXICANS: 4 Sky Bearers
1. Created before the sun, and after the flood;
2. Associated with directions;
3. Associated with colors;
4. Associated with the wind;
5. Associated with the rain;
6. ? ? ?
7. Associated with shells, turtles, spiders, and bees.
Among mythological and ritualistic ideas common to both the
Mayans and the Mexicans, we note the similarities as listed in the table
below.
MYTHOLOGY AND RITUAL COMMON
TO MAYA AND MEXICANS
1. The Sacred Almanac Complex; 2. An eighteen-month calendar and five unlucky days;
3. Nine Lords of the Night;
4. Thirteen heavens; 5. The Sun God-Moon Goddess Myth;
6. Maize taken by ants from a rock;
7. Maize associated with colors;
8. Maize connected with crop fertilization; 9. The Kukulkan Complex;
10. Human sacrifice;
11. Arrow Sacrifice;
12. The Venus Calendar;



ANCIENT AMERICA 293
13. Hunting God and Morning Star;
14. Witchcraft initiates swallowed by a snake.
Professor Thompson, alluding to the similarities listed above, con
cluded: 'in many cases, if not most cases, I believe the ideas were
passed on to both Maya and Mexicans from a mutual cultural ances
tor." (246, p. 240) The mutual cultural ancestor was not named by
Professor J. Eric Thompson. Professor Leo Wiener, on the other hand,
felt certain that the mutual cultural ancestor could be traced back to
Africa. Wiener contended that groups of nine gods were frequently
mentioned in the Pyramid Texts of ancient Egypt, and refers the
reader to Sir Wallis Budge's Gods of the Egyptians. (32, Vol. I, p. 85
fT.) He then noted: "In America, we have the same Nine Lords of the
Night frequently recorded in Mexico." (273, p. 130) According to
Professor Wiener, the nine Lords of the Night were known to the
Mossi of the Western Sudan, who presumably had a hand in transmit
ting the information to the New World. "The 9 Lords of the Night,"
we are further informed, "led to the 13 Lords of the Day, which either
represented the 1 3 hours of the day, the 1 3 heavens or, possibly, the 1
months." (273, p. 130) The Sky Bearers of Ancient America, Dr. Churchward thought,
could be traced back to the four sons of Horus of the ancient Egyptian
Pantheon. He found the same symbolism in the western Sudan. "The
people of Bavili, Bini, and Yorba, West Africa, have the same under
the names of (1) Ibara, (2) Edi, (3) Oyekun, (4) Oz-Be, and build
these names, with Ifi, the Son of God—i.e. Horus—into the walls of
their houses." (48, p. 320)
Gerald Massey, in the first volume of The Natural Genesis, has a
chapter on the Typology of the Four Quarters, and he traces this sym
bolism from Egypt to all parts of the Old and New Worlds. Massey, in
his day, was regarded as something of a visionary. Today, we have
scholars adopting his ideas, even though most of them never took the
trouble to read any of his works. As this is being written, we have be
fore us a book entitled Lord of the Four Quarters by John Weir Perry,
Professor of Psychiatry at the University of California, and Lecturer at
the C. G. Jung Institute. According to the publisher of Professor Per
ry's book: "These givers of life, in their simultaneous sacred and
human forms, were a universal experience of the archaic world. Here a
Jungian psychiatrist traces their appearance throughout legend from



294 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the Nile to the New World, revealing the source of Jung's racial arche
type, which survives in the psyche of contemporary man." Starting
with the four sons of Horus in Egypt, Massey showed that the symbols
of these sky gods in Egypt were: (1) Man, (2) Ape, (3) Jackal, (4) Hawk.
In the Biblical Book of Ezekiel, Chapter I, we read of the four Faces
of the Celestial Beasts, which were: (1) Man, (2) Lion, (3) Ox, (4)
Eagle.
In the Book of Revelations of St. John, Chapter IV, Verse 7, the
symbolism of the four quarter signs of Zodiac were pictured as follows:
(1) Lion, (2) Calf, (3) Man, (4) Bird.
Traveling on to India, we have a similar symbolism, where the Lords
of the four quarters are represented by the following zootypes: (1)
Cow, (2) Ape, (3) Eagle, (4) Serpent.
Massey pointed out: "These four are universal. They belong to the
first circle that was quartered according to the cardinal points." (168,
Vol. I, p. 407)
In the Hindu mythology, we read of Mount Meru, situated at the
North Pole. This mythical mountain had four sides, each of a different
color, and each color was coupled with a point of the compass, as fol
lows: East—White, South—Yellow, West—Black, North—Red.
Massey connects the colors of the four faces of Mount Meru with
the mythology of the Ages of Metals. The ages and the corresponding
colors are: Golden Age—Yellow, Silver Age—White, Copper or
Brass Age—Red, Iron Age—Black. (See 168, Vol. I, p. 412.)
The zodiac, which we have discussed in an earlier chapter, seems to
have begun with the four quarter constellations and their principal
stars, and later was expanded from four signs to twelve. The four origi
nal signs and their stars were:
Stars Constellations
Corleonis IjCO
Antares Scorpio
Formalhaut Aquarius
Aldebaran Taurus
(For additional data, the reader might consult Massey's The Natural
Genesis, Vol. II, p. 207, and Creation Records Discovered in Egypt, pp.
136-39, by George St. Clair [219].)



ANCIENT AMERICA 295
Dupuis and Volney traced the earliest system of astronomy back to
the ancient Ethiopians. Massey thought Egypt a more likely source;
but there seems to be no doubt of the common origin of all the ancient
systems. In the words of Gerald Massey: "No serious student of the
subject can doubt that the Arabic, Chaldean, Hindu, Chinese and
Greek systems of ancient astronomy were derived from a common
source. Egypt alone is known to be old enough to account for that unity
of origin; and the chief types constellated in the heavens are of Egyp
tian origin and belong to the Kamite- mythology." (168, Vol. II, p.
214)
Among the ancient Chinese, the four spirits of the four quarters
were: (1) Black Warrior—North, (2) White Tiger—West, (3) Red
Bird—South, (4) Blue Dragon—East. (See 168, Vol. I, p. 410.)
Lord Raglan, in an anthropological study of great merit, The Temple
and the House, has a chapter on the four quarters, wherein the fol
lowing opinions were expressed: "The legendary emperors of China
were said to have controlled the four quarters of heaven. They were
sometimes paired with four spirits, symbolized by the White Tiger of
the West, the Green Dragon of the East, the Red Bird of the South, and
the Dark Warrior of the North." (197, p. 163)
We must now say something about the strange calendar systems of
Ancient America, for they, too, display a kinship with similar com
plexes in the Old World. The ancient Maya devised a very accurate
calendar, and their mode of time measurement was passed on to the Za
potecs and Aztecs, where it survived in a somewhat simplified form.
The Mayan calendar, like that of the Aztecs of Mexico and the Incas of
Peru, was originally a moon calendar; that is to say, it was based on the
lunar year. Beginning with a lunar year of 12 months, each of 30 days'
duration, the Mayan astronomers later introduced a sacred calendar
called the Tzolkin, which was made up of 1 3 months, each consisting
of 20 days, making a total of 260 days for the year. The Tzolkin Cal
endar, the Tonalamatl of the Aztecs, was made up of a series of 20-day
signs, combined with the numbers 1 to 13, inclusive. The two series in
terlocked like two wheels, one with 13 cogs, the other with 20. The
smaller number wheel made 20 revolutions, while the larger day wheel
made 13 revolutions; after which the number-cog and the name-cog
were again lined up the same as at the start of the operation. Thus a
day bearing the same name and the same number would recur after
13 x 20, or 260 days. This lunar calendar was called by the Aztecs the



296 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Tonalamatl, or the Book of the Good and Bad Days. A copy of this calendar, as reconstructed by Lewis Spence, is given below:
Number Name Sign Presiding God
Tonacatecutli
Quetzalcoatl
Tepeyollotl Ueuecoyotl
Chalchihuitlicue
Teciztecatl
Tlaloc Mayauel Xiuhtecutli
Mictlantecutli
Xochipilli
Patecatl Tezcatlipoca
Tlazolteotl Xipe
Itzpaplotl
Xolotl Tezcatlipoca
Chantico Xochiquetzal
We gladly let Lewis Spence explain the high points of this ancient
calendar. The outlandish names are for the most part unpronounceable
in the English language, but let us pay attention to Spence and we can
not fail to get the gist of the tonalamatl:
It will be seen from this list that the fourteenth day-sign takes the number 1 again. Under this arrangement, each day-sign has a number which does not recur in connection with that sign again for a space of 260 days, so that each day-sign, if taken along with its number, has reference to a sepa
rate day in the calendar. Thus the first day, Cipactli, is in its first occurrence
/ Cipactli; in its second, 8 Cipactli; in its third, 2 Cipactli; in its fourth, 9 Ci
pactli; and so forth. No day in the tonalamatl was described merely as Cipactli, Coatl, or Calli, its name being incomplete without the number in the sequence, just as we would not say Thursday in October instead of Thursday the 7th of October, or Thursday, the 28th of October. It was called ce
cipactli, one crocodile, or ome coatl, two snake, as the case might be.
Each of the 20 tonalamatl divisions or weeks of 1 3 days each, as they are wrongly but usefully called, had also a presiding god of its own. (237, pp. 35-36)
1 Cipactli Crocodile
2 Eecatl Wind
3 Calli House
4 Cuetzpallin Lizard 5 Coatl Serpent
6 Miquiztli Death's head
7 Mazatl Deer
8 Tochtli Rabbit 9 Atl Water
10 Itzcuintli Dog
11 Ozomatli Monkey
12 Malinalli Grass
13 Acatl Reed
1 Ocelotl Ocelot
2 Quauhtli Eagle 3 Cozcaquauhtli Vulture 4 Ollin Motion 5 Tecpatl Flint-Knife
6 Quiauitl Rain
