ANCIENT AMERICA 297
The Maya devised another calendar, called the Haab, which con
sisted of 18 months, each of 20 days, giving a total of 360 days. To this
was added 5 days, called Uayeb, which corrected the year to 365 days.
Silverberg has noted: "The actual year is about 365 days, 6 hours long,
and the Mayas, aware of this, periodically adjusted the calendar to ac
count for this, just as we do with leap years. But the Maya calendar was
even more precise than ours, by a matter of some seconds a year."
(228, pp. 120-21) The Aztecs of Mexico adopted the improved Mayan solar calendar,
and merged it with their tonalamatl. In other words, their calendar was
a combination of both the Tzolkin and the Haab. For an explanation of
how this was done, we again turn to Lewis Spence:
Now this tonalamatl, or moon calendar naturally lost its significance as a
time count when a solar calendar was adopted. . . . But instead of adopting
the solar calendar and casting the old moon calendar aside, the Mexicans
combined both, the tonalamatl contributing the names of the days and the
solar calendar the divisions or months of the year, in which the days found
positions. The two calendars thus ran side by side, each recommencing
whenever it reached its own limits. ... A specific year was known by the
tonalamatl sign with which it began. As there were 20 day signs, and as 5
(the least common multiple of 365 and 20) goes into 20 exactly 4 times, the
year could begin with one of four signs only. These were Acatl, Tecpatl,
Calli and Tochtli. (237, p. 36)
This combination lunar and solar calendar was called the Calendar
Round. To us it seems cumbersome, but it certainly was ingenious, for it worked smoothly. The day-numbers associated with the day-names making up the ritual year were capable of running a gamut of 13
changes; and, as a result, a particular day with a particular number can
occupy a particular month position only in every 13 X 4 or 52 years;
which comes to a total of 18,980 days. We learn from Professor Radin:
"This period the Mayas called the Calendar-Round, and it is of vast im
portance both among the Maya and the Aztecs in connection with
their astronomy and their whole ceremonial life." (193, p. 74) The Mayan astronomers were not satisfied with the Tzolkin, the
Haab, and the Calendar-Round; for they also constructed a Venus Cal
endar. This calendar, based on the phases of the planet Venus, was a
triumph of human ingenuity. The planet Venus has a year of 584 days,
and the four phases of the same were Morning Star (236 days), Supe
rior Conjunction (90 days), Evening Star (250 days), and Inferior



298 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Conjunction (90 days). The equivalence in length of eight solar years
of 365 days each, and of five Venus years of 584 days each was recog
nized and accurately calculated; and we have good reason to believe
that a discrepancy of eight hundredths (0.08) of a day between the
mean Venus year and the true Venus year was corrected by a marginal
subtraction of two days after a period of 25 revolutions of that planet.
The Zapotecs utilized a ritual calendar of 260 days, which was prob
ably derived from the Maya. In addition, they tied it in with the astral
symbolism of the sky gods of the compass directions. As Radin ob
served: "In one of the old codices, they are represented as follows: The
first, the east, is a figure wearing as a helmet-mask an alligator's head;
he is a good and fruitful deity. The second, the north, has as his mask a
death's head; he signifies drought and death. The third, the west, wears
the head of an unknown animal. The fourth figure, the south, wears
the vulture's head. There is a fifth figure representing the center. His
colors are those of the gods of the night-heaven and twilight and the
symbols accompanying him refer to war." (193, p. 158) The ancient Maya seemed to have a profound knowledge of mathe
matics, and if their culture was an offshoot of the early Olmecs, then
we are led to look to Africa as a source of this knowledge. Professor
Wiener did just this, and his labors were not fruitless. For he has in
formed us: "We get a good insight into Mayan mathematics by
studying the corresponding numeration in Guinea." (273, p. 144) Pro
fessor Wiener gives us an insight into this by an examination of the
number system of the Bambaras of Guinea, in West Africa, relating it
to the Maya system, as follows:
It is obvious that in the vigesimal count they called 5 a heap, and 4 heaps
became the complete count, hence when the Mayas took over the Negro
numeration, tu was used for both 5 and 20. Indeed, we have the Maya hut,
complete, all, hence there can be little doubt that Maya hotuy 5, was originally the first complete heap, hence 5, and this produced ho, 5. From Ho-tu
baq, 500, it is clear that tu also meant 20, that is the complete heap, and here
we can show that the Mexicans got their numeration, as regard the larger numbers, entirely from the Mandingos, and bequeathed it to the Mayas.
(273, p. 145)
Students of Amerindian mythology are familiar with a black god of
merchants. Professor Wiener, in studying the Codex Peresianus,
traced this deity back to his African source. "Here we have," he con
cluded, "the complete story of the Negro who crossed the ocean as a



ANCIENT AMERICA 299
merchant and brought the art of extracting pulque from the agave plant
and distilling it on fire, and became the god of the travelling merchants
and was ultimately merged with Cak." (273, p. 105)
We are not trying to show that the ancient cultures of the New
World were entirely of African origin. There were other influences;
and we shall take due notice of them. In the customs, traditions, and
myths of ancient America we constantly meet with parallels that are so
alike that they cannot be due to coincidence or chance. Mummification
was widely practiced in ancient Mexico and Peru; and, according to
Sir Grafton Elliot Smith, this making of mummies possessed Egyptian
elements of associated ritual, such as the use of incense and libations,
and accompanying beliefs concerning the wanderings of the soul in the
underworld. Along with these we have corroborative details indicating
the influence of Ethiopia, Babylonia, India, Indonesia, China, Japan,
and Oceania on the various features of the process as practiced in the New World.
The Aztecs of old related a myth of a war in heaven, in which the
rebel angels met with defeat. They also held that the first man was
made of clay, and that the earliest woman was fashioned from a bone of
the first man. The Aztec Eve was pictured on the ancient Mexican
monuments as conversing with a big male serpent, who was, of course,
the old Devil in disguise. These same people had a tradition of the uni
versal Deluge. "There were giants in the earth in those days." (Gene
sis, VI, 4) And the Mexican Noah was one of them. He and six other
giants built an ark, which enabled them to survive a great flood. After
the waters had subsided, birds were sent out to search for dry land, and
the ark landed on a mountain peak. One of the giants present in the ark
was Xelhua the Architect who, in later days, planned a pyramid tall
enough to reach up to the very windows of heaven. As the work on
this edifice got under way and the structure approached the clouds, the
gods became angry and hurled fire down on the pyramid, toppling it
over and killing many of the workmen. The work could nor be re
sumed, because the gods caused each family engaging in the enterprise
to start speaking a strange new language, which the other could in no
way comprehend. The ruins of this pyramid at Cholula were shown to
Baron von Humboldt when that celebrated explorer visited Mexico,
and are described by him in his American Researches. The Aztecs also
believed in the doctrine of the Trinity; and the members of this triad
were (1) Yzona, the Father, (2) Bacab, the Son, and (3) Echiah, the



300 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
Holy Ghost. Bacab, or Quetzalcoatl, was the Aztec Christ, and he was
born of the Virgin Sochiquetzal. Like the Christian Savior, he was
tempted, and fasted for forty days. He was also a crucified savior, for
he is shown in the Borgian MS as on a cross with nail marks on his
hands and feet. After the crucifixion, he arose from the dead, after de
scending into hell for a period of three days and three nights. He was
represented as a man of black complexion; and thus falls into line with
other black savior gods, such as Osiris, Horus, Krishna, and Jesus
Christ. The Mexicans were expecting the Second Advent of Quetzal
coatl when the Spaniards invaded the country in the sixteenth century.
In 1894, Sir Edward B. Tylor, pioneer English anthropologist, ad
dressed a remarkable communication to the British Association for the
Advancement of Science, the title of which was "On the Diffusion of
Mythical Beliefs as Evidence of the History of Culture." The close
parallels between certain religious ideas common to the Mexican Az
tecs and the Chinese Buddhists are shown in the following extracts
from the pen of Professor Tylor
In the religion of Old Mexico, four great scenes in the journey of the
soul in the land of the dead are mentioned by early Spanish writers after the
conquest, and are depicted in a group in the Aztec picture writing known
as the Vatican Codex. The four scenes are first, the crossing of a river; sec
ond, the fearful passage of the soul between the two mountains which clash
together; third, the soul's climbing up the mountain set with sharp obsidian
knives; fourth, the dangers of the wind carrying such knives in its blast.
The Mexican ^pictures of these four scenes were compared with more or
less closely corresponding pictures representing scenes from the Buddhist
hells or purgatories as depicted on Japanese temple scrolls. Here, first, the
river of death is shown, where the souls wade across; second, the souls have
to pass between two huge iron mountains, which are pushed together by
two demons; third, the guilty souls climb the mountain of knives, whose
blades cut their hands and feet; fourth, fierce blasts of wind drive against
their lacerated forms, the blades of knives flying through the air. It was ar
gued that the appearance of analogues so close and complex of Buddhist ideas in Mexico constituted a correspondence of so high an order as to pre
clude any explanation except direct transmission from one religion to an
other.
It has been urged by certain anthropologists of the evolutionary
school that no direct contact between Chinese Buddhism and the reli
gion of ancient Mexico can be demonstrated; nor is it necessary to as
sume such contact to explain the close similarities in these beliefs. This



ANCIENT AMERICA 301
school of thought holds that, since both the Amerindians and the Chinese are Mongoloids, they both possess a Mongol type of mind, and
thus tend to think in a similar manner. This contention is quite absurd,
being refuted by the cold hard facts of the case; for we know that the
Chinese got many of these ideas from India, and the Indians were not
Mongoloids.



CHAPT E R E I G H T E E
The Evolution of Civilization:
Summary and Conclusions
MANY readers of this chapter will find it difficult, or perhaps even im
possible, to give assent to some of its implications and conclusions. This
is understandable, since nearly all the so-called world histories and
histories of civilization, so .popular in contemporary academic circles,
are based mainly on what is known as European civilization. This spe
cies of parochialism gives a false picture of human history; and few stu
dents become aware of the fact that European civilization, speaking his
torically, is a product of the recent past, and that European culture was
not indigenous, but was derived from the older civilizations of Africa
and Asia. Th^ Western World takes great pride in its accomplish
ments in the .ields of technology and science, and conveniently forgets
that Christian-Europe wallowed in the savagery of the Dark Ages until
the arts and sciences of the Moors were introduced into that backward
land, mainly by Jewish scholars, merchants, and physicians. A group of
Jewish scholars settled in England under the protection of William of
Normandy, and established a school of science at Oxford; and it was at
this school that Roger Bacon learned the Arabic language and studied
Moorish science. As Dr. Robert Briffault has stated: "The medical
school of Montpellier was founded on the pattern of that of Cordova
under Jewish doctors. The example was imitated at Padua, and later at
Pisa, where together with the Canons of Avicenna (Ibn Sina), and the
Surgery of Abul-Kasim, which until the seventeenth century remained
the textbooks of medical science throughout Europe, were taught the
mathematics and astronomy of the Moors. Those were the nurseries
which were one day to bring forth Fallopius, Vesalius, Cardan, Har
vey, Galileo." (20, p. 152)
302



SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS 303
The first civilized Europeans were the Greeks, who were largely
civilized by the Egyptians, who were Africans. The Romans absorbed
some of the Greek culture, and then lost it at the onset of the Dark
Ages. The recivilization of Europe was brought about mainly by the
Moors, also a people of African origin. The Robber Barons of Europe
were clever enough to adopt the new civilization and use it for taking
over the world. They invaded the New World, destroyed its cultures,
enslaved and murdered its peoples, and opened up new lands (stolen
from the inoffensive owners thereof), and then appropriated the natural
resources of these new lands for the beriefit of the predatory ruling
classes of Europe. New territories were opened up to the hungry peo
ples of Europe, and helpless Africans were kidnapped into slavery so as
to form a servile labor force for the enrichment of the bandits of Eu
rope. The rulers of Europe sent out their emissaries, who stole entire
continents, appropriated the land, and slaughtered and reduced to
bondage the helpless dwellers of those unhappy lands. Due to their pri
ority in the Industrial Revolution, the Europeans for a while dominated
the world; but a series of revolutions and wars finally led to a decline of
this European supremacy. The French Revolution resulted in an up
surge of the spirit of nationalism that led to the Napoleonic Wars, and
ultimately turned Europe into an armed camp. World War I in 1914
was a turning point; for the Europeans had formed a habit of murder
ing, robbing, and oppressing the downtrodden colonials of Africa, Asia,
and the Americas; but now they turned on each other. Europeans slew
each other by the millions and then drafted the so-called inferior races
to help them murder their own blood brothers. This carnage brought
on the Russian Revolution, which established Communism as a force to
be reckoned with. A world-wide economic depression prepared the
ground for a second world war. The so-called democracies of Europe
and America decided to pit the Nazism of Hitler against the Commu
nism of Stalin. Hitler refused to cooperate, so the so-called free world
had to destroy Hitler and, at the same time, they were forced to aid
and abet Stalinism. World War I brought on a Communist Revolu
tion; World War II not only helped to consolidate this totalitarianism,
but established it on a solid foundation in China. The Europeans are
trying to hang on to what is left of their days of greatness, but the age
of European dominance in the world is on the decline and there is no
sign that this trend will ever be reversed.
The one good thing the Europeans did was to cultivate the study of



304 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
the sciences after they had been indoctrinated by the Moors. European
science was almost strangled in its cradle, for both church and state
were hostile to it; but the invention of the printing press saved the day
for science. Knowledge could now be diffused so widely and with such
great facility that no effective censorship was possible. Modern science
survived the Inquisition, the Wars of Religion, and the epidemic of
witchcraft, and reached its majority in the Age of the Enlightenment,
toward the end of the eighteenth century. At that time, the transition
from the medieval to the modern world was completed. The role of the
French thinkers of the Age of Reason in that transition has been admi
rably presented by Briffault:
The French 1 8th century is one of the grand climacterics of the history
of human growth. All the seeds which had been germinating in Europe
since the twelfth century ripened then into fruit; a new era began, in its
significance one of the epochs of most concentrated glory in the evolution
of the race. ... As in seventeenth century England, science expanded in
eighteenth century France, widely and eagerly cultivated, popularized in
crowded lecture rooms and was there shaped for the first time into that or
ganized body of knowledge and systematized enquiry which was to bear
immediate fruit in the conquests of the nineteenth century. ... It was by
those men, Bayle, Montesquieu, Voltaire, Diderot, D'Alembert, Volney,
Holbach, Condorcet, and their contemporaries who cast aside all conven
tional formulas, resolved to think for themselves, and what is more, to speak
out boldly what they thought, to own no other sanction or criterion than
rational thought, that the world has been transformed. Behind them and
around them stood medievalism in all its ignorance and darkness and tyr
anny over life and mind, . . . after them is a changed world, the modern
world. It was those men who threw open the portals from the one into the
other. (20, pp. 224-25)
Writers of textbooks on the history of civilization usually begin their
accounts with either Egypt or Babylonia, and dismiss all traditions of
earlier cultures as idle speculations. Many very able students of ancient
affairs have dissented, and we think they have a case worth presenting.
As an example, we cite Lloyd P. Smith, an American scholar of the last
century.
Thus alike on the starlit plains of Chaldea [Mr. Smith noted], and in the
rainless atmosphere of Egypt, astronomy for ages became the one great oc
cupation of the learned; and such was their progress in it, that they ascer
tained the length of the year with nearly as much accuracy as we know it



SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS 305
ourselves. Even the precession of the equinoxes was to the Egyptian priests
a familiar fact. . . . The great pyramid of Cheops was built on lines ascer
tained by astronomical observations. It faces the four cardinal points, and
the tunnel which pierces its Northern slope is in reality a telescope forever
turned to that point of the heavens touched by the polar star at its lowest
declination. A crystal lens has lately been discovered on the site of Nine
veh, and Godfrey Higgins claims that even the telescope itself was known
to a very few of the Egyptian priests, and kept by them in private for the
use of those only who were initiated in the higher mysteries. (235, pp. 8-9)
Canon Rawlinson, in his famous Ancient Monarchies, stated: "There
is said to be distinct evidence that they (the ancient Mesopotamians)
observed the four satellites of Jupiter, and strong reason to believe that
they were acquainted likewise with the seven satellites of Saturn."
(200, Vol. II, p. 577)
The Italian astronomer, Eugene Antoniadi (1870-1924), held that
the priests of ancient Egypt possessed a secret scientific tradition which
was known only to the initiates of the mysteries, and since this knowl
edge was never published, the common people were ignorant of it.
Professor A. C. D. Crommelin (1865-1935), an English astronomer,
was of the opinion that the stars were first grouped into constellations
by the Egyptians about 4000 B.C. However, the contemporary French
astronomer, Pierre Rousseau, argues that this date is far too late. "In
fact," Rousseau observed, "the origin of the constellations is lost in the
mists of time. We find them among the Chinese and among the Su
merians, among the Egyptians and among the Incas, and it is notewor
thy that certain figures are the same everywhere. The Bear and the
giant Orion are recognized on the banks of the Nile as on those of the
Yellow River, so it is possible that this celestial typography sprang from
a common fund of knowledge, to which every people made its contri
bution." (215, p. 29) Rousseau's contentions seem to be confirmed by Or. Giorgio de
Santillana, Professor of the History and Philosophy of Science, in the
Massachusetts Institute of Technology. We are informed by De San
tillana:
We are indebted to Leo Frobenius and the German Historical school for
the theory of culture circles, . . . and if justice will be done, eventually to
the achievements of W. H. R. Rivers and the British School of DirTusion
ists. The point is this: that what we observe as primitive conditions are,
with very few exceptions . . . only what is left of the rise and fall of past



306 MAN, GOD, AND CIVILIZATION
higher cultures; what appeared to be a universal steady state of superstitions from which thought grows is only the common denominator to which
decaying civilizations run in the end. . . .
Once the possibility had been revived of a universal history of culture,
the road was opened to promising rediscoveries and new adventures: we are
able not only to put to good use the labors of great nineteenth-century
scholars like Boeckh, Ideler, Brugsch, and such of their eighteenth-century
forerunners as Charles Dupuis, but to recognize that only when fitted into
this rediscovered old-fashioned historical frame, could the amount of new
data presented to us by Thureau-Dangin, Kugler, Boll, and Seler be inter
preted and evaluated in a reasonable way. Thus the work of L. de Saussure
and Laufer, of Eisler and Gundel, after due critical revision, yield now re
turns beyond expectation. It is to be hoped that work in progress by Wer
ner, Stecchini and von Dechend will bring further light in this far reaching
enterprise. . . .
We have the Sumerian names of constellations and planets, surviving in
Babylonian cuneiform tablets, but we have reason to think that they were
inherited from unknown predecessors. As concerns the Egyptian names of
constellations, it is evident from the most ancient star lists that they were,
already then, no longer grasped in their original significance. So we con
clude that what looked to us like a point of departure is only a phase in a
line of thought which stretches back into the dim regions of predynastic
origins everywhere. But the extraordinary wealth of rites, tales, and tradi
tions found more or less uniformly around the world, in certain distinct
parts of Africa, in China, Polynesia, and Mexico, points to a time of great
migrations, and also to a center of diffusion . . . in the far back millinaries
where archaeological excavation yields only uncertain clues. We are, in
fact, beginning to understand that the vast protohistoric material of myths
and legends of gods and heroes founding cities, bringing culture, undertak
ing great voyages . . . can be deciphered as the technical language of the
yet unknown archaic astronomers who are also responsible for the naming
of the constellations. Surely, it implies a conscious intention, not mere idle
fancy, to read those figures out of the confusion of stars. Someone before
history must have blocked them out for reasons known to himself, and with
such an authority that they were repeated without question, substantially
the same from Mexico to Africa and Polynesia—and have remained with us
to this day. (220, pp. 10-13)
Ancient men may have had a far wider knowledge of mathematics than we have up to now credited them with. Dr. Alexander Thorn,
Professor of Engineering Science at Oxford University, studied the re
mains of hundreds of ancient stone temples, and found that four thou
sand years ago the ancient temple-builders utilized an accurate unit of



SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS ^07
length of 2.72 feer. All students familiar with higher mathematics are
aware of the fact that the transcendental number e (Epsilon), which is
the base of the natural system of logarithms, is approximately equal to 2.71828. Reduced to two decimal places, this becomes 2.72. This num
ber is important not only in higher mathematics, but also in the bank
ing business. Let us assume that we deposit one dollar in a bank and that the banker pays us 100 percent interest, and allows an infinite
number of interest periods annually. Under these conditions at the end
of one year the dollar would earn $1.72 in interest, so that the principal
($1) plus interest ($1.72) would come to $2.72. How did the ancient
temple-builders compute this number? We can only guess, for no record of how this discovery was made has come down the stream of his
tory to us.
The Ethiopian origin of the culture of ancient Egypt is now clear to
all students not blinded by anti-African racist obsessions. This was
clearly shown by Gerald Massey in his great work, Ancient Egypt:
"The Egyptian record when correctly read will tell us plainly that the
human birthplace was a land of the papyrus reed, the crocodile, and
hippopotamus, a land of great lakes in Karua, the Koloe of Ptolemy,
. . . that is in Equatoria, from whence the sacred river ran to brim the
valley of the Nile with plenty. The track of civilization with cities
springing in its footprints is seaward from the south, not upward from Lower Egypt, which was a swamp when Upper Egypt was already the African home of civilization. The Egyptian always gave priority to the
south over the delta in the north." (163, Vol. 1, p. 255)
Massey's conclusions have been confirmed by the researches of such able scholars as Harold P. Cooke, of Cambridge, who noted:
The worship and rites of Osiris may have passed into Egypt, I think,
from the neighboring state of Ethiopia. . . . Herodotus says in his history, that following the Nile to the South, you will come to a great city, Meroe.
. . . The people there have but two gods—that is Zeus and Dionysus or
Bacchus. . . . Now Zeus must be Amen or Amun. Dionysus is no doubt Osiris. These statements, if true, are significant. This singular paucity of
gods, may well argue an older tradition than any that Egypt could boast of.
(52)
A later student of the early history of mankind has made some saga
cious remarks on the culture of ancient Egypt, which we are pleased to
cite:
Until comparatively recently the classical view has been that the civilization of Egypt arose in the Delta, whether it was brought by Asiatic invaders



308 MAN, COD, AND CIVILIZATION
from across the isthmus of Suez. . . . As a matter of fact rhc early history of
Egypt was a write-up effected by the Heliopolitan priest-scribes, possibly
not before the fifth dynasty. . . . In outline the sequence of early Egyptian
history has been presented much as follows: the earliest political units were
towns governed by Mayors and worshipping local deities. Later the towns
developed into vermes or provinces each of which had a ruler identified with
the local god. The nomes of the Delta then coalesced into two kingdoms, while the nomes of Upper Egypt remained independent of one another,
.ater, one of the two Delta kingdoms absorbed the other while the nomes of Upper Egypt formed a separate kingdom. The god I lorus was the patron of
the northern kingdom and Set that of the southern realm. Then the
southern kingdom subjugated the northern and the capital of the whole land
was established at Heliopolis (near Cairo). Eventually this united kingdom
fell apart and finally the ruler of the southern kingdom, Men, Meni or
Menes, subjugated the northern realm and established his capital at the city
of the white walls, or Memphis. There is no archaeological evidence for all
these complicated happenings. There are no signs of any northern (i.e. Delta)
cultures older than those of southern Egypt. (23, pp. 120-21)
Again the conclusions reached by Gerald Massey many years ago
are being confirmed by later research. It is high time that this great
Egyptologist should get the recognition that he should have received in
his own lifetime.

"Nearly all the so-called world histories and histories of civilization, so popular in contemporary academic circles, are based mainly on what is known as European civilization," says John G. Jackson. "This species of parochialism gives a false picture of human history; and few students become aware of the fact that European Civilization, speaking historically, is a product of the recent past, and that European culture was not indigenous, but was derived from the older civilizations of Africa and Asia."
In setting history straight, the author has explored a wealth of ancient and classical as well as contemporary literature. From 276 sources, ranging from Diodorus Siculus and Herodotus to Robert Briffault, Sir E.A. Wallis Budge, Charles Darwin, W.E.B. DuBois, Sir James George Frazer, Joseph McCabe, Lewis Henry Morgan, Bertrand Russell, Lewis Spence, and H. G. Wells, Jackson has drawn some soundly documented, often iconoclastic conclusions.
He shows, for example: • How Professor Arnold Toynbee's statement that the black race has made no creative contribution to civilization is false • The evidence for and against the lost continents of Lemuria, Mu, and Atlantis • Why the environment is a limiting agent rather than a determining one • How all available information isagainst the theory of male superiority
• Why the scientists who looked chiefly to Asia as the original home of man were wrong • How the myths of Egypt reveal an astro-religious system, and tell a true story of astronomical progress,calendar correction,and theological changes before the time of written history • Why a civilization may slip backward, but must be pushed to go forward
Man, God, and Civilization is a fascinating primer in geology, archaeology, biology, and sociology, as well as a sophisticated anthropological study. To every student of man's nature and history, it offers a wealth of controversial, provocative material derived from authoritative sources and solid scholarship.
